1
Chapter 1
A hooded silhouette stood motionless amid the flowing river of pedestrians, watching.
"Karim," Aya whispered, holding her gaze firmly on the table. "Three o'clock. Don't be obvious."
The blue neon of Yana Café cast an electric haze across the tiled terrace, transforming the evening crowd into silhouettes against the amber glow inside. Aya Amrani lifted her Turkish coffee, inhaling the cardamom-laced steam before taking a careful sip. 
Karim stiffened. "I don’t see anything," he whispered. “We only landed a few hours ago. How could they already be onto us?”
"Building corner. Hooded figure." Aya tilted her head toward Amina on her right, who was scrolling through her phone with affected boredom. "Amina, do you clock them?"
Amina Said glanced up, her expression bored as she scanned the street with practiced nonchalance. The faded markings on her forearm, remnants of their encounter with a djinn in Morocco just over two weeks ago, were visible beneath her pushed-up sleeves. She took a long sip of her mint tea before answering.
Before flying to Turkey, the three of them had witnessed the opening of a sealed crypt in Morocco’s Atlas Mountains and watched djinn materialize before their eyes from another plane of existence. Aegis had funded the dig, hoping the Amulet of Idris could tap raw energy from the djinn’s realm to rewrite the world’s power grid. 
Instead, the spirits unleashed nearly caused environmental collapse across Morocco, took their friend Abdelatif with them into the void, and forced them to destroy the amulet before the death toll spread beyond Morocco. They escaped with scars: Amina’s permanent mark that still tingled like static, Karim’s nightmares of being trapped in the collapsing tunnel system beneath the Atlas Mountains. 
Aegis had lost its super-battery as their base went into full meltdown in the Sahara, but not its appetite. Now, the shadowy organization that had pulled their strings wanted another relic, and Istanbul’s streets were the next hunting ground.
"Yeah, I’ve noticed him," Amina said as a roguish grin shot across her face. "He’s been there since just after we arrived. Hasn’t moved. I’ll deal with him, habibi."
Amina moved to stand, but Aya's hand shot out, gripping her wrist. "Don't. Too many witnesses."
"I wasn't going to create a scene," Amina argued, but settled back into her chair. “I hate sitting around waiting.”
Karim, unable to resist, turned his head fully toward the figure. By the time his eyes found the spot, there was nothing but empty space where the watcher had stood. "I think they're gone," he said, turning back to the table. “Did you see where they went?”
"No. Perfect timing," Aya said, banging her fist on the table. "Just as we start paying attention."
"Salah warned us they'd be here," Karim said quietly. "Aegis doesn't let go."
"Then they're after the same thing we are." Aya's jaw tightened. "The Lens."
Amina's hand drifted toward the markings on her arm. "We beat them to it. I'm not interested in a repeat of Morocco."
"Neither am I," Aya said. "That's why we're here."
The waiter, a lanky kid with a long ponytail and a barrage of silver earrings, glided up, carrying a sweating pitcher.
“Başka bir şey ister misiniz?” he asked.
“Şimdilik sadece su, teşekkürler,” Karim answered.
The waiter refilled their water glasses and was gone.
"Nice Turkish," Aya said, pushing Karim’s shoulder.
"A few weeks of intensive study will do that," Karim replied with a smirk. "But it’s really not good. Still, the basics might be useful, and there wasn’t any more research I could do until we were here on the ground."
"Better than my pathetic attempts." Aya leaned forward, lowering her voice. "So, are we going to talk about the plan here?"
"Dismantling a global shadow organization that's hunting ancient artifacts of immense power with only your father’s incomplete research as our guide? It’s not complicated," Amina said dryly. "Just another Tuesday."
Karim laughed. “Oh yes, and just the simple revelation that djinn are real, and who knows what other supernatural terrors await...” His voice trailed off, but they all felt the weight of his unfinished thought.
"That's why we're here," Aya said. "To make sure it never happens again. The Astral Lens, if we’re right and that’s what they’re after in Turkey, stays out of their hands."
Amina's dark eyes flicked to Aya's face, then away. "Quite the career change we’ve had. Though I’m not sure I like the new benefits package."
“People do change,” Aya said, fingers drumming the handle of her cup. “Six months ago, I’d have stolen the Lens and sold it to some megalomaniac like Léglise.”
Amina’s mouth curved into a half-smirk. “Radical rebranding: Aya Amrani, inconveniently heroic.”
Karim laughed. “Hero or thief, labels are flexible. I, for one, still consider myself an archaeologist and historian. But what matters is we’re a team now, and we have an enemy to stop. The three of us here, plus Salah and Omar.”
“Then I suppose we won’t waste time arguing old résumés. Or thinking about past experiences too much.” Amina’s gaze lingered on Aya before sliding away. Amina rubbed at the markings on her arm, which looked to any outsider like a series of Amazigh-inspired tattoos.
"How have they been?" Aya asked quietly, nodding toward Amina's arm.
Amina's hand stilled. "Manageable."
"That's not what I asked."
"They hurt sometimes," Amina admitted. "More when I dream. It wakes me up."
Aya felt an urge to reach across the table, to offer comfort, to wrap her former partner in an embrace. But that was the past. Before everything had changed. Before Karim. The murky waters of their new dynamic made her hesitate, her hand hovering momentarily before retreating to her coffee cup.
Karim cleared his throat, clearly sensing the sudden shift in atmosphere. "Should we head back to drop off our things? It’s time to check into the apartment, and there's still daylight to explore our new neighborhood. Who knows how long we’ll be here."
"Always the practical one," Aya said. “Yeah, it’ll be good to at least enjoy the area before we have our next steps."
They settled their bill and stepped out onto the tiled streetside terrace of the café, now getting much busier than when they'd arrived. The early evening air carried a hint of salt water from the nearby port, mixed with the aromas pouring out of restaurants’ open windows.
A ten-minute walk later, they were climbing the stairs to the fourth floor of a hilltop Ottoman building near Moda. The trendy neighborhood on Istanbul's Asian side was a world away from the tourist-packed streets of Taksim across the water. The apartment door opened to reveal a stylish living space with a blend of contemporary furnishings and traditional architecture. Three comfortable sofas surrounded a wooden coffee table, with floor-to-ceiling windows offering sweeping views over the sprawling city.
"Omar outdid himself," Aya said, moving immediately to the windows and pulling one open to let in the breeze. The rooftops of Kadıköy spread before them, the Bosphorus glittering in the distance, and beyond that, the silhouette of Istanbul's European side with its minarets and towers puncturing the darkening sky.
Somewhere out there, hidden in the bones of this ancient city, lay the Astral Lens, the device her father had spent years researching before his death. Çelebi's masterwork. Capable of channeling energy from deep space itself, focusing power that made the Amulet of Idris look like a spark beside a bonfire.
If Aegis found it first, Morocco would indeed be a footnote.
The plan was simple: find the Lens before they did, then vanish. Leave Turkey. Hide the components somewhere Aegis would never reach. Guard them forever if necessary. No activation, no catastrophe, no more supernatural fallout.
Her father had died before finishing his research. Now it was hers to complete. And to bury.
"Not bad for a carpet salesman," Amina said.
"Well, he’s always been just a bit more than a carpet salesman," Aya said with a laugh. "And hopefully his connections are as invaluable as he thinks."
Karim threw open the sliding terrace doors. “So far,” he said, taking in the view, “I have complete faith in him.”
"I'll take this room," Amina said, claiming the bedroom closest to the fire escape.
“Three exits,” Aya noted. “Not bad.”
Karim tilted his head toward the next room, with another terrace and views out over the Bosphorus. “That one’s huge. Care to share?”
Aya smirked. “You need to ask?”
He shrugged and scratched his head. “Professional courtesy.”
Amina’s voice drifted from her doorway. “You two negotiate sleeping arrangements; I’m testing the fire escape, just in case.”
Aya tossed her a salute, then turned to Karim. “Try not to steal all the blankets this time.”
“No promises.”
They stashed their passports in a hollow book, slid their notebooks and research beneath a couch cushion, and rigged Amina’s micro-tripwire across the terrace door.
Karim snapped the last sensor into place. “Perimeter secure. Time for some street reconnaissance? I've been reading about Tellalzade Street," he said. "It's not far, and it’s known for vintage shops and record stores. And Kadıköy Square is supposed to be amazing this time of day. We could check those out, then get dinner?"
Aya took a long look, surveying the apartment and their security measures. Satisfied, she nodded.
"Lead the way, Professor," Amina said.
They spent the next hour wandering through Kadıköy's streets. The district felt crowded and alive, but relaxed. Students with colorfully dyed hair moved between cafés, street musicians competed with the sounds of conversations and laughter, vendors sold everything from antique books to handcrafted jewelry, while packed bars and restaurants dotted every corner. 
The streets themselves were a maze. Some wide boulevards were lined with plane trees; other narrow alleys were barely wide enough for two people to pass, but all sloped dramatically toward the Sea of Marmara below. When they reached Tellalzade Street, it charmed them with narrow passages lined with shops selling vintage clothing, antique cameras, and records from decades past. 
Aya watched with amusement as Karim excitedly examined a collection of Ottoman-era photographs, remarking on every detail. "So you’re in your element?" she asked when he finally tore himself away.
"A couple of months ago, I was analyzing pottery shards in the dust and heat with no prospect of it ever meaning anything," Karim said, taking her hand as they walked. "Now I'm on an adventure in Istanbul, a place I’ve always wanted to visit, tracking an ancient Lens with mysterious powers, along with two beautiful master thieves. Life is not bad.”
Amina was several paces behind them, either examining a shop window with intense focus or deliberately giving them space. Aya couldn’t decide. The distance she maintained wasn't just physical. Since they had defeated Léglise and sealed the djinn away for good in the Atlas, Amina had kept an emotional barrier firmly in place.
"I don’t know what’s up with her," Aya said quietly. “She has flashes of normalcy, but then she’s so distant.”
"She’s still processing. And aren't we all?" Karim said. “She’ll come around.”
They continued to Kadıköy Square, and the energy intensified. Street performers entertained crowds with acrobatics, and food vendors filled the air with the smell of grilled meat. Near the tram stop, a vendor in a red fez rang a brass bell, flipping his elastic ice-cream cones on a long metal paddle just out of reach of giggling tourists. 
A clarinetist accompanied by a saz player threaded a haunting Anatolian melody through the square; every so often, the clarinet swooped into a jazz-like riff that set the crowd clapping. A drummer with a worn davul kept the pulse, his heel stomping the pavement.
As they walked through the square, taking it all in, the sun began to set, painting the city in gold and amber.
"There's a traditional meyhane nearby that Omar has on his list of recommendations. Piraye Taş Plak. Apparently, they play classic Turkish records while you eat, and the food’s good."
"Sounds perfect," Aya agreed. "Amina?"
Amina, who had been photographing a wall of street art, nodded. "I could eat. Do they have alcohol? I could go for a drink."
“Indeed, they do,” Karim said. “Let’s go.”
The meyhane was warm, intimate, with walls covered in vintage travel posters and shelves laden with old records. A phonograph in the corner played traditional music, the crackling quality of the recording creating an atmosphere out of time.
They were seated at a corner table and presented with menus. Karim ordered a selection of mezze and a bottle of anise-flavored rakı, Turkey's national drink.
The bottle of rakı arrived, clear as spring water, with tall, narrow glasses alongside a pitcher of still water.
Karim filled their glasses and lifted his own first. “Birinci kadeh, first glass. To finding the truth before Aegis does.”
They clinked. Aya took a cautious sip, eyes watering. “Tastes like a black-licorice bonfire.” 
Karim laughed. “Come on, you’ve been to Turkey before.” He tapped the water pitcher.
“I’m not usually somewhere long enough to enjoy the culture.” She added water, and the liquid bloomed to a milky white. “Ah, science is magic.”
Amina raised her untreated glass of pure rakı. “Here’s to the woman who convinced thieves to play heroes! Try not to get us killed, Amrani.”
Karim banged the table. “Şerefe, cheers.”
More clinks, bigger swigs, and rounds of laughter.
Aya exhaled, cheeks flushing. “Why do people drink this voluntarily?”
Karim laughed. “We’re probably supposed to pace it. Mezze, water, conversation.”
“Well, too late for that,” Aya rasped, still smiling.
Servers covered the table in waves. Glossy eggplant smothered in garlic sauce. Midye dolma, mussels snapped open and stuffed with cinnamon-scented rice and pine nuts. Tender, raw çiğ köfte rolled in crisp lettuce. Grilled octopus drizzled with olive oil and lemon.
They devoured the feast in near silence until plates were cleared and a bottle of wine appeared.
"Oh, thank god. I'll switch to some wine, and you two can finish the rakı," Aya said, pouring a large glass. "Omar's contact should reach out tomorrow. Someone who's been tracking Çelebi's work for years. Until then—"
Her phone buzzed against the table. She glanced at the screen, then sat up straighter.
"Salah?" Karim asked.
Aya nodded, scanning the encrypted message. Her expression darkened.
"What is it?" Amina leaned forward.
"He intercepted new Aegis communications. They've confirmed a recovery team in Istanbul. At least six operatives currently, more soon." Aya scrolled further. "And they're not just looking for the Lens. They already know it was built in pieces. Multiple components, hidden separately. Çelebi scattered them after the Sultan destroyed his observatory."
Karim refilled his rakı glass. "So it's a race for fragments."
"A race we have to win." Aya set the phone face down. "Who knows what happens if they assemble even part of it..."
"They won't," Amina said, taking the rakı bottle from Karim's hand and topping off her own glass. "We got to the Amulet first. We'll get to this."
"Salah says one more thing." Aya's voice dropped. "They've got a lead we don't. Something about Çelebi's original workshop location. He's trying to intercept more, but—"
"But we're already behind," Amina finished.
Silence settled over the table. The phonograph crackled through a song change.
“I’m getting worried about his contacts. We need to move,” Aya said.
"Well then, may he hurry the hell up," Amina said finally, lifting her glass. "But for now, here's to the plan, whatever's left of it.”
The evening progressed with more food, more rakı, and more white wine. After three more rounds of drinks and two record changes, the album switched to Barış Manço’s deep voice. Aya felt a knot between her shoulders loosen.
Karim drummed his fingers on the table in sync with the bass line. “Feels good not to sprint for our lives.”
Amina leaned back, swirling her rakı. “A quick ceasefire with reality.” Her eyes locked on Aya, light but firm.
Aya lifted her glass in reply. “Then here’s to savoring the armistice. Tomorrow we’re back in the trenches. But for now, there’s nowhere I’d rather be.”
Amina locked eyes with Aya, flashing a wide grin that she couldn’t help but return. Aya looked away, then back, unable to break their eye contact before Karim placed his hand on her knee under the table.
It was nearly midnight when they finally left the meyhane, pleasantly full and slightly drunk. The streets of Kadıköy were still alive, but they opted to head back to the apartment, aware that the next day would be full and heavy.
They climbed the stairs to their floor, Karim fumbling with the keys, Amina still humming the last song from the phonograph. For a moment, standing in the dim hallway outside their door, Aya let herself enjoy the simple pleasure of the evening. Good food, good company, the promise of real work ahead.
Tomorrow, they would begin the search in earnest. But tonight belonged to rakı and laughter and the warmth of knowing they weren't facing this alone.
Chapter 2
Aya awoke before Karim, slipping from their bed without disturbing him. She found Amina already up, sitting cross-legged on the terrace, eyes closed in meditation.
Aya hesitated before joining her, not wanting to interrupt. She was turning to leave when Amina spoke without opening her eyes.
"Still creeping around like a cat, I see."
"Old habits," Aya replied, stepping onto the terrace. The morning air was crisp, light. "How long have you been up?"
"Couple of hours." Amina finally opened her eyes. "Couldn't sleep. I never can lately."
Amina rose to her feet in one fluid motion. "Coffee's made."
They moved to the kitchen, where Amina poured thick Turkish coffee into small cups. The rich aroma filled the space, momentarily displacing the tension of the previous night.
"You still take it black?" Amina asked.
"That’ll never change.” Aya accepted the cup.
Their fingers brushed during the exchange, and Aya felt Amina's eyes linger on her for a moment too long before she turned away.
Karim emerged from the bedroom, hair tousled from sleep. "Morning," he mumbled, making a beeline for the coffee. "Any disturbances?"
"All quiet," Amina reported.
"What's the plan for today?" he asked, accepting a cup from Amina with a grateful nod.
"First, breakfast at a café, watch the streets, see who’s tailing us," Aya said. "There’s no telling when Omar will get in touch with our contact here in Istanbul, so we need to be ready.”
"So, sip coffee while stressed about surveillance, ready to spring into action any time. That sounds relaxing," Amina added dryly.
Forty minutes later, they were seated at an outdoor table at Rafine, a sleek café with concrete floors, exposed brick walls, and artfully primitive wooden tables. The clientele was young and alternative, wearing ripped fishnets, chunky boots, and covered in tattoos.
"Easy to forget this is an ancient city," Karim said, looking around at the thoroughly modern scene.
"That's Istanbul," Aya said, breaking off a piece of her simit, a circular bread covered in sesame seeds. "Twenty-seven centuries of history mingling with hipster coffee shops, pubs, and tech startups."
She paused mid-bite. Across the street, partially obscured by a newspaper kiosk, stood a familiar hooded figure.
"Four o'clock," she said casually, not changing her relaxed demeanor. "Our friend is back."
Karim didn't turn, having learned his lesson. Instead, he picked up his phone as if to check messages, angling it to use the reflection in its dark screen. "Same as yesterday?"
"Can't tell from here," Aya said, taking a deliberate sip of her coffee. "But same build, same position. Just watching, not approaching."
Amina stretched languidly, using the motion to scan their surroundings. "One more possible. Behind us, blue jacket, pretending to read on his phone."
"What's our play?" Karim asked, panic creeping into his voice.
Aya considered their options. "Amina, when I stand up, I want you to head toward the market. Take the long way around. See if blue jacket follows. Karim, you pay the bill, then head back to the flat. I’ll approach our hooded friend."
"Isn’t splitting up usually how people get killed?" Karim asked.
"It's controlled," Aya replied. "We need to know who they're most interested in. And if it's all of us, we need to know how many operatives they have.”
Aya stood first, dropping her napkin deliberately, using the moment as she bent to retrieve it to scan their surroundings one final time.
"Ready?" she asked.
Amina nodded almost imperceptibly, then rose and headed south toward the Kadıköy fish market. As predicted, the man in the blue jacket pocketed his phone, waited twenty seconds, then followed.
Aya set a two-hundred lira note on the saucer, then whispered, “See you soon.” She strolled away while Karim waited for the card reader.
She crossed the street, keeping the kiosk between herself and the hooded man. The figure clocked her advance, turned, and slipped into a side lane. Aya followed at a brisk tourist’s pace—phone raised, snapping idle photos—until the lane spat her into the fish market’s roar of ice and gulls.
The hooded man drifted through the stalls, never faster than the crowd, always one vendor ahead. Aya mirrored the path, weaving past crates of sea bass, letting a tourist group create cover. When the man darted left toward a stack of Styrofoam boxes, Aya cut right, circling a row of shrimp tanks to intercept.
She arrived at the junction just in time to see the figure swing onto a fire escape and ascend, two rungs at a time.
Aya debated the chase, alone on unfamiliar terrain. She sprinted, boots clanging on steel, and popped onto the roof in time to glimpse the figure vault a gap and disappear down a stairwell three buildings away. 
Aya drew up short and caught her breath before her phone vibrated twice in rapid succession.
Karim: Inside. The apartment is still clear. You safe?
Amina: Lost tail near the ferry. Heading back now. Keep eyes up.
Aya sighed and typed a quick message: On my way. Rooftops gave me the slip. Then, she started the trek home.
Chapter 3
When Aya arrived back at the apartment, Karim was waiting, but they found no sign of Amina. Aya checked her phone. No new messages. The apartment appeared undisturbed, alarms still in place, security functioning.
"Should we be worried?" Karim asked, checking the time. "It's been almost an hour."
"Give her time," Aya said, though concern had begun to gnaw at her. "She knows how to handle herself."
"This isn't how I imagined my first proper day in Istanbul," Karim said.
"No guided tours of the Hagia Sophia?" Aya asked.
"Maybe later," he replied with a small smile. "After we figure out who's trying to kill us."
"They're not trying to kill us," Aya said. "Not yet, at least. They're assessing. Testing our capabilities. Monitoring."
"Is that supposed to be comforting?"
"Yeah. It means we have time."
Karim nodded, then went to retrieve his notebook from its hiding place. He spread his notes across the coffee table, focusing on the task at hand as a way to channel his nervous energy.
"I can’t just pace around waiting and stressing. I’m going to dive back in. If we assume Çelebi was working in this area during the Ottoman period," he said, "there must be records. The Ottomans were meticulous bureaucrats."
Aya joined him, grateful for the distraction, pushing concern for Amina temporarily aside. "Where would those records be kept now?"
"Most likely the Başbakanlık Ottoman Archives, on the European side. There might be local records here in Kadıköy, though, especially since Çelebi lived and worked in this area. That’s what I’m looking for.”
Aya pulled out her phone, and they got lost in a search for possible records rooms that could house the historical documents, if they still existed.
The sound of keys in the lock drew them both to their feet. Amina entered, looking disheveled but unharmed.
"Well, you're late," Aya said with a grin, trying to suppress the fear and relief in her voice.
"Had to make sure I wasn't followed," Amina replied, dropping onto the sofa. "Blue jacket was good. Really good. I thought I’d lost him when I sent the message, but then he appeared again. I managed to reverse it on him, though, and tailed him after he thought he lost me."
"Did you learn anything?" Karim asked.
She leaned forward. "Blue jacket met with someone else, a woman we haven’t seen before. Early-thirties, auburn hair chopped to the jaw, dressed more venture-capital than field operative. I didn’t see her face, but I could pick either of them out.”
Aya met her eyes, a silent understanding passing between them. It was familiar, that look, and for a moment, it was as if nothing had changed between them.
"That’s good,” Aya said. “For now, I suggest we separate and explore different areas. Make it harder for them to track all of us."
"Are you sure?" Karim questioned. "After what just happened?"
"Controlled separation," Aya said. "I'll walk the older parts of Kadıköy, look for historical buildings that would be the right age. Ask people if they’ve been seeing anything strange lately. Karim, check out some of the bookshops. You said Tellalzade has antique book dealers that we didn’t get to, right? See if you can find any local histories that mention astronomical studies during the Ottoman period. Who knows what the shopkeepers will say, who they may know. Who else may have been around asking questions."
"And me?" Amina asked.
"Take a break," Aya said softly. "You've been on high alert since Morocco. Walk the shoreline, look at the water, clear your head."
Amina looked like she might protest, but then nodded, understanding the tactical advantage of having one team member appear less engaged.
"Keep your phones on, check in regularly," Aya instructed. "And Amina—"
"I know," Amina interrupted. "Be careful. I've been doing this as long as you have, remember?"
"Longer, if I remember correctly," Aya said.
Amina rolled her eyes. “So, I’ll take my veteran rest, I suppose.”
"Meet back here at five," Aya said. "Hopefully, we have word from Omar by then about how to proceed. We need to get after this Astral Lens. I can’t believe it’s still radio silence; I’m getting worried something’s gone wrong."




After making her way down the steep street, the Moda shoreline stretched before Amina, the Bosphorus a glittering expanse of blue-green water dotted with ferries and container ships. She walked alone, a small paper cup in hand, taking occasional sips of the strong, over-sweetened Turkish coffee.
The boardwalk was busy with joggers, elderly couples walking arm in arm, and young people skateboarding, lounging on benches, staring at their phones, or out at the sea.
Amina found an empty bench overlooking the water and sat, allowing herself to enjoy the simple pleasure of sunlight on her face. It had been three weeks since Morocco. Three weeks of looking over her shoulder, waking from nightmares with the sensation of ancient power crawling beneath her skin, controlling her.
She rolled up her sleeve, examining the markings on her forearm. In the bright sunlight, they were barely visible, faded like an old tattoo, a ghostly reminder.
She traced one of the patterns with her finger, and for a brief, disorienting moment, felt a flutter of awareness that wasn't her own, an echo of the djinn's presence that had nearly consumed her in the Atlas Mountains.
"Still there, aren't you?" she whispered. "Just waiting. But what do you want with me?"
The sensation faded as quickly as it had come, leaving her alone again with the sounds of the sea and traffic.
And then there was Aya, playing on the back of her mind.
Seeing her again had been harder than Amina anticipated, and watching the intimacy she shared with Karim stirred emotions Amina had thought were safely buried beneath collapsed tombs and passing time. Desire she was used to, but jealousy was new. She always took what she wanted, even if it was off limits, but this was different.
For nearly two years, they'd been lovers and partners. Passionate, volatile, and ultimately unsustainable, as both pursued their separate agendas in their high-stakes world. Their relationship had ended abruptly after a job in Egypt went sideways. Amina had made it out on her own—she always did—but the betrayal had severed whatever bond they'd forged. They'd gone their separate ways.
Until Morocco forced them back together, their lives fully enmeshed for the foreseeable future, within reach but unable to touch.
Amina finished her coffee, crumpling the cup in her fist. Dwelling on the past wouldn't help their current situation. Aya had moved on. So would she. She had to. But a large part of her didn’t want to. That part wanted to make a move before it was too late.
But Amina knew she needed to be dialed in. She’d never been such a mess on a job before, and no job had ever felt as important. It was time to be herself again, which also happened to be the version Aya had fallen for. 
She stood, prepared to continue her walk, when something made her pause. A prickling sensation at the base of her skull. The awareness of being watched that had kept her alive countless times.
Casually, she bent to retie her boot, using the movement to scan her surroundings. The boardwalk remained filled with ordinary people going about their day. No one was paying particular attention to her.
Yet the feeling persisted.
Amina continued walking, maintaining an unhurried pace while remaining hyperaware of her environment. 
She took a deliberate wrong turn, doubling back along a different street to see if anyone altered their course to follow her. Nothing.
She was about to dismiss the feeling as paranoia when an old man coming from the opposite direction bumped into her shoulder as he passed, making them each stumble. As they did, he leaned close, his breath warm against her ear, stubble sharp against her cheek.
"Aegis is watching," he whispered. "Wizard Pub. Tonight. 8:00."
By the time she turned, he had disappeared into the crowd. They were no longer simply being watched.
Chapter 4
"Wizard Pub. Tonight. Eight." 
Amina's words hung in the air of their apartment like smoke.
Karim paced the living room, hands raking through his hair. "It's obviously a trap."
Aya pulled up Google Maps and studied the neighborhood around the bar, mentally mapping exit routes. "Maybe. But it's also the first direct lead we've had since we arrived."
"From a random old man who bumped into Amina on the street?" Karim's voice rose. "Who just happens to know about Aegis? And happens to know where we should go?"
"That's exactly why it feels legitimate," Amina countered, leaning against the wall. "If Aegis wanted to trap us, they wouldn't be this obvious. They've been tracking us since we arrived and could have grabbed us at any time."
Aya nodded. "They want information, not us. At least not yet. I think there’s a better chance this man is on our side."
Karim stopped pacing. "Or they're making it so obvious that we think exactly that, and we essentially turn ourselves over.”
"I don’t think so," Aya said. "This is a heavy metal bar, which tells me it’ll be far too loud for anyone tailing us to hear our conversation. That’s a good sign. Plus, we need to know who's contacting us and why. This man might know who has us under surveillance. Sitting here isn't giving us answers, and we still haven’t heard from Omar."
"We could scout it first," Amina suggested. "Check the place, see if there's anything suspicious before committing."
"Okay. Assuming we go," Karim said, "we need a signal. A way to communicate if things go sideways. And a rendezvous point other than this apartment." He ran his fingers along the spines of books on the shelf absently.
Amina pushed herself off the wall. "I'll go alone. Less risk that way."
"Absolutely not," Aya and Karim said together.
A ghost of a smile crossed Amina's face. "At least you two agree on something."
"We go together or not at all," Aya said firmly. “Too risky now.”
Karim checked his watch. "It's almost seven. If we're doing this, we need to decide."
The Bosphorus glittered through the windows as Istanbul’s lights began to illuminate the darkening sky.
"Well, I vote we go," Aya finally said. "But armed. And with a clear escape plan, like Karim said."
Amina nodded. "Agreed."
They both looked at Karim, who exhaled slowly. "Fine. But at the first sign of trouble—"
"We're gone," Aya finished. "No heroics."
Karim nodded. “I guess a metal bar in Kadıköy is less scary than a US military base in the remote Sahara.”
Thirty minutes later, they stood across the street from Wizard Pub. The bar's illuminated sign hung over the entrance, which was slightly below street level, where groups of black-clad patrons clustered, cigarettes glowing like fireflies in the darkness. Loud guitars and guttural screams poured straight onto the pavement.
"Perfect cover," Amina replied. "Loud music, dark interior.”
Aya scanned the street once more. "Remember the plan. One hour max, unless anything changes. We stick together. Anything goes wrong, we have our headings and regroup point."
“Check and check,” Karim said.
They crossed the street, the music growing louder as they approached. Inside, the pub was exactly as its exterior promised—dimly lit, brick walls lined with black band posters, the air thick with smoke and the scent of beer. Blue lighting cast a fantastical glow over the crowd of black t-shirts, jeans, and leather.
The main room was lined by small wooden tables and benches, each overflowing with groups downing pints of Efes, laughing and nodding their heads to the music. To their right, a long bar stretched along the wall, showcasing an impressive array of spirits and decorated with animal skulls and horns. 
Behind it stood a bartender with long hair and a beard, wearing a black shirt emblazoned with the phrase "Mors Certa, Hora Incerta.” Death is certain, but its hour is not.
"Where exactly are we supposed to meet this mysterious contact?" Karim asked, leaning close to be heard over the thundering bass.
"They didn't specify," Amina said. "I assume they'll find us." She looked them up and down. “Shouldn’t be difficult; you two stand out a bit. A sundress seemed appropriate for a metal bar?”
Aya shrugged. "I save my black tactical gear for more pressing times. Let's get drinks. Act natural."
They approached the bar, where the hulking bartender greeted them with a friendly smile. "Ne içmek istersiniz?"
"Üç bira, lütfen. Efes," Karim replied, holding up three fingers.
The bartender nodded, pouring three pints of the malt beer and sliding them across the counter. Karim paid in cash, and they found an empty table in the back along the wall, positioning themselves with clear views of both the entrance and the bar.
"Now what?" Karim asked after they'd settled.
"We wait," Aya said, taking a sip of her beer. "And watch. And enjoy ourselves."
They didn't have to wait long. Twenty minutes later, as Aya was about to get up for another round of drinks, a woman approached their table carrying four pints. She was striking. In her early thirties, with sharp features, elaborate eyeliner, and dark hair cut in a severe bob that framed her face. She wore a faded Turkish Iron Maiden t-shirt. A silver necklace with an unusual geometric pendant hung at her throat.
"Mind if I join you?" she asked in lightly accented English, not waiting for an answer before sliding onto the bench next to Karim and passing the beers around.
The three exchanged glances. Amina's hand disappeared beneath the table, likely reaching for her knife.
Aya spoke cautiously. "Thanks. Are you a fan of Moroccan carpets?"
The woman smiled. "Only if they're genuinely handwoven from outside Fez."
Aya’s posture relaxed. "Omar sent you?"
"Not directly," the woman said. "But yes, I'm your contact. My name is Leyla Yilmaz."
"How do you know Omar?" Karim asked.
“The trade, mainly through Abdelatif,” Leyla said. “But we first met in person after I got caught between customs officials and a shipment of Roman glass that ‘accidentally’ disappeared from the Ankara Museum’s restoration lab. Omar knew the right bribes, I knew the packing slips, and together we slipped the crates back into the museum storeroom before anyone filed a report. Since then, whenever antiquities go missing—or need to—he’s my first call.”
At the mention of Abdelatif, a somber silence had fallen over the table.
"And after what happened in Morocco," Leyla continued, lowering her voice, "this time, Omar reached out through a more secure channel."
"Why all the cloak and dagger?" Amina asked. "Why not just call us?"
Leyla leaned forward, her voice barely audible over the music. "Because Omar fears that there are eyes and ears on him at all times, and they’d easily be able to monitor any digital communication. That would extend to the three of you and any well-known associates. We have to be careful.”
Her fingers drummed on the table before continuing. "He passed word that they're watching his shop, his riad, monitoring his communications. Salah too. They’re being extremely cautious. He's trying to work around it with Salah's help, developing more secure methods of communication, but for now, he's using a network of contacts to relay messages."
"And you're one of these contacts?" Karim's skepticism was evident.
"I am. I’m the friend he told you about in Istanbul." Leyla took a folded piece of paper from her pocket and slid it across the table to Aya. "This should prove it. I received coded instructions from him two days ago, along with this."
Aya unfolded the paper to find a sketch of a familiar pattern. It was the same marking that had been on the Amulet of Idris in Morocco. Below it was a note in Omar's unmistakable handwriting: "The flower opens at midnight. Trust her. -O."
Aya nodded, passing the note to Karim and Amina. "Okay. We're listening."
Leyla smiled, revealing dimples on her cheeks. "Good. Because I've been researching Hazarfen Çelebi for years, and his alleged creation of the Astral Lens. It's said to be a device, like a cosmic magnifier, that’s designed to focus energy from specific planetary alignments into a controllable beam."
"We’ve heard the same about the Lens. What kind of alignments?" Karim asked, leaning forward.
"Mars, Jupiter, and Saturn forming a perfect triangle with the Pleiades as backdrop," Leyla replied. "The texts I’ve seen describe it as capable of harnessing extreme radioactive energy, pulling from deep space. Like a solar panel, but not limited to the sun and able to pull from the entire universe."
"And Aegis wants it because they’re hungry to control energy production," Aya concluded. "Yes, that aligns with everything we know so far."
"Apparently," Leyla said, “but Omar’s message and this chat are the first I’m hearing of this group, the Aegis.”
Karim leaned in. “Well, Aegis is a quasi‑corporate black‑ops outfit. They bankroll strategic digs to go after mythological powers, front as philanthropists, then weaponize whatever they can grab.”
“A month ago in Morocco,” Aya added, voice low, “they opened a djinn crypt in the Atlas to test an artifact called the Amulet of Idris. The power that was unleashed was devastating, and we barely stopped them. With it, they thought they could control global energy production.”
Leyla’s eyes widened. “So that chaos report in the Marrakech papers about wildfires, storms, collapsed buildings, that was you?”
“Mostly them,” Amina said, downing her second beer. “We just made sure it didn’t get worse.”
Leyla swallowed hard. “Understood. If Aegis is on Çelebi’s trail too, we’re on a clock. But, I doubt they have much. Everyone who has ever searched has hit dead ends, and mainstream academia has dismissed Çelebi's work as fantasy for centuries. My research suggests otherwise."
"Why would academia dismiss it?" Karim asked.
Leyla grimaced. "Because it challenges the established narrative that Ottoman science was derivative of earlier Greek works. My own theories about Çelebi's advanced astronomical knowledge and alchemical experiments weren't well-received, and they’re unprecedented without much evidence. He and his team kept things very secretive."
"So, you were ostracized after publishing your theories and turned to a quiet, simple life of working with Omar and his associates?" Amina asked.
"Professionally executed," Leyla laughed with a bitter smile. "Denied tenure, publications rejected, research funding cut. But I’ve continued my work independently."
"Okay. And what have you found?" Aya asked.
“Çelebi’s trail kept circling back to the Asian side of the city,” Leyla said, tapping the table for emphasis, “but I’ve been missing some key details—”
“My father’s work might be able to help if we combine notes,” Aya interjected. “He was an archaeologist at UC Berkeley. He spent time chasing Çelebi references, too, apparently. We found a lot of research in his notes.”
“Berkeley.” Leyla’s head snapped up. “Hassan Amrani was your father?”
“You knew him?”
“I traded letters with a Dr Amrani until his replies stopped years ago,” Leyla said softly. “Brilliant marginalia. Sketches, cipher notes. He got further than anyone in understanding how Çelebi’s lens systems actually worked.”
“Yeah. He’s no longer with us,” Aya said, voice steady but tight. “Whatever he sent you is all that’s left of that research, in addition to the notebooks we have.”
Leyla nodded. “I’m so sorry to hear that, but I had my suspicions. Those emails and letters are at my flat. You can have them. Together, we might uncover the missing coordinates I’ve been hunting.”
For the next hour, they had another round of drinks while Leyla outlined her research, explaining how Çelebi had likely collaborated with Taqi al-Din at his observatory before it was demolished in 1580. How he had combined astronomical knowledge with glassmaking techniques, potentially creating a lens that could focus cosmic energy.
"The problem," she explained, "is that the lens was dismantled after Sultan Murad III ordered the observatory destroyed. The story is that Çelebi hid the components and his notes throughout Istanbul, scattered for his followers to continue the work following his persecution. These stashes could be in Istanbul, or potentially beyond. There are rumors of secret societies, but no proof. From there, the story dies."
"And you think you can find these components?" Karim asked.
"With your help, hopefully. If your father's notes contained elements I've been missing, references to Kadıköy that align with patterns I've observed, there’s a great chance." She turned to Aya. "I've made progress, but we need to keep pushing, work together if we’re going to solve this before Aegis."
"Okay, sure," Amina said bluntly. “But you seem eager to throw yourself into a pretty massive fight. Beyond some academic curiosity, what skin do you have in the game?”
Leyla met her gaze directly and took a long breath before answering. 
"I… if you must know, my colleague, Emre, disappeared three months ago while following a lead about Çelebi's workshop. The last message I received from him mentioned being followed by a man in a hood. I knew it had to be them once I received Omar’s communication about people hunting Çelebi’s work." Her voice hardened. "I want to find the lens before they do because it’s my life’s work, and I want to find Emre or get revenge on his behalf. He’s one of my oldest friends."
Amina studied Leyla’s expression, unblinking. But something in Leyla’s tone, equal parts grief and grit, decided for Aya. They had an ally, intel, and a starting point. After weeks of static, the compass needle twitched.
Aya lifted her glass. “To new partners and old ghosts.”
They all clinked glasses, and the beer went down like a small victory.
Leyla set down her empty glass with a decisive clink. "You know what? We should celebrate properly. Hit another bar after this one? Maybe somewhere with dancing?”
Aya shook her head. "We should get back. Plan our next moves, review what we know—"
"The real work starts tomorrow," Amina cut in. "And once we begin digging, Aegis will be all over us. We'll be locked in like Morocco for the foreseeable future." She leaned back, arms crossed. "One night of celebration before the fight. And, I will note, I can still take anyone Aegis throws at me, even five beers in."
"We don't know this area," Aya pressed. "We don't know her." She gestured toward Leyla. "No offense."
"None taken," Leyla said with a slight smile.
Karim touched Aya's arm. "She's Omar's contact. And Amina's right, who knows what we're about to throw ourselves into. One night won't hurt."
Aya looked between the three of them, outnumbered. She sighed. "Fine. One hour. Then we're done."
"That's the spirit, you party animal," Amina said dryly.
Karim approached the bar to settle the tab, as Leyla scurried behind him and murmured to the bartender. Moments later, four shots of an herb‑laced rakı variant appeared, green and wicked. Aya and Amina stood and followed them to the bar.
“One for the road,” Leyla said, raising her glass high. “I know a place with actual dancing, not just head‑banging. Let’s celebrate the start of great things.”
“I could definitely blow off a little steam,” Amina said.
They downed their beers and herbal-tasting shots before leaving the pub, following Leyla into the cool night.
“It’s just around the corner,” Leyla called back.
When they turned, however, they hit a living wall of bodies. Every bar on the narrow lane had blown its seams wide open. Patrons swarmed the cobblestones shoulder‑to‑shoulder, drinks sloshing as they danced. A DJ crammed into an upstairs window blasted Anatolian psych‑funk that rattled drainpipes and set the crowd surging in waves. Bare bulbs strung between balconies lit the street with a warm glow.
Leyla shouted above the groove, “This happens sometimes when the bars spill over, late at night. The whole street becomes one venue.”
“We might survive this job after all,” Amina said with a laugh.
Karim twirled Aya under a canopy of lights and a swirl of bodies, laughter breaking free.
"Well, maybe this was a better idea than I expected," Aya said.
Leyla shouted again over the music. "I'm going to grab drinks from that bar!" She pointed toward a narrow doorway across the street, its interior dark compared to the pulsing lights outside. "Four beers?"
"Make mine a gin and tonic," Aya called back.
Leyla flashed a thumbs-up and disappeared into the crowd.
She pushed through the packed doorway into a cramped bar, the music muffled but still vibrating through the walls. The space was narrow, lined with mismatched stools and a sticky wooden counter. She scanned the room.
One man sat alone in the far corner, gripping a mug of tea. Late forties, frameless glasses, close-cropped blonde hair, and an expensive watch catching the low light. His posture was relaxed, but his eyes tracked her the moment she entered.
Leyla approached the bar and ordered three Efes, along with a gin and tonic, from the bartender. Then she drifted toward the corner table, stopping just close enough to speak without being overheard.
"I'm in," she said quietly, rearranging the contents of her purse and wallet, not looking at him. "They trust me."
Malik's voice remained pleasant, conversational even, the tone someone might use to discuss weekend plans. But his eyes were flat, empty of anything human. "Good. How much do they know?"
"They have Hassan Amrani's research. The daughter has his notebooks. Combined with what I have, we'll find the components."
"Then you'll accelerate the timeline." It wasn't a question. "I'll deploy additional operatives to apply pressure. Keep them moving, keep them afraid. Fear makes people careless and fast."
Leyla's jaw tightened. "And Emre?"
Malik took a slow sip of his tea. "Emre remains comfortable. Fed. Unharmed." His eyes finally met hers, cold and flat. "That continues only as long as you remain useful."
"I'm doing everything you asked."
"You're doing what's necessary." He set down his glass. "When the alignment approaches, you will take the components from them and deliver them to me. All of them. No hesitation, no warnings, no sudden attacks of conscience."
"I understand."
"Do you?" Malik leaned forward slightly. "Because I've seen your file, Dr. Yilmaz. You're clever. Resourceful. The kind of person who thinks they can find a third option." His voice dropped. "There is no third option. Deliver the components, and Emre lives. Fail me, and I will send him back to you in pieces before I come take the Astral Lens for myself. Are we clear?"
Leyla's hands trembled at her sides, but her voice stayed steady. "We're clear."
"Then go. Get them moving now, the alignment approaches. And remember, I'll be watching every step."
Leyla steadied her breath as she pushed through the crowd toward the street, carefully balancing the drinks.
By the time she emerged into the pulsing lights, her expression had reset to easy warmth. She raised the glasses overhead, weaving back to Aya, Amina, and Karim.



Back in the street, the other three danced until sweat beaded into the neckline of Aya’s dress, Karim swaying his hips to the rhythm, and Amina keeping pace while her gaze never left Aya. 
As the music shifted to a pulsing electronic beat, Amina appeared at Aya's side, a dangerous glint in her eye. "Remember Bulgaria?" she asked, voice low enough that only Aya could hear.
Aya did remember. The underground club, the heat, the dress Amina had worn, the way she’d styled her curly hair. The way they’d gone back to their hotel room and made love repeatedly into the early hours of the morning, before everything had fallen apart between them. 
Before she could respond, Amina had pulled her into the crowd, their bodies pressed into a familiar rhythm.
They moved together, Amina's hands light on Aya's hips, bodies grinding close enough that Aya could smell the familiar scent of her hair, could feel an old physical desire course through her, almost uncontrollably.
The atmosphere shifted, charged with memory and possibility.
Aya glanced over her shoulder to find Karim watching them, his expression unreadable in the dim light. She caught his eye, and he raised his glass slightly, either in encouragement, confusion, or resignation. The moment stretched between them, uncertain.
Amina leaned closer, her lips near Aya's ear. "He's not the jealous type, is he?"
"We haven't had reason to find out.”
Amina's laugh was soft and knowing. "Maybe not yet. But I feel like pushing that boundary."
Aya’s mouth opened, unsure how to respond, when Leyla appeared beside them, her face flushed from dancing. "This is amazing!" she shouted over the music. "I haven't done this in—"
She stopped mid-sentence, her expression freezing as she looked past them toward a darkened doorway across the street. Aya turned to follow her gaze.
There, half-hidden in shadow, stood a hooded figure. 
A camera flash from the crowd glinted off metal, a suppressed pistol held low in their left hand. The light caught features for a split-second, but not long enough to identify before the darkness swallowed them again.
"We need to go," Leyla yelled over the music, reappearing and setting the drinks on a table. "We’re putting all these people in danger.”
“Shit, the hunt’s begun early,” Aya said.
Chapter 5
"Sorry for the mess," Leyla said, clearing a stack of books from a worn leather couch. "I don't usually have visitors to my apartment."
The space defied the sleek minimalism of their own rented apartment. Instead, it resembled an archive that had collided with both an art studio and a library.
"Don't apologize," Aya said, already scanning the room with undisguised interest. "This is impressive."
The apartment occupied the top floor of a crumbling 19th-century building, with high ceilings and peeling paint. What it lacked in modern amenities, it made up for in character—and contents.
Every surface held artifacts of Leyla's obsessions. Shelves overflowed with leather-bound manuscripts and academic journals; walls disappeared beneath overlapping maps adorned with pins and handwritten notes; a massive desk near the window supported a computer surrounded by stacks of photocopied images and documents.
They’d fled the street party twenty minutes ago and seemingly evaded any tails back to Leyla’s, which had seemed safer than their exposed apartment.
"Is that what I think it is?" Karim asked, pointing to a framed document hung prominently on the wall.
Leyla smiled. "An original copy of Cihannüma. Seventeenth century, but it references Hazarfen's theories."
Karim approached it reverently. "I've only seen digital reproductions."
"The university library had it gathering dust in storage," Leyla said. "When they fired me, I liberated it."
"You stole it?" Amina asked. “Okay, Leyla, I like your style even more.”
"I prefer 'rescued.'" Leyla moved to a walnut table in the center of the room. "They didn't value it. I do." She began clearing the surface, stacking papers and books on the floor.
Aya noticed a collection of corkboards leaning against one wall, pinned with newspaper clippings, photographs, maps, diagrams, all annotated in Leyla's cramped handwriting.
"This is thorough," Aya said.
"You can say it. Obsessive," Leyla replied without looking up. "I've been tracking Çelebi for almost ten years now. The academic community calls it a waste of time. A conspiracy theory. They're wrong."
Once the table was cleared, Leyla disappeared into another room, returning with a cardboard tube. "After what happened tonight, I think we need to accelerate our timeline. Aegis knows you're here, of course, and now they know we've connected. That was to be expected, but not so soon.”
She extracted and unrolled a large sheet of heavy paper across the table, weighing the corners with small metal sculptures. It was a woodcut reproduction, yellowed with age, depicting a domed structure surrounded by smaller buildings.
"This," Leyla said with reverence, "is Taqi al-Din's observatory in Istanbul, constructed in 1577 and demolished just three years later."
The detailed illustration showed an elaborate complex, with astronomers working at various instruments under a massive dome.
"Wait," Karim said, leaning closer. "Is that...?" He pointed to a figure sketched in the corner, partially obscured but distinct in posture and dress.
Leyla's eyes lit up. "You see it? Most scholars miss it entirely."
"The clothing doesn't match the other astronomers," Karim observed. "And he's standing apart from the others, not quite part of the official group."
"Exactly." Leyla's voice quickened with excitement. "I believe that's Hazarfen Çelebi—not yet known by that name—working unofficially with Taqi al-Din. The historical record shows no connection between them, but this piece suggests otherwise."
"Why would his presence be hidden?" Aya asked.
"Because his theories were radical, borderline heretical for the time," Leyla replied. "Most considered him, even his presence, dangerous. And Taqi al-Din was the Sultan's official astronomer, bound by orthodox interpretations. For them to be collaborating, al-Din would have been risking execution, because Çelebi was exploring something different."
Amina, who had been circling the apartment examining Leyla's collection, rejoined them. "Different how?"
Leyla moved to a glass display case and returned with a cloth-wrapped bundle. She unwrapped it carefully, revealing several ceramic shards with distinctive patterns in deep crimson, cobalt, and turquoise.
"These fragments were fired in the imperial Iznik workshops," she explained. "I found them buried near the observatory site depicted in this woodcut."
Karim gently lifted one of the shards, examining its glossy surface. "I specialize in pottery. The under-glaze crimson is unmistakable. This is definitely Iznik work, late 16th century."
"But look at the pattern," Leyla urged. "It's not floral or calligraphic like traditional Iznik pottery."
The piece Karim held featured an intricate geometric design surrounding what appeared to be a stylized lens or eye.
"These aren't decorative pieces," Leyla continued. "I believe they're prototypes—experiments in creating what Çelebi called 'celestial glass,' a material that could capture and focus cosmic energy during specific star alignments."
"You're suggesting Çelebi was working with the imperial potters to create components for the Astral Lens?”
"Precisely." Leyla placed the fragments back in their cloth. "Çelebi wasn't just an astronomer or an inventor. He was blending disciplines in ways no one had attempted before, combining astronomy, alchemy, and material science. All of this pulled from his extensive travels and connections to other similar thinkers from his time, like your Idris."
Karim’s eyes took on a familiar, wide glint. "If you're right, this would redefine our understanding of 16th-century scientific development."
"It would," Leyla agreed. "It challenges too many established narratives about Ottoman science for it to be popularly considered."
She returned to the table and unrolled a second document. It was a faded map of Istanbul with the Bosphorus dividing its European and Asian sides.
"According to my research, Çelebi continued his work in secret after the observatory's destruction. He established workshops on both sides of the city." She pointed to several locations marked in red ink. "These are sites where I've found references or evidence of his presence."
Aya studied the map, noting that marks clustered around Kadıköy. "So he did work primarily in this area?"
"I believe so," Leyla said, nodding. "But I've hit dead ends repeatedly. Maybe with your father’s work, I can figure out what I’ve been missing."
Aya hesitated. Her father's notebooks had been her most closely guarded possession since his death, filled with his observations, theories, and personal reflections. She’d offered them in excitement, but sharing them meant exposing not only valuable research but intimate glimpses into the man who had shaped her life.
Meeting Leyla's eager gaze, she made her decision. There was no reason for half-measures now. "I have some of his work with me."
Aya removed her backpack and withdrew a leather-bound notebook, worn at the edges from years of handling. Her father's name was embossed on the cover: Dr. Hassan Amrani.
Leyla's eyes widened. "You brought it with you tonight?"
"I try not to travel without it. We feared someone may have gotten into our apartment," Aya said. She placed the notebook on the table, letting her hand linger on its cover before stepping back.
Leyla opened it reverently, turning pages filled with Hassan's neat handwriting, sketches, and equations. "This is incredible. His mind was extraordinary, as I remember."
"He dedicated his life to understanding the past," Aya said softly. "Sometimes at the expense of the present."
Amina placed a hand on Aya’s shoulder.
"There," Karim said suddenly, pointing to a page filled with what they had learned in Morocco were astronomical notations, and they all leaned in. "That's consistent with Ottoman star charts from the period Çelebi would have been working."
Leyla’s expression lit with recognition. "Yes! And look here—" She flipped forward several pages, finding a passage where Hassan had written about an "astral concentration device" theorized by an unnamed Ottoman scholar.
"He was tracking the same research paths," Leyla said. "But coming at it from the archaeological record rather than historical texts." Leyla blinked, then rubbed her temples with a laugh. “But we’re still half‑soaked in rakı. This is huge, I need to have my wits about me.”
She ducked into the kitchenette, set a slim copper cezve on the gas stovetop, and heaped in finely ground coffee and sugar. The room quickly filled with the syrup‑thick aroma of coffee as the brew foamed up the neck, and she poured it into squat porcelain cups.
“Liquid sobriety,” she declared, handing them around. 
“Right,” Leyla said, clapping once. “Let’s earn this caffeine.”
Pages fluttered, maps overlapped, pencils scratched. After minutes passed in silent absorption, Karim called everyone to his station.
“Look here,” he said. His finger traced a star chart Hassan had penciled in the margin. “Longitude matches Al‑Din’s meridian line, but the declination is off by four degrees. Why?”
“Precession?” Aya guessed.
Leyla shook her head, already thumbing through her printouts. “No, Hazarfen corrected for that. Wait—” She slid a vellum overlay on top of the chart. The misaligned star now rested in the dead center of a sketched lens. “He wasn’t mapping the sky; he was choosing a focal star.”
Amina whistled, low and impressed.
Minutes later, Karim angled a pottery shard under the desk lamp. “Same eight‑point star that’s in the notebook. Hassan drew it, Çelebi fired it, and you found it in the rubble.”
“Triangulation confirmed,” Leyla said.
They began constructing a rough timeline on scrap paper: observatory demolished, secret workshops open, fragments dispersed. 
Coffee cooled, forgotten on the table. By the time the morning call to prayer drifted in through peeling window frames, the map of Kadıköy was a constellation of red circles, and a plan was finally tangible.
"Wait," Karim said, flipping back through Hassan's notebook with renewed focus. "Your father referenced specific components throughout his notes, but never in one place. Let me see if I can reconstruct the list."
He grabbed a blank sheet of paper and began writing as he cross-referenced Hassan's sketches with Leyla's research. "Hassan mentions a glass fragment, something he calls the 'primary lens element.' A hollow cylinder, possibly for channeling energy. Then there are references to calibration rings. He sketches two of them, inner and outer."
Leyla leaned over his shoulder, excitement building. "Yes! And my research mentions a focusing array with multiple glass elements working in concert. Plus, what the texts call a 'crystal heart,' probably the central focusing element."
"There's more," Karim continued, his finger tracing a particularly dense page of calculations. "Hassan was obsessed with something he called the crown lens. He writes that it's not just a component but the control mechanism for the entire system. And here—" He tapped a margin note. "He references a 'stabilization wafer' that appears in Çelebi's original designs."
"So we're looking for at least seven major components," Aya said, studying the list Karim had written.
"Eight, technically," Leyla corrected. "The glass fragment, the cylinder, inner and outer calibration rings, focusing array, crystal heart, crown lens, and the stabilization wafer—though that last one might be purely theoretical since it sounds critical and we’re pretty sure this thing has never been used."
Amina whistled low. "That's one hell of a scavenger hunt."
"It's not just about finding them," Karim said, his expression growing serious as he read more of Hassan's notes. "Look at this. He references two operating regimes: stable and unstable. With all components properly assembled, including the crown lens and the wafer, the system can operate in a controlled manner. But with a partial array it can still hypothetically work..." He paused, reading the cramped handwriting. "But he calls it 'catastrophically chaotic.'"
"Meaning what, exactly?" Amina asked.
"Meaning if someone tries to activate this thing without all the pieces," Karim said, "the results could be worse than the complete Astral Lens falling into the wrong hands."
"So, it's clear they were building this cosmic magnifier, and making great progress," Karim continued, setting the list aside. "Using specific materials to focus energy from celestial alignments."
"But for what purpose?" Amina asked from her perch on a windowsill, where she sat beside a pile of historical maps of the area, smoking a cigarette out the window.
"Power," Leyla answered. "The kind of power that doesn't require fuel or conventional energy sources. Imagine harnessing cosmic radiation through a precisely crafted lens during specific astronomical events."
"Like a solar panel, but for cosmic energy. Like radiation?" Aya asked.
"More profound than that," Leyla said. "The texts suggest it could affect the fundamental forces that bind reality. Gravity, time, perhaps more."
Amina stood, putting out her cigarette while her other hand unconsciously moved to the faded marks on her arm. "Like the Amulet."
A heavy silence fell over the room. The Amulet of Idris had opened a doorway to forces beyond their understanding, forces that had nearly consumed them all.
"So they’re still playing the same game. If Aegis gets hold of the Astral Lens," Aya said slowly, "they could replicate what they wanted to try in the Atlas Mountains. Or worse."
"Then we find it first, or we stop them. Again," Karim stated firmly. “That’s why we’re here.”
Amina shook her head. "But in Morocco, we knew exactly where the Amulet was, and they still beat us to it. We got lucky, playing off Léglise’s megalomania, knowing he’d make mistakes. We might not be so lucky. Here, we have theories and fragments, but no clear location. We’re flying blind."
Leyla spread out another map, this one more detailed, showing Kadıköy's streets and landmarks. "Well, here’s the best lead from everything we’ve put together. Based on our combined research, I believe Çelebi established a workshop somewhere in this area." She circled a section of the map encompassing several blocks of the oldest part of Kadıköy.
"That's dozens of buildings," Aya observed. "We can't search them all without attracting attention."
"We won't have to," Karim said, excitement building in his voice. "Look at your father's notations about architectural markers. They’re specific symbols Çelebi incorporated into buildings where he worked. A lot of them may still be intact. Like we said earlier, Istanbul’s a patchwork of East meets West, old meets new. I bet most of this architecture has been preserved."
He turned to a page in Hassan's notebook where several symbols were sketched: a crescent intersected by a straight line; a star with eight uneven points; a circle containing what appeared to be waves.
"If we can identify these markers on buildings in the area Leyla indicated, we can narrow our search," Karim continued. “They’ll be hidden as design elements.”
"So this is a way of marking sites connected to Çelebi's work, his followers, and their organization. A way for the underworld to communicate in plain sight,” Amina said. “Not bad, Çelebi.”
Aya felt a familiar mix of pride in her father’s work and the weight of his absence. "So we develop a search grid,” she said. “Map these symbols throughout old Kadıköy."
"Starting here," Leyla agreed, marking several points on the map. "These buildings date to the right period. If they contain these markers, we'll have our first solid leads."
As they devised their search plan, Karim continued paging through Hassan's notebook, comparing notes with Leyla's research.
"Wait," he said suddenly, his voice sharp with discovery. "Sorry to do this again, but look at this."
He pointed to a small symbol Hassan had drawn repeatedly in the margins of several pages, a simple design resembling an eye with rays emanating from it.
"I've seen this before," Leyla said, frowning. "But I can't place where."
"It appears whenever he mentions Kadıköy specifically," Karim noted. "Almost like a location marker."
“That’s a cartographer’s mark, an old one. Like an artist’s signature.” Leyla rifled through a stack of documents on a nearby shelf, extracting a yellowed sheet protected in a plastic sleeve. "Here!"
It was a centuries-old map of Kadıköy, the paper brittle with age. Near the coastline, barely visible unless you knew to look for it, was the same eye symbol.
"What's at that location now?" Aya asked, leaning closer.
Leyla overlaid the antique map with her modern one, aligning landmarks that had survived centuries of urban development. The symbol is aligned to an area near the historic Karacaahmet Cemetery.
"One of the oldest Ottoman burial grounds in the city," Leyla whispered. "Dating back to the 16th century."
"Çelebi's time.”
"But couldn’t this cartographer’s signature appear in a ton of places on different maps from the time? I’m sure he made more than one. And why would he mark a cemetery?" Amina asked.
“Impossible to know,” Leyla said. “This is the only map I’ve seen bearing this cartographer’s symbol. And he did work with Taqi al-Din, so he would have interacted with Çelebi, if we’re all right.”
Aya's mind raced through possibilities. "So this cartographer could be communicating a hidden location, like the architectural symbols, for the members of the secret society within the Ottoman court. Maybe a hidden workshop? Secret meeting place? Or—"
"His own burial site in the cemetery," Leyla finished. "What better place to hide crucial information than on a tombstone? Hidden in plain sight for centuries, always protected from demolition, destruction."
The four exchanged glances, implications settling over them like dust.
"We need to go there," Aya said. "Now."
"Not so fast," Amina cautioned. "If your father identified this location, Aegis easily could have too. They’re always one step ahead of us. We need to be careful."
"Agreed," Leyla said, reaching for her jacket. "But we also need to hurry; they know we're making progress."
As they gathered their materials, preparing to leave, Aya held Hassan's notebook up to the window, examining the eye symbol under better light.
"This is odd," she murmured. "There's something here I didn't notice before."
The others gathered around her as she tilted the notebook, revealing faint indentations on the page—impressions left when Hassan had pressed too hard while writing on another page, sitting atop the notebook.
"It looks like..." Aya squinted. "Arabic numerals? A date."
"Can you make it out?" Karim asked.
"1580," Aya said slowly. "That’s the year the observatory was destroyed."
"And underneath?" Leyla prompted, seeing more indentations.
Aya's expression changed from concentration to shock. "A name. Not Çelebi, but... well, I’m not sure what this inscription is." She looked up at the others, her voice dropping. "Ayn-ı Semavi."
"The Eye of the Heavens," Leyla translated.
"Or, the Astral Lens," Amina breathed.
Chapter 6
The morning mist still clung to the cypress trees of Karacaahmet Cemetery as they met up outside the entrance, approaching in near unison after a lengthy counter-surveillance route.
The streets around the cemetery were already crowded with groups gathering on corners beneath banners. 
"Labor protests today," Leyla said, noting Aya's curious look. "The unions have been organizing for weeks. Wage disputes, pension reforms. Half the city will be shut down by afternoon."
"Will that be a problem?" Karim asked.
"Depends," Amina said, watching the growing crowds. "Could give us cover. Or complicate things if it turns ugly."
"It usually does," Leyla said quietly. "But the cemetery should be far enough from the main routes. We'll be fine."
"The oldest section is over here," Karim said, leading them deeper into the cemetery. His boots thudded along a clay path where fallen needles muffled every step. "The layout hasn't changed much since the sixteenth century. If Çelebi left markers here, they'd be among these stones."
The cemetery sprawled across Üsküdar’s slopes. Marble headstones rose on either side between the dense cypress trunks. Some were crowned with stone turbans, or Sufi felt caps, others chiseled with tulip reliefs that marked women’s graves.
Karim knelt beside one of the older stones, running his fingers over the carved text. "The calligraphy is exquisite. Look at these letter forms. And, we’re right, sixteenth-century." He glanced up at Aya. "Well, these are definitely from Çelebi's era. Let’s look for any of these architectural symbols. Maybe the cartographer’s signature. Anything that could hint at Çelebi and his followers."
They spread out among the graves, each taking a different row. Aya found herself drawn to a section where the stones looked older, more weathered. She photographed each one methodically to reference the inscriptions later.
"Found something," Amina called from several rows over.
They converged on her position. She stood before a modest gravestone, less ornate than its neighbors. Where other stones bore elaborate calligraphy describing the deceased's virtues and lineage, this one had simpler text and something more.
"Look at the base," Amina said, kneeling to brush away accumulated dirt and moss.
As she cleared the stone's foundation, symbols emerged. Not the typical Islamic calligraphy, but patterns.
"It's the same as in our notes," Aya whispered. "The crescent intersected by a straight line. And here—" she pointed to a series of marks that looked almost like scratches until you knew what to look for, "—these are numbers."
Leyla crouched beside them, her excitement palpable. "Could they be more coordinates?"
Aya was already pulling out her phone, opening an astronomy app she’d downloaded when she and Karim had been poring over Hassan’s coordinates in Marrakech. "Read them to me."
Leyla quickly translated. "Right ascension: 4 hours, 35 minutes. Declination: positive 16 degrees, 30 minutes."
Aya input the coordinates, then looked up with a wide smile. "Aldebaran. The eye of Taurus. It would have been visible from Istanbul during the winter of 1580. That’s the year the observatory was destroyed.”
"Çelebi or his followers were marking graves to signal to others in secret," Leyla said. "Were they creating a star map? Why? And why hide it here?"
They documented the stone carefully with photographs from every angle, careful notes on its position, and inscriptions. As they worked, the sun climbed higher, burning away the morning mist. 
Other visitors began arriving at the cemetery, forcing them to work more discreetly.
Over the next two hours, they located four more graves with similar markings. Each bore coordinates for different stars: Regulus in Leo, Spica in Virgo, Antares in Scorpius, and Fomalhaut in Piscis Austrinus.
"These are all first-magnitude stars," Karim read from his phone as they compared notes. "The brightest in their constellations. And all would have been visible from Istanbul at different times of the year."
"There's a pattern forming," Leyla said, spreading their hand-drawn map on a flat tombstone. She had been plotting each marked grave's position within the cemetery, adding an ‘X’ to the grid. "Look at the arrangement."
As she connected the dots between the marked graves, a familiar shape emerged.
"It could be an eight-pointed star," Aya said. "Just like the symbol on the pottery fragments."
"And in your father's sketches," Karim added.
"So we’ve found five graves," Amina said. "Well, I think we can all count. So, if we’re right, that means there should be—"
Leyla laughed. "Yeah, we need to find three more."
They expanded their search, but as the morning wore on, frustration began to set in.
“If these graves make a star pattern, we can’t have missed them. One should be right here, exactly where I’m standing. Maybe they never finished the pattern, or they got interrupted,” Amina called.
"Wait," Aya said suddenly, stopping dead in her tracks. She stared at their rudimentary map. "We're wrong. The graves are in a pattern, but not the one we think."
"What do you mean?" Amina asked, jogging over and peering over her shoulder.
Aya took out her father’s notebook and tapped it excitedly. "These aren't just random stars. Look at how my father sketched them in relation to each other—he wasn't mapping the stars as they appear in the sky." She flipped through pages quickly, finding a specific diagram. "He was mapping how they'd appear if projected onto the cemetery itself. The cemetery is the canvas!"
Karim's eyes lit up. "Of course! The gravestones aren't just marked with star coordinates. They're physically arranged to mirror the astronomical positions."
"Exactly," Aya said. "The cemetery layout itself is a star map."
Leyla grabbed the notebook, studying the diagram, then pulled out a compass from her pocket. "If we use these five graves as known points in an eight-pointed star pattern..."
"We can triangulate the exact positions of the missing three," Karim finished, already calculating angles on his phone.
"That’s damn impressive," Amina whispered. "How to hide something in plain sight for centuries."
Karim looked up from his calculations. "I have coordinates for the missing points. They should be approximately fifty meters northwest of here, thirty-five meters east, and—" he pointed toward a cluster of cypress trees, "—just beyond those trees."
"Let's go," Aya said, heart racing. 
The morning heat intensified as they followed Karim's calculations to the first projected point. As they walked, she kept an eye over her shoulder, monitoring the other visitors to the cemetery, waiting for any sign of trouble.
Sweat beaded on Aya's forehead as she pushed aside overgrown grass around a weathered tombstone. "Nothing here," she sighed, brushing dirt from her hands.
"The stone could have been moved," Leyla said, scanning the surrounding area. "Or destroyed during renovations."
Amina, perched on a nearby grave marker, shook her head. "Or we're completely wrong again."
"Let's check the second point," Aya insisted.
The second location proved equally disappointing, revealing a section of newer graves, clearly from the wrong century. Frustration settled over the group like the thickening humidity.
"This doesn't make sense," Karim said, rechecking his calculations. "The pattern is precise. These points should complete the star chart."
They trudged toward the third location, following a narrow path between densely packed graves. The cypress trees created a tunnel of shadow and light, their footsteps crunching on fallen needles.
"Maybe we need to reevaluate—" Leyla began, then stopped abruptly. "Wait. What's that?"
Ahead, partially hidden by overgrown shrubs, sat a simple marble bench. Its weathered surface blended so perfectly with the surrounding stones that they might have walked past it entirely.
"That would be just a bench," Amina said, disappointment evident.
"No," Aya gasped, already moving forward. She knelt beside it, eyes scanning the marble surface. "Look at the base."
There, almost invisible beneath centuries of grime, was the familiar eight-pointed star symbol etched into the stone.
"This is it," Karim said, dropping to his knees beside Aya. His shoulder pressed against hers as they both leaned in to examine the carving.
Their fingers brushed as they traced the symbol, and they exchanged an exhilarated smile, the thrill of discovery momentarily eclipsing everything else.
"Help me move it," Aya said, already pushing against the bench.
The marble groaned against the earth as all four strained against its weight. Slowly, the bench shifted, revealing what lay beneath: a flat circular stone with a depression in its center, like a shallow bowl carved into the earth.
"It's not a grave marker," Leyla said, stepping back. "It's something else entirely."
