"Look at these," Leyla said, pointing to tiny symbols carved around the rim of the depression. "More notations. And here—" she indicated a series of marks that looked like rays emanating from the center, "—is this a light path? Or energy flow?"
Aya’s fingers found a nearly invisible seam around the edge of the stone. "It's not solid. There's a gap here."
She pressed her fingertips into the narrow crack and pulled upward. The stone didn't budge.
"Let me," Amina said, producing a compact crowbar from her backpack. She wedged it into the seam and applied careful pressure.
A soft scraping sound emerged as the circular stone lifted slightly. Working together, they eased it up and away, revealing a hollow space beneath.
Inside the cavity sat a metal box, roughly the size of a large book. Its copper surface had oxidized to a mottled green, but the elaborate filigree work remained intact, depicting swirling, elongated orbital patterns.
"Impressive metalwork," Leyla whispered, her voice thick with reverence. “At first glance, the age seems correct.”
The box was sealed with dark wax that bore a familiar impression: the eye symbol from Hassan's notebook. Aya reached for it with trembling hands, lifting it carefully from its hiding place.
"So, it's been here for over four hundred years," she said. "Waiting."
The wax seal cracked with a sound like breaking ice as Aya carefully pried it open. Inside, wrapped in what appeared to be oilcloth treated with beeswax, lay a tightly rolled document. The ancient fabric crackled beneath her touch as she gently unfolded it.
The ink had faded to a rich sepia. Diagrams covered the parchment—furnaces, workbenches, and unknown instruments, all rendered with detailed precision.
As they huddled over the fragile paper, a distant sound filtered through the cemetery's quiet. Voices raised in unison, the rhythmic chanting faint but unmistakable.
"Do you hear that?" Amina asked, lifting her head.
Aya nodded, but didn't look up. "Probably a tour group."
They carefully photographed each section of the plans with their phones, while the chanting grew gradually louder, more distinct.
Karim frowned. "That doesn't sound like tourists."
A single police siren wailed in the distance, then another. Leyla straightened, her expression concerned. "Okay, you’re right. We need to hurry," she said. 
"Well, these are plans for a workshop, a laboratory," Karim said, leaning in to study the plans. "Look at the equipment. Glass furnaces, grinding wheels, polishing stations."
"I can’t believe it. Not just any lab," Leyla said. "The lens-making facility. See these specifications along the margins? They're describing different types of glass. Crystal, flint, even what looks like early optical crown glass."
"Some of these materials shouldn't have existed in the sixteenth century," Karim noted. "The chemical formulations, they're incredibly advanced."
Amina pointed to notations in one corner. "These look like instructions for combining materials. Creating specialized glass with specific properties."
"For focusing cosmic energy," Aya said quietly. "This is how Çelebi and his followers made the Astral Lens, if he was successful."
Karim traced the star pattern on the plans. "Look at this notation in the margin. It references the eight-pointed star we found in the cemetery graves. I think the burial locations aren't just symbolic; they're marking the cache sites where Çelebi hid the components. Each grave corresponds to one piece of the lens."
"So the cemetery isn't just a clue," Leyla said, eyes wide. "It's a map to eight specific locations throughout the city."
Karim nodded. “Or far beyond Istanbul, potentially.”
As they studied the document, the sound of sirens and chants grew louder, eroding their concentration.
"That's a lot of police, and a lot of people coming our way," Amina said, looking up from the plans. “Neither’s my favorite type of company.”
"We should go if they’re coming this way," Aya said, carefully rolling up the document. "Too much attention."
They quickly replaced the stone lid, stored their discovery in Karim’s bag, moved the bench back into position, and made their way toward the cemetery entrance while the sounds of disturbance grew louder.
Through the gates, they could see crowds gathering in the streets. People were streaming in from all directions, signs and banners held high overhead. Police vehicles were arriving in force, officers in riot gear beginning to form lines.
"What are they protesting?" Karim asked.
“Something about wages,” Leyla said. “Sorry, I’ve been checked out of the news ever since Emre disappeared. I’ve been absorbed in my work.”
"Well, I don’t think we’ll have time to find out," Amina replied with a tilt of her head. "Look."
There was a figure standing across the street from the cemetery entrance. Even among the growing crowd, the dark hood was unmistakable.
Chapter 7
"Shit. We've been made," Aya yelled.
More hooded figures were becoming visible, clearly not part of the protest, positioning themselves at strategic points around the cemetery entrance.
"We need to blend with the crowd," Aya continued. "Use their numbers to our advantage."
They stepped out of the cemetery gates just as the first wave of protesters surged past on the street. As they merged with the flow of bodies, the hooded figures moved to follow, but the density of the crowd made tracking difficult. Aya led them through the thickest parts of the protest. Bodies jostled in every direction, signs were raised overhead, and chants Aya couldn’t understand echoed around them.
Then, suddenly, a massive line of riot police blocked their path, shields locked together as protesters pressed forward.
"This way," Leyla called, pulling them out of the crowd. She moved with practiced efficiency, checking sight lines before committing to the side street, then led them in a zigzag pattern that kept them out of direct view from the main thoroughfare. "Stay low, single file," she added.
Aya followed Leyla’s lead, studying her closely, used to taking on the role herself.
Free from the tangle of bodies, they broke into a run, Leyla setting a pace that suggested she'd done this before. But, as they turned the corner, they found themselves face to face with three more hooded figures walking calmly in their direction.
The tallest of the three stepped forward, lowering his hood to reveal the face of a man in his forties, with close-cropped blonde hair and sharp, calculating eyes behind frameless glasses.
"Ms. Amrani," he said, his voice cutting through the chaos behind them. "My name is Malik. We need to talk."
Aya shifted her weight, ready to move. "I don't think we have anything to discuss."
Malik's smile didn't reach his eyes. "I think we do. About your father's research. About Çelebi. About what you just found in that cemetery."
The other two figures flanked him, their faces still obscured by their hoods. One was shorter, stockier, with gloved hands clenched into fists. The other was tall and wiry.
"How do you know anything about my father?" Aya demanded, buying time as she assessed their options. The alley behind Malik was too narrow for an escape, and the protest behind them was becoming more volatile by the second.
"Hassan Amrani was a brilliant archaeologist," Malik said, his tone calm, almost reverent. "We worked together for years on Ottoman astronomical innovations. I learned more from him than from any university, any intelligence service, any organization that's employed me since."
Amina edged closer to Aya, her voice barely audible. "Three meters to your left. Small alleyway. Fire escapes to the roofs."
Aya didn't acknowledge her, instead keeping her eyes fixed on Malik. "Funny, he never mentioned you or Aegis."
"Hassan kept many things to himself, I’m sure," Malik replied calmly. "He always had secrets from me, no matter how closely we worked together. Including what he discovered about the Astral Lens. Including why he chose to abandon our research and disappear. Including the real reason he was in Colombia when he died."
Five more of the hooded figures they'd seen flanking the protest had suddenly converged behind Malik. He acknowledged them with a nod.
Distant voices rose in anger, the chanting becoming more aggressive. Back on the main street, the protest surged against the police line, who took to banging their batons against their shields in warning.
"I'm not here representing Aegis corporate interests," Malik continued, his voice steady despite the chaos building around them. "I'm here because you're continuing Hassan's work, and you have a right to understand what he discovered. What frightened him enough to hide it. What he died protecting." He paused, meeting Aya's eyes directly. "And what humanity desperately needs, whether it frightens us or not."
"You mean like the cooperation you forced from Leyla's colleague?" Amina cut in, her hand hovering near her concealed knife.
A flicker of surprise crossed Malik's face before his composure returned. "Emre made his choice; we did not make it for him."
"You know where he is, then?" Leyla demanded, stepping forward.
"Safe," Malik replied smoothly. "And he will remain that way, depending on how this conversation ends. We are not killers, unless our hand is forced."
The shorter hooded figure took a step toward them, hand sliding beneath their jacket. Aya caught a glimpse of metal.
A sudden crash echoed behind them as protesters broke through a police barricade. Shouts and screams erupted, followed by a series of distinctive pops as tear gas canisters were fired.
"Last chance," Malik said, and his voice carried disappointment rather than threat. "I'm asking you to trust what Hassan would have wanted if he'd lived long enough to see the crisis we're facing. Climate collapse, resource depletion, civilizational stagnation. Conventional solutions aren't working fast enough. The lens represents a fundamental shift in human capability—not just power, but transformation. The kind of evolutionary leap our species needs to survive."
"The plans stay with us," Aya said firmly. They were outnumbered, outgunned, with their backs to a growing riot. But chaos could be an ally if used correctly.
Malik sighed. "Hassan made the same choice, choosing safety over progress, fear over courage. I respected him too much to force his hand then. But I can't extend that same courtesy indefinitely when the stakes are this high." He nodded to his companions, who began to advance.
The acrid scent of tear gas reached them just as the first canister bounced into the side street, trailing white smoke. Behind it, a surge of panicked protesters poured around the corner, fleeing the gas.
"Now!" Aya shouted, seizing the moment.
She grabbed Karim's arm and pulled him toward the narrow gap between buildings that Amina had spotted earlier. Amina and Leyla followed instantly, disappearing into the crush of bodies as protesters flooded the area.
A hand grabbed at Aya's jacket, but she twisted free, ducking low and pushing through the crowd.
Malik shouted, but his words were lost in the chaos. 
The tear gas burned Aya’s eyes, blurring her vision as she pulled Karim through the narrow passage. Behind them, she heard Amina's sharp command—"Left! Go left!"—directing Leyla through the maze of alleyways.
They emerged onto another street filled with fleeing protesters. Aya's lungs burned, her eyes squinting and streaming. Through the bloodshot haze, she spotted Amina pulling Leyla out of the side street.
"This way!" Karim shouted, pointing toward a covered bazaar where merchants were hurriedly closing their stalls.
As they reached the bazaar entrance, Aya chanced a look back. Through the thinning crowd, she glimpsed Malik standing perfectly still amid the chaos, watching them with an expression that wasn't anger or frustration but something closer to regret. Even at a distance, she could feel the weight of his gaze, not threatening but evaluating, as if he’d seen it all before, was only going through the motions.
Then they were inside the bazaar, weaving through narrow aisles between shuttered shops, the sounds of the riot diminishing behind them.
"Keep moving," Aya gasped, wiping her eyes. "They'll be tracking us."
As they navigated the narrow bazaar aisles, Leyla instinctively avoided the sight lines from the entrance, chose a path that kept them behind vendor stalls, and somehow knew exactly which passages connected to different exits. "Cameras are mounted every forty meters in these older bazaars," Leyla called, pointing them toward a blind spot. "Standard municipal surveillance grid."
How does a fired academic know Istanbul's surveillance infrastructure? Aya wondered, but there was no time to ask.
"Did we lose them?" Leyla asked, her voice ragged.
"For now," Amina yelled, glancing back at the entrance. "But they know what we have, and they know where we’re staying."
They eventually emerged onto a quieter street several blocks from the protest and finally slowed to catch their breath.
"So, that was Aegis in Turkey," Karim said.
"And they know exactly who we are and what we were up to. Probably always have," Aya added. "And Malik was back focusing on my father’s work."
"He's the one who's been following us since we arrived," Amina said. "Bastard."
Leyla leaned against the wall of the brick building, her face pale. "And they do have Emre."
Aya checked her bag, making sure the document was still secure. They had the architectural plans. They had Malik’s name. And they had confirmation that Aegis was actively hunting them, but stopping short of actually harming them, for now.
But as they caught their breath on that quiet Istanbul street, Aya couldn't shake the feeling that they were still missing something crucial. Something her father had known, something she increasingly suspected may have cost him his life.
Something that Malik desperately wanted to find.
Chapter 8
Tear gas still burned in their lungs and eyes as they walked toward the Bosphorus.
"Everyone accounted for?" Aya managed between deep breaths.
"Yeah," Amina panted, wiping streaming eyes with her sleeve. Her typically infuriatingly composed appearance was now disheveled, her dark hair streaming wildly.
Karim leaned against a shuttered storefront, chest heaving. "Well, that escalated quickly."
"Too quickly," Leyla agreed, checking over her shoulder. "Do you think Aegis orchestrated that, and then it got out of hand? Such a random place and time for a protest.”
“How the hell are we supposed to know? Anything’s possible with these guys,” Amina said.
“But it seems they’re afraid to move against us in public for some reason. They just linger, watching and waiting for an opportunity. Menacing but never attacking. We need to not give them any more,” Aya said. “I don’t think they’ll open fire in public. One round on camera and every counter‑terror statute slams shut. Their cultural‑consultancy cover dies on the evening news.”
"And we need to look at that final inscription," Karim said. "The one we photographed before everything went sideways."
Aya brought up the image on her phone. Even through the screen's glare, the elaborate Ottoman calligraphy from the last gravestone they'd documented was impressively intricate. Centuries of weather had softened its edges but hadn't erased its message.
"It’s in Turkish. Leyla?" she prompted, holding out the phone.
Leyla squinted at the screen, tilting it from the light, as her lips moved silently while she worked through the archaic text.
"It's definitely a reference to 'Ayn-ı Semavi,'" she muttered. "And here—" her finger traced a grouping of characters "—this word. 'Mercek.' It means lens."
Karim clapped his hands. "First concrete textual evidence we've found. That’s something, right?"
Amina glanced nervously behind them as a wave of fleeing protesters rushed past. "I, for one, am not ready to celebrate, mister positive. Can we discuss ancient Turkish somewhere that doesn't carry a threat of tear gas or prison?"
"It is affirming, but she's right," Aya said. "We can't stay exposed like this. And moving as a group makes us more visible and easier to track."
A police helicopter thundered overhead, its searchlight sweeping the streets below. They pressed back against the building until it passed.
"Split up?" Leyla suggested after the rotor noise faded.
Aya nodded, mind already working through logistics. "You and Amina hit the historical archives. See if you can find any municipal records about glass or lens workshops in old Kadıköy. Cross-reference with what we found in the cemetery."
"All the government buildings will be chaos with the protests," Leyla warned.
"Exactly," Amina interjected. "Perfect cover. Security will be focused on the streets, not researchers inside."
"What about us?" Karim asked.
"Back to the apartment," Aya decided. "We need to get to my father's other notebooks, compare everything. See what else he discovered about Ayn-ı Semavi. There might be connections we missed."
"Rendezvous, habibi?" Amina asked, already scanning Google Maps for the clearest route north toward the archives.
"Text on our secure line when you're clear, and we can find a secure place to compare anything we uncover." Aya touched Amina's arm briefly. "And I know you’re getting antsy for some action, but be careful.”
Amina’s eyes held Aya’s. "You too.”
They split into pairs, Amina and Leyla heading north through the backstreets while Aya and Karim took a circuitous route back to their apartment that Omar had rented, avoiding main thoroughfares and keeping to the shadows of Kadıköy's older buildings. As they separated, Aya caught Amina's eye. A flicker of shared caution passed between them that neither voiced. Then Amina turned and followed Leyla into the crowd.



The apartment felt like a sanctuary after the morning's chaos. Aya dead-bolted the door behind them and immediately moved to the windows, scanning the street below, while Karim checked all their sensors. No sign of surveillance.
"Clear?" Karim asked, already spreading the architectural drawing across the dining table.
"For now." Aya helped him weigh down the corners with coffee cups. The aged paper wanted to curl back into its centuries-old roll.
In the clearer light of their apartment, their eyes newly flushed with water and soothed with eye drops, the drawing's details were even more impressive. The workshop layout was meticulously planned, with separate areas for different stages of lens crafting. Annotations in Ottoman script filled the margins, some in what might have been Çelebi's own hand, others added later in slightly darker ink.
"This level of precision, wow," Karim murmured, tracing the equipment specifications with a reverent finger. "Whoever drafted this understood optical principles far beyond my understanding. I need to study whether any of these theories have ever even been published."
Aya retrieved her father's other notebooks, along with his other journals, which they'd brought from Morocco. She spread them out beside the architectural drawing, looking for connections.
"I think—yes. Here," she said, finding the relevant section. "My father tried to sketch something similar. Look at this lens configuration he was guessing at."
The page showed Hassan's interpretation of what appeared to be the same focusing mechanism depicted in Çelebi's workshop plans. Her father had added contemporary physics notations, trying to understand the principles behind the design.
"These equations," Karim said, leaning in to study Hassan's calculations. "He's describing energy amplification through crystalline lattice structures. But the mathematics..." He shook his head. "Some of this looks like extremely theoretical physics.”
Aya turned pages, finding more of her father's dense notations. Sketches of lens configurations, refractive indices, and calculations for focal lengths. And everywhere, the same symbol. An eye within radiating lines.
"Multiple components are the difference in Çelebi’s sketch," Karim observed, tracing the connections between elements. "Not just one lens, but a system of lenses. Primary focusing element here, secondary amplification array here, and this..." He pointed to a central chamber in the diagram. "This looks like a containment vessel."
"But what did Çelebi use it for?"
"That's the question, isn't it? It’s not like we’ve mastered the ability to harness energy from deep space. Maybe it’s too volatile, like with the Amulet of Idris, and Çelebi found out the hard way." Karim moved to another of Hassan's journals, flipping through pages. "We only have this bit here."
He pointed to a cryptic passage, written in Hassan's personal shorthand: “Doesn't create energy—channels what exists. Cosmic background radiation, stellar emissions, and darker sources. Ottoman 'celestial fire.' Zero-point energy. ???”
The entry ended abruptly.
"If this is real, if Aegis gets this technology, I think the implications are far worse than the Amulet of Idris in the Atlas.” Karim shook his head. 
"They won't. We won’t let them." Aya injected her voice with far more confidence than she felt. She hated to see Karim lose his enthusiasm. And she hated that her mind couldn’t stop worrying about how Amina was doing.
"How can you be so sure?"
She met his eyes across the table. "Because we’ve stopped them before. And I’ve seen what we can all do together. You’re amazing, and I can’t imagine anyone on their side could outsmart you.”
Karim walked behind her and wrapped his arms around her, pulling her tight. "I hope you’re right. And we’ll do whatever it takes."
Aya turned and kissed him. She wanted to put all his worries at ease, push all other thoughts from her head.
His lips were soft against hers as he drove her backward until her thighs found the desk. He lifted her onto the desktop and tugged her shift free while she fumbled with the belt at his hips.
"We should probably finish our research," she murmured against his lips, not making any move to pull away.
"Definitely," he agreed, already walking her backward toward the bedroom, his mouth never leaving hers.
They stumbled through the doorway, shedding the rest of their clothes as they went. They laughed between kisses, the urgency making them clumsy. When Karim's hands slid along the curve of her spine, her laughter dissolved into a sharp intake of breath.
"God, I want you," he whispered, his voice rough with desire as he guided her to the bed. “I can’t believe we have some alone time.”
Aya pulled him down on top of her, appreciating the heat of his skin against hers. She ran her hands over the lean muscles of his back.
His mouth moved to her neck before working his way down as she arched against him, a soft moan escaping her throat while his hands mapped every inch of her, firm.
As he worked his way back up, Karim whispered Aya’s name against her ear, and she answered by pulling him closer, deeper. Then she flipped them, straddling him, hair tumbling in curls down her chest. Karim’s hands cupped her waist, guiding while Aya took control.
When they finished, relief washed over them both in trembling waves, together, leaving Aya clinging to him.
They lay side-by-side afterward, skin cooling in the late afternoon air, neither willing to break the spell with words. Karim's fingers traced idle patterns on her shoulder while her head rested on his chest, rising and falling with each breath.
"That was..." he began, but seemed unable to find the right word.
"Exactly why I had us come back to the apartment," Aya finished for him, smiling.
He laughed, the sound vibrating through his chest beneath her ear. "I was going to say 'perfect,' but I couldn’t agree more. I’ve never been happier with one of your plans."
She propped herself up on an elbow to look at him, suddenly struck by the vulnerability in his eyes. For all his intellect and growing boldness, there was still something wonderfully open about him, something untouched by the darkness they faced.
Chapter 9
Aya checked her phone for the third time in ten minutes. Still nothing from Amina. 
"She's fine," Karim said from the bed, still tangled in the sheets. "Probably just buried in ledgers and losing track of time." 
"Probably." 
But Aya's instincts prickled. Amina always checked in. Always. She paced to the window, watching the city lights begin to sparkle as the sun descended. 
Behind her, she felt Karim's presence, his warmth as he wrapped his arms around her naked body. 
"When this is over," he said quietly, "when we've stopped Aegis for good, what happens to us?" The question caught her off guard. 
"What do you want to happen?" she asked.
"I want to keep waking up next to you. Preferably without anyone trying to kill us." His voice was soft but serious. "I want us to be together. Actually together. Not just between crises."
Aya smiled and squeezed him tight. "That does sound nice."
You don’t get both vengeance and happily ever after, she warned herself. But she’d been in this exact position before, with Amina, and she wanted to try harder this time.
"I'm serious." He tilted her chin up to meet his eyes. "I want us to be together."
The slight shake in his voice made her throat tight. "Me too," Aya whispered, and kissed him again, slower this time. She knew she meant it, she just wasn’t sure how to balance it with all the other things she knew she meant, too.
Eventually, hunger drove them from bed, and they tossed on some nondescript clothes.
"That place we saw, Zeytin?" Karim suggested as they locked up. "It's quiet, really nice outdoor space. And it looks chilled out in case we hear from Amina, so she and Leyla could meet us there."
"Sounds delightful.”
The walk was peaceful, the evening air cool against their skin. Any sign of a protest had vanished, leaving the streets to their usual evening crowd: couples walking hand in hand, groups of friends heading to bars, the flow of Istanbul life.
Zeytin Kafe & Bar welcomed them with a garden-style layout, nestled on a tree-lined side street, a lush oasis in the heart of the city. Massive trees rose through the wooden decking, their trunks wrapped with fairy lights that cast a warm glow across the wooden tables.
"This is perfect," Aya said, taking in the leafy canopy overhead and the gravel underfoot. "Good call."
They chose a table near the back corner, where they could sit with their backs to the wall and maintain sight lines to both entrances. A waitress approached with menus.
"Thanks, but we know what we want. Two Guinness, please," Karim said.
"And two of the mantar soslu döküm burgers," Aya added. “That’s the mushroom sauce, right?”
As the waitress confirmed and walked away, a large orange tabby cat appeared from beneath a nearby table, stretching languidly before sauntering over to inspect the newcomers. He paused at Aya's feet, looking up with imperious amber eyes.
"Hello there," she murmured, reaching down to offer her hand for inspection.
"Resident security system," Karim joked as the cat rubbed against Aya's hand and she petted his head. "When I was looking, I read a review of this place that mentioned Sultan. Apparently, he's quite selective about who he graces with his presence."
The cat gave Aya's fingers a cursory sniff before hopping onto the empty chair between them, curling into a tight ball of fur.
"I'm honored," Aya said, scratching behind Sultan's ears. “He reminds me of the street cat that lived at Salah’s in Essaouira, Marmalade.”
Their beers arrived, still settling, dark and velvety with creamy heads. The first sip was a welcome change from the rakı.
"Şerefe," Karim toasted, raising his glass.
"B’saha," Aya added.
They clinked glasses, tapped them on the table, and took a drink. For the moment, with the dappled light playing through the leaves above and the soft murmur of conversation around them, Aya could almost pretend they were just another couple enjoying Istanbul's night scene.
To be fair, she considered, they’d gotten to enjoy Istanbul, and each other, far more than she’d expected to for being essentially at war with the Aegis.
Their burgers arrived steaming on wooden boards. Thick patties topped with a rich mushroom sauce that spilled over the sides of the brioche buns. Hand-cut fries filled the remainder of the board, sprinkled with herbs and sea salt.
"You know, it’s crazy," Karim said, "six months ago I was cataloging broken pottery shards and writing grant proposals in the dust of the Atlas Mountains. Mythological artifacts, shadowy conspiracies, these were all Indiana Jones to me. Now I'm decoding Ottoman star maps and running from corporate mercenaries."
"Regrets?" Aya asked, sipping her Guinness.
"None." His answer was immediate, certain. "Well, maybe the tear gas. But everything else? Worth it."
They ordered another round of Guinness and then shared a plate of köfte with roasted vegetables. Sultan remained curled on the chair, occasionally opening one eye to monitor their progress, particularly when the köfte arrived.
"I think this should be our first stop," Karim said, his voice lowering, "when this is all over, I'd like to come back to Turkey. Properly. See the sites, visit the museums. Maybe even find some legitimate archaeological work."
"I'd like that too," Aya replied, her hand finding his across the table. “We could work together.”
The garden was illuminated entirely by string lights now, creating their own canopy of stars beneath the actual night sky that had been Çelebi’s focus 500 years prior. The lamppost in the center cast pools of light across the gravel, while the small lime-green building at the back housed the kitchen and bar, its windows glowing warmly. Sultan suddenly perked up, ears swiveling toward the entrance before he disappeared beneath another table. 
A moment later, Aya's phone buzzed against the wooden tabletop. A message from Amina had come in, and Aya tried to mask just how relieved she was to see that Amina was okay. “Archives were a goldmine. Heading back now. You won't believe what we found.”
"Looks like we're back on," Aya said, showing Karim the screen.
"I guess that's the end of our nice, quiet evening." He drained the last of his Guinness. "But I'm so glad we got to have it, especially with what lies ahead."
Aya leaned across the table and kissed him, then stood. "I'll go get the bill."
Her phone buzzed again before she could rise. 



Three blocks away, Amina and Leyla emerged from the archives into the cooling evening air.
"So you'll head back to meet them?" Leyla asked, checking her watch. "I should get home, organize what we found."
"Yeah." Amina pulled out her phone, typing quickly. "I want to compare the workshop registrations against Hassan's cemetery coordinates. See if there's overlap."
"Sounds good. Tomorrow morning?"
Amina nodded, but her eyes remained guarded. She'd been watching Leyla all day. The way she knew exactly which bureaucrat to charm, which records room held the oldest ledgers, which archive clerk owed favors, which probing questions about Hassan’s research she slipped in while Amina was distracted. Too smooth. Too connected for someone supposedly blacklisted from academia.
"See you then," Amina said, then added casually, "Thanks for your help today. You know your way around."
"Years of research." Leyla’s eyes widened slightly, then she smiled. "You learn the systems."
They separated at the corner, Leyla heading north while Amina turned toward the apartment. But Amina didn't move right away. She waited, watching Leyla's retreating figure, noting the way she glanced back once, just once, before disappearing around a corner.
Something was wrong. She typed another message to Aya: "One more thing…"



Leyla waited until she was two blocks away, certain Amina wasn't following, before ducking into the alcove of a shuttered newsstand. She pulled out the burner phone Malik had given her and dialed.
He answered on the second ring. "Report."
"We found workshop registrations from 1580. Three confirmed locations in Kadıköy that match the cemetery pattern. But there's a problem."
"Go on."
Leyla's grip tightened on the phone. "Amina. She's suspicious. Has been all day. Watching me, testing me with questions, and noting how I move. I think she's going to warn Aya."
Silence on the line, then: "How certain are you?"
"Very. She's good, better than I anticipated. If they start questioning me directly tomorrow, my cover won't hold."
"Then we don't wait until tomorrow." Malik's voice shifted, became calculating. "Where is she now?"
"Walking back to their apartment. Alone. Sent Aya ahead to the restaurant."
"Perfect. I'll have a team intercept her. You stay clear and establish your alibi. Be seen in a public place for the next two hours."
Leyla's stomach twisted. "What are you going to do to her?"
"Nothing permanent. We need her suspicious nature redirected toward gratitude." A pause. "And you're going to be the one she's grateful to. My men will make it messy enough to be convincing, but leave enough breadcrumbs for you to follow. You'll arrive with backup, eliminate a few expendable assets, and extract her heroically. After that, her suspicions will seem like paranoia."
"You want me to kill your own people?"
"Three or four operatives are meaningless compared to what we're building. And Dr. Yilmaz, the alignment is approaching. We can't afford delays." His voice hardened. "This accelerates the timeline. Once you've rescued her, they'll trust you completely. You push them toward the remaining component locations. Hard. No more delays."
Leyla thought of Emre. His face in that last video Malik had shown her, gaunt but alive. "I understand."
"Good. Get to a café, order dinner, and pay with a credit card. I'll contact you when it's time for your heroic rescue."
The line went dead.
Leyla stood in the shadows, phone heavy in her hand. Somewhere ahead, Amina was walking into a trap Leyla had just armed.


Another message from Amina came through on Aya’s phone, which she read in disbelief.
"One more thing. I don't fully trust Leyla. Something's off. She knew exactly where to find records today. Asked a lot of questions about Hassan's notes on component locations. Maybe I'm paranoid, but my gut says she's not telling us everything. Watch yourself, and don’t discuss anything sensitive with her until we talk."
Aya showed Karim the message, who frowned.
"She did know a lot about surveillance infrastructure during the protest," he said slowly. "For an academic."
"But Omar vouched for her," Aya reasoned, though the words felt hollow even as she said them.
Karim shrugged.
Aya pocketed her phone. "We'll talk to her tomorrow. All of us. Clear the air."
But a small knot of stress had formed in her stomach that the Guinness couldn't dissolve.
They walked back slowly, arms linked against the cooling night air. The streets were quieter now, most shops closed, only occasional groups of partiers passing by. The domestic normalcy of it struck Aya: walking home after dinner, slightly buzzed, discussing mundane things like whether they needed to buy coffee for the morning. She liked it.
“Did you hear that?” Karim asked, stopping abruptly, alarmed.
They were a block from their building when she heard it for the second time. A muffled cry, sharp and suddenly cut off.
"That sounded like—" Karim began.
But Aya was already running.
She rounded the corner at full sprint, just in time to see a black sedan pulling away from the curb two buildings ahead, followed by the screech of tires. Through the rear window, illuminated briefly by a streetlight, she caught a glimpse of a struggle—dark hair, hands pressed against glass.
Then Amina's face appeared as she thrashed about on the inside, pressed close to the window. Their eyes met, Aya’s wide with panic and Amina’s narrowed with rage. Her mouth formed words Aya couldn't hear through the glass.
The car's tires squealed again as it accelerated downhill, tearing through the quiet street. Aya pushed herself harder, boots pounding pavement.
Then the car swept around the corner and vanished into the Istanbul night.
"Amina!" Aya screamed, skidding to a halt at the intersection. Her voice echoed off the buildings, answered only by the fading growl of the sedan's engine.
Karim caught up, breathing hard. "Was that—"
"They took her." Aya's voice was deadly calm. "Aegis has Amina."
Chapter 10
The car's taillights disappeared around the corner, taking Amina with them. Aya stood frozen in the middle of the street, her scream still echoing off the apartment buildings.
"License plate?" Karim gasped, hands on his knees as he caught his breath beside her.
"Too dark," Aya replied, eyes fixed on the empty street, panting out the words. "Black sedan, tinted windows. Maybe government plates. They were white with red text. Heading north toward the Bosphorus. And where’s Leyla? They might have her, too."
"We need to call the police."
Aya shook her head sharply. "And tell them what? That an international shadow organization just kidnapped our friend? We'd spend the night in interrogation while they search our apartment and find everything."
"Then what's the plan?" Karim straightened, voice rising with panic. "We can't just let them take her!"
"I'm thinking!" Aya snapped, picturing a series of horrible fates that could await Amina if they didn’t act quickly, then softened. "Sorry. Let me call her first. Maybe they want to talk."
She pulled out her phone with trembling fingers and dialed Amina's number. Straight to voicemail.
"Damn it." She lowered the phone, mind racing. "We need to get back in the apartment, then figure out our next move."
"You think she'll be okay?" Karim asked as they started jogging back toward their building.
Aya's pace quickened. "I don’t know. She's survived worse."
"But these are the same people who—"
"I know exactly who they are," Aya cut him off. "And so does Amina."
They turned back onto their street, both scanning for any sign of more Aegis operatives. The night felt suddenly hostile, shadows deepened, sounds amplified.
"She'll fight," Karim said, his voice uncertain as they approached their building. "She wouldn't go down easily."
"No, she wouldn't," Aya agreed, then added after a hesitation, "But she's also a survivor first."
"What does that mean?" Karim asked, casting her a sideways glance.
Aya slowed her pace slightly, regretting the words that were about to come out of her mouth. "In Egypt, when things went bad... she chose herself. Left me to deal with the consequences."
"But she came back in Morocco, she didn’t leave us."
"You’re right. But it was different circumstances." Aya's voice tightened. "She came back because it aligned with her interests."
"You don't really believe that," Karim challenged. "I've seen how committed she is to our cause. How she looks at you."
Aya didn't answer immediately, unsure of Karim’s intent. When she did, her voice was softer. "That's what scares me. If they figure out how much she cares..." She shook her head. "Amina, with nothing to lose, is dangerous. Amina, with something to protect, is vulnerable. I just hope she can tap back into the former if she needs to."
They reached their building and climbed the stairs in tense silence, each lost in their own thoughts.
"Maybe she'll escape on her own," Karim ventured as they approached their floor. "She's resourceful, clever. She might have already—"
He stopped mid-sentence. Their apartment door hung ajar again, only this time the wood around the lock was splintered.
Aya drew the knife from her boot. "Stay behind me."
"Like hell," Karim whispered. "We go in together."
She nodded, a fleeting smile of appreciation crossing her face before her expression hardened again. They pushed the door open with synchronized caution.
The apartment was dark, but enough streetlight filtered through the windows to reveal the destruction inside. The coffee table lay overturned. Sofa cushions had been slashed open, white stuffing spilling out like wounds. In the kitchen, every cabinet stood open, contents strewn across the counters.
"Clear the bedrooms," Aya whispered. "Don't touch anything they might have rigged."
They moved through the apartment methodically, clearing each room. The devastation was thorough. Mattresses were flipped, drawers were emptied, and even the bathroom medicine cabinet was ransacked. But their most valuable items, the documents from the cemetery and Hassan's notebooks, were still in Aya's bag, which hadn't left her side all evening.
"Look," Karim said from the living room.
He stood by the overturned coffee table, pointing at the floor. Dark droplets led from the entrance toward the kitchen. Blood, still wet.
"She fought back," Aya said, kneeling to examine the trail. "Made them work for it."
"You sound proud," Karim said.
"I am." Aya touched the droplets lightly. "Let's hope it's their blood, not hers."
Her phone buzzed with a message from Leyla: “Finally made it home. Archive materials are secure. Have you all linked up yet? When should we meet tomorrow?”
"Shit. We need to warn her," Karim said, reading over Aya's shoulder.
Aya was already typing: “Get somewhere safe asap. They took Amina. Your place could be compromised. Meet us at the backup location at dawn. Come armed. Tell no one.”
“I don’t know where to start,” Aya sighed. “We need to find her.”
Karim put his hands on her shoulders. "What would Amina do if our positions were reversed?"
"Find me, and kill anyone who stood in her way with no consideration of any consequences," she answered immediately. "I mean us, stalk whoever took us, and end them."
Aya’s mind flashed back to Egypt. Being left for dead, watching the woman she loved flee to safety. But that was then. How quickly the old assurances had come back.
"Wait,” Aya said. “Her phone."
"You said it went to voicemail."
"Yes, but..." Aya navigated to her contacts, found Amina's entry. "There's a small chance that if Amina’s been putting up enough of a fight that the tracking hasn't been disabled yet because they’ve been too busy trying to restrain her. It looked like they only had two guys on her, plus the driver. Should work even as long as the battery hasn’t been pulled."
She tapped the icon. A map opened, showing their current location. She held her breath and refreshed the screen.
A blue dot appeared across Kadıköy, moving slowly through the streets.
"Got her," Aya breathed. "She's about two kilometers northeast. Moving slowly."
"Or someone has her phone," Karim pointed out.
"Then we find that someone, and figure out why they have our friend’s phone." Aya was already heading for the door. "Either way, it's our only lead."
They descended the stairs two at a time, bursting onto the street. The blue dot on Aya's screen moved steadily through Istanbul, leading them away from the waterfront into an older, more industrial area.
"Wait," Karim said after they'd gone six blocks. "Stop for a second."
"We can't stop. We're losing—"
"No, listen." He pulled her into a doorway. "We're being followed."
“Sorry, you’re right. Good catch.” Aya pulled out her phone, angling it to use the screen's reflection. Behind them, about thirty meters back, two figures in dark outfits had stopped when they did, lurking about a block behind them.
"I see them," she continued. "They're not trying very hard to hide."
"Maybe they want us to know they're there."
"Or they're herding us." Aya studied the map again. Amina's signal had stopped moving, hovering over a building another two kilometers ahead. "We need to lose them before we get there."
They slipped out of the doorway, maintaining their previous pace. At the next intersection, they turned right, then immediately ducked into an alley. They pressed against the wall, listening for footsteps.
The suits passed by the alley entrance, then stopped. Aya could hear them speaking to each other in rapid Turkish.
"Now," Aya whispered.
They sprinted down the alley, emerging onto a busier street filled with bars and restaurants.
"There." Karim pointed to a pub with a green awning and glowing Guinness sign: Teacher's Pub. Through the windows, they could see it was crowded, with what looked like live music starting.
They pushed through the door into a wave of warmth and noise. It was everything Aya could hope for in the moment—dimly lit, packed with people, and loud enough to swallow conversation. The interior was all dark wood and brass fixtures, walls decorated with vintage photographs and beer advertisements. A small stage in the corner hosted a guitarist just beginning his set, the opening chords competing with the crowd's chatter.
They wound their way to the bar, which ran along the wall beneath shelves crowded with bottles and beer steins. The bartender, a broad-shouldered man with long salt-and-pepper hair, raised his eyebrows in question.
"Guinness," Karim called over the noise, holding up two fingers.
While they waited for their drinks, Aya scanned the pub. Tables filled the main floor, each surrounded by groups deep in animated conversation. More seating lined the windows, offering views of the street, not ideal for their purposes. A darker section toward the back looked more promising.
Their beers arrived. Aya paid with cash and then led Karim toward the rear of the pub, where they found a small table partially obstructed behind a wooden pillar but with a view to the door.
"Can you still track her?" Karim asked once they'd settled.
Aya checked her phone. "Signal's stationary. About six blocks from here. Either that’s where she is, that’s where they shut it off, or that’s where they ditched her phone." She took a long drink of the Guinness, helping to calm her racing thoughts.
“Or that’s where they want to lure us,” Karim said.
Aya shot him a severe look.
The guitarist finished tuning and launched into a cover of something Aya knew was by Radiohead but couldn’t name. The crowd quieted slightly, attention turning toward the small stage.
"Sorry, we’ll find her," Karim said, leaning close to be heard. "And this is smart. Hiding in plain sight. Just like Çelebi’s gravestones. How long until we head toward her signal?"
"We wait fifteen minutes, make sure we've lost our tail, then move."
“Solid plan.”
"Amina would already be three moves ahead." Aya's hand tightened on her glass.
"Hey." Karim massaged her leg under the table. "We'll find her. She's tough."
"Salah’s tough, and I keep picturing how broken he was when we rescued him in the Sahara base. Tough doesn't stop the Aegis."
"Neither does stress or guilt. But we need to let Leyla know what’s up."
Aya’s thumbs flew over her encrypted chat: “Hot zone. Aegis tail. In Teacher Pub. Going dark & relocating after we locate Amina.”
Three dots pulsed, then Leyla’s reply popped: “Copy. See you at fallback rendezvous from this morning if burned.” 
Before Aya could respond, the pub door opened again. Two men entered and stood just inside the entrance, scanning the crowd methodically.
"Don't turn around," Aya murmured. "Two potentials. Stiff, alert."
"Our friends?"
"Different ones. But they’re slightly too clean-cut for this place. Vibe’s off."
The men began moving through the pub, checking each table with professional thoroughness. The music and crowd provided cover, but it wouldn't hide them for long.
"Kitchen?" Karim suggested, nodding toward a swinging door marked "Staff Only" behind the bar.
"They'll have someone watching the back if they came in so brazenly. We could take them, but if we start a fight in here, we’re all going to jail."
The men were halfway through the room. In moments, they'd reach the rear section.
Aya made a split-second decision. She pulled Karim close and kissed him, angling their bodies to shield their faces from the searching men. Not the most original trick, she thought, but sometimes classics worked best.
She kept one eye slightly open, watching the reflection in a brass beer tap behind the bar. They paused, scanned past the embracing couple, then continued toward the back of the pub.
"Now," she whispered against Karim's lips.
They slipped from their table, moving swiftly but not obviously toward the front entrance. Their pursuers were still checking the pub, pretending to be looking for a good spot to see the music. 
The guitarist launched into a louder song, the crowd responding with enthusiasm that mercifully got everyone standing and swaying along.
Aya and Karim were three steps from the door when one of the men turned.
Their eyes met across the crowded pub. For a heartbeat, everything froze. Then the man's hand moved toward his jacket.
Aya didn't wait to see what he was reaching for. She grabbed Karim's hand and yanked him outside through the open door, breaking into a run the moment they hit the street.
Aya pulled out her phone without breaking stride, checking Amina's location. Still stationary, now five blocks northeast.
They cut through an alley, vaulted a low fence, and emerged onto a quieter street lined with shuttered shops. Behind them, footsteps echoed. Their pursuers had cleared the pub and were giving chase.
"This way," Aya hissed, pulling Karim into another alley, then behind a dumpster, crouched low, then spun around as their two pursuers rounded the corner.
Aya clocked the movement, and the CCTV dome above the alley. No shots, she calculated. Too many phones. One discharge in Kadıköy and counter‑terror units would flood the grid, and whatever shield Aegis was hiding behind would evaporate.
The first man was drawing his weapon when Aya closed the distance in three fluid strides. She caught his gun hand at the wrist, twisted sharply, and drove her knee into his forearm. The weapon clattered away as he cried out. Without pause, she pivoted and swept his legs, sending him crashing face-first into the brick wall.
The second pursuer had his gun half-drawn when Aya spun toward him. She ducked under his panicked swing and rose behind his guard. Her palm strike caught him just under the jaw, snapping his head back. As he staggered, she grabbed his jacket and used his momentum to slam him into the opposite wall.
Both men lay unconscious on the cobblestones. Karim stood frozen, holding an empty crate he'd grabbed from the dumpster. 
"Help me search them," Aya said, already moving. They found two phones and two radios.
Aya quickly crushed the radios under her heel, then straightened. "Now we run."
Three blocks. Amina's signal hadn't moved.
They approached the building cautiously. It was an aged, nondescript commercial structure, five stories of weathered concrete with rows of grimy, stained windows. No lights, no obvious security, just a metal door with a keypad lock.
"We need to get inside," Karim whispered.
Aya was already examining the keypad. The buttons were worn unevenly. 2, 5, 7, and 9 showed the most use. She tried several combinations before the lock clicked open on 5-9-7-2.
The door revealed a stairwell illuminated with flickering fluorescents. They climbed quickly but quietly, following the tracker to the third floor. The signal led them down a hallway past several unmarked doors to what appeared to be an abandoned office suite from the early 2000s.
This door wasn't locked. It swung open at Aya's touch, revealing a large room with overturned desks, stacks of chairs, and scattered papers. The windows had been covered with newspaper, letting in just enough streetlight to navigate.
"There," Karim said, pointing.
On the floor near the far wall lay a phone, Amina's phone, its screen cracked but still functioning.
"Oh god," Aya breathed.
The wall was splattered with blood, still wet, dripping down the cracked plaster. More blood pooled on the floor, far too much for anyone to lose and survive.
But there was no body.
Aya knelt beside the phone, her need for action warring with rising panic. She forced herself to observe, to analyze. The blood spatter was wrong somehow, but she couldn’t place it.
"It's staged," she realized. "Look at the splatter. It's not from impact. Someone threw this blood against the wall."
"To make us think..."
"That she's dead." Aya picked up Amina's phone, noting the deliberate placement. "They wanted us to find this. Wanted us to think we were too late."
A slow clap echoed from the doorway.
They spun to find Malik standing in the entrance, flanked by four men with guns drawn. His expression was somewhere between amusement and respect.
"Very good, Ms. Amrani," he said. "That only took you seconds. You may be every bit as perceptive as your father. However, you did still willingly run into this building without any reconnaissance, and against any proper training, so not exactly full marks.”
Aya rose slowly, Amina's broken phone still in her hand. "Where is she?"
"Safe. For now." Malik stepped further into the room, his men fanning out to block any escape. "Whether she stays that way depends entirely on you."
"What do you want?"
"What I've always wanted. What your father died trying to hide." His eyes glittered in the dim light. "I want you to finish his work, Aya. I want you to find the Astral Lens. And I want you to give it to me."
The blood on the wall continued its slow drip, counting out the seconds in the sudden silence.
Chapter 11
Malik's words stung like the tear gas that still clung to the back of Aya's throat. She closed her fingers on Amina’s broken phone until the cracked glass cut in. Blood had started to congeal on the wall; its metallic smell cut through the abandoned building's dust. Four men in black framed Malik, rifles lowered, but hungry for a reason to raise them.
"Well," Aya said, her voice steady as she clocked the rifles, calculating their odds. "Now we know what you want. I want you to tell me where Amina is, and to never talk about my father again."
Malik's smile was patient, almost paternal. "Your loyalty is admirable, Ms. Amrani. Your father had the same quality. It's what made him so formidable."
"I believe I just asked you not to talk about my father."
"Hassan understood what was at stake. The lens could reshape our understanding of our place in the universe. Help us understand what natural resources are really at our disposal. It could expand space travel, push us further into the future than any modern technology could ever dream of. Hassan knew this.”
"And you killed him for it."
Malik stepped further into the room, his polished shoes avoiding the staged blood pool. "You’ve been misled. I'm offering you a choice. Work with us. Complete your father's research. Help us find the lens."
"Or what? You'll kill us just like him?"
"I did not kill your father," Malik said calmly.
The words hit bone. Lies or truth, Aya considered. Her nails dug into her palm around the phone.
Malik gestured to his men. "The lens exists. Your activities today prove you're close to finding it. Why not benefit from what you discover instead of fighting a losing battle? Imagine clean energy beyond anything currently possible. 
“Scientific, controllable, not like the djinn. Your father was brilliant but shortsighted. He couldn't see past centuries of superstition to grasp the opportunity. You saw the potential of the Amulet of Idris in Morocco to harness unthinkable energy. Join us, and have a leading voice in how it’s used, free from blunt instruments like Léglise. We are not him. We are not his organization. He went rogue and double-crossed even us.”
Aya glanced at Karim. His face was taut with tension, but his eyes were alert, calculating angles and distances. Four guns plus Malik. No good options for escape.
A muffled thud came from somewhere deeper in the building.
Malik frowned. "What was—"
Another thud, followed by a strangled cry that cut off abruptly.
One of the operatives clicked his radio. "Team Two, report."
Static.
"Team Two, respond."
More static. Then, faintly: "...need backup... she's..."
The transmission ended in a gurgle.
Malik's composure cracked. "You two, check the south corridor. Now."
The two men moved toward the door. They'd barely crossed the threshold when a body crashed into them from the hallway—another man dressed in a dark hoodie, his throat slit. 
The collision sent all three sprawling.
A smoke canister clinked across the floor, then another from the hall, and gray swallowed the room.
A voice in Turkish barked outside the door.
Automatic fire ripped through the haze, muzzle flashes strobing.
Aya grabbed Karim and dove behind a support beam as bullets chewed into the wall where they'd been standing. Through the smoke, she could make out struggling figures, the sharp screams of pain.
Then, through the chaos, a familiar voice: "Stay down!"
Leyla burst through the smoke, assault rifle in her hands. She fired controlled bursts, driving Malik's remaining men toward the windows. Behind her came Amina, bloodied and with a slight limp, but very much alive, a combat knife in each hand.
Amina moved like Aya had seen countless times, closing with surgical precision on one of Malik's operatives. The man turned to fire, but she was already inside his guard. Her knives found two gaps between his body armor plates. He dropped with a wet gasp.
"Fall back!" Malik shouted, firing blindly through the smoke as he retreated toward the door.
“I’m coming for you, Malik,” Amina yelled. “You will die. It may not be right now, but it will be very, very soon.”
Leyla advanced, laying down a relentless volley of gunfire. Her jittery, academic demeanor had vanished, replaced by something harder, professional. She moved surgically, checking corners, controlling lanes of fire.
Half a minute later, the gunfire thinned; Malik and the survivors were already gone. The smoke began to clear, revealing three bodies on the floor, all wearing Aegis tactical gear.
"Amina!" Aya called and rushed to her side.
Blood ran from a gash above Amina's eye, and she favored her left leg as she walked, but she bared a grin that almost concealed the pain in her eyes. "Hey, babe. You miss me?"
"How did you… What happened?"
"Let's not have this conversation here," Leyla interrupted, slinging the rifle over her shoulder. "He’ll be back with reinforcements if we linger. We need to move. We were lucky."
They helped Amina down the stairs and out through a service entrance. Leyla led them through a maze of back alleys, hopping a fence, and emerging in an unfamiliar, residential section of the city.
"Where were you?" Aya asked as they hurried through the night. "After they took you—"
"They grabbed me as I entered our apartment," Amina said, grimacing as she limped. "Black bag treatment, and eventually tranquilized me in the car. Woke up in that building with Malik asking questions about the lens, about what we'd found today."
"How did you escape?"
"I didn't. Leyla found me." Amina glanced at their companion, and a complicated expression passed across her face, gratitude wrestling with the suspicion she'd harbored hours earlier. The suspicion was lost. "Turns out our new friend has some serious skills."
"After your messages, I called in some old contacts for backup," Leyla said. "When they separated to search for you two, we made our move."
"And you killed those men," Karim said, studying her from a few paces behind.
Leyla shrugged. "Yeah, they would have killed us. The Aegis doesn't leave witnesses."
She led them to a shabby apartment building, its facade crumbling but distinct. They climbed four flights of protesting stairs to a heavy wooden door marked only with a number.
"A safe house," Leyla said, producing a key. "Not on any of my official records. Who knows what else is compromised."
Inside was a sparse but functional space: mattresses on the floor, basic kitchen facilities, and walls lined with maps of Istanbul. Heavy curtains covered the windows, and Aya noted the exits: main door, fire escape, and direct access to the roof.
"So, I take it you're not just an archaeologist who did a little work for Omar in the past," Aya said, helping Amina to one of the mattresses.
"Well, I am an archaeologist," Leyla corrected, finding a first-aid kit. "But the Turkish intelligence service paid for my graduate studies. In exchange, I occasionally helped with operations that are easier to access as a scholar."
"You're a spy."
"Was. Past tense. I left that life behind years ago." She began cleaning Amina's wounds. "But old habits and old contacts can be useful when death squads come calling."
As Leyla worked on Amina's injuries, Aya noticed her friend’s hands shaking slightly, the adrenaline wearing off, and reality setting in.
"They knew everything," Amina said quietly. "About the cemetery, the inscriptions, what we found. They have people watching every move we make."
"Not every move," Karim said. "They didn't know about Leyla's safe house."
"Give them time." Amina's voice was bitter. "I'm starting to think we're in over our heads, again. Malik has resources we can't match, just like in Morocco. Maybe we should—"
"What? Give up?" Aya sat beside her. "Let them win?"
"Survive." Amina met her eyes. "I've been doing this long enough to know when the odds are impossible. We could walk away. Disappear. Let someone else be the hero."
"You don't mean that."
"Don't I?" Amina touched the fresh bandage on her forehead. "I didn't sign up to save the world, Aya. Maybe it's time I went back to what I'm good at."
Before Aya could respond, Karim's phone buzzed. He looked at the screen, brow furrowing in surprise.
"It's Salah."
They exchanged glances. They hadn’t heard from Salah or Omar directly since they’d arrived, and the current plan, communicated by Leyla, was radio silence except for emergencies.
"Answer it," Aya decided.
Salah's face appeared on the screen, his normally calm features tight with urgency. "Alhamdulillah. I've been trying to reach you for hours."
"We had some trouble," Aya said dryly. “How are you, Salah?”
"I'm hanging in there, man," Salah said, his voice carrying his familiar laid-back cadence despite the urgency in his eyes. He adjusted his position, wincing slightly as he leaned forward. "Leg's still giving me hell, but the doctors say I dodged any permanent damage." He flashed the easy grin Aya remembered, though it faded quickly.
His dreads were pulled back in a loose knot, and the healing scar on his temple caught the light when he turned. "Omar's been checking in, making sure I don't push too hard, but this tech stuff?" He tapped his laptop. "I don’t need my surf legs, you know? Been diving into these Aegis networks, riding the encryption all the way down."
He paused, his expression hardening, the same transformation Aya had witnessed in the Moroccan desert when danger closed in. "Listen, what I've found isn't good. Not good at all, brothers and sisters. Whatever chill we thought we bought ourselves after the Atlas? It's gone."
"I've been monitoring Aegis communications, waiting, watching. The good news is I’m in their system now, and I don’t think they have any idea. After today's events, that’s as good as confirmed. Their chatter exploded. They're not being as careful about encryption anymore. And they were not happy with how things went down in al-Maghrib or with how Léglise buried their op. They can’t have another repeat. They’re panicking about something.”
"About what?"
"Tomorrow. Something big is happening at noon. All their teams are being recalled to Istanbul. It sounds like they have operations right now in Phnom Penh, Tromso, Tbilisi, and outside Dar es Salaam, at least those are the ones that I know of." He leaned closer to the camera. "And I've been tracking their movements in Turkey through some hacked CCTV footage. You need to see this."
Salah clicked something loudly, and the screen shifted to grainy security footage from a camera that was definitely in Kadıköy. Aya watched as teams of Aegis operatives moved through the streets, clearly coordinating an operation.
"I've identified at least four dozen operators," Salah continued. "Plus support staff, vehicles, a boat on the Bosphorus. This isn't just a retrieval team; it's another small army, just like the Sahara. Their resources are barely dented."
"Can you identify their leadership?" Leyla asked, moving to look at the screen.
"Working on it. So far, I've got one match."
A clear image appeared: Malik, caught by a traffic camera, his hood pulled back to reveal his face.
"His name is Malik Osman. He’s German, a former spy for BND. But that's not the interesting part. Brace yourselves."
Salah sighed before he pulled up another image—older, grainier, but unmistakably the same man. He stood in what looked like an archaeological dig site, surrounded by other researchers.
"That's my father," Aya gasped.
"Taken eight years ago at a conference in San Jose," Salah confirmed. "They knew each other, Aya. Malik worked with your father."
Aya stared at the image, trying to reconcile the smiling young man standing next to her father with the cold professional who'd just tried to kill them. It hadn’t been bluster. He had known Hassan.
"I've been going through old academic databases, following the connections. Malik wasn't just at one event with your father. They frequently appeared at the same sites, the same conferences. Twice, they presented on the same panel. It would appear they were friends, Aya."
"You're saying Malik knew about the lens that long ago, before the Aegis," Karim said.
"I'm saying Malik might have been looking for it first. Your father's research into Çelebi and the Astral Lens? Those weren't brand new ideas he developed. He and Malik may have already been investigating those connections together. Then something happened.”
"And Malik joined Aegis," Leyla finished. "Or was recruited by them."
"The question is," Aya said, her voice hard, "did Malik betray my father?”
The safe house fell silent as the implications sank in.
"Whatever happened between them," Salah said finally, "Malik and Aegis want the lens badly enough to risk deploying an army that Erdoğan could safely claim is threatening Turkish sovereignty. Enough that they’re pulling resources from all their other projects. And based on what I'm seeing in these communications, he thinks you're the key to finding it somehow."
"Why me?" Aya asked. "Why not just follow the research he did with my father?"
"Maybe he tried. Maybe he hit the same dead ends your father did." Salah paused. "Or maybe there’s another personal connection, like how he left you clues for the Amulet. I don’t know."
"I need to see those papers," she said. "The ones Malik and my father presented together."
"Already sending them to Karim's phone," Salah replied. "But Aya... be careful. Malik isn't just some hired gun. He might know things about the lens that even you don't, and with his past… he’s the real deal."
The call ended, leaving them in tense silence. Amina had stopped talking about leaving. Leyla was already checking her weapons. And Karim was pulling up the first of the academic papers on his phone.
"'Astronomical Alignments in Ottoman Sacred Architecture,'" he read. "By Dr. Hassan Amrani and Malik Osman."
Aya leaned over to look at the screen. Her father's familiar writing style was there, but interwoven with another voice. One that was younger, eager, and equally brilliant. Together, they'd been mapping connections between Ottoman buildings and celestial events, tracing patterns.
"Oh my god," Aya whispered, seeing it suddenly. "They weren't just researching. They were following clues. These papers, the way they read—they're breadcrumbs. My father and Malik were documenting their actual hunt for the lens."
She grabbed the phone, scrolling rapidly through the document. "Look at this section. They're describing the Süleymaniye Mosque, but something's off about these measurements."
Karim pulled out his own phone. "Let me pull up the architectural guide." His fingers flew over the screen. "I've been wanting to visit it since we arrived, so I already downloaded some reference materials."
He held up his phone, showing detailed floor plans and elevation drawings. "These dimensions in your father's paper don't match the current building specifications at all."
"Maybe they made an error? Or were they referencing a historical version of the building?" Amina suggested.
"My father didn't make measurement errors," Aya said firmly. "And neither would Malik. They were both too meticulous."
Karim was comparing the documents side by side now. "Wait, look at this. The prayer hall dimensions in their paper show it's three meters narrower than the current structure. And this dome height calculation..." He paused, double-checking his math. "That's impossible given the existing supports."
Leyla leaned over to study both screens. "What if they weren't measuring the current mosque? Look at the date this was published. 2018. And here in the acknowledgments, they mention being 'unable to access sealed historical chambers due to structural concerns.'"
"What kind of structural concerns?" Aya asked.
Karim was already searching. "There were major earthquakes in the 17th and 18th centuries that damaged parts of the complex. Many chambers and passages were sealed off for safety." His eyes widened as he read further. "But there was restoration work between 2015 and 2018 that reinforced the foundations and restored damaged sections of the mosque... potentially made some of those sealed areas structurally sound again."
"Potentially making some of those sealed areas structurally sound again,” Aya said. "If the restoration work strengthened the foundations, those same chambers might be accessible again.”
“And any structural barriers that stopped them might no longer exist," Karim said.
"We need to get to the Süleymaniye," Aya said. "Tonight. Before Malik realizes we've figured this out."
"Are you insane?" Amina struggled to sit up. "You know Malik wrote that. He knows what it says, and for all we know, he leaked it to Salah. You want to walk into what's obviously a trap, again?"
"No. I want to get there first. My father and Malik were partners once, but something went wrong. Maybe my father discovered something about Malik's true intentions. Maybe that's why he ran, why he hid his research."
"Or maybe," Leyla said quietly, "your father discovered something about the lens itself. Something that made him realize it should never be found."
Outside, Istanbul waited, hiding secrets in its stones along with a new fuse, ready to be lit.
Chapter 12
Spotlights reflected off Süleymaniye Mosque’s minarets, piercing the darkness. Aya crouched in the shadows of a nearby building, her eyes fixed on the structure. The academic paper on her phone had become their unlikely guide so far.
"Four guard rotations," Amina whispered, lowering her binoculars from her partially bandaged head. "Two at the main entrance, one circling the perimeter. Another inside, stationary."
"They'll have security cameras, too," Leyla said. "But it’s just a typical mosque, no reason for heightened security. There’ll be blind spots. And the 2015–2018 retrofit shored up sealed substructures, but the camera grid still treats those passages as bricked off. Modern coverage misses the old ingress.”
Karim was absorbed in his phone, overlaying modern satellite images with 16th-century maps. "According to this, there's an astronomical alignment connecting specific points in the architecture. Look at how they describe the angle of shadow cast by the western minaret during the solstice."
"And my father and Malik were encoding the location of something beneath the structure,” Aya said.
"Or within it," Leyla corrected. "Süleymaniye was built on the site of older structures. There are Byzantine ruins beneath parts of it, and Ottoman architects often incorporated existing foundations."
Aya studied the security pattern once more. "It's now or never. Amina, are you good to move?"
Amina rolled her shoulder carefully. "I've worked with worse."
The cobblestones were slick as they made their way toward the mosque complex.
"Guard," Aya whispered as they reached a small maintenance gate.
They pressed against the stone wall as a flashlight beam swept past. When it vanished around the corner, Aya produced an electronic bypass and placed it against the security panel attached to the ancient gate. After a tense moment, the override engaged, and they squeezed through a gap in the gate, careful to avoid making it creak all the way open.
Inside, the mosque's courtyard was filled with an eerie nighttime calm. The main prayer hall was locked for the night, but according to Karim's analysis, they needed to access the northeastern corner of the complex.
"This way," he whispered, leading them toward a small auxiliary building. "The paper mentions 'the shadow of knowledge falls upon the waters of wisdom at midnight.' It's not poetic; it's literal. There's a small fountain structure that aligns with—"
"That," Aya finished, pointing to an unassuming marble fountain nestled against the complex's outer wall. In daylight, it would be overlooked by visitors admiring the mosque's grander features.
They crept to the fountain, and Leyla knelt beside it, analyzing the intricate carvings. "As with the cemetery, I don’t think these are just decorative patterns."
"The eight-pointed star," Aya said, studying the ancient markings. "To signal to Çelebi's followers, I suppose."
Amina kept watch, her back to them, eyes scanning for guards. "Just hurry it up. We've got about twelve minutes until the next patrol comes by, if they don’t change protocol."
Karim aligned his phone with the star pattern in the sky. "According to the paper, there should be a corresponding point exactly seven meters at bearing 247 degrees."
Aya paced off the distance, ending at what appeared to be a solid wall. "Nothing here."
Leyla joined her. "Ottoman architects built in layers." She pressed her hands against different stones, methodically testing each. On the fifth try, a stone shifted slightly. "Pressure mechanism," she said, her eyes bright with excitement. "Needs more force. Karim, come help us."
Together, the three pushed against the stone with all their weight. For a moment, nothing happened. Then, a section of wall pivoted inward slightly, releasing pressure and revealing a short, narrow passage.
"No way," Karim whispered, awe overriding caution. "A hidden entrance that managed to remain undiscovered for centuries?"
"Not entirely undiscovered," Aya said. "My father and Malik found it. But I doubt they ever got here because of preexisting damage. Malik may have a better idea now, which would mean he could guess where we are."
The passage beyond was pitch black. Aya turned on her phone's flashlight, revealing rough-hewn stone steps descending into darkness. Cool air flowed upward, carrying the musty scent of dust, decay, and damp. She was transported back to Morocco in an instant, turning to Amina, fearing any reaction from the markings on her arm.
"Ten minutes," Amina warned, taking up position at the entrance without any sign of possession or pain. "I'll keep watch here. If I whistle, run back immediately."
Leyla produced a small LED lantern from her bag. "I came prepared."
The stairs descended steeply, each step worn smooth by feet that had traversed them centuries ago. After thirty steps, the passage leveled out into a small antechamber. Three archways branched in different directions.
"Which way?" Aya asked, examining the worn inscriptions above each arch.
Leyla studied the Ottoman script. "Left says 'path of stars.' Center is 'path of elements.' Right is 'path of light.'"
"Stars," Karim said immediately. "Çelebi was an astronomer first."
The left passage narrowed as they progressed, the ceiling dropping until they were forced to crouch. Then, without warning, it opened into a chamber that drew gasps from all three.
The room was circular, nearly fifteen meters across, its domed ceiling painted with constellations that glittered with a reflective pigment. Around the perimeter stood workbenches of stone and wood, their surfaces cluttered with equipment that looked untouched for centuries: glass-blowing tools, alchemical apparatus, and instruments that defied immediate identification.
"This is it," Leyla whispered, moving toward the center of the room. "Çelebi's actual workshop. And it doesn’t look like anyone’s beaten us to it."
Aya approached one of the workbenches, examining what appeared to be grinding stones, polishing instruments, and specialized clamps, all tools for lens-crafting.
Karim had already begun documenting everything with his phone. "My god, this is incredible. I’ve never discovered anything like this."
In the center of the room stood a large stone table inscribed with concentric circles and astronomical markings. Atop it rested a series of drawings on curling, brittle paper. Leyla carefully unrolled one.
"This has to be his Astral Lens," she whispered.
Karim joined her. "Yes, it's the system of lenses and reflective surfaces."
"Designed to focus and amplify energy as we guessed?" Aya asked.
Leyla moved to another bench where a star chart had been painted directly onto the stone surface. "It’s exactly as I thought, as was rumored. Cosmic energy, from deep space. Çelebi believed certain stellar alignments created energy pathways that could be captured and channeled. Look around us. This is all dedicated to harnessing the powers, still beyond our comprehension, in the universe."
"That's impossible," Karim said automatically, then caught himself. "At least, according to conventional physics."
"Conventional physics doesn't account for djinn either," Aya said. “Or some really deranged things Amina and I have found in Thailand or Bulgaria.”
While they examined the documents, Aya paced the chamber, her thief's instincts cataloging everything. On the far side, partially hidden behind a fallen shelf, she discovered what appeared to be a furnace.
"Here’s his forge. This is how he crafted the lens components," she called to the others. "Look at the design."
Karim joined her, examining the strange apparatus with growing excitement. “All just meters below those who would have executed him.”
"The Ottoman Empire was at its scientific height then," Leyla said, carefully examining glass fragments in a nearby cabinet. "The Süleymaniye complex included a hospital, observatory, and multiple madrasas. It would have been the perfect cover for Çelebi's more esoteric work, to cover the coming and going of his circle without arousing suspicion."
"So, we’re guessing the lens was created to harness energy during specific astronomical alignments. Unlimited energy drawn from the cosmos itself,” Aya said.
"Yeah, the same thing Aegis wanted from the Amulet in Morocco. I’m not seeing anything that contradicts our existing ideas," Karim said.
Leyla's face darkened. "But still no idea why they took Emre."
“Or why haven’t they killed him, or us for that matter. They need something from one of us,” Aya said.
Amina's sharp whistle cut through the conversation. Time was running short.
"We need to document everything and go," Aya said, immediately shifting to extraction mode. "Karim, photograph all the documents. Leyla, is there anything we should take with us?"
Leyla hesitated before moving to a small wooden box on one of the workbenches. She opened it carefully, revealing several glass fragments wrapped in ancient cloth. Her eyes widened. "These match the descriptions from your father's notes. The glass fragment, the primary lens element we talked about.
