These could be test pieces, or possibly even the actual component Çelebi hid here."
As they worked rapidly to document the workshop, Amina appeared at the chamber entrance, her expression hardened. "Didn’t you hear me? Guards changed the rotation pattern. We need to leave. Now."
"Just a minute," Karim insisted, photographing the final documents. "This could be the key to understanding the entire lens assembly. To give us an upper hand, finally."
"We don't have a minute. I can’t predict the guards’ movements anymore," Amina snapped. "Unless you want to explain to Turkish authorities why we're trespassing in a national monument, armed to the teeth."
"This is more important," Leyla countered, carefully wrapping glass fragments in cloth. "These components might be the only surviving pieces of the lens."
"More important than our freedom? Our lives?" Amina's voice rose. "We need to be smarter than we have been. We lost Abdelatif, and we barely escaped ourselves in Morocco. Then we allowed ourselves to get lulled into security in Istanbul, all while the Aegis amassed forces around us. I, for one, would not like to get ambushed again."
"We are being smart," Karim insisted. "We're being thorough."
"You don't understand what's at stake," Amina said, her eyes flashing. "I've got these marks crawling under my skin that burn every time we get close to something like this. I can feel it again. This isn't just some big historical discovery; it's dangerous."
"We know it's dangerous," Aya said, trying to defuse the situation. "That's why—"
"No, you don't know," Amina cut her off. "None of you understand what it's like to have been touched by whatever power was in that Amulet. To feel it responding to this place, right now."
"Amina," Aya warned, seeing where this was heading. “What are you feel—”
“You’re all treating this like a museum heist,” Amina said through gritted teeth. “It’s not.”
Karim, still photographing the star chart, didn’t look up. “We’re treating it like science because that’s what it is.”
“Science?” She tapped the marks, now glowing faintly beneath her sleeve as they had in the presence of djinn. “The thing in my veins is not science. Every step closer to that lens, it stirs. It wants something.”
Karim finally raised his head. “We’ll contain it.”
“You don’t get it, professor.” Her voice cracked, softer now, aimed at Aya. “Morocco nearly killed me. The only reason I stayed or even came here was…” She swallowed. “Was you, Aya. I’d follow you anywhere. Hell, I have. I’ve never stopped caring about you, and I’ll never love anyone like I loved you. And nobody in this room understands that history but us, and I know you’ve moved on. But can you listen to me, for once? I’m not one of you academics, but I have some kind of connection to these powers, and we need to get the fuck out of here, now!”
The workshop fell silent. Karim froze, his phone still raised to capture the star chart. His expression shifted from frustration to something carefully, deliberately blank.
Aya felt her chest tighten and regretted every chance she’d passed on telling Karim about her past with Amina. "I... yeah," she said finally, her voice strained with forced neutrality. She cleared her throat. "Okay. We should go."
"Okay, she's right," Karim said, suddenly businesslike. "Security is our priority. We've got what we need. We all care about each other, and our well-being should come first right now."
Amina folded her arms and stormed back out of the workshop.
The silent tension crackled as they gathered their findings and prepared to leave. Just as they were about to exit, Aya noticed something in the star chart that had been partially hidden by dust. Brushing it away, she revealed the eye within radiating lines that had appeared throughout her father's notebook.
"Wait," she said. "The Celestial Gate." The diagram in Hassan’s journal matched the central element of the lens assembly on the workbench exactly.
Before she could elaborate, Karim's phone vibrated. He glanced at the screen, his face paling. "It's Salah."
He activated the speaker. Salah's voice came through, tight with urgency. "Where are you? Tell me you're not still at the mosque."
"We're just leaving," Karim said. "Why?"
"Because I'm tracking Aegis comms and they've converged on your location," Salah said. "They know you're there, and they're moving in. Now."
The distant sound of boots on stone echoed down the passage, growing louder with each second.
Aya met Leyla's eyes, then Karim's, the recent tension momentarily forgotten in the face of immediate danger.
"Too late," Aya said. "They're already here. Grab anything that we might need."
Chapter 13
"Stand back," Leyla whispered, reaching into her bag. She produced what looked like a road flare, but smaller. "Phosphorus smoke device."
"You just happen to have that?" Karim asked.
"After they snatched Amina, yeah, I came prepared." She struck the cap against the stone. "Cover your mouths and stay close."
The device hissed to life, instantly filling the narrow passage with dense, billowing smoke. Aya pulled her shirt over her nose and mouth, gesturing for the others to follow as she led them not toward the entrance, but deeper into the passage network.
"Wrong way," Amina said through a cough.
"Trust me," Aya said. Her instincts told her Çelebi had a backup plan. "We’ll take the ‘Path of Light.’"
They stumbled through the thickening smoke, following the right-hand passageway. Behind them, shouts echoed off stone walls as their pursuers entered the workshop.
The passage narrowed, then began to slope upward. Light infiltrated the darkness, the soft, ambient glow of the mosque's exterior illumination filtering through what appeared to be a sewer drain.
"There," Aya pointed to a metal grate overhead. "Karim, boost me up."
He interlaced his fingers without comment, and Aya stepped into his hands while he lifted her toward the grate. It was old wrought-iron, corroded at its moorings. She pushed against it, feeling it give slightly.
"Amina, your knife."
Amina passed up a combat blade. Aya wedged it into the grate's edge, working it back and forth until, with a screech of protesting metal, one corner broke free. She pressed her back against the side of the opening, widening it until the entire grate shifted.
"Got it," she grunted, pushing the grate aside. Cool night air rushed in.
One by one, they hauled themselves up through the opening, emerging behind a decorative wall in the mosque's outer courtyard. They had bypassed the main security perimeter, but could see shadowy figures moving quickly across the grounds, sticking to the shadows.
“Convenient for Çelebi and his collaborators to avoid detection. Clever,” Karim said.
"Northwest corner," Leyla whispered, pointing toward a service gate. "It's our only shot."
They crouched behind ornamental planters, Aya's heart hammering against her ribs. The courtyard stretched before them, its marble surface gleaming under the ambient lighting. Between them and the perimeter wall lay fifty meters of open ground, broken only by decorative pools and geometric flower beds that would provide minimal cover. The shadows were their only hope.
"On my count," Aya whispered, watching the shadows move along the eastern colonnade. "Three... two..."
They broke from cover in unison, staying low as they sprinted toward the first marble fountain. Amina reached it first, pressing her back against its rim while scanning for threats. Leyla and Karim dove behind a raised planter.
The sound of boots on stone grew louder, more organized. Aya counted at least six sets. They had seconds before the search pattern reached their position.
She caught Amina's eye and pointed toward the next piece of cover, a larger fountain with ornate stonework. Amina nodded, then gestured to Karim and Leyla, indicating they should angle toward the maintenance building instead.
They moved in coordinated bursts, using the decorative architecture as stepping stones across the exposed space. Behind them, the search was closing in, as flashlight beams swept methodically across the courtyard, probing every shadow.
While Aya prepared for the final sprint to the perimeter wall, every floodlight blazed to full intensity. 
The courtyard erupted in harsh white light, transforming their shadows into stark silhouettes against the ancient stone, and a voice cut through the night, amplified by a handheld speaker.
"Ms. Amrani and friends. How remarkable to find you here."
Malik stood at the center of the courtyard, surrounded by at least a dozen of his soldiers. Unlike them, in full combat gear and night vision goggles, he made no pretense of concealment, dressed in a charcoal suit that absorbed the light. He held up a hand, and the closing circle of agents halted.
"I truly admire your persistence," he continued, his voice echoing off the mosque. "Hassan would be proud."
Aya assessed their tactical position, yet again counting just how outnumbered they were. Four operatives by the northwest gate. Three more were approaching from the east. The wall behind them was too high to scale quickly.
"What do you want, Malik?" Leyla demanded.
His eyes shifted to her casually. "Dr. Yilmaz. Your continued involvement remains somewhat unexpected, and I must say we underestimated you. See, Aya, we’re not some omniscient conspiracy or invisible hand. We make mistakes, too."
Leyla tensed beside Aya.
"Emre is safe," Malik said, addressing Leyla directly. "Conducting research willingly, actually, more willingly than you might think. He's brilliant enough to recognize what we're working toward. He could use your expertise as well, and I'm genuinely hoping you'll join him."
"Why should we believe anything you say?" Karim demanded.
"Because I'm not asking you to work for Aegis. I fear that no matter how truthful our claims of employment were, Philipe Léglise made that path impossible with his megalomania in Morocco. I'm asking you to work for humanity's future." Malik's voice remained calm despite the armed standoff. "The lens represents a threshold moment. Either humanity takes this evolutionary step, accepts the risks inherent in transformation, or we continue our slow slide toward extinction while pretending incremental solutions will save us. Hassan understood the stakes even if he couldn't accept them. His fear was human, but it was also a failure of courage at the moment courage mattered most."
"Like you offered my father?" Aya challenged.
Something passed across Malik's face—genuine grief, perhaps, quickly controlled. "Hassan made his choice, and I respected him too much to force his hand. I wanted him as a colleague, not a prisoner. His death was…" He paused, and for the first time, his composure cracked slightly. "His death was the universe's way of removing the man who could have helped us navigate this transition safely. Now we proceed without his wisdom, his caution, his ability to see both the power and the danger. That's the real tragedy."
While the conversation drew on, they were being systematically surrounded, but the agents were maintaining distance, clearly following orders to capture, not kill or provoke.
"I'm offering you a choice," Malik continued, his voice taking on the tone of a teacher trying to reach a stubborn student. "Join the research. Help shape how this knowledge is used. Your father's perspective, his caution, his ethical framework, and of course, his research, which I know you possess. We need his voice, his work. I'm not asking you to abandon his principles. I'm asking you to complete what he started with the wisdom he would have brought if he'd lived."
"And if we refuse?" Aya asked.
"Then you force me to proceed without you, which means without Hassan's influence, without the ethical guardrails he would have insisted on. You make the very outcome you fear more likely." Malik gestured toward the mosque. "I'd rather not leave you to the Turkish authorities for breaking into a historical monument, but I will if you make progress impossible. Some sacrifices are necessary."
Aya exchanged glances with the others. Amina's slight nod toward the decorative pool. Leyla's eyes flicked toward the scaffolding on the adjacent building. Karim's subtle shift of weight, preparing to move.
"We need a few minutes to discuss," Aya called back, playing for time.
"By all means." Malik smiled thinly. "Though I should mention that my patience, unlike my resources, is finite."
Aya turned her back to Malik, drawing her team into a tight huddle. "So, we’re obviously not cooperating with these bastards,” she whispered. “On my signal, Amina goes left toward the wall they’re still restoring, with the scaffolding. I think it’s best if Leyla and Karim go right, toward the maintenance building. I'll create a diversion and go for the gate."
"They'll catch at least one of us," Amina murmured. “I mean it won’t be me, but—”
"But they can’t catch all of us. Plus, I have a feeling they’ll go for me, and we’re completely cornered otherwise. Whoever escapes, meet at the secondary location," Aya replied. "Whoever gets free, gets help. They’re not going to kill us; none of the shots they’ve taken have been at us. They’ve been suppressive. They need something from us still, and they don’t think they can get it if we’re dead."
Karim's eyes met Aya’s, pained but resolute. "Be careful."
Aya nodded, then spoke loudly for Malik's benefit. "We need more assurances."
"Of course," Malik called back. "Perhaps if you shared what you've discovered so far as a gesture of good faith?"
“Actually,” Aya said, letting Malik see the small muslin sachet she produced. “You might like to see this up close.” 
Malik’s eyes widened at what Aya knew was a mixture of magnesium filings, fine flour, and crushed potassium nitrate. She flicked her lighter and tossed the pouch into the air. A blinding flash and whoosh of white spark shower erupted, stunning every eye for two precious seconds.
Leyla followed with a tactical smoke pellet amidst the distraction. Gray vapor flooded the portico. Cameras whited out, guards searched for targets.
The team exploded into motion. Amina vaulted over a low ornamental wall with her typical feline grace. Leyla and Karim broke right, sprinting toward the maintenance building. Aya herself charged directly at the nearest Aegis operative, catching him off guard. She ducked under his grasp, using his momentum to send him sprawling into the pool. As he fell, she attempted to wrestle his gun free, but it slipped through her hands.
Chapter 14
Chaos erupted across the courtyard. Malik barked orders as his team scrambled to intercept the fleeing group. Two agents moved to block Amina, but she was already scaling the scaffolding, ascending with ease despite her injuries. Aya had seen her in worse shape; she knew she would get free.
Aegis rifles snapped up but hesitated. The mosque's CCTV cameras hung overhead. Malik's men awaited orders. So, Aya thought, not as long a reach in Istanbul, without the US military on speed dial. But for how long?
Amina was already over the wall, disappearing into the darkness. Leyla and Karim sprinted for the maintenance building, navigating between construction materials with Karim's knowledge of the grounds.
Aya ran for the gate, legs pumping, lungs burning, her side screaming.
From the haze, Malik's voice carried clearly, remarkably calm despite the chaos: "We're not executing people on holy ground for the sake of schedules. Capture only." 
His team hesitated, clearly surprised by the order. "Sir, we can still—"
"No fatalities," Malik repeated firmly. "Hassan's daughter doesn't become a martyr in a mosque courtyard. That's not how this ends. Shut down the floodlights and pull back before authorities arrive. We have what we need from tonight."
The first shot rang out just as Aya reached the maintenance building. Leyla pulled her through the door as a rubber bullet ricocheted off the frame. Karim slammed it shut behind them, wedging a metal bar through the handles.
"It won't hold long," Leyla panted.
"It might not need to." Karim was already moving through the cluttered space, navigating between equipment and supplies. "I studied these plans for hours when we couldn’t make sense of the old measurements in Malik and Hassan’s publications. There should be a municipal access tunnel through here. Connects to the water system."
“There,” Aya called, pointing to the back wall.
There was a heavy steel door with Turkish warning signs. Leyla attacked its lock.
After a series of rattling bludgeons, the lock yielded, and Leyla pulled the door open to reveal a narrow utility tunnel lit by sparse emergency bulbs. "Move."
They plunged into the tunnel as the barricaded door behind them began ripping free from the wall under impact. The access passage stretched before them, curving away beneath the streets of Istanbul.
"What about Amina?" Karim asked as they ran.
"She knows where to go," Aya replied, confident it was true.
Water dripped steadily from pipes overhead, each drop echoing in the confined space. Their footsteps splashed through shallow puddles as they ran, the sound magnified and distorted. Behind them, shouts echoed.
"Left here," Karim gasped, pointing to a junction where another passage branched away from their route. The new tunnel was smaller, forcing them to duck as they ran. Cables and pipes lined the walls.
Following the passage as it curved upward, the sound of pursuit had faded, but Aya didn't trust the silence. The tunnel system was a maze. Their hunters could emerge anywhere, and they could hit a dead end.
After what felt like an hour but was probably fifteen minutes, they reached a maintenance shaft with a rusted ladder leading upward. City lights filtered down through a heavy grate overhead, along with the muffled sound of traffic.
“Let’s hope it’s movable,” Leyla said.
Karim climbed first, testing the grate's weight before straining to push it aside with a metallic screech. “Finally,” he said.
One by one, they hauled themselves up through the opening, emerging in a narrow alley between two residential buildings. The mosque's minarets were visible in the distance, at least six blocks away.
Aya slid the grate back before rolling two overflowing garbage cans on top of it to hopefully block the light and provide more resistance.
"Let's go," Aya urged, running around two corners before mercifully finding an empty taxi and speeding back to Leyla's safehouse.
They arrived twenty-five minutes later, exhausted and on edge. The apartment was dark and empty, with no sign of Amina.
"She'll make it," Aya said, as much to convince herself as the others. "She always does."
While Leyla re-secured the perimeter, Karim moved to the far side of the room, unpacking their findings from the mosque. An emotional barrier between them felt palpable; a sudden chasm opened by Amina's words.
Aya approached cautiously. "Karim, about what Amina said—"
"Not now," he cut her off, his voice carefully neutral. "We need to analyze these finds while they're fresh in our minds."
"We should talk about it."
"Should we?" He looked up, his expression carefully controlled. "What is there to say? You and Amina were together, and I feel like you had every opportunity in the world to tell me, and that I had a right to know. I feel like a fool. There are still a lot of feelings there, and it’s unclear where you stand now, which makes it very unclear where you stand with me."
"It isn't like that," Aya pleaded. "It was complicated, and it ended badly, long before Morocco. It’s ancient history."
"Clearly, it still matters to Amina. I… I’m not blind either." He returned to arranging the photographs from the workshop, creating deliberate distance. "But we have more important concerns right now."
Before Aya could respond, desperately trying to reassure Karim, a sharp rap on the door froze them all. Leyla drew her weapon, approaching cautiously.
"It's me, and I come alone," came Amina's voice. "Open up, I’m goddamn exhausted. Some of us had to do some actual fighting."
Leyla verified through the peephole before unbolting the door. Amina slipped inside, disheveled but unharmed.
"Lost them near the bazaar with only a few minor scuffles," she mumbled, avoiding eye contact with both Aya and Karim. "Used a couple of rooftops to create distance, then doubled back."
An uncomfortable silence descended, the team's usual post-mission relief dampened. 
Leyla busied herself brewing coffee. Aya watched her, searching for signs of anything else her new ally might be concealing.
"I need to check our new findings against my notes," Karim said, retreating to a corner with his materials.
Amina moved to the opposite side of the room, examining and cleaning her weapons with unnecessary thoroughness. Aya found herself stranded in the middle, isolated by the emotional currents flowing around her.
The fragile silence was broken by the chime of Aya's secure phone. She answered immediately and put the call on speaker.
"Hot damn, you're still alive!" Omar's voice burst through the speaker, a mixture of relief and excitement. "Mashallah, I've been trying to reach you for hours but couldn’t get a call through. Is everyone okay?"
"We're all here," Aya confirmed. "Banged up but intact. It’s good to hear from you. I assume you’ve been in regular contact with Salah?"
"Hamdulillah, and that I have," Omar said rapidly. "But listen, I’m going to jump right in. I found something in Abdelatif's study."
At the mention of their fallen friend, Aya, Karim, and Amina instinctively drew closer.
"What did you find?" Aya asked.
"Well, beyond being a sultan of underworld connections, slash king of carpets, slash trying to coordinate everything for you field-worthy assets, remember how I told you I'd continue studying his materials after you left Marrakech? Turns out there are heaps of references to an Ottoman secret society called 'Hat-ı Sükût' or the Silent Meridian. You know the old man fancied himself some kind of medjay, illuminati, protector, badass. He ate this stuff up. Well, these were dedicated to protecting scientific secrets, and there are some passing references to astronomical events."
"Like, presumably the Astral Lens?" Leyla asked.
"Exactly my guess," Omar confirmed. "But here's where it gets interesting. It looks like Abdelatif believed there was a modern incarnation of this society that endured, or at least was founded by people who discovered their archives and were taking up the mantle."
Karim looked up from his notes. "Well, I guess it could be the Aegis that he suspected. Maybe in an earlier form."
"Speaking of, there's more," Omar continued. "Salah intercepted some Aegis comms, and you're going to find this interesting." He pulled up encrypted messages on his tablet. "Aegis headquarters is furious with Malik. Listen to this: 'Subject team has had multiple opportunities for termination. Cemetery, safehouse, Süleymaniye Mosque. Recommend immediate extraction with extreme prejudice.'" Omar looked up. "That's corporate-speak for 'why haven't you killed them yet?'"
"And Malik's response?" Leyla asked, tension in her voice.
"This is where it gets interesting." Omar scrolled through the messages. "Malik told his superiors, and I'm quoting directly: 'Hassan Amrani's daughter is more valuable alive than dead. Her group is solving archaeological puzzles that our entire research division couldn't crack in three months. The team has located two workshop sites, decoded multiple ciphers, and is consistently one step ahead of our intelligence. I propose a strategy of allowing them to work unimpeded, maintaining surveillance, then negotiating a partnership at critical junctures. Living ones can be persuaded.'"
The words hung in the air.
"More than that," Omar continued, reading further. "There's this bit: 'Hassan's daughter inherited his brilliance. She can be convinced. Every confrontation is a recruitment opportunity. Patience required.' Then his superior's response: 'Patience expired. Terminate if non-compliant.' And Malik's final message: 'Recommendation noted. Will proceed with the current strategy until components are secured. If his daughter is half as capable, she's worth the patience.'"
“That would be why they coordinated with police and weren’t willing to start a massive firefight,” Aya said. “He thinks I’ll turn, be on his side.”
"Basically good news, I think. I do love my friends not getting shot at any more than necessary," Omar confirmed. "But after a while, that kind of pressure might make him more dangerous. A cornered scorpion strikes hardest, you know?"
"What do we know about this Silent Meridian?" Karim asked.
Aya’s mind shot to Malik. Her father. A new generation of Hat-ı Sükût.
"From the little I could find in Abdel’s study, they first formed during Suleiman the Magnificent's reign," Omar said. "Originally, astronomers and mathematicians within the Ottoman court were delving into new approaches to astronomy, and they wanted to learn as much as they could."
"Çelebi was likely a member?" Leyla asked.
"Almost certainly," Omar said. "The society operated in plain sight for decades, then went underground after Sultan Murad III destroyed Taqi al-Din's observatory in 1580. Allegedly, they continued in secret, preserving their knowledge through coded messages, hidden workshops, and secret society rituals. Think freemason type stuff."
"Like the hidden workshop we just found," Amina said.
"That sounds like it would tick the boxes, my fair lady. But, this is the big boy," Omar's voice lowered. "Check Abdelatif's journal, the one you brought from Morocco that had some of the first references we found. This may be crazy, but I have reason to believe there's a hidden pocket in the binding.”
Aya’s eyes darted to her bag, with the journal inside. It was a similar tactic her father had used.
“Abdelatif was a weird geezer, you remember,” Omar said. “Always stitched his own journals. Hand‑sewn, wax‑thread, proper bookbinder’s knots. That crimson one? It never left his side. Not in the desert, not at market, not even when he slept.”
“Maybe.” Aya retrieved the battered journal from her bag. She'd carried it since leaving Morocco, a tangible connection to their fallen friend, along with her dad’s journals. Running her fingers along the leather binding.
“And I remembered an old job,” Omar continued. “Back when we were running a small smuggling operation through Tangier customs, he showed me a trick: slit the spine of a hardcover book, hide the forged manifesto, stitch it closed again. It could be a long shot, but ever since that memory came back to me, the more I believe there had to be a reason he clung to that dusty old thing."
“Here,” Aya murmured. She took out her knife and slid the blade beneath the inner hinge, teasing apart a row of whip-stitches. Threads snapped one by one; the leather separated, revealing a narrow cavity in the spine’s hollow.
Inside lay a thin, narrow, tissue-wrapped object. She drew it out delicately, the book’s covers closing again with a hollow clap, as though they’d exhaled a secret kept far too long.
"What is it?" Karim asked.
Aya unwrapped the tissue paper carefully. Inside lay a small fragment of glass or crystal, its surface etched with microscopic patterns.
"It’s impossible. It looks like a piece of the lens," she breathed. "But how could Abdelatif have had it?"
Karim leaned closer, examining the fragment under the lamplight. "Wait. Look at the edges. No weathering, no oxidation. This isn't four hundred years old." He turned it carefully. "The craftsmanship matches Çelebi's designs, but the glass itself... this was made recently. Within the last few decades, maybe less."
"Abdelatif made it himself?" Amina asked.
"Or had it made." Aya pulled out her father's notebook, flipping to a section she'd glossed over. Dense technical annotations about material compositions and fabrication techniques. "Here. My father wrote about this. 'Several secondary components can be reproduced given precise specifications and appropriate materials. The calibration rings, focusing tubes, and stabilization elements are technically achievable with modern glassmaking. However, others possess properties that cannot be replicated, including specific mineral compositions found only in the original artifacts."
"So some pieces can be recreated," Leyla said slowly, "but not all of them."
“Based on my father’s work a generation ago,” Aya said. “So even that could be outdated.”
"Which means we have no way of knowing which components Aegis has already manufactured," Karim finished. "They could have half the assembly ready. They might only need an irreplaceable piece or two."
"The crown lens," Aya said. "My father's notes called it the control mechanism. If that's one of the pieces that can't be recreated..."
"Then that's what they're really hunting," Amina concluded.
"I’m speechless. Abdelatif never mentioned this to me." Omar said.
"Me neither," Aya said. "Where did he get it? When did he get it?"
"His family collected old trinkets, relics, whatever, for generations. That’s why he was so interested in antiquities and historical mysteries. He might not have known its significance. But why hide it like this?"
Amina approached cautiously. "May I see it?"
Aya held out the delicate sliver. As Amina's fingers touched the glass, the fragment began to glow with a soft light that radiated the colors of the entire color spectrum. Simultaneously, the faded markings on Amina's arm flared with the same luminescence.
Amina gasped, nearly dropping the fragment. "Fuck. It... it recognizes me. What does this mean?"
The glass continued to pulse in her palm, casting luminous reflections across the safehouse walls. The team stared in stunned silence as the alchemical glass responded to the supernatural energy still lingering within Amina's body.
"It’s like the djinn," Karim whispered. "The lens is somehow connected to the same power."
“Sure would be great if Abdelatif was still with us to tell me what the hell is going on.”
Through the phone, Omar's voice was barely audible. "What's happening?"
No one answered. They were transfixed by the glowing fragment and the implications of its response to Amina's touch, a bridge between the cosmic science of Ottoman alchemists and something similar to the supernatural forces they had confronted in the Atlas Mountains.
The fragment's light intensified, casting Amina's face in an eerie glow as the markings on her arm pulsed in perfect synchronization.
Chapter 15
The fragment of glass shimmered with increasing intensity. Amina's arm trembled as the markings responded, lines glowing beneath her skin like living calligraphy, no longer resembling Amazigh symbols but taking on thin lines connecting fixed points and forming geometric patterns. A map of the night sky.
"Someone take this," Amina gasped, holding out the crystal shard.
Karim carefully plucked it from her palm with a cloth from his archaeology kit. The glow dimmed immediately, though it didn't extinguish completely.
Examining the fragment, he said, "Well, I think it’s obvious, but it's responding to whatever residual energy remains in Amina's markings from the djinn encounter."
"Energy transference," Leyla breathed, leaning closer. "Maybe Çelebi didn't just create a lens to focus cosmic radiation. He could have created a system to harness energy from other dimensional planes."
Through the phone, Omar's voice crackled. "Did you say dimensional planes?"
"I’m thinking of it like tuning a radio," Karim said, setting the fragment on a clean sheet of paper. "The djinn exist in a parallel dimension that occasionally intersects with ours. The Amulet of Idris in Morocco was designed to open a doorway that we were able to close. This lens..." He gestured to the pulsing crystal. "I don’t know, maybe this could create a permanent channel instead of a doorway."
Amina rubbed her arm, the markings now faded back to look like typical tattoos. "I can feel it. Like it's trying to communicate."
Aya approached her cautiously. "Are you okay?"
"It doesn't hurt," Amina said, avoiding Aya's eyes. "It feels... familiar. Like in the Atlas Mountains, but more controlled. Directed. Lighter. And no headaches or anything."
Karim photographed the fragment from multiple angles, then compared the images to the workshop blueprints they'd discovered. "This might match one of the smaller components in Çelebi's designs. There are at least six major pieces needed to realize his plans, possibly more."
"Deliberately separated, it would seem," Leyla said, "to prevent misuse."
"Omar," Aya addressed the phone, "did Abdelatif’s notes mention where other components might be?"
"No," Omar replied. "Nothing like that. But I’d start with places connected to the Silent Meridian. Though I understand hunting down the secret hiding places used by a secret, hidden organization may be a little tricky.”
“What about this symbol we keep seeing?” Karim pulled up their photographs of Çelebi's workshop on his tablet, indicating a symbol etched into one of the lens casings. "Look at this marking. It appears on several components, but it doesn’t have anything in common with the celestial imagery that appears on the glass or in Çelebi’s work."
"It’s a merchant's mark," Leyla identified immediately. "Ottoman traders used these to identify their goods.”
Leyla frowned and snapped a close-up of the symbol with her phone. 
“Give me a minute. I have a copy of an old guild registry.” 
She flung open her laptop as they crowded around and watched her search through a folder of her doctoral research, then scroll through a carousel of Ottoman trade sigils, then brightened. 
“Here. Match confirmed.” She rotated the screen for them: the same crescent-and-loop emblem beside a terse line of Ottoman Turkish. “Kapanizade. Imperial glassmakers, chartered 1703, but family workshop documented in Kadıköy since 1590.” 
She lifted the screen beside the etched mark. Perfect overlay. “So if any descendants kept fragments of Çelebi’s work, odds are good they’re in the shop or stashed in their storeroom.” 
"Do they still exist?" Aya asked.
"The family name persists," Leyla confirmed. "According to Google, there’s a Kapanizade antique shop here in Kadıköy. It’s over 100 years old, family owned.”
A fragile excitement built in the room.
"We should go first thing tomorrow," Karim said.
"We need to go mid-morning. It’s Saturday, so there’ll be a market," Aya countered. "Malik will be watching known historical sites, and an antique market may qualify. It’s a stretch, but we’ll need to be safe and try to use the market as a bit of cover—crowds, multiple exits, disguises. Mid-morning will be busiest."
“Then we rest for now,” Leyla decided. “You’re right, dawn’s too quiet. We hit the bazaar mid-morning, shoulder-to-shoulder with tourists. Crowds are cover.” 
Karim nodded, exhaustion finally registering. “Four hours’ sleep, then rotate watch. Amina, you can take the full night of sleep. We’ll cover you."
"I'm fine," Amina insisted, though her fingers still traced the markings on her arm. "This isn’t like what happened in Morocco."
"If that fragment reacts to Amina's markings," Omar said through the speaker, "the other components might do the same. It could help locate them.”
"We'll keep you updated, Omar," Aya promised. “Good work.”
"Be careful," Omar said.
The call ended, leaving them in tense silence as they gathered supplies and weapons, preparing to reenter the chase in the morning.
"Ready?" Aya asked when they'd finished.
Three nods, no words.


"There," Aya pointed toward a narrow side street branching from the main bazaar. "Kapanizade Antiques is on the corner."
Shoppers flowed between stalls, voices rising in a cacophony of haggling and conversation. Spice merchants competed with textile vendors, their colorful wares creating a kaleidoscope effect beneath canvas awnings.
They moved through the crowd as two pairs, Aya with Karim and Leyla with Amina, maintaining casual separation while keeping visual contact. Aya was pleased at how naturally Karim played his role, despite the new strain between them. His arm draped loosely over her shoulder, the perfect image of a tourist couple exploring Istanbul's markets, though his touch felt mechanical now, performance rather than affection.
"Eleven o'clock by the guys selling records," Aya said. She had spotted the man without turning her head: cropped hair, rigid posture, scanning rather than shopping. Aegis.
"They're already here," Karim said quietly. His hand tightened slightly on her shoulder. "How?"
"I guess they're trying to watch the entire area around the clock."
"There's another twenty meters ahead. Let's abort," Amina’s voice came through their own earpieces. "Too exposed."
"No," Aya countered. "The crowd works in our favor. Let’s head to the shop, but stagger the rest of the way, and each be ready to run if we need to."
They continued through the market, adopting the meandering pace of tourists.
Kapanizade Antiques occupied a pristine building with ornate wooden trim. The display behind the window featured intricately etched glassware that caught the morning light, casting prismatic light across the cobblestones.
"Ready?" Aya asked.
"Go," Leyla confirmed from her position thirty meters away. “We’ll hang for a few and be right behind you.”
Aya and Karim entered, a small bell announcing their arrival. The shop was a treasure trove of artifacts: ornate brass astrolabes hung alongside mother-of-pearl inlaid mirrors, while glass cases displayed everything from antique jewelry to ceramic tiles bearing calligraphy. Vintage carpets covered portions of the floor, and shelves lined the walls with books, manuscripts, and scientific instruments from centuries past.
An elderly man looked up from behind a counter crowded with pocket watches and compass roses, his face deeply lined around sharp eyes.
"Hoş geldiniz," he greeted. "Welcome."
"Thank you," Aya replied with an exaggerated smile. "Your collection is beautiful."
"Five generations of my family’s craftsmanship in this very shop. Much more before that," the man replied in accented English. "Please, browse at your leisure and let me know what it is I can help you with."
Leyla and Amina entered moments later, pretending not to know Aya and Karim as they moved to the opposite side of the shop. The old shopkeeper watched them with polite interest.
"We're interested in antique scientific instruments," Karim said casually. "I understand your family once supplied the Ottoman imperial observatory."
The shopkeeper's expression shifted subtly. "Well. You are a student of history?"
"An archaeologist, actually. Researching Hazarfen Çelebi's work."
At the mention of Çelebi, the shopkeeper's demeanor changed entirely. He straightened, eyes darting between his customers and the street beyond.
"I think you should leave," he said quietly.
"Sir, we just want to—"
"Others have come asking these questions," the man interrupted. "And each time, bad things followed. I cannot help you."
"Who?" Aya asked, abandoning pretense.
The shopkeeper hesitated, then sighed. "A few men, just the other day. Very insistent. Claimed I was in extreme danger. They claimed to represent a historical foundation. They asked odd questions. I could not help them, and the next morning, my shop’s window had a brick thrown through it. Covered in blood."
"When?" Leyla asked urgently, joining them.
"Two days ago. I just had the glass replaced yesterday. They left only a card and said to call if anyone inquired about… certain items." His gaze hardened. "I don't know what trouble surrounds these artifacts, but I want no part of it. I won’t tell them you came, but you need to leave."
Amina approached, rolling up her sleeve to reveal the markings. "Do you know anything about this?"
The shopkeeper recoiled slightly, then studied the marks with growing wonder. "The signs of djinn," he whispered. "Is this a tattoo? But why? How did you—"
"It's complicated," Amina cut him off. "Please, we need to find Çelebi's work before those men do. I know you don’t want anything bad to happen, but they’re the bad things, and if we don’t stop them, then they’ll happen to all of us."
The old man nodded slowly, looking at them each in turn before seeming to reach a decision. "Wait here."
He disappeared into a back room, returning moments later with a leather roll. He spread it across the counter, revealing what appeared to be repair tools for delicate glass instruments.
"These belonged to my great-great-grandfather," he said, his voice cracking slightly. "He served the last Ottoman masters of astral science. The tools themselves are ordinary, but this—" He indicated an unassuming brass cylinder among the implements. "This is something else."
As he spoke, Amina's markings began to tingle. "That's it," she confirmed. "I can weirdly feel it."
The shopkeeper nodded grimly. "Take it and go. Quickly."
Karim reached for the cylinder, but Aya caught his wrist. "How much?"
"No payment," the shopkeeper insisted. "Just promise you won't return to this shop or tell anyone where you got it. The men who came—I don’t want any trouble."
Aya nodded. "Thank you."
Karim carefully pocketed the cylinder, but before they could leave, Leyla glanced out the window and tensed. "We've got company."
Outside, three men were converging on the shop from different directions, but moving casually. Leyla ran to the door and engaged the deadbolt.
"Is there a back exit?" Aya asked the shopkeeper.
Under the shop’s warm lamplight, the old man’s shoulders sagged, and the proud façade slipped, revealing a tremor in the hand smoothing his waistcoat. His eyes searched each of their faces as though memorizing them.
Finally, he nodded. "Through the storeroom. It opens to the alley behind."
"Thank you," Aya said, already moving. "Lock the doors after we leave. You can say we threatened you with a knife, if you need to."
They raced through the storeroom, and its bolt thudded home behind them. They threaded a claustrophobic passage stacked with moth-chewed carpets, shouldered open a warped delivery hatch, and spilled into a back alley of garbage cans and stray cats.
Amina spoke as soon as they emerged. "Leyla and I will head north toward the ferry terminal. Karim and Aya, draw them east. All rendezvous at the secondary location in one hour."
"Take this," Karim said, handing the brass cylinder to Amina. "It's responding to you, and who knows what it’s for. Plus, they’ll probably come after us if they have to choose. You'll keep it safer."
Amina nodded, accepting the object without comment. Then they separated, moving in opposite directions down the alley.
Aya and Karim turned, pushing back through the morning market crowd with purposeful strides. They hadn’t gone more than fifty meters when Aya spotted the first man, wearing a leather jacket, sunglasses, and moving with predatory focus.
They increased their pace, weaving between stalls. The bazaar's labyrinthine layout offered temporary advantage, but also limited their exit options. Each turn revealed more potential pursuers, some openly pushing through crowds, others maintaining strategic positions at intersections.
"It feels like they're herding us," Aya said as another avenue yielded another muscular, nondescript man walking toward them, alone. "They’re forcing us toward the eastern exit."
"Then we don't go east," Karim said. "This way’s open."
They ducked into a diagonal alley, emerging into a quieter section of the market dedicated to textiles. Rolls of silk and cotton created a colorful maze of merchandise. Aya guided Karim between displays, using the fabric panels as cover.
The respite lasted seconds. A voice crackled over the market's public address system with an announcement in Turkish that caused shoppers to glance around with sudden suspicion, alarm.
"What are they saying?" Aya asked, catching the shift in atmosphere.
"I think they're describing us," Karim tried to translate, expression darkening. "Something about terrorism. Money. A reward, maybe."
"Shit," Aya realized. "Malik’s smart."
A man shopping with his two children pulled them close and began pointing at Aya, who turned to move forward quickly. The shopkeeper to their left pulled out his phone and began recording them, forcing Aya to pull her scarf around her face. 
The crowd that had been their cover transformed into an obstacle, hundreds of potential civilian informants surrounding them.
"New plan," Aya decided, pulling Karim. "Up. We just need to get a block or two away. Even if we have to fight our way out, we need to do it away from this crowd."
The corridor led to a maintenance staircase. They ascended rapidly, emerging onto the rooftop of the market building. The flat expanse stretched before them, connecting several structures in the bazaar complex.
"The cylinder," Aya said as they ran across the rooftop. "Did you see anything unusual about it?"
"It's engraved with the same patterns as the fragment," Karim replied, keeping pace. "I think it's a focusing tube, designed to channel energy between lens components, like the shaft of a telescope."
They reached the edge of the current building, facing a gap of nearly two meters to the next roof. Without hesitation, Aya backed up several steps, then sprinted forward and leaped across. She landed with practiced grace, turning to see Karim prepare for his jump.
As he landed beside her, the rooftop access door behind them burst open. Two men emerged along with a police officer.
"Durun!" the officer shouted. "Stop!"
"Keep moving!" Aya urged, pulling Karim toward the far side of the building.
They raced across the rooftop as a bullet pinged against the ventilation pipes behind them. There was a fire escape, rusted but intact, that could return them to street level.
Aya swung over the edge first, descending rapidly, while more bullets pinged off the dilapidated railings. 
Halfway down, the metal groaned ominously beneath her weight. She jumped the remaining distance, landing in a controlled roll on the cobblestones below.
"Hurry!" she called up to Karim.
He was three meters from the ground when a support bolt sheared away with a sharp crack. The entire fire escape lurched sideways, metal screaming against brick as aged mortar crumbled.
"Jump!" Aya shouted, positioning herself beneath him.
Karim pushed off from the collapsing structure, but his trajectory was wrong. Too far left, too fast. Their bodies collided, and they were sent sprawling across the uneven cobblestones.
The impact drove the breath from her lungs. Karim's elbow caught her in the ribs as they went down, and something sharp raked across her side as she rolled. The fire escape crashed down behind them in a cascade of rusted metal and masonry dust.
For a moment, they lay tangled together, both stunned. Aya felt warmth spreading along her ribs, but the adrenaline masked the pain.
"You okay?" Karim asked, helping her up with shaking hands. Dust covered his hair and clothes.
"Fine," she lied, pressing a hand to her side. When she pulled it away, her palm was slick with blood that looked black in the alley's shadows.
They hurried through a series of increasingly narrow alleys, the sounds of pursuit fading behind them. Istanbul's ancient streets once again became a maze of twisting passages and sudden dead ends, but they knew there were eyes everywhere. 
Twice, they had to backtrack until finally, in a quiet alley lined with overflowing garbage bins, they paused to catch their breath. The pain in Aya's side had intensified to a throbbing ache.
"Let me see," Karim insisted, noticing her discomfort.
"It's nothing," she protested.
"Aya."
She relented, lifting her shirt to reveal her bare torso, a jagged gash along her ribs just beneath her bra.
"Must have happened when the fire escape collapsed. I didn’t feel it at first," she said as he examined the wound.
"We need to clean and bandage this. It’ll need stitches," Karim said. He pulled a single alcohol wipe and bandage from his bag.
"I'm no field medic, but after what happened to Salah at the base, I vowed to always be slightly more prepared. This should hold until we reach the safehouse," he said, securing the bandage. 
His fingers grazed her skin, and he looked up. He left his full hand on her side, holding her while they had locked eye contact.
"Thank you," Aya said so softly that it was almost a whisper.
A slow clap echoed in the alley, and they turned to find Malik standing at the entrance, alone and apparently unarmed.
"Touching," he said, ceasing his applause. "Though I must say, you're making my job unnecessarily difficult."
Aya reached for her weapon, but Malik raised his hands in a placating gesture.
"I'm alone," he said. "And here to talk, not fight."
"What do you want?" Karim demanded, positioning himself slightly in front of Aya.
"I came without my team, not as the face of the Aegis, but as one person to another. To make a final appeal and deliver a final warning." Malik's gaze fixed on Aya. "You’re winning, that’s great, but it’s narrow. How long can it hold? And even if it does, your father died because he found the truth, Aya. Do you really want to follow him?"
Chapter 16
"What truth?" Aya asked through gritted teeth, her voice steady despite the throbbing pain in her side. "What exactly did my father discover?"
Malik moved into the alley, maintaining distance. He looked different, his suit jacket missing, shirt collar open, a faint stubble darkening his jaw.
"Hassan discovered what the Ottomans had truly built," he said. "Not just a lens to observe cosmic phenomena, but a key that could unlock forces beyond comprehension."
"We've heard this sales pitch many times before," Karim replied. "Unlimited power, reshape the future for the better, et cetera."
Malik's smile was thin. "That's the simplified version we tell our investors. The reality is more complicated." He leaned against the alley wall, eyes never leaving Aya's, and for a moment, something in his expression softened.
"Your father and I were colleagues first, friends second. We spent three summers working together at various sites. Damascus, Cairo, Istanbul. In the summer of 2008, we were in his Berkeley office at two in the morning. He'd been translating Çelebi's notes for sixteen hours straight, subsisting on terrible coffee and determination. 
“When he found the passage describing the lens, his hands were shaking. Not with fear, with pure joy. He looked at me with this expression, like a child discovering magic is real, and said, 'Malik, we might be holding proof that human consciousness can interface with cosmic energy. That every ancient text about divine power might be describing actual physics we've forgotten.' We stayed up until dawn working through implications, building frameworks, planning research."
The warmth faded from his voice, replaced by something harder. "That was Hassan at his best. Curious, brilliant, unafraid. Before the fear took over and destroyed everything we'd built."
"If you were such friends," Aya countered, "why are you hunting his daughter?"
"Because you have what we need to complete his work, and I don’t want to harm you, but I can’t hold the Aegis back much longer. After this meeting, the guns will be aimed to kill. I want to finish his work together, as he would have wanted."
"His work or yours?" Karim asked.
Malik's expression hardened. "The distinction became irrelevant when he abandoned our research without warning. Nearly ten years of collaborative effort disappeared overnight along with crucial findings."
"He had his reasons," Aya said, though uncertainty flickered across her face. How much did she truly know about her father's final projects?
"Indeed, he did." Malik pushed away from the wall, taking a step closer. "Hassan discovered something that frightened him. Something about the lens that made him decide it should remain lost. I don’t know what it was, but I believe his fear was misplaced."
"Why?" Karim demanded.
"Because I've seen what the lens components can do even in isolation," Malik said. "The energy they channel doesn't need to be inherently destructive. It's transformative." He fixed his gaze on Aya. "Your father was a brilliant archaeologist and scientist, but conservative in his thinking. He couldn't see past the lens's potential for misuse to recognize its possibilities for transcendence. Humanity doesn't have the luxury of safety anymore. Not with climate collapse accelerating, not with resources depleting, not with conventional solutions failing. We either evolve or we die. That's the choice Hassan couldn't make."
Aya's jaw tightened. "So, you're the visionary, and he was the coward? That's your perspective? And that’s meant to win me over?"
"Your father was no coward," Malik replied sharply. "He was cautious. Principled. He believed some discoveries should remain theoretical. I respect that position, but I don't share it; I see more nuance. That's why I'm offering this moment of truth between us." He spread his hands. "Cooperate willingly. Join our research team. Honor your father's brilliance by completing what he began."
"What he abandoned," Aya corrected.
"What he thought he needed to protect the world from," Karim added.
Malik's expression hung somber. "You sound just like him. The same certainty, the same conviction that caution equals wisdom." He straightened, adjusting his cuffs. "Perhaps you should know the full truth about Colombia, about what Hassan discovered that made him run."
Aya stiffened. "What about Colombia?"
"Your father's last excavation, his accident, his demise. The official report stated a collapse due to rushed safety precautions in the unpredictable chambers." Malik's voice softened. "But that's not entirely accurate."
"You weren't there," Aya said, but uncertainty crept into her voice.
"No, but I received his final message." Malik withdrew his phone, tapped the screen several times, then turned it toward them. "He sent this the night before his death."
The screen showed an email, date-stamped with September 19th, 2018. The day before Aya received the call that had changed her life. 
The sender was Hassan Amrani’s UC Berkeley email, the message brief but clear: Found a connection reading last night between a Khmer observatory and the Ottoman techniques. Same principles, working independently. The implications are staggering. Have to bury this. All of it. Call me urgently.
Aya hesitated. "This proves nothing. And emails can be faked.”
"It proves he found something significant," Malik replied. "Something that connected civilizations across time and continents. The lens wasn't an Ottoman anomaly; it was one manifestation of a universal principle."
"If that message is real," Karim interjected, "it suggests he wanted to stop the research, not continue it."
"An emotional reaction to a profound discovery," Malik said. "He deserved the chance to process it properly. To make an informed decision." He shook his head. "A chance he never got."
"Because of the accident," Aya said, the accusation clear in her tone.
Before Malik could respond, the distant wail of police sirens cut through the morning air, growing louder by the second.
"That's not my team," he said, suddenly alert. "Local police. Someone must have reported your position."
Karim moved protectively closer to Aya. "Convenient timing for your heart-to-heart to end."
"Believe what you will." Malik checked his watch, then the alley entrance. "But we all need to leave. Now."
The sirens grew closer. Malik hesitated, then made a decision. "Go," he said, gesturing toward the opposite end of the alley. "I'll delay them."
"Why would you help us?" Aya demanded.
"Because I doubt deported people can help me find the remaining lens components," he said. "And because—" He paused, something almost human crossing his stoic features. "Because Hassan would have done the same for me, once."
Seconds later, the screech of tires at the alley entrance cut off any response Aya might have made. A police vehicle blocked the opening, its lights casting rotating blue patterns across the grimy walls.
"Go," Malik ordered again, turning toward the police car and raising his hands in a disarming gesture. "Official business," they heard him call out as he produced an ID from his pocket. "Intelligence operation, need assistance tracking suspects..."
Aya and Karim bolted toward the opposite end of the alley; Aya's injured side was putting up a fight. The narrow passage curved sharply before opening onto another street.
The vehicle was just turning the corner when a black sedan screeched to a halt beside them, its passenger door flying open.
"Get in!" Leyla shouted from behind the wheel.
They dove into the car, Karim pulling the door shut as Leyla accelerated away from the sound of police sirens. The sedan's engine growled as she pushed it through the gears, taking the next corner hard, pressing them against the door panels.
"Amina?" Aya asked, glancing at the empty back seat.
"Already at the new location," Leyla said, eyes fixed on the road as she navigated a series of rapid turns. "She made it to the ferry terminal with the artifact."
"New location?" Karim asked.
"Had to abandon the safehouse," Leyla said, turning down a one-way street to evade a police car that roared past. "Too compromised."
"Where did you learn to drive like this?" Karim asked as she threaded the sedan between two delivery trucks with centimeters to spare.
"MİT defensive driving course," she said absently, then caught herself. "Long story."
Twenty minutes of evasive maneuvering later, they emerged onto the coastal road, blending with regular traffic. No pursuit vehicles visible, no sirens in the distance, just another car among thousands.
"How bad is it?" she asked, glancing at Aya's bloodstained shirt.
"Manageable," Aya said, though the bleeding had intensified during their escape. "How did you find us?"
"Tracked your burner phones," Leyla said. "Triangulated your position when you stopped moving for more than two minutes. Probably a good idea to ditch those now, too.”
She guided the sedan away from the coast, climbing into a residential district of modern apartment blocks interspersed with older villas. Eventually, she turned into an underground parking garage beneath a newer building, using a keycard to access the secure area.
"Floor sixteen," she said as they entered the elevator. "Apartment 1602."
The new apartment was smaller than the previous one, but with better security: electronic locks, modern fixtures, and panoramic windows offering clear views of surrounding streets. Amina was waiting, the brass cylinder and glass fragment laid out on the dining table alongside a street vendor's lunch spread.
"Thought you might be hungry," she said by way of greeting. Then she noticed the blood. "Oh shit. Let me see."
Aya submitted to Amina's examination, wincing as she peeled back Karim's makeshift bandage. "First aid kit?" Amina asked.
"Bathroom cabinet," Leyla called, securing the door behind them.
While Leyla and Karim examined the artifact, Amina led Aya into the bathroom.
"This is going to hurt," Amina warned, threading a curved needle from the first aid kit. "But it needs proper stitches, or it'll keep bleeding."
Aya sat on the edge of the bathroom counter, her shirt discarded, trying not to flinch as Amina cleaned the wound with antiseptic. The alcohol burned, but Amina's touch was gentle, her fingers steady despite the tremor Aya noticed in her hands.
"Hold still," Amina whispered, leaning closer. "This first one's always the worst."
The needle pierced her skin, and Aya sucked in a sharp breath. Amina's free hand instinctively moved to Aya's thigh, a steadying presence.
"Sorry," Amina whispered, not moving her hand away. "Almost done."
As she worked, Aya found herself studying the concentration etched in the lines around Amina’s eyes, the way she bit her lower lip when focusing. This close, Aya could see the faint scar above Amina's eyebrow from their escape from an underground bar in Bulgaria, and could smell the familiar scent of almond on her hair.
"There," Amina said softly, tying off the final suture. Her fingers lingered on Aya's skin as she applied the bandage, hands steadier after the precision work. "That should hold."
Their eyes met and held. For a moment, the bathroom grew impossibly small, charged with old memories, unresolved tension, and an increasing awareness that she was still topless.
"Thank you," Aya said, her voice barely above a whisper.
Amina's hand was still on her thigh. "I meant what I said at the mosque. About following you anywhere." She paused, vulnerability flickering across her features. "I know things are complicated with Karim, but—"
"Amina—" Aya began.
"I know." Amina stepped back, the spell broken. "I just needed you to know."
As they reentered the dining room, Karim looked up from his examination. "You should tell them what Malik said."
Aya nodded and recounted their conversation, including the revelation about her father's final message from Colombia. "Classic manipulation."
"But the email could be genuine," Karim pointed out gently. "It matches what you’ve said of your father's movements before his death."
"Even if it is," Aya said, "all it proves is that he found something significant enough to reconsider his research. Not that he'd want Aegis to have it. And even if Malik is better than the others in the organization, he’s still in the organization. He reports to them."
"Agreed," Leyla said, spreading out photographs they’d taken and printed from the mosque workshop across the table. "But it raises an important question: what did Hassan discover that connected Khmer observatories to Ottoman technology?"
"Cultural cross-pollination isn't uncommon," Karim noted with a shrug. "Separate civilizations often develop similar solutions to universal problems."
"But we're not talking about pyramids, agriculture, irrigation, or simple architectural similarities," Leyla argued. "The lens technology appears to be based on understanding cosmic principles that modern science hasn’t explored."
Amina leaned forward, studying the workshop photos. "Maybe it's not about cultural exchange. Maybe it's about the same natural phenomena being observed independently."
"Like convergent evolution," Karim said, nodding appreciatively at Amina's insight. "Different species developing similar traits in response to similar environmental pressures."
"Different cultures developing similar technologies in response to the same cosmic phenomena," Aya elaborated. "But that still doesn't explain where Çelebi obtained his advanced understanding."
Leyla pulled up one of the workshop photographs on her laptop. It showed a partial map, etched into the workshop wall. "We might have a clue. Look at this."
The map showed central Anatolia, with particular focus on a region labeled in Ottoman script.
"Cappadocia," Karim translated. "The area with those famous rock formations and underground cities, where tourists go for balloon rides."
"Not just any underground cities," Leyla said. "Some of the most extensive subterranean complexes in the world."
"I read somewhere they extend over twenty levels deep," Karim added, pulling up information on his phone. "And according to this, most chambers have never been properly explored. Still sealed."
"Perfect for hiding something," Amina said.
"What's the connection to Çelebi?" Aya asked.
Leyla pulled up additional images on her tablet. "Beyond the map he had in his workshop, according to historical records, Çelebi wasn't originally from Istanbul. He came from a small village near Cappadocia and only later moved to the capital to join the imperial observatory."
She zoomed in on the workshop map, highlighting markings around specific sites in the Cappadocian region. "These symbols, they could be coordinates.”
Aya leaned closer. Having grown up watching her father work, she recognized the system immediately. "These are archaeological reference points. Marking specific subterranean chambers within the underground network."
"Okay," Leyla said. "So, Çelebi tested his lens prototype in Istanbul, but maybe he hid the complete blueprints and components in Cappadocia's underground city. It was his homeland, removed from imperial oversight, famous for being impenetrable. Perfect for concealing his most advanced work."
"The underground cities were originally built for protection from invaders," Karim added. "Some date back to the Bronze Age, but were expanded over millennia. Early Christians used them to hide from Roman persecution. It would be incredibly secure, and still largely unexplored."
"Well, it sounds like Çelebi repurposed them to hide something that needed to stay hidden," Amina said.
Aya studied the markings more carefully, comparing them with a modern map. "These markings indicate a specific district, Göreme. And these numbers correspond to a depth measure. He was mapping something at least seven levels below the surface."
Karim met her eyes briefly, impressed, a momentary bridge across the growing emotional chasm between them that extended back to their first days working side-by-side.
"So we go to Cappadocia," Amina stated, breaking the moment. "Find the underground chamber, locate the remaining lens components, and put an end to this shit."
"Not so simple," Leyla said. "Cappadocia has hundreds of chambers across thousands of square kilometers. These markers narrow it down, but we're still searching a massive area. We need something more precise."
"Maybe we already have them," Aya suggested, turning to the artifacts. "These components seem to be more than just parts of the lens. Maybe they're keys, designed to lead to the next piece. And, Amina’s a walking artifact detector."
Karim carefully lifted the glass fragment from its box. "The fragment responded to Amina's markings from the Amulet. You think it can provide more specific guidance?"
Amina extended her hand. "Let me try something."
Karim passed her the fragment, and Amina walked to the window, angling the lens to the stars. As before, it began to glow upon contact with her skin, the planisphere markings on her arm responding with a matching luminescence. But this time, something new happened. The fragment's light intensified, projecting patterns across the apartment walls.
"What's happening?" Leyla gasped.
"It's reacting to the night," Amina said, her voice strained. "To the stars becoming visible. What does that mean?"
The projected light coalesced into distinct patterns, projected symbols, and scripts that shifted and flowed across the walls like living calligraphy. As they watched, transfixed, the patterns resolved into recognizable words.
Karim stepped closer, reading aloud: "Taş çiçeklerin altında beni bul."
"What does it mean?" Aya asked.
"'Find me beneath the stone flowers,'" Leyla translated, her voice thick with wonder.
"Stone flowers," Karim whispered. "That's what locals call Cappadocia's fairy chimneys—the cone-shaped rock formations."
The fragment pulsed once more, intensely, before dimming to a steady glow. The message remained briefly projected on the wall, then faded like smoke.
"Well," Aya said into the stunned silence. "I guess we are going to Cappadocia."
Chapter 17
"Stone flowers," Leyla repeated, pulling up satellite images of Cappadocia on her tablet. The region's distinctive landscape filled the screen, showing a surreal terrain of conical rock formations rising from the Anatolian plateau. "The locals call the fairy chimneys 'Peribacaları.’ Fairy chimneys or stone flowers, depending on the translation."
"Göreme has the highest concentration," Karim noted, examining the images over her shoulder. "But which specific formation? There are thousands."
"At least we can narrow it down. The markings from the workshop indicated a specific district and underground network, then we can hope the artifacts guide us when we get there," Aya said.
Amina carefully placed the still-glowing fragment on the map, where it illuminated the paper with an ethereal blue light. The brass cylinder from the antique shop seemed to respond, its etched patterns briefly glimmering. "The components are connected," she observed. "They want to be reunited."
For the next hour, they worked methodically to identify possible locations, cross-reference historical accounts, and study tourist access points to Cappadocia's underground cities.
"The main underground complexes are Derinkuyu and Kaymaklı," Leyla explained. "Both are partially open to tourists, but extensive sections remain unexplored or restricted."
"Perfect hiding places," Aya said. "Çelebi could have sealed off chambers centuries ago that remain undisturbed. I mean, especially considering the secret chamber he and his people had at the mosque."
"We'll need supplies," Karim said, making notes. "Proper digging equipment, lighting, and climbing gear."
"And plenty of weapons," Amina added. "Aegis won't be far behind."
"I have a contact in the region," Leyla offered. "A former colleague who should be willing to provide support without asking too many questions. I’ll call him, and he can prepare everything for our arrival."
"How soon can we leave?" Aya asked.
Leyla quickly searched on her phone. "First flight is tomorrow morning at 6:05 with Pegasus Airlines. Approximately an hour in the air to Nevşehir Kapadokya Airport, then another hour by road to Göreme. Could be there around 8:30."
"Maybe we should split up," Amina said. "Take different routes, minimize the chance of us all being intercepted."
They continued planning, mapping contingencies, escape routes, and rendezvous points. As the strategy took shape, Aya noticed Karim becoming increasingly withdrawn, his contributions precise but distant. When Leyla and Amina moved to the kitchen to plate the late dinner that Amina had picked up, Aya took the opportunity to approach him.
"Hey," she said softly, standing beside him at the window. "You've been quiet."
Karim kept his gaze fixed on the Istanbul skyline. "Just focused on the mission."
"Karim..." Aya began, searching for the right words. "About what Amina said—"
"It's fine," he interrupted. "Really. Your past is your past."
"It's not fine," she insisted. "I can see it's bothering you, and we can't afford that kind of distraction right now."
He turned to face her, his expression carefully composed. "Right, because of the mission. What do you want me to say, Aya? That I'm not surprised you didn't tell me about a significant relationship you had with the person we’ve been living with? That it doesn't matter if the person I've been living and working side-by-side with for months was your lover? Does this not make me feel like you were deceiving me?”
"I should have told you," she said. "But it was over long before Morocco, before you and I ever met. I didn’t expect to see her again."
"You said that. But then why hide it?"
"Because it ended badly. Really badly," she admitted. "Because some parts of my past I prefer not to revisit. With anyone. But I’ll tell you the full story when we have time."
A subtle shift came over his expression. "And now?"
Aya glanced toward the kitchen, where Amina's voice carried. "Now, we need to focus on the mission, and we still need Amina to do that," she said firmly. "But I really care about you in every way I’ve said. I want all the things we said we want. A future together. Once we've secured the lens, once Aegis is no longer hunting us, we'll have that, and a much longer talk about our lives. Properly, every painful detail."
Karim studied her face, then nodded slowly. "Okay. Mission first." He hesitated. "But I need to know one thing: is there still something between you two? Any feelings?"
The question hung in the air, direct and unavoidable. Aya held his gaze. "Amina was the most important thing in the world to me once. Those feelings don't just disappear. But what I feel for you is different. Real. Present."
It wasn't a complete answer, but it was honest.
"Thank you," he said quietly. "For not pretending it's simpler than it is."
The moment was interrupted by a soft clearing of the throat. They turned to find Amina standing a few paces away, her expression uncharacteristically vulnerable.
"Sorry to interrupt," she said. "But I needed to say something." She took a breath. "To both of you."
Aya instinctively moved closer to Karim.
"I shouldn't have said what I did," Amina continued. "It was unprofessional and unfair to both of you." She straightened, meeting their eyes directly. "I'm not sorry for our past, and I still care for you, Aya. I’ll always love you, and I won't pretend otherwise. But I shouldn't have dropped it like that, and I also respect the hell out of you both. We have bigger problems, and we can only face them together."
The words momentarily stunned them both.
"Thank you," Karim said finally, with a pained smile.
"We're good," Aya said.
Amina nodded, some of her customary poise returning. "Good. Because the food’s spread out and Leyla's about to get us on a line with Salah."
On cue, Leyla's secure phone chimed from the kitchen. She answered immediately, switching to speaker as they gathered around.
"So you're all alive?" Salah's voice came through, tense with concern.
"All present and mostly intact," Aya said. "What's happening on your end?"
"Complications, sister, complications," Salah said. "I've been monitoring these Aegis communications and tracking their movements, and it’s coming at an unprecedented scale."
"Looking for us?" Karim asked.
"Partly, khoya. But there's more." The sound of keyboard clicks carried through the speaker. "I tapped some footage that you all really need to feast your eyes on. Sending it now."
Leyla's tablet pinged with an incoming file. She opened it to reveal security camera footage from what appeared to be a large, imposing government building. The timestamp showed it was recorded earlier that day.
The grainy video showed a conference room where several figures sat around a polished table. Malik entered, accompanied by two men who appeared to be bodyguards. The two men in suits, seated at the table, rose and greeted him.
"Who are they?" Aya asked.
“Shit,” Leyla said. “That’s Deputy Minister Mehmet Saruhan. He commands the Interior Ministry’s counter-terror apparatus.” She indicated the second official. “And that’s Professor Cemal Dündar, the Director-General for Cultural Heritage and Museums. Every excavation permit or antiquities seizure in Turkey crosses his desk.”
"Aegis has some hot-shot government connections now, it would seem," Salah confirmed. "I’m thinking it’s the kind of thing where they have official authorization to 'recover stolen antiquities' and 'apprehend foreign agents operating on Turkish soil.'"
"That's us," Amina said dryly.
"Yes, ma’am. And it gets worse," Salah continued. "Based on a couple of emails I phished from a secretary, they've been granted access to national security resources. Airport surveillance, border control, police cooperation."
"So we're not just running from Aegis anymore," Aya said. "We're evading the entire state apparatus, and we need to get on a flight."
"How did they secure that level of cooperation?" Karim asked.
"Money," Leyla suggested. "Influence. Perhaps blackmail. We know how extensive their network is, how many corporations and billionaires are linked. Plus, maybe they've convinced officials the lens has national security implications. That type of thing plays well in this administration."
"All of the above," Salah confirmed. "But there's something else driving their urgency, a specific timeline."
He sent another file that contained an astronomical chart with dates and coordinates. "This was extracted from communications between Malik and what I can only guess is Aegis headquarters. They're preparing for some kind of celestial event occurring in three days."
Karim studied the chart. "It's a planetary alignment. Mars, Jupiter, and Saturn form a perfect triangle with this specific star constellation as backdrop."
"The Pleiades," Salah identified. "The Seven Sisters, you might call them. Yeah, your boy loved some Star Trek as a kid and got big time into space."
"Well, this matches Çelebi's notes on identifying specific alignments," Karim said, pulling up images from the mosque workshop. “His calculations attempted to predict times when the lens would be most powerful. I guess this is one of them."
"A three-day window," Aya murmured. "That's all the time we have to find the remaining components and secure them before Aegis can, and they attempt an activation."
Amina stared at the artifacts on the table, the fragment still pulsing with faint light. "We should destroy them."
Three pairs of eyes turned to her.
"I'm serious," she continued, her voice hard. "Smash the fragment. Melt the cylinder. If the lens requires all components to function, destroying even one should render the entire system useless. Problem solved."
"That assumes we know how many total components exist," Karim countered. "What if there are backups? Alternative pieces Aegis has already manufactured? We know Abdelatif probably produced this lens."
"Then we destroy those other ones too when we find them, and take the fight right to Malik once we figure out where he plans on arranging this big Astral Lens convergence." Amina's hand moved unconsciously to her arm. "I've felt what this kind of power does. In Morocco, the Amulet nearly consumed me. Nearly consumed all of us. Some things shouldn't exist."
Leyla leaned forward, her expression thoughtful. "The instinct is understandable, but destruction might not be as simple as it sounds. These aren't ordinary artifacts. They're designed to channel cosmic energy, so breaking them could release whatever's stored inside."
"She's right," Karim said reluctantly. "Remember what happened when we destroyed the Amulet? The energy discharge nearly leveled the Atlas Mountains. Took Abdelatif with it." He shook his head. "If these components contain even a fraction of that stored power, smashing them in an Istanbul apartment with millions of people on the line could be catastrophic."
Amina's jaw tightened, but she didn't argue.
"So we can't destroy them safely," Aya said slowly. "And we can't just hide them and hope Malik gives up; he's too invested, too close to the alignment."
"Then what's the play?" Amina demanded. "We keep collecting pieces until we've assembled exactly what he wants?"
"I hear you, but we can’t risk leaving any available if we don’t know which Malik may have already replicated," Aya said, flipping through Hassan's notebook to a page she'd studied multiple times. "Look at this. My father's notes from his final years. He references something he calls the 'crown lens.’ It looks like it’s not just another component, but the termination mechanism. The control system." She traced her finger along Hassan's handwriting. "He writes that without it, any activation becomes irreversible. With it, the system can be safely powered down even after ignition."
"So it's a kill-switch to refocus or close the energy vacuum," Leyla said, leaning closer to read.
"Yeah," Aya continued. "And as far as my father could figure out, it's the only kill switch. My father experimented with a partial array; the notes are vague, but he mentions catastrophic results.
