He concluded that partial assemblies would be more dangerous than complete ones because they lack the control mechanisms."
Amina's hand moved to her arm. "Look what happened with the Amulet in Morocco when we destroyed it. The energy had to go somewhere. It nearly tore apart half the Atlas Mountains and took Abdelatif with it."
"Exactly," Aya said. "And if we destroy what we have now, we don't prevent Malik from trying, we just guarantee he tries with an incomplete system that can't be safely terminated."
"But we'd deny him the components," Karim argued. "Without these pieces, why would he attempt anything?"
"Because he probably already has a few components," Leyla said quietly. "Aegis has been moving equipment. Large cases under heavy security. They're not starting from zero, Karim. Malik has resources, access to other caches we don't know about, maybe even duplicates of some components. While you were dealing with Léglise’s project, he’s been here in Turkey. Before that, he was here working on this for years."
"So if we hide what we have," Amina said slowly, "Malik still attempts activation at the alignment with whatever partial array he's assembled. And without this crown lens as the termination mechanism—"
"He might trigger something he can't stop," Aya finished. "Uncontrolled dimensional fractures across Istanbul. Millions at risk. Who knows?"
"Then what's the play?" Karim asked, frustration cracking through his voice. "We can't destroy them safely, we can't hide them and hope he gives up—"
"We need to keep the crown lens under our control," Aya said. "We find it before he does. And if he makes his move at the alignment—and I think he still might make his move even without all the pieces because he's too invested to stop—we're there. In the room. With the one component that gives us the power to terminate whatever he starts."
"You're talking about walking into his ritual," Karim said. "Bringing him what he needs."
"No," Aya countered. "I'm talking about making sure someone with the kill switch is standing next to the ignition if he throws it. Our choice is whether we face that from a distance and hope for the best, or whether we position ourselves to stop what he starts."
Leyla nodded slowly. "So we collect components and try to protect them and prevent an attempt. If he goes ahead and has more than we expect, it’s less about preventing his attempted activation. It's about surviving it."
"And making sure Istanbul survives it," Aya added. She looked around the table, meeting each person's eyes. "This is a more dangerous plan than hiding or running. But it's the only one that accounts for what we can't control: Malik's resources, his determination, and the reality that he's going to activate something at that alignment no matter what we do."
The silence that followed felt heavy with acceptance.
"Okay, we find the crown lens first," Amina said, finally. "And we make damn sure we're the ones holding it when the moment comes."
"I’ll leave this all to you guys,” Salah interrupted. “But Leyla filled me in, and I took care of the details. Your flights are booked. Different carriers, different times, all converging on Nevşehir tomorrow morning. I've sent the details and made sure to make the transactions untraceable."
"Thank you, Salah," Aya said.
“Peace, my people,” Salah said. “If there’s anything else, I’ll be in touch.”


Two hours later, exhaustion finally claimed them, one by one. Karim dozed on the couch, Aya retired to the bedroom to rest her injury, and Amina stretched out on the floor with characteristic disregard for comfort. Leyla waited until their breathing had settled into sleep rhythms, then silently retrieved the burner phone from her bag. 
She moved to the hallway outside the apartment, easing the door shut behind her with practiced care. She dialed the memorized number.
Malik answered immediately. "Report."
"They've found the next lead. Cappadocia. Çelebi hid components in the underground cities near Göreme." She kept her voice low. "The fragment projected a code when Amina held it. Some kind of encoded message system."
"The components are guiding them." Malik's voice carried satisfaction. "Hassan theorized they were designed to be reunited. Excellent."
"They're planning to leave tomorrow morning. Different flights, different routes."
"I'll have teams in position. We'll maintain surveillance but won't intercept. Not yet. Let them do the excavation work. The underground cities are treacherous, and Hassan's daughter has proven remarkably capable at navigating Çelebi's puzzles."
Leyla's grip tightened on the phone. "So the alignment is in three days, like Salah said."
"Correct." A pause. "The crown lens is the priority. Everything else is secondary. If they locate it, you take it. Whatever it costs."
"And if they find other components first?"
"Let them collect everything. A complete array is more valuable than fragments. But the crown lens..." His voice hardened. "That piece doesn't leave your sight once they find it. It's the control mechanism. Without it, activation is unpredictable. With it, we shape what happens next."
Leyla pictured them, sleeping inside. They trusted her now. Completely.
"I understand," she said.
"One more thing. I'm accelerating the pressure. They'll detect our surveillance soon, perhaps tonight. Fear will keep them moving fast, and speed means mistakes. When they reach the underground cities, they'll be desperate enough to take risks they'd normally avoid."
"You're herding them."
"I'm guiding them toward the finish line. As you pointed out, we are running short on time." The line crackled briefly. "And Dr. Yilmaz? Emre asks about you. He's worried. I told him you're doing everything necessary to bring him home."
The words landed like a blade between her ribs.
"Three days," Malik said. "Then this ends."
The line went dead.
She ended the call and stood motionless for a moment, listening. Nothing but the building's ambient creaks and distant traffic. 
Back in the apartment, everyone remained asleep. She returned the phone to her bag, then settled into the armchair by the window, her designated watch position.



Aya awoke to a strange electronic tone emanating from the apartment's security system, a low, pulsing alarm that gradually increased in volume.
"What's that?" Karim asked, jumping from the sofa.
Leyla ran to a panel near the door, swearing under her breath as she read the display. "Perimeter breach,” she said.
"How?" Aya demanded. "We switched all devices, used burner phones."
"It's not our communications," Leyla replied, switching to another screen. "I don’t know, but they’re here."
On the table, both artifacts had begun to pulse with increasing intensity, the fragment's blue glow synchronizing with the cylinder's amber light.
"Oh shit, they're communicating," Amina realized, the markings on her arm tingling in response. "Calling to each other, and to the remaining pieces. If Aegis has even one component, as we suspected, they could be using it to track us right now."
Aya swore under her breath. "How long until they’re in?"
"Minutes," Leyla estimated, already moving to gather essential equipment. "Maybe less."
"We need to split up now," Aya decided immediately. "Different routes to Cappadocia, just as we planned. Amina, you and Karim take the cylinder. Leyla and I will take the fragment. With my side injured, I think it’s the best plan."
Everyone nodded.
"We stay ahead of Aegis and keep the components separated until we reach Cappadocia. Let’s go,” Amina said.
As they gathered their equipment and notes, the security panel emitted another warning tone.
Leyla announced, "We have three minutes, maximum."
The team gathered essential items. Leyla distributed new phones. "Secure lines, untraceable. Try to use only in an emergency."
"Karim," Aya said as they prepared to separate. "Be careful."
He met her gaze, words passing unspoken between them. "You too."
Amina secured the cylinder in a pouch. "See you all in fairyland.”
The security panel issued a final warning, a rapid series of beeps indicating imminent breach.
"Time to go," Leyla called, leading them toward the emergency exit.
They slipped into the night, splitting into pairs before reaching the street. As Aya and Leyla moved toward the east, Karim and Amina moved west, the artifacts pulsed in their separate containers.
Chapter 18
"Stairwell B leads to the parking garage," Leyla said as they sprinted down the hallway, checking a floor plan illuminated by emergency lighting. "Amina and Karim, take the left exit."
They pushed through a fire door into the stairwell just as the heavy thud of boots echoed. Leyla froze, holding up a fist. They pressed against the wall as voices carried down the concrete shaft.
"Team Two in position," a male voice reported. "Deploying ES-7 capture devices on floors sixteen through fourteen."
"ES-7?" Aya mouthed silently.
"Electrostatic restraint nets. Military prototype. At least they're not here to kill; they want us alive, no matter what Malik said."
They descended as quietly as possible, counting floors as they went. Above them, doors banged open. When they reached the parking garage, Leyla guided them toward a maintenance door rather than the main entrance.
"They'll have the vehicle exits covered. Hopefully not this one," she said, producing a small electronic device from her bag. She attached it to the door's alarm contacts, bypassing the system.
The door opened onto a narrow alley behind the building. Rain had begun to fall, slicking the cobblestones and reducing visibility.
"We need to reach the ferry terminal," Leyla whispered. "It's our best exit route."
They zigzagged between overhead balconies. The rain intensified, plastering Aya's hair to her face and soaking through her clothing.
"Two men, leather jackets, northwest corner," Aya called.
Leyla nodded, steering them forward, adopting the hunched posture and hurried pace of locals eager to get home.
"Fuck. Drone," Leyla warned suddenly, glancing skyward. A small quadcopter hovered above, its camera swiveling to scan the crowd.
"Split up," Aya decided. "Meet at point B."
Without waiting for confirmation, she veered toward an overflowing bar, with what appeared to be students huddling close under the minimal shelter. Leyla disappeared in the opposite direction, melting into the night.
Aya stayed with the group until the men had moved on, then broke away toward a side street that would lead to the waterfront.
Eventually, she reached the Bosphorus, where the ferry terminal bustled with late-night commuters heading to Istanbul's European side. She scanned the area, identifying two more possible Aegis operatives or plain-clothes police stationed near the ticket barriers.
She pulled out her phone, sending a quick encrypted message to Amina and Karim: “Airport plan compromised. Need new tickets. Nevşehir-Kapadokya. Different carrier. Tonight, if possible.”
Amina's response came seconds later: “Already safe and working on it. Turkish Airlines has three seats on the 2 AM flight. Handling fourth.”
Aya texted back: “Confirmed.”
She slipped the phone back into her pocket. Changing flights was risky but necessary. Salah had booked them on separate morning flights, but if Aegis was moving now, and had access to government systems, those reservations were potentially already flagged. Better to move immediately.
Movement caught her eye, and through the rain, she recognized Leyla, boarding a small motorboat. She diverted toward a commercial pier where fishing boats bobbed against their moorings.
Aya picked up her pace. By the time she reached the boat, Leyla had the engine idling. Aya leapt aboard, grimacing as the impact jolted her injured side.
"The ferry’s well covered, but it doesn’t look like they expected us there," Leyla said, untying mooring lines. "This gets us across to the European side, where we can circle back to the airport via taxi."
The boat pulled away from the pier just as shouts erupted behind them. Flashlight beams cut through the rain, converging on their position. The engine roared as Leyla opened the throttle, sending them skimming across the choppy water of the Bosphorus.
"Amina and Karim?" Leyla asked over the engine's growl.
"En route to the rendezvous. I just messaged," Aya replied, watching the receding lights of Kadıköy. "How did you arrange this boat so quickly?"
"I didn't," Leyla admitted. "Borrowed it from someone who won't miss it until morning."
The crossing took fifteen minutes, water spraying over the gunwales as they cut through rolling waves and got lashed with the rain. Leyla navigated, keeping them away from major shipping lanes while maintaining their heading toward the Galataport marina.
They docked at a small private marina, abandoning the boat without ceremony and emerging onto a quiet street lined with high-end boutiques, now shuttered for the night. Leyla checked her watch.
"We have ninety minutes before boarding closes," she said, scanning the area. "Sabiha Gökçen is on the Asian side, so we need to cross back over the water."
"We just came from there," Aya said, pressing a hand to her throbbing side.
"Exactly why they won't expect us to return." Leyla was already moving, leading them toward a main avenue where taxis occasionally cruised past. "Aegis will be watching the Istanbul Airport now, on this side."
Aya's phone chimed with an encrypted message from Amina: “Tickets confirmed. Boarding passes mobile. Still need your ID scan.”
"Cover me," Aya murmured, ducking into a recessed doorway. She quickly took a photo of her forged passport, sending it through. Within seconds, Amina replied with a digital boarding pass.
Leyla flagged down a passing taxi. The driver, a middle-aged man with salt-and-pepper stubble, eyed their rain-soaked appearance with suspicion.
"Sabiha Gökçen," Leyla asked him, adding something in Turkish that made him nod vigorously.
"What did you tell him?" Aya asked as they settled into the back seat.
"That we're flight attendants late for our shift and there's a substantial tip if he gets us there in thirty minutes." Leyla's smile was thin. "Amazing how quickly traffic laws become optional."
The driver proved her right, weaving through traffic with alarming speed. They crossed the Bosphorus via the July 15 Martyrs Bridge, its suspension cables illuminated against the night sky.
"Amina and Karim?" Leyla asked quietly.
"Already there. They took a more direct route." Aya glanced at her phone. "They've secured the boarding passes, and we’re checked in."
"Your old friend is impressive."
"She always was." Aya gazed out at the city lights reflecting off the Bosphorus. "Amina can talk or fight her way into anywhere."
They lapsed into silence as the taxi sped through the night. Eventually, the airport's terminal came into view, its curved roof glowing with ambient light.
Leyla paid the driver with a generous tip, then turned to Aya. "Amina sent me the info. My boarding pass is for a later flight. But I have standby for your flight, and a good word that there’s a no-show passenger."
"And if something goes wrong?"
"Stick to protocol. Cappadocia, central square, noon." Leyla adjusted her glasses. "Remember, they're looking for four people traveling together. We're just strangers who happen to be flying on the same night."
With a final nod, they separated. Aya approached the terminal entrance, shoulders relaxed, expression neutral, just another weary traveler eager to be home.
They separated again, standing in different security lines. Aya selected a lane supervised by a younger officer who appeared tired, less attentive. She presented her boarding pass and forged passport, maintaining the relaxed posture of a routine business traveler.
The officer barely glanced at her documents before waving her through to the scanner. As she stepped into the full-body imaging device, she maintained regular breathing, consciously relaxing muscles that would otherwise betray her anxiety.
A moment of tension as the machine completed its cycle. The officer monitoring the display frowned slightly, tapping his screen. Aya prepared for confrontation, mentally cataloging escape routes.
"Injury?" the officer asked, pointing to her side where the bandages and wound created an anomaly on the scan.
"Insulin pump," Aya lied. "I can show you documentation."
He waved her through with disinterest. "No need. Have a good flight."
Beyond security, Aya caught sight of Karim clearing his own checkpoint, the bag containing the cylinder passing through X-ray without incident.
Aya sat down beside him to retie her shoes and spoke without acknowledging him. "Amina?" she asked.
"Already through security and headed to the gate," Karim confirmed without glancing her way. "No complications. Leyla?"
“Just behind us,” Aya confirmed, and they separated.
The final test came at the gate.
As the line inched forward, Aya saw the agent speaking into his wrist microphone, potentially receiving updated intelligence.
Amina was already seated when Aya boarded, her eyes fixed on the window, watching the ground crew perform final checks. She'd made subtle adjustments to her appearance, dulling her attractive looks. Her hair was pulled back in a messy bun, thick reading glasses perched on her nose, her usual confident posture replaced by something more timid.
"Cutting it close," she murmured as Aya slid into the seat beside her.
"Long story on the getaway," Aya whispered. "Then my bandages flagged their system, and I about panicked."
Amina hesitated. "Listen, again, about what I said earlier—"
"Seriously, not now," Aya cut her off, keeping her voice gentle. "We'll have time for that conversation later. No more apologies until then. Right now, we need to focus on staying alive and finding what we came for."
Amina nodded. "Whatever you say. Karim's three rows behind us. Leyla should have a standby seat toward the front in Business class. We've got the aircraft covered."
"Any sign of hostiles?" Aya asked, scanning nearby passengers without turning her head.
"Two possibilities," Amina replied, pretending to adjust her seatbelt. "Woman in a suit by the lavatory. Keeps checking her watch and scanning faces. And a man in row twelve—athletic build, situational awareness too high for a civilian."
"They might just be security officers," Aya noted. "Every flight has air marshals these days."
"Maybe." Amina didn't sound convinced.
The cabin door remained open well past the scheduled departure time. Flight attendants conferred in hushed tones near the galley. Aya's anxiety mounted with each passing minute.
Finally, the distinctive sound of the door mechanism engaged. Relief washed over her when Aya noticed a flight attendant escorting a late arrival to the business class cabin—Leyla, slightly disheveled but maintaining perfect composure.
"Ladies and gentlemen, we apologize for our delay tonight," came the captain's voice over the intercom. "We've been cleared for departure and will be pushing back momentarily, scheduled to get into Cappadocia about one hour after scheduled."
The plane began its slow retreat from the gate, taxiing toward the runway. Only when they accelerated into takeoff, the pressure pushing Aya back into her seat, did she allow herself a relaxing deep breath.
Istanbul fell away beneath them, the sprawling megalopolis becoming a distant light against the darkness.
The aircraft banked eastward, climbing through cloud layers and the dissipated storm toward cruising altitude. The cabin lights dimmed to a calm blue. Aya slipped her hand into her pocket, fingers brushing against the pouch containing a piece of Çelebi’s glass. Even through the protective material, she could sense its power, as if recognizing they were drawing closer to its original home.
As the jet continued its journey through the night sky, Aya gazed out at the stars visible above the cloud layer. Somewhere in that vast expanse, celestial bodies were aligning just as they had in Çelebi's time, creating a window of opportunity that would soon close.
Three days to find the remaining components. Three days to prevent Aegis from assembling a device of unfathomable power. Three days that would determine whether ancient secrets remained buried or emerged to reshape the modern world. Again.
The thought should have terrified her. Instead, it filled her with a strange sense of purpose. Her father had dedicated his life to this quest, and now the responsibility had passed to her, a legacy written in stardust and blood. 
Cappadocia beckoned.















2
Chapter 19
The aircraft touched down with a jolt an hour and a half later, tires bouncing on the tarmac of Nevşehir Kapadokya Airport. Aya remained motionless in her seat as other passengers stirred around her.
"One step closer," Amina murmured beside her, gazing through the small window at the austere landscape, barely visible through the darkness.
Aya kept her movements casual, deliberately avoiding eye contact with Karim and Leyla as they made their way through the small terminal. 
Aya and Amina headed for the AutoUnion rental car counter, Karim and Leyla not far behind them at the Europcar booth. 
"We need to rent a car for three days, please," Aya told the attendant, sliding over her forged license.
The young man with tired eyes and a name tag reading "Ali" processed the paperwork, and ten minutes later, they were loading their bags into a dusty silver Fiat.
"Definitely feels like we're being watched," Amina said.
Aya started the engine. "Probably are. The question now is whether it's Aegis or local police on alert. We’re stuck with both."
"Still, we stick to the plan. Göreme, central square. Leyla made contact with her friend here, right?"
"Should have," Aya replied, pulling onto the two-lane highway that cut through the stark landscape. "Though I'm not entirely comfortable putting our lives in the hands of another ex-criminal whose name we don't even know."
"You trusted me when we met in Florence," Amina pointed out with a half-smile. "And we both know how sketchy my résumé was."
"Different circumstances."
"Different how?" Amina tilted her head, studying Aya's profile. "Because you were dying to sleep with me?"
Aya's grip tightened on the steering wheel, and she kept her eyes locked on the road, watching the first light of morning threatening on the 5 o’clock horizon. 
"Because I'd seen your work. I knew your capabilities, plus Léglise vouched for you, and I trusted him then. At that time, I was the unproven element."
"Fair enough, habibi."
They drove in silence for several minutes, the landscape gradually transforming around them. Rolling hills gave way to more dramatic topography: pale, undulating rock that appeared almost liquid, as if the earth itself had turned liquid and then froze mid-flow.
"I see why they call them stone flowers," Amina said softly as the first fairy chimneys came into view. They were towering, conical formations crowned with darker stone caps, rising from the valley floor like misshapen obelisks. "They look alive. They're beautiful."
"And hiding something beneath them," Aya added, eyes still locked on the horizon, refusing the glance over at Amina. It was supposed to be Leyla beside her, until the plan got upended.
Signs for cave hotels and balloon tours appeared with greater frequency as they approached Göreme. Despite the hour, hot air balloons already dotted the sky, drifting dots of color. 
Aya checked her phone. A text from Karim confirmed that he and Leyla had secured transport and would meet them as planned. "We all made it through the night," she said. "So far, so good."
"Don't jinx it," Amina warned, her eyes fixed on the side mirror. "I think we've got a tail."
Aya glanced back through the rearview mirror. A black SUV with tinted windows maintained a consistent distance. "Government plates?"
"Can't tell from here," Amina replied. "But they turned when we did at that last roundabout."
Aya considered their options. "Let's find out if they're following us or just heading to Göreme. There aren’t many other directions to head out here, to be fair."
She abruptly turned onto a smaller road that branched away from the main route. The SUV continued straight.
"False alarm?" Amina asked.
"Maybe." Aya frowned, unconvinced. "Or they're just smart enough to know they were made.”
They continued on the secondary road, which wound through a valley filled on either side with the fairy chimneys. The formations loomed closer here, some carved with windows and doors, evidence of the human habitation that had transformed these natural structures into homes and churches for centuries.
"This place feels... different," Amina said, her voice dropping to just above a whisper. She rolled up her sleeve, revealing the markings. In the morning light, they seemed more pronounced than they had been in Istanbul, the constellations more distinct. "It has that ancient, forbidden feeling. If these powers are from space, I can’t think of anywhere that looks more alien, save anything Gaudí built."
Amina winced suddenly, pulling her arm close to her body.
"What's wrong?" Aya asked, glancing over with concern.
"It's burning," Amina replied through gritted teeth. "Not like in Morocco, not that intense, and it feels different, but definitely responding to something here."
"Çelebi was from this region," Aya said. "And we know there may be a tie to Cambodia from my dad’s Khmer research. There may be a more significant connection to the powers in Morocco than we understand."
Amina nodded, taking slow, measured breaths until the sensation ebbed. "The fragment is reacting too. I can feel it pulsing."
They rejoined the main road just outside Göreme, the small town coming into view ahead. It was a collection of stone buildings and cave dwellings nestled among the fairy chimneys, with tourist buses already parked in designated areas, depositing visitors who clutched cameras and guidebooks.
Aya navigated through the crowds and construction until they reached the central square, parking in the shadow of a small mosque. They sat in silence, obscured from view by a wall of ATMs that occupied the center of the street, watching the gradually increasing flow of people as the day established itself.
"There," Amina said suddenly, nodding toward a café across the square.
Karim sat at an outdoor table, a Turkish tea and a guidebook in front of him. He gave no sign of recognizing them, though Aya knew he'd spotted their arrival.
"And Leyla?" Aya asked, scanning the area.
"By the tour booking office. Pretending to compare balloon packages."
Sure enough, Leyla stood examining a display of brochures, her hair pulled back in a practical ponytail, hiking clothes replacing her usual more sophisticated attire.
Aya's gaze shifted beyond Leyla to a man in a canvas jacket who seemed too interested in the same tour posters. His posture was wrong for a tourist. Too alert, too balanced on the balls of his feet.
"We've got company," she murmured. "Nine o'clock, canvas jacket. Military bearing."
"I see him," Amina confirmed. "And another by the fruit stand. They're not even trying to be subtle."
"They were expecting us," Aya said, frowning. "Malik knew we'd come here."
“Or we didn’t cover our tracks enough in the apartment. We left in quite a hurry; didn’t even get to eat my kebab.”
They exited the car casually, retrieving their backpacks as if they were just two more tourists arriving for an adventure. A glance back at the square confirmed that Karim had observed their arrival and was now settling his bill.
"Let's wait for them at that café," Aya suggested, nodding toward a small establishment with cave-cut seating areas. "More private, easier to talk and check if anyone’s trying to listen."
The café was nearly empty, too early for the main breakfast rush. They selected a table in a recessed alcove that offered sight lines to the entrance. A sleepy-eyed waiter took their order for coffee and Turkish breakfast with minimal interest.
Karim arrived next, sliding into a seat across from them with practiced casualness. "We clear?"
"As much as we can be," Aya replied. "But Malik's people are already here. I counted at least two watching the square."
"Three," Karim corrected, his voice low. "One positioned on the rooftop of a bar, overlooking the main approach. Professional setup."
Leyla joined them last, her manner relaxed but her eyes constantly moving. She sat beside Karim, accepting the coffee that arrived shortly after.
"I've made contact with my asset," she said without preamble. "He runs a hotel now on the eastern ridge, in addition to his other businesses. We'll meet him there at noon."
"And he’s completely trustworthy?" Amina asked.
Leyla's gaze flickered to Amina's markings, visible beneath her rolled sleeve. "I have trusted Hamza with my life. And he’s one of the few people who might understand what happened to you in Morocco." She hesitated. "And he has... personal reasons to resist Aegis."
"Which are?" Aya prompted, sensing Leyla was holding back.
Leyla sighed, setting down her coffee cup. "I suppose now is as good a time as any for full disclosure," she said, her voice dropping further. "My connection to all this runs deeper than I initially revealed, but we’re in the shit pretty deep now, so gloves off."
The three looked at her expectantly.
"I didn't just correspond with Emre academically," she continued. "We worked together in Turkish intelligence before I left for academia. Specifically, we were with a division of MİT that specialized in protecting cultural heritage sites with national security implications."
"Cultural heritage with security implications?" Karim repeated skeptically.
"Turkey sits at the crossroads of civilizations," Leyla explained. "Some ancient sites contain more than just historical significance. Some harbor secrets that could... destabilize current power structures if misused."
Aya's brow furrowed. "And Çelebi's lens fell into that category."
"Exactly. For years, we monitored various groups showing interest in Ottoman astronomical research. Most were harmless academics, but occasionally we'd encounter more concerning entities."
"Like Aegis," Amina guessed.
"Not initially," Leyla replied. "At first, it was just one researcher. Brilliant, dedicated, and seemingly genuine in his academic pursuits." She paused. "That was Malik."
The revelation hung in the air between them.
"You worked with Malik?" Aya asked, voice sharpening.
"He wasn't Malik then, at least not to me. We knew him as Professor Osman. He approached our department as an academic seeking access to restricted archives. His credentials were impeccable, his research methodology sound. And for three years, I was his primary intelligence liaison."
"What happened?" Karim asked.
"He changed," Leyla said. "Or perhaps he revealed his true nature. He began seeking increasingly sensitive materials, becoming obsessed with legends. He wanted access to sealed sections of underground complexes here in Cappadocia."
"And you granted it?" Amina asked.
"Some of it," Leyla admitted. "Until I realized he was building a parallel research team. That's when I flagged him as a security concern." She took a sip of her coffee. "A week later, my supervisor called me in, said the investigation was closed, and transferred me to a desk position, maintaining a catalog."
"He had protection," Aya guessed.
"The highest levels," Leyla confirmed. "Within months, he'd disappeared from academic circles entirely. This begins my obsession with the lens and Çelebi, trying to put a stop to whatever he was up to, fearing the power he was amassing. Emre was recruited to the agency because of his expertise in the Cappadocia region. His family has lived there forever, and as time went on, he suspected that he was best positioned to stop Malik. Emre helped me continue to monitor Malik, tracking a pattern of acquisitions and expeditions that made no sense unless you understood what he was truly seeking."
"The lens," Karim said.
"Not just the lens," Leyla corrected. "The knowledge behind it. As we know, Çelebi wasn't just creating a device; he was developing a comprehensive understanding of cosmic energy manipulation that centuries later remains beyond conventional science."
"And Emre?" Aya asked. "What happened to him?"
Pain flashed across Leyla's face before she controlled it. "Three months ago, he called me, excited. He'd found something in an abandoned section of Derinkuyu, something he only found because his family protects some of the old tunnels. He said he’d found markings similar to those in Çelebi's manuscripts. He sent photos, promised more details after a final expedition." Her voice tightened. "That was the last I heard from him. His apartment was cleared out, and his university position was mysteriously terminated. It was as if he'd never existed. Anyone I asked denied ever knowing such a man, or quickly hurried away in fear."
"Until Omar contacted you," Amina said.
"Yes. When he mentioned Aegis and the lens, everything connected. Malik had resurfaced with organizational backing, continuing what he'd started years ago."
The implications settled over the group. Aya studied Leyla's face, searching for any sign of deception but finding only controlled anger and determination.
"There's one more thing," Leyla said after a moment. "Hamza, my contact here, was working extensively with Emre. He's been searching for him as well since his disappearance. When I mentioned your interest in Çelebi's work, he agreed to help on the condition that we also help find Emre."
"You think he's still alive?" Karim asked.
"I have to," Leyla replied, her professional demeanor cracking slightly. "If Malik took him, it's because he needs his expertise. Emre knew these underground networks, but he learned it all from Hamza, and you heard how Malik dangled him when he confronted us outside the mosque."
Amina suddenly inhaled sharply, faint blue light emanating from beneath her sleeve. "Whatever's here, it's affecting the fragment too."
Aya checked, and sure enough, the glass fragment in her bag emitted the same soft glow, visible even through the fabric.
"We need to move," Aya decided, glancing around the café. "When the components are reacting this strongly, Aegis has to be able to track them."
“Assuming they’ve got even a shard of their own,” Karim said, “they can listen for this same harmonic and triangulate.”
"Hamza's," Leyla said, already standing. "He'll know what to do, I think he knows more than he may realize, based on Emre’s recent work. He knew him well. Follow me."
They settled their bill quickly, exiting the café in pairs, Karim with Leyla, Aya with Amina. The square had grown busier, providing better cover as they moved through the crowds.
"Eleven o'clock," Aya murmured as they passed a souvenir shop. "Man in the blue jacket, talking into his collar."
"I see him," Amina confirmed. "And he definitely sees us."
They quickened their pace slightly, winding through narrower streets that climbed the hillside above Göreme. The tourist traffic thinned as they ascended, making them more conspicuous.
"We're exposed here," Amina warned, her discomfort making her voice tense.
"Almost there," Aya assured her, pointing toward a weathered building perched on the ridgeline ahead. A hand-painted sign beside its blue door read "Hamza's Cave Pension" in both Turkish and English.
As they approached, Aya couldn't shake the sensation of being watched—not just by the Aegis, but by the landscape itself. The fairy chimneys loomed around them like ancient guardians, their hollowed faces seeming to track their progress.
A man sat in a wicker chair outside, his deeply lined face turned toward the valley below. As they drew nearer, he rose, bringing with him a lingering smell of dampness.
"You're earlier than expected," he said, dark eyes assessing them with unnerving precision. "But perhaps that's for the best. They're already closing the net."
"Are you Hamza?" Aya asked.
The man nodded. "And you are Leyla's friends, seeking what should perhaps remain lost." His gaze fixed on Amina, or more specifically, on her arm where a map of the heavens glowed. "Ah," he said softly. "The djinn have touched you."
Amina stared back at him in surprise. "You recognize these?"
"I’m the storyteller around here. Old stories," Hamza said. "Stories most dismiss as superstition. Most everyone else always does, anyway." He gestured toward the blue door. "Come inside. We have more to discuss before your friends arrive."
As Aya followed him into the cool interior of the cave dwelling, she couldn't help but feel they'd crossed some invisible threshold. The stone flowers of Cappadocia had guarded their secrets for hundreds of years, secrets that endured only in the minds of people like Hamza. Now, those secrets were beginning to stir.
Chapter 20
The carved chambers of Hamza's lodge felt like stepping back in time. Stone walls, worn smooth by centuries of hands, opened up through the hillside, following the natural contours of the rock. Modern conveniences had been carefully integrated, with electrical wiring running along ancient grooves and copper plumbing threading through what looked like Byzantine archways, but they seemed almost apologetic in their presence, deferring to the enduring stone.
Aya found herself studying the space as they entered, cataloging exits and sight lines out of habit. The main chamber centered around a weathered wooden table surrounded by chairs of mismatched upholstery. Decades of hospitality had worn comfortable patterns into every surface.
But it was the massive brass instrument in the central alcove that made her stop short. Aya had no idea how it worked, but she knew what it was, and it told her they were in the right place.
A wide brass disk almost as big as a tabletop sat upright in a wooden stand, its outer rim carved with tiny tick marks like a clock face and its surface engraved with circles within circles like a map, packed with numbers, symbols, and curved lines. Over that engraved face sat a thinner layer of brass cut into an intricate web, like the framework of an antique globe, with small pointed tabs that marked specific stars and faint names scratched beside them in fading script. At the center, a single pin and a rounded knob gleamed, polished bright. It was a device built to measure the sky and turn starlight into answers.
The astrolabe dominated the space like a bronze sun. Nearly three feet across, its surface caught the lamplight and threw back patterns of shadow and gleam. Unlike the pristine museum pieces she'd seen during various jobs over the years, this one bore the patina of actual use. Its brass was darkened by countless hands, engravings worn smooth in places where fingers had repeatedly traced celestial paths.
"My family has lived in these stones for more than fifteen generations," Hamza said, settling into what was clearly his customary chair. The deep lines of his face seemed to deepen in the lamplight. "We were here before the sultans gave this place the name Cappadocia."
His gaze found Aya and held, measuring. “Amrani,” he said, as if tasting the name. "Your father's work reached even here. Hassan Amrani. He was… spoken of with respect among those who understand what we guard."
His dark eyes moved to Amina, who had unconsciously begun rubbing her arm again. The underground journey seemed to have intensified whatever connection existed between her and the artifacts they carried.
"You're in pain," he observed. It wasn't a question.
Amina looked up, startled to be so easily read by a stranger.
"This might help," Hamza continued, rising carefully. He moved to a cabinet carved directly into the cave wall. "Though I should warn you, the relief is only temporary, and the recipe is older than I can properly source."
From within the cabinet, he produced a small clay jar. The pale salve inside gave off scents of sage and cedar, with something else underneath, sharp and earthy, like the mountain air after a thunderstorm.
Amina hesitated, then dipped her fingers into the mixture. As she spread a thin layer over the patterns etched into her skin, her expression shifted from wariness to surprise. The constant burning sensation that had plagued her since entering Turkey began to ease almost immediately.
"Better?" Hamza asked.
She nodded, unable to suppress a soft sigh of relief. "How did you know it would work?"
"I didn't," he admitted with a smile, scratching his head. "But some old stories suggested that when certain energies stir, much like when Emre was interested in this place, particular herbs could provide comfort. It is my grandmother's recipe, and I thought it was worth trying."
The sound of approaching footsteps interrupted them. Karim and Leyla appeared in the doorway, escorted by a younger man who shared Hamza's angular features.
"Uncle," the young man said. "Perimeter is secure. No immediate concerns, but there's unusual vehicle activity in town. Too heavy for this time of year."
"Thank you, Mehmet. Bring tea for our guests, then return to your watch on the eastern approach."
After his nephew departed, Hamza turned his attention to Leyla. "It's been too long since you've visited us."
"For that, I’m sorry. I’m also sorry because I know what we're asking," Leyla said, her expression reflecting both gratitude and concern. "The risks involved—"
"Are relative, considering what is concerned," Hamza said with a dismissive wave. "Now, your message said you needed sanctuary and a guide through the underground cities, not lengthy discussions, though I admit curiosity about what brings such an unlikely group to my door."
His gaze moved across the room, taking them all in. "Show me what you've brought from Istanbul," he said.
Aya exchanged glances with the others, then carefully placed the glass fragment on the table's worn surface, the piece from Abdelatif's journal. Karim followed suit with the brass cylinder from the Kapanizade shop. Leyla added the cloth-wrapped fragments from Çelebi's workshop, spreading them beside the others. Four components, catching the lamplight as they settled onto the wood.
For a moment, nothing seemed to change. Then Aya noticed it. A faint vibration in the air, like the ringing echo of a bell she hadn't heard ring. Behind them, the massive astrolabe continued its lazy rotation. Or had it been moving before? She couldn't quite remember.
Hamza had gone very still, his weathered hands flat against the table. "Do you hear that?" he asked softly.
"Hear wh…" Karim started to say, but stopped. There was something, not quite sound, not quite silence. The astrolabe's brass began catching the light differently, throwing patterns that seemed almost deliberate.
"I thought it was decoration," Hamza said, staring at the astrolabe as if seeing it for the first time. "My great-grandfather's obsession with this thing. But look—" He pointed with a trembling finger. The same patterns etched into the artifacts were worked into the astrolabe's rim, so worn and tarnished they'd been nearly invisible until this moment, when they seemed to catch every available trace of light.
Hamza's eyes widened, and he murmured something imperceptible under his breath. Something warred in his voice when he finally spoke aloud. "I always assumed they were just stories. Legend to keep a boy interested in family history."
"You've seen these before?" Leyla asked.
"No, definitely not," Hamza said slowly, his attention split between the artifacts on the table and the increasingly active astrolabe. "But the symbols carved into their surfaces, those I recognize. My great-grandfather etched similar patterns into that astrolabe, but I thought it was for decoration." He shrugged.
Karim approached the astrolabe, bending low to study it. The astrolabe's rotation continued to accelerate until its movement became almost hypnotic. The intricate brass work threw shifting patterns of light and shadow across the chamber walls, creating an effect that was both beautiful and slightly unsettling.
"The fragment projected a message when we found it," Aya explained, watching Hamza's face for any sign of deeper knowledge. "Something about finding what we seek 'beneath the stone flowers.'"
Hamza nodded. "The stone flowers," he repeated, "you're in the right place. But as far as I know, this astrolabe has only served as a decoration." He paused, considering it again. "But, I do remember my grandfather polishing it for days on end, saying that one day it would sing. I never understood what he meant. He was an old man; he may have been losing it."
As if responding, the astrolabe's rotation stabilized into a steady, purposeful rhythm. The complex harmony created by its interaction with the artifacts resolved into something approaching melody, hauntingly beautiful, slow.
"What exactly did your great-grandfather tell you?" Aya asked. "In our experience, folklore can provide a lot more actionable answers than you might think."
"Fragments, mostly. Stories about buried treasures in the deep places beneath the fairy chimneys." Hamza's weathered hands moved to specific points on the astrolabe's rim, following patterns his fingers seemed to remember without conscious direction. "He taught me a child's rhyme about following the shepherd's path when the seven sisters dance with the wandering stars. Like poetry."
"The Pleiades," Karim said immediately. "And planetary alignment. Your grandfather was encoding actual astronomical coordinates in children's verses."
"That's the constellation Salah was focused on," Leyla said.
Before anyone could respond, Amina gasped. Her tattooed arm flared beneath the salve's temporary suppression, lines of blue tracing the constellation pattern. The glass fragment on the table responded in kind, its luminescence intensifying until it cast stark shadows across the chamber. "Here we go again. Something's happening," she managed through gritted teeth. "The components are reacting to that sound, I think."
Mehmet's urgent voice called from the chamber entrance. 
"No time for tea then. Vehicles approaching the valley in haste," Hamza translated, any sense of wonder hardening. "Government plates."
"Aegis," Aya said. "They've found us."
"Then we have little time. This is what happened when they came for Emre," Hamza said, moving toward a solid section of carved wall.
Leyla went still. "They came here? To your home?"
"They came everywhere," Hamza said, his fingers already finding barely visible depressions in the stone. "But they didn't find what they were looking for. Neither did Emre, for that matter."
Leyla's hands had curled into fists at her sides.
Before Hamza could press the hidden mechanism, an explosion shook the hillside. Dust rained from the ceiling. Outside, shouts rang out in Turkish, the clatter of heavy boots on stone.
"Now," Hamza said, pressing the sequence with practiced speed. "We go now."
The previously solid stone wall pivoted inward as it had at the Süleymaniye Mosque, revealing a hidden darkness beyond that carried the damp scent of vast, enclosed spaces.
"Grab the components," Aya ordered, already moving. Karim swept the artifacts into his pack while Leyla secured the wrapped fragments. Another explosion, closer this time, rattled the chamber. The astrolabe's song cut off abruptly.
"The astrolabe," Karim said. "We need to understand—"
"We need to move," Hamza said. "It sang and settled on the Shepherd. See?”
Aya caught a last glimpse. The rotating pointers had locked into a new geometry, a cluster of gemstones flaring in a pattern that echoed the marks on Amina’s arm.
They plunged into the passage as a third explosion blew out the lodge's front entrance. The hidden door swung shut behind them with grinding finality, sealing them in darkness. Aya's ears popped from the pressure change.
"No going back now," Amina muttered.
Chapter 21
The descent was immediate and steep, the carved passage so narrow their shoulders brushed both walls. The temperature plummeted with each step, their breath forming clouds in the suddenly frigid air. Somewhere far below, water dripped with metronomic persistence.
The rhythm wasn’t constant. Every few beats, the drip sped up, then slowed again, as if the mountain were testing a pulse. Aya felt it in the air too, a minute change in pressure that made her ears tighten and release, like the tunnels were breathing.
"Keep moving," Hamza urged from the rear. "These passages were carved to withstand siege engines and earthquakes, but Aegis will find other ways in."
The stone steps beneath their feet were worn to dangerous smoothness. Aya led by touch, her left hand on the wall, counting steps in the darkness until Karim's headlamp flared to life behind her, throwing long shadows.
Above them, muffled through tons of rock, came another explosion. The passage shuddered. Dust sifted from the ceiling.
"They're trying to blast through," Hamza said. "They won't succeed here, but they'll be searching for other entrances. My nephew knows to seal what he can and disappear."
The tunnel branched ahead. "Left where winter water speaks," Hamza said, taking the leftward path where a subtle chill in the air suggested hidden moisture. "Straight past the seven windows."
They moved quickly past a series of narrow vents cut high in the walls, ancient air shafts that connected to the surface far above, though no light penetrated their depths.
Hamza didn’t slow, but his voice dropped. “Listen to the water. Down here, passages don’t stay open just because someone wants them to. When the stone drinks, some routes close, some fill. We keep moving.”
As they descended, carvings began to appear in the walls. Simple scratches at first, then increasingly elaborate work. Some inscriptions were in Ottoman Turkish, others in scripts that Karim photographed without being able to immediately identify.
"Look at this," Amina said, stopping beside a particularly complex panel. Circles nested within eight-pointed stars, their edges marked with powder-fine graduations. "These match some of those astrolabe designs."
Her markings had begun to glow more steadily as they descended, turning her into a walking source of blue light. Under that illumination, faint pigments in the rock began to wake with traces of azurite blue and whispers of red ochre.
"The Silent Meridian," Aya said, studying the equations carved into the walls. "They didn't stop working when they lost the official observatory. They went underground."
She felt Hamza’s eyes on her as she uttered the words, but he remained silent.
"And they kept working for centuries," Karim added, photographing as he moved. "Look at the dates. Some of these inscriptions are from the 1570s. Others are much later. Generations of scholars adding to the same body of knowledge."
The passage opened suddenly into a larger space. They emerged onto a ledge overlooking what Aya's light couldn't fully illuminate, a vast chamber that swallowed their beams. The acoustics were unsettling. A boot scuff to the left would echo from high on the right; someone's breath would return as a chorus of whispers from multiple directions.
"As above, so below," Leyla said, her light finding the phrase repeated in various languages across the visible walls. Beside it, astronomical diagrams, mathematical proofs, and alchemical symbols.
The air shifted, a slow inhale through hidden fissures, and the dripping she’d been hearing all along swelled closer, more insistent, as if water were rising somewhere they couldn’t see.
"We need to keep moving," Aya said, though she ached to study it all. "Whatever's down here, Aegis can't find it."
Hamza led them along the ledge to a narrow archway. "The deep places I mentioned," he said. "Beyond this point, I've only been once. When my grandfather made me swear I would remember the path."
They passed through the archway into passages that grew older with each step. The Ottoman inscriptions gave way to symbols that predated even those. The air changed quality, growing cleaner, drier, and impossibly fresh for a space sealed for centuries.
"There," Hamza said finally, pointing ahead.
The passage opened into a spherical chamber that made Aya stop and stare.
It was perfectly round, as if they stood inside a stone planet. At least forty meters across, the walls curving into a high dome. Someone had hammered a heaven across that dome. Stars of silver and gold, constellations inlaid with copper wire, planetary tracks picked out in gems that fractured their flashlights' feeble beams into hard rainbows.
At the center rose a disk of polished obsidian, knee-high, black as a still pool. Silver lines ran across it like computer circuitry, measured paths joining shallow mounting cradles and precisely machined slots.
"Incredible," Hamza whispered. "I've never—" He caught himself, palm to his chest as if steadying his heart. "I've never been this far."
"It's beautiful," Karim whispered, stepping toward the platform before catching himself and glancing at Aya. She nodded once. Careful.
They fanned out slowly. Aya circled the wall clockwise, photographing inscriptions. Leyla took the opposite arc, her fingers hovering over engraved script. Karim crouched beside the platform, studying tick marks cut around its edge. Amina lingered near the entrance, eyes fixed on the ceiling.
"Look," Karim said, pointing to the marks. "These aren't ornamental. They're indices. Celestial positions." He angled his light, tracing patterns. "As above—"
"So below," Amina finished from behind them.
Leyla called out from her section of the wall. "This inscription. Old numbers I can't immediately decipher."
Aya crossed to her. The inscription sat chest-high in a band around a shallow recess, a braid of numerals and notational marks.
Leyla mouthed numbers under her breath, rechecking against a second line, then a third. "Sea-level," she said finally. "Not latitude-longitude. These are heights. Above the sea, then below again. Repeating on a cycle."
Aya studied the pattern, counting under her breath. "Wait. Those intervals. Twelve hours, twenty-four minutes between peaks. That's not geographic, that's temporal."
"Temporal what?" Leyla asked, though understanding was dawning in her expression.
"Tidal," Aya said. She turned to Amina, who had moved closer. "These are tide calculations."
Amina's face lit with recognition. "Like Maya Bay."
"Exactly like Maya Bay." Aya felt the familiar current between them, the way they'd once moved through problems like two parts of the same mind. "The hidden shrine. It was only accessible—"
"—during the low tide window," Amina finished, already pulling out her phone. "Forty-seven minutes twice daily when the water level dropped enough to expose the entrance."
"And we almost missed it because we were using standard charts," Aya added. "The local fishermen had different measurements based on—"
"—on reference points that predated modern sea level changes." Amina looked up from her phone. "These numbers aren't using modern baselines either."
Leyla watched them, brow raised. "You two have done this before?"
"A few times," Aya said, deliberately not meeting Karim's gaze from across the chamber where he'd paused in his documentation. "Amina, what if these are calibrated to Ottoman-era waterlines?"
"Give me a second." Amina scrolled rapidly through databases on her phone. "Mediterranean sea level in the 16th century was approximately... Here. Fifteen to twenty centimeters lower than present day along the Turkish coast."
"So we adjust all these measurements down," Aya said, already doing the mental math. "And these hash marks between the numbers. Leyla, could those be quarter-tide indicators?"
Leyla studied them with fresh eyes. "Yes. They could be. Which means this isn't just tracking high and low, it's mapping the entire tidal cycle."
Aya traced the full sequence. "For timing something that needs to happen at a specific point in the cycle. A window that opens and closes."
"Just like the shrine," Amina repeated softly, and for a moment their eyes met. Aya saw dawn breaking over the Andaman Sea, salt water rushing through hidden channels, the desperate race against rising tides that had nearly drowned them both.
Karim cleared his throat from his position by the platform. His expression was carefully blank. "Tidal calibration against a moving reference."
Aya felt the distance between them, the crack that moments like this only widened.
Leyla ran more calculations. "Southwest shore… If you map Ottoman bearings to modern charts, that line lands on—" She stopped herself. "Coast. Warm coast."
"Bodrum peninsula," Aya said. "It has to be."
Chapter 22
While Leyla and Karim studied the tidal calculations, Aya circled the chamber's perimeter. More inscriptions revealed themselves with each step.
Diagrams showed underground complexes connected by water channels. Architectural plans. And in one alcove, a detailed illustration of a human figure with arms outstretched, spine aligned along carved channels. Lines radiated from the body to carefully drawn lenses and mirrors, each marked with dense notations.
Aya's chest tightened as she studied it. "A human conduit."
Amina had drifted to the diagram. "Like the door mechanism in Morocco."
The illustration was technical, not symbolic. Whoever operated this device wouldn't just be using it. They would become part of it.
Aya moved closer to Amina, close enough to speak without the others hearing. "We don't have to do this. If this device requires what I think it does—"
"I know what you're thinking," Amina cut her off, her voice steady despite the slight tremor in her hands. "And you're probably right. These marks, the way the artifacts respond to me—it's all connected." She met Aya's eyes. "But we both know I'm the only one who can make this work, if it comes to that."
The weight settled between them, and Aya felt something surface that she struggled to suppress. It wasn't the time. She had something good with Karim, and she couldn't risk everything they were working toward. But she couldn’t lose Amina either.
Karim's voice broke the moment. "Look at these grooves in the platform rim. The Pleiades marks are worn." He glanced at Aya. She gave him a small nod.
He pressed.
Something clicked deep beneath the obsidian. Above them, the metal heaven shivered, one constellation sliding a degree while others compensated. A corresponding segment of the ring rotated under Karim's hand.
"Hidden mechanism," he whispered.
"Try another," Aya said.
He pressed Deneb. A low clunk echoed. The dome's copper lines shifted again. On the third press, a panel near Aya's shoulder sighed open on perfectly balanced hinges, revealing a shallow alcove.
Cold air seeped out, smelling of cracked leather and ancient ink.
Aya peered inside without touching anything. A single bound folio wrapped in oiled cloth, and behind it a brass panel etched with geometric patterns.
"Documents," Karim said carefully. "And instructions."
"Photograph first," Aya said.
Karim documented everything, each corner checked, each detail preserved. The folio's cover was stamped 1658.
They gathered around as Karim gently lifted the wrapped bundle, setting it on the least dusty section of the floor. His gloved fingers found the edge of the oiled cloth, testing its integrity before peeling back the layers.
The folio beneath was bound in dark leather, tooled with the eight-pointed star pattern. Karim opened it with infinite care.
The first page was dense with script; ink faded to brown but legible.
In a clerk's crisp hand: Successful activation under last alignment. Luminous phenomena were seen as far as the upper vineyards. Decision: hide until we can be wise.
A second line below, different handwriting:
Coastal workshops complete what mountain chambers begin. The sea sets the clock we cannot command.
"A network," Karim said. "This place is the brain, and other places are the hands. One of those hands points west."
Leyla pulled out her phone, inputting the tidal calculations they'd decoded. "The numbers sit exactly on the Bodrum peninsula if you translate them forward."
"Another chamber in the tides," Karim said.
"How far?" Aya asked.
"Southwest coast of Turkey," Leyla said. "Maybe they scattered these chambers across the region in case one was compromised."
Aya nodded. "Süleymaniye Mosque, Cappadocia, Bodrum. A whole network."
Amina had not moved from near the platform. "I can feel the sea when you say that," she said quietly. "Salt air at the back of my tongue."
"Amina, don't touch anything," Aya warned.
But Amina was already stepping toward the platform, as if drawn by invisible threads. The blue fire in her arm blazed, visible even in the dim light.
"We have what we came for," Aya started. "Let's go."
Amina didn't seem to hear. She slid the glass fragment from her pack and hovered it above the central mounting cradle, her hand shaking.
"We have no idea what that'll do," Aya warned.
"It's already calling me," Amina said and placed it.
The chamber answered like a held chord freeing itself. Blinding light flowed from the fragment into the silver lines, a star going supernova in slow motion. The obsidian shifted by measured ticks. Above, constellations strobed. Sound layered, a low harmonic Aya felt in her throat, then another in her ribs, then a third that seemed to come from the mountain itself.
Amina grabbed the platform edge to keep from falling. Her eyes rolled back, exposing the whites.
"Amina," Aya said, rushing to her side.
Amina's voice came in pieces between the cadence of that deep music. "Salt. Incense. Ancient stones watching ships come in. Bells ringing in the wind—" She flinched, gasping. "Water that fills and empties and waits between. So clear. Turquoise. It's Bodrum."
Karim's phone was already out, recording what he could.
The platform turned another fraction, then something unlocked inside the dome. A second panel breathed open in the adjacent quadrant.
"Incredible," Karim said, moving toward it. "Another component."
Nestled in the revealed alcove was a brass and crystal assembly the size of two fists. The brass formed a skeletal framework, delicate as lacework, while multiple lenses of different colored glass were suspended within it at precise angles. Behind it, mounted to the alcove's rear wall, was a brass plate cut with slots and notches—a receiving array with six primary mounting positions and smaller sockets between.
"This one's different from the fragment and cylinder," Leyla said. "More complex. Look at the lens arrangement."
"The focusing array," Karim breathed. "If our theories are correct, this would be the primary lens group. The part that actually bends and concentrates the incoming energy."
Amina's vision had cleared. The constellation map on her arm flared brighter as she approached. "It knows me," she said. "Just like the others. But this one's stronger."
"Don't touch it yet," Aya warned, but she knew they couldn't leave it behind.
"If this is here and the tidal calculations point to Bodrum," Karim said, "then they distributed the actual components. Scattered them across multiple sites to prevent anyone from assembling the complete device, the Silent Meridian."
"Unless you had the necessary knowledge to find them all," Leyla added.
Aya opened her backpack, extracting protective cloth. As her fingers brushed the brass framework, she felt a subtle vibration, as if the component hummed at a frequency just below hearing. "We have to take this."
A sound cut through the chamber's harmony: a higher-pitched grind, methodical, coming from the corridor they'd used.
"They found us," Leyla said flatly. "Shit."
Hamza's face looked very old suddenly. "No one else can find this. I had no idea what I was helping protect."
"They have the state's resources," Aya said. "Every sensor. Every tunnel map. We helped lead them here."
"We need to move," Leyla said.
The platform gave one last pulse that thudded through Aya's boots. From beyond the far wall, faintly, other notes answered.
Aya lunged forward and pulled the fragment free. The light didn't die so much as fold itself down. The obsidian stilled. The dome's starlight returned to its ordinary gleam.
"Exit?" Amina asked, voice ragged.
Hamza shook his head. "I only know the way we came."
"That one's occupied," Aya said.
The grinding from the corridor grew louder. Voices became audible, barking orders in Turkish.
"Spread out," Aya ordered. "Look for seams, pressure points, anything unusual."
They scattered around the chamber's perimeter. Leyla attacked the wall nearest the corridor. Amina tried the section near the first alcove, pressing inscriptions in sequence. Nothing happened.
"There has to be another way out," Karim said, voice tight. "No one would build a chamber like this with only one exit."
Hamza stood in the center, turning slowly. "The stories never mentioned—they only said the stars would always guide us home."
A loud crash echoed from the corridor. They were running out of time.
"The constellations," Aya said suddenly, looking up at the dome. "Karim, when you pressed those stars before, the whole system shifted. What if there's a specific sequence that opens an exit?"
Amina glanced at her arm. "The platform. It's still active somehow. I can feel it."
Karim, half-crouched over the platform rim, swore under his breath. "Look at Orion now." He pointed up. The hunter's belt had shifted with the last rotation, and where his shoulder star had been dull, it now showed a bright, polished gem with a tiny channel beneath it.
"Counter-release," he said. "If you align the Pleiades and Deneb in this order, Orion returns to winter position and—"
"Just do it," Amina said, already moving. She reached the shoulder star, finding the gem's polished surface was a grip. She pressed and slid it along the minute channel.
A seam in the wall became a line, then a crack, then a panel swinging inward on stone pivots so perfectly balanced the door moved with a breath. Cold air beyond. A slate-black passage.
Hamza was frozen. "But no one knows these routes. Not me. Not my ancestors. Not anyone."
"We do now, and they will soon," Aya said. "I'm sorry, Hamza, but we need to go. If we're already gone, maybe they won't damage anything here. Once we stop Aegis, we'll do everything we can to protect this work."
Hamza met her eyes, and in that moment, he made a decision. He stepped closer, lowering his voice so only she could hear. "Your father. He was an archaeologist, yes?"
"Yes."
"Leyla told me he died trying to protect sites like this from people like Malik. Maybe just like Emre." Hamza's hand gripped her shoulder. "I see this in you, too. Not fear, but the principle beneath it."
Aya opened her mouth but paused. "I'm not—I haven't always been someone who protected things."
“My family guarded these secrets by hiding for fifteen generations,” Hamza said. “That era is over. They have drones, sensors, maps, and resources my ancestors never imagined. You can move in the world they control. I can’t. So yes, if anyone carries this now, it’s you.”
The voices in the corridor were closer now, echoing off the stone.
“I don’t know if I’m the right person to carry it,” Aya said.
"Worth isn't about what you have been," Hamza said. "It's about what you will choose to do when it matters most." He released her shoulder, stepping back. "I believe Çelebi and his followers scattered these components because they believed some knowledge should be earned, not stolen. They would have wanted someone like you to find them. To understand them and make the right decision for humanity."
Aya looked into his eyes, feeling the weight of a burden that had passed for generations. A burden her father had likely died for. "I won't let them turn this into a weapon," she said. The words felt like an oath. "Whatever we discover in Bodrum, whatever the complete device can do, we'll make sure it's used wisely. Or not at all."
Hamza nodded slowly. "Then the guardianship passes to you. When this is over, when Aegis is defeated, come back. Help me preserve what's here. Not hidden, but protected. Shared with those who will honor it."
"I will," Aya said. "I swear it."
A smile creased Hamza's face, decades of worry easing slightly. "Then go. The mountain will keep its secrets a little longer."
The emergency passage was claustrophobic, barely shoulder-width, built to empty a room in an emergency, not to impress. Irregular steps alternated with steep rock slopes. Twice, the floor broke into waist-deep trenches they had to jump.
Behind them, light strobed from the chamber until it faded to complete darkness.
They ran by the only clock that mattered: the mountain's breath and their own. The passage dropped into a chute that burned Aya's forearms as she controlled her slide. They landed in a lower corridor where the air moved with purpose and smelled faintly of goat.
The world above, somewhere ahead.
Chapter 23
The emergency passage spit them out into predawn darkness, lungs burning, legs shaking from the climb. They'd emerged behind what Hamza identified as an abandoned shepherd's hut carved into the hillside, another remnant of his family's network, forgotten by everyone except those who knew to look for it.
Aya dropped her pack inside the hut's entrance and bent double, hands on knees, forcing her breathing to steady. The wound in her side pulled with each inhale, the stitches Amina had sewn protesting the abuse. Around her, the others collapsed against stone walls, equally spent.
"We're clear," Leyla said from the doorway, scanning the ravine below with night-vision binoculars she'd pulled from her pack. "No movement. No lights."
"They'll still be after us," Amina said through deep inhales. "They always are, but at least we’re ahead of them for now."
"Then we use the time we have," Aya said, straightening despite her body's complaints. She moved to the narrow window slit, looking out over the dark expanse of Cappadocia's valleys. Somewhere out there, in the maze of fairy chimneys and hidden chambers, was another piece of Çelebi's puzzle. 
Another satellite workshop, another place for Çelebi’s followers to gather and work in private, after the mosque in Istanbul and the star chamber they'd just escaped, plus whatever awaited them in Bodrum. They needed them all to assemble the complete Astral Lens back in Istanbul, to understand what they were dealing with, to prevent Malik and Aegis from having the Astral Lens, and to understand what her father had learned.
But first, they needed to rest. And think.
Karim spread his jacket on the dirt floor and sat heavily. "How long do we have?"
"Two, maybe three hours until dawn," Hamza said, checking his watch. "After that, the sun angle we need will only last twenty minutes before it shifts too far."
"Sun angle?" Karim asked.
"For the markers," Hamza explained. "The stories speak of finding the shepherd's path when the seven sisters dance with the wandering stars." He glanced at the artifacts in Aya's pack, their faint glow visible even through the fabric. "I think it describes a specific time that reveals the way forward. The Pleiades and the planetary positions, illuminating what's been hidden."
Aya pulled the glass fragment from her bag, watching it pulse with soft blue light in the darkness. It was responding to something, even now. To their proximity to the next site, perhaps, or to Amina's presence. "So we wait for dawn’s approach, let the fragment guide us to the exact location, and hope we can get in and out before Aegis catches up."
"That's the only plan I see," Leyla said. “Then on to Bodrum and the coast.”
"Yet another very detailed, foolproof plan," Amina said, but without some of her usual bite. She looked exhausted in a way Aya hadn't seen before, not physically spent but hollowed out, as if something was consuming her from within.
Aya moved to sit beside her. "How are you feeling?"
Amina held up her arm. The blue constellations glowed faintly in the darkness, pulsing in rhythm with the glass fragment in Aya's hand. "A lot worse," she admitted quietly. "The burning hasn't stopped since we entered that chamber below. It's getting stronger, not weaker." She rubbed at the map of the night sky, grimacing. "Like something is pulling at me. Or trying to pull something out of me. I don't know how to explain it."
"The connection to the artifacts?" Karim asked.
"Maybe. Or more djinn?" Amina's voice was uncertain. "In Morocco, I felt their presence like someone breathing behind me that I couldn't see. But now..." She shook her head. "Now it feels like something’s trying to push through. Like the marks are a door, and something on the other side is knocking harder and harder, and might rip me apart in the process. I don’t know if I’m strong enough for this."
The vulnerability in her words hung in the small space, reminding Aya of why she'd loved this woman once. Still did. It just had to be different now.
"Get some rest," Aya said, squeezing Amina's hand. "All of you. I'll take first watch."
"I'll stay with you," Hamza said. "These are my mountains. I should be useful."
The others settled into uneasy rest while Aya and Hamza maintained vigil at the window. The old man was quiet for a long time, his weathered face thoughtful in the darkness.
Eventually, he gestured toward the artifacts. "Knowledge is too dangerous to be hoarded but too valuable to be destroyed. That's a difficult balance."
"I'm not sure I know how to walk that line," Aya said. "I've spent my whole life taking things, selling them to the highest bidder. Now I'm supposed to be a guardian? It doesn't feel real."
"My family has protected these secrets for fifteen generations without understanding their purpose. Perhaps that was wrong. Perhaps the knowledge should have been shared, studied, and preserved openly. But we did what we thought was right." He met her eyes. "Now that burden passes to you. What will you do with it?"
Aya looked out at the predawn landscape, at the fairy chimneys rising against the lightening sky. "I swore to you I'd protect this. Not hide it, but protect it. Make sure it's either used wisely for good or not at all. I meant that."
"I believe you did."
They lapsed into silence as the sky gradually shifted from black to deep purple. Aya found herself thinking about her father, about the research that had consumed his final years. Had he stood in places like this, weighing the same impossible choices? Had he known, in Colombia, that his decision to hide rather than share could cost him his life? That it would leave her alone?
"The guardianship passes to you," Hamza had said in the star chamber. At the time, caught in the urgency of escape, she'd barely processed the words. Now, in the quiet before dawn, their full weight settled on her shoulders.
This wasn't just about stopping Aegis anymore. It was about deciding what came after. If they succeeded in keeping the lens from Malik, what then? They'd found two of Çelebi's workshops—the mosque in Istanbul, the star chamber below. They knew of a third in Bodrum. But then what? Find out how to safely destroy it all? Study it? Lock it away for another four centuries? Or assemble the lens themselves and find a way to use it that Hassan would have approved of?
"Movement," Hamza said softly, pulling her from her thoughts. He pointed toward the eastern ridge. "Dawn patrol. Probably on Aegis payroll if they’re moving with that much purpose."
Aya squinted at the distant figures picking their way along the ridgeline. Four of them, spread in a search pattern. They were still over a kilometer away, but the net was tightening. Either they'd predicted where the emergency passage would emerge, or they had more people than Aya had realized. Maybe both.
"Time to go," she said, moving to wake the others. "Sun's going to be up soon. If we're going to find this place, it has to be now."
They gathered their gear in tense silence, checking weapons, securing packs. Aya caught Karim's eye and saw her own determination reflected. He offered her a hesitant smile in return.
As they slipped out of the hut into the cold morning air, Aya looked back once at the small shelter that had given them a few hours of safety. Then she turned toward the fairy chimneys rising in the distance, toward whatever Çelebi and his people had hidden there four hundred years ago.
The artifacts pulsed stronger now, responding to the approaching dawn. Beside her, Amina sucked in a sharp breath and pressed her hand against her arm, where the blue marks flared brighter.
Hamza led them through challenging terrain, and Aya could feel the lack of sleep coursing through her body. Loose scree slithered underfoot with every step, each shift threatening to send them sliding down slopes that dropped away to nothing. Knife-edge ridges connected one precarious perch to another, with hundred-meter falls on either side.
Aya had taken two ibuprofen from Amina's kit, but they were barely touching the deep ache that had settled into her ribs. She pressed her hand against the wound, feeling the heat of inflammation through her shirt, but kept moving.
They were heading for a cluster of three fairy chimneys that appeared in Çelebi's workshop maps, though Hamza hadn't known their significance until now. The artifacts were leading them there, too, growing warmer and brighter with each kilometer they covered. Aya could feel the glass fragment's heat through her pack, pulsing in a steady rhythm. She couldn’t imagine how Amina must feel.
Halfway across a narrow ledge, Hamza's ankle rolled on loose stone. He stumbled, throwing his weight backward to avoid the drop. Karim moved instantly, sliding under the old man's shoulder without a word, taking half his weight.
"Easy," Karim said. "We've got time."
"Not much," Hamza grunted, but accepted the support.
They continued in silence, speaking only when necessary. The mountain carried sound with unnerving clarity. A cough or the scrape of a boot on stone could echo across the ravines like a gunshot. Somewhere behind them, Aegis was surely in pursuit, following whatever trail of disturbance they'd left in the star chamber.
Aya and Amina took point, scouting each section before waving the others forward. It was familiar work, the rhythm of movement and watch they'd developed in a dozen countries. For a few moments, they could almost pretend it was just another job, just another score.
Except Amina kept stopping every few minutes, pressing her hand against her arm, breathing through whatever sensation the marks were causing. Each time, the pause grew longer. Each time, the blue glow grew brighter.
"There," Amina said finally, pointing ahead with a trembling hand. "Three formations, arranged in a triangle. That's what we're looking for, right?"
Aya pulled up the photo on her phone, the one they'd taken of Çelebi's map in the underground workshop. The fairy chimneys matched exactly: three towers rising from the valley floor like ancient guardians, their distinctive caps eroded into shapes that might have been random but weren't.
"That's it," she confirmed.
They pressed on, descending into the valley proper as the sun climbed higher. By the time they reached the valley floor, the eastern rim was almost fully lit, throwing long shadows across the ground.
Three fairy chimneys rose ahead, big as lighthouses, their points like dark hats tipped against the sky.
