"From the base," Karim said, already lifting his phone for a photo. "It resolves to an eight-pointed star. It has to."
"Wait," Aya said.
Her eyes tracked the slopes, the scatter of loose stones, the line where shadow gave way to light. The geometry they'd seen below ground, the Pleiades points and Orion pivot, had sketched a pattern into her head and refused to leave.
Amina stood still, eyes half-closed, as if listening for something that wasn't sound. The blue fire in her arm blazed now, visible even in the hint of daylight. She tilted like a compass trying to find north, and Aya fought the urge to wrap her arms around her for support.
"Here," Amina said softly, eyes still closed. Her voice was strained, barely controlled. "It's here. Right here. The pull is almost unbearable now." She opened her eyes, and they were glassy with pain. "Like the marks are trying to tear free. Like something is trying to claw its way out of my skin."
As they gathered around, the sun imperceptibly raised another degree. At that precise angle, a beam slid through an oval in the rock flank and painted a long rectangle on the ground below. The edges were too clean for an accident. The color of the dirt inside that bar of light ran just a shade warmer than the soil around it, and when Aya dropped a length of paracord with a carabiner as a plumb, the line cut the rectangle near the perfect center.
They didn't cheer. They knelt.
The top layer was a crust, easy to peel back with flat shards and a length of juniper root levered like a trowel. Under that, the soil came away. Concealment, not defense. Karim brushed carefully, fingertips and breath, revealing a ring of fitted stone as fine as anything in a museum.
"Every piece of this is surreal. I can't believe it," Karim said.
"Yep, still real history out there, doctor," Leyla said, wiping away the last of the debris.
A circular hatch lay beneath their hands, its face carved with astronomical marks so dense they seemed to drink the sun and hold it. Amina's blue marks, burning like smoldering coals, picked up the beam and answered with their own fire. She hissed, sharp and involuntary, more pain than surprise.
"No hinges," Karim said, following the edge with a knuckle. "No pry marks either."
Aya set her palm over the central sigil, depicting the eye with radiating lines that had followed them from the first time she'd seen it in her father's notebook.
It was as cold as river water. Nothing moved.
"Try the face, not the edge," Amina said, a little breathless. "It's a check. Not a lock."
Aya pressed again and held, feeling the grain of the carving. The rings on the stone rotated slowly, planets along a track, constellation marks sliding into alignment with their current sky. No clicks, no theatrics. Something inside acknowledged the position and slowly gave way.
As if on hydraulics, the hatch lifted weightlessly, revealing more darkness below, with only a spiral of handholds cut into the shaft.
"Down we go," Aya said, switching on her phone's light.
Chapter 24
They descended in the thin cones of light from Aya's phone, Karim's headlamp, Amina's small torch, and Leyla's phone. The stone absorbed the beams as the air grew thick, damp. Somewhere lower, water ticked like a metronome counting down their descent.
Amina's labored breathing echoed in the shaft above them. With each rung down, Aya heard her struggle, heard the small sounds of pain she was trying to suppress.
Whatever was about to happen, they were close now.
Very close.
The shaft opened into an antechamber barely large enough for all five of them to stand. Aya swept her phone's light across the space. The room was carved with precision, walls meeting floor at perfect ninety-degree angles. Drainage channels ran along the base of each wall. The air smelled clean, dry, impossibly fresh for a space that hadn't been opened in centuries.
Aya pushed through into the main chamber and stopped, her breath catching.
The workshop was circular, roughly fifteen meters across, with a domed ceiling that disappeared into shadow above their lights. The space felt less like a room and more like the inside of an enormous mechanism, every surface and element placed with calculated purpose. Workbenches lined the perimeter in a perfect circle, each station dedicated to a different stage of the lens-making process. Grinding wheels, polishing stations, calibration frames, all rendered in brass and stone and glass that caught their lights and threw back fractured rainbows.
"My god," Karim said through a gasp.
Aya moved deeper into the room, cataloging every detail. On one bench, optical instruments of staggering complexity: astrolabes with moving parts so fine they must have taken years to craft, measuring tools that looked more like jewelry than scientific equipment. On another, glass vessels of different shapes and sizes, each labeled in script with what she guessed were chemical formulas.
Manuscripts filled specially carved niches in the walls, protected in sealed leather that had hopefully kept air and moisture at bay for four centuries.
"Look at this," Karim said, his fingers hovering over a set of glass molds on one workbench. "These are negative crescents for grinding curved lenses." He shook his head in wonder. "This shouldn't exist. This level of optical engineering shouldn't have been possible without a computer."
But it was possible, Aya thought. Çelebi had figured it out, working in secret beneath the very institutions that would have executed him for heresy if they'd known. And he'd built this place to preserve that knowledge, to pass it on to whoever was meant to find it.
The room itself was a work of art, Aya realized. Not just the instruments it contained, but the space itself. It was the workshop of a genius who'd been four centuries ahead of his time.
Hamza positioned himself at the entrance, one weathered hand pressed against the stone frame. "I'll keep watch," he called. "Take what you need, but quickly. They won't be far behind."
For a moment, his silhouette seemed to merge with the rock itself, as if he'd been carved there centuries ago alongside Çelebi's secrets.
"My grandfather told me something," he said, not turning around, his voice carrying with resonance in the chamber. "About guardianship. He said the Silent Meridian had a rule: when the choice comes between protecting the knowledge or protecting yourself, you choose the knowledge. Always. Because guardians are replaceable. What we guard is not."
Aya felt coldness spread through her chest. "Hamza—"
"We're not planning on dying today," Karim said, voice falling flat in the stone chamber.
Hamza smiled, but it didn't reach his eyes. "No one ever does. But my nephew just radioed. Aegis vehicles are approaching from three directions with at least fifteen operatives. They're not coming to negotiate." He looked at each of them in turn. "So I'm asking: when the choice comes, will you choose as Hassan did? Or will you hesitate?"
Before anyone could answer, Mehmet's voice crackled over Hamza's radio, urgent and rasping: "Uncle, they're at the perimeter. Two minutes, maybe less."
Aya nodded, trying to pick up her pace. Every minute they spent here was a minute Aegis could use to close the gap. They needed to document, to understand, to take what they could, and then they needed to disappear before Malik's forces found this place, too.
But it was the center of the room that drew her attention most insistently.
At the heart of the chamber sat a platform of polished obsidian, its black surface inlaid with silver lines that formed an intricate geometric pattern. The design reminded her of the platform they'd seen, but more complex, more deliberate. Multiple cradles and slots had been carved into the surface at precise intervals, each one clearly designed to hold something specific.
"This is it," Leyla said, approaching the platform with careful steps. "The assembly point. Where all the components would have been tested together, a trial before bringing them out into the real night sky."
Aya pulled the glass fragment from her pack. Near the platform, it blazed with intense blue light. She moved to the obsidian surface, examining the slots, and found one that matched the fragment's dimensions perfectly.
"Here," she said, sliding the fragment into place.
The moment it seated, the fragment's glow intensified. The silver inlays in the platform beneath it began to illuminate, tracing pathways from the fragment outward in spreading branches of light. But the light stopped after only a meter, fading at points where other slots remained empty.
"Karim, the cylinder," Aya said.
He pulled the brass piece from his bag and examined the platform. Another slot, this one shaped for its dimensions. He placed it carefully, and the process was repeated. Light spread from it, tracing silver pathways, but again stopping at empty slots.
The platform hummed beneath their feet, a low vibration that Aya felt in her bones. But it was incomplete. Waiting.
"The glass fragment and cylinder fit perfectly, but the platform still needs more," Karim said, studying the empty slots around the perimeter. "Based on Hassan's component list, I'd guess those empty spaces are for the calibration rings, the focusing array, and the crystal heart. This platform isn't the lens itself; it's the key to unlocking the next cache where Çelebi hid more components."
Behind Aya, Amina shrieked.
Aya turned to see Amina standing at the platform's edge, frozen. The blue marks on Amina's arm blazed with the same intensity as the pieces they’d inserted in the platform assembly. And the platform was responding. The silver inlays nearest to Amina began to glow brighter, pulsing in rhythm with her marks.
"Amina?" Aya asked carefully.
She took a step back, but the platform's response only intensified. Blue light erupted from its surface, visible pulses that kept the rhythm of the marks on her skin. The silver inlays began to shift, rearranging themselves into new patterns. Into script.
"It's reacting to you," Leyla said, moving closer to read the emerging symbols. "It's... writing something."
The script continued to form, flowing across the obsidian surface like liquid silver, solidifying into language. Leyla knelt beside the platform, her fingers tracing the air above the symbols, translating as they appeared.
"'The touched must choose,'" she read aloud. Her voice grew quieter as she continued. "'The vessel must be purified. Only the willing sacrifice can unlock the guardian's gift.'"
The words hung in the air, heavy with implication.
Karim's eyes widened. "It's a security system," he said slowly. "Çelebi designed it to only open for someone who's touched this supernatural power. Only one of his followers that he’d already shown and worked with. So if anyone ever came back and accessed his work without him present, this would guarantee they’re one of his close confidants. It's—"
"A test of intent," Hamza finished from the doorway. "To prove you seek knowledge, not power. That you're worthy of what he's hidden here. That Çelebi trusts you. But he never accounted for someone coming with djinn energy; he only considered the energy from his own Astral Lens, but they appear to work the same."
Everyone turned to look at Amina.
She stood perfectly still, staring at the platform. The blue fire on her arm was so bright now that Aya could barely make out the individual constellations. Amina's face had gone pale.
"Amina," Aya said softly, taking a step toward her.
"I can feel it," Amina whispered. Her eyes never left the platform. "It's... calling to me. Pulling at the marks. At what's underneath them." She finally looked up, meeting Aya's eyes. "It wants to complete a transformation. To purify me of this curse."
"Purify how?" Karim asked.
Amina moved closer to the platform, drawn by something she couldn't resist. Her hand reached out, hovering over the obsidian surface. The moment her fingers came within inches of it, she cried out softly and jerked back.
"It showed me," she said, her voice trembling. "In that touch. It showed me what it wants to do." She cradled her marked arm against her chest. "The connection. The sensing ability. Everything the djinn gave me when they marked me in Morocco. It wants to burn it out. Extract it. Purify me back to what I was before."
The implications settled over the group.
"You'd lose it," Aya said. "The ability to sense the artifacts. To feel when they're near."
"All of it," Amina confirmed. She laughed, but there was no humor in it. "I'd be useless in this quest."
"You've never been useless," Leyla said firmly.
But Amina wasn't listening. Her eyes had gone distant, calculating. "And the energy release. If this is anything like what happened in the star chamber, the pulse would be—"
"Massive," Karim finished. "They'd feel it across the entire valley. Maybe farther."
"Aegis would know exactly where we are," Amina said. "They'd be on us in minutes."
Aya watched her friend process it all, saw the understanding dawn in stages. This wasn't just about losing an ability. It was about becoming the reason Aegis found them. About sacrificing the one thing that had made her indispensable to their mission. About choosing between who she'd become and what they needed.
"There has to be another way," Aya said. "We can—"
"There isn't another way," Amina interrupted. Her voice was flat, resigned. "We all know it. This is Çelebi's final test. The ultimate security measure. You want his most dangerous knowledge? You prove you're worthy by giving up any dangerous power of your own."
She looked around the workshop, at the instruments that represented centuries of genius, at the slots in the platform still waiting to be filled. Then she looked at her arm, at the marks that had tortured her for months.
"I should want this," she said quietly. "I've spent every day since Morocco hating these glowing tattoos. Wishing they'd never touched me, that they'd just disappear." Her hand traced the glowing patterns. "But now that I might actually lose them..."
She trailed off, and Aya saw the conflict playing across her face. The admission she didn't want to make.
"They made you feel special," Aya said softly, understanding.
Amina nodded, not meeting her eyes. "I've spent my whole life being good at things. Being useful. Being the one who could do what others couldn't. And these marks?" She gestured at her arm. "They made me essential. Irreplaceable. You needed me because of what I could sense, what I could feel that none of you could, even if you all had moral high ground on me." Her voice cracked slightly. "Without them, I'm just... maybe not enough."
"That's bullshit," Karim said, surprising everyone with the force of it. "You've been essential to this team from the beginning. Your tactical skills, your combat training, your ability to read situations—none of that came from this curse that you’ve carried. That's all you."
"He's right," Leyla added. "The marks are a tool. But you're the one who knew how to use them. There's a difference."
Amina looked at Aya, searching for something. Confirmation. Permission. Absolution.
Aya moved to stand directly in front of her, taking both her hands. "You matter without them," she said, holding Amina's gaze. "You always have." She squeezed her hands gently. "But I also won't pretend this doesn't cost you something. Risk something. It does. And if you don't want to do this, we'll find another way. Even if it takes longer. Even if it's harder."
"There isn't another way," Amina repeated. She looked at the platform, then back at Aya. "And even if there was, we don't have time. Aegis is closing in. This is the mission. This is what we came for." She took a shaky breath. "But I need you all to know that I'm scared. Not of the pain, but of what comes after. Of who I'll be without this, what it might take from me in the process. Of whether I'll still matter."
"You'll always matter to all of us," Aya said, her voice firm over the fears she suppressed.
Amina held her gaze for a long moment, then nodded slowly. She pulled her hands from Aya's and turned to face the platform.
"Okay," she said, her voice steadier now. Decision made. "I'll do it."
Across the workshop, Karim had drifted toward an astrolabe. His hand hovered over its brass surface, but he wasn't really seeing it. He was watching Amina steel herself, watching her prepare to sacrifice something irreplaceable.
This was the discovery of a lifetime, he thought. This workshop, these instruments, the knowledge contained, should be preserved, studied, and shared with the world. He'd become an archaeologist to protect exactly this kind of history, but they were here to take what they needed and likely lose the rest to Aegis. Or destroy it themselves to keep it from becoming a weapon. His dreams of scholarly study and academic recognition seemed small and selfish next to what Amina was about to do.
She was giving up power. He was mourning knowledge. But maybe they were both learning the same lesson, that some things mattered more than what you wanted for yourself.
His eyes landed on a small brass wafer tucked beneath a folded note on a nearby shelf. Later. That decision could come later. Now, they needed to help Amina through what came next.
"How do I do this?" Amina asked, staring at the platform.
"The center," Leyla said, reading the continuing script. "You stand in the center, and it will do the rest. But you have to choose it. Consciously. It has to be willing."
Amina nodded. She rolled up her sleeve fully, exposing the marks from wrist to shoulder. They blazed in the dim workshop, beautiful and terrible.
She stepped onto the platform.
Chapter 25
The moment Amina’s feet touched the obsidian, the entire surface erupted with light. The silver inlays blazed white-hot, tracing pathways from the edges toward the center where Amina stood. The glass fragment and brass cylinder in their slots flared to blinding intensity. And beneath it all, the platform began to hum, a deep, resonant tone that seemed to whir as it echoed off the stone walls.
"I'm here," Aya said, moving to the platform's edge. "We're right here with you."
Amina looked at her and smiled, though her eyes were already tightening with pain. "Always the hero," she said. "When did that happen to us?"
"We grew up a bit," Aya said.
The platform's hum intensified, rising in pitch. The light beneath Amina's feet began to swirl, patterns forming and reforming, like the rotating earth. And then it began.
Amina's back arched, her mouth opening in a gasp that became a cry. "Oh god," she managed. "It's—"
Blue fire erupted from her arm, shooting upward in brilliant streams. 
For a moment, Aya wasn't in Cappadocia at all. She was back in the Atlas Mountains, watching blue fire pour from Amina's body as their guide Abdelatif stepped into the stream of djinn energy. She'd reached for him, and her fingers had closed on empty air as he was pulled toward the Amulet. The look on his weathered face: peaceful, resigned, knowing. She'd screamed his name as the artifact imploded, as crystal shards tore through him, as he fell. She'd pressed her fingers to his wrist and felt nothing. No pulse. Just silence and the mountain collapsing around them.
Not again. She couldn't lose someone else to these ancient powers, not Amina, not like this.
"We're right here with you,” Aya called.
Rather than manifesting as semi-corporeal djinn, the platform was drawing the energy from her marks like venom from a wound, transferring it downward through the obsidian.
"Talk to me," Aya urged, moving closer despite the heat radiating from the platform. "Tell me what you're feeling."
Amina's jaw locked, muscles cording in her neck as she fought to form words through gritted teeth. Her back arched violently, vertebrae cracking audibly as her spine bowed beyond natural limits. "Hooks—" The word came out strangled.
Her fingernails scraped against the obsidian, the keening screech of keratin on stone, and then they cracked, one after another, as she clawed for purchase against something only she could feel. Sweat poured down her face, mixing with tears, dripping onto the obsidian where it sizzled and evaporated instantly.
"Like hooks. Dragging through every vein. Every capillary." The blue fire intensified, no longer just flowing from her arm but erupting from it in violent bursts. Amina's forearm rippled, the skin moving as if something living writhed beneath. The constellations weren't just fading; they were moving, migrating down her arm toward her wrist like a time-lapse of a night sky, gathering at the point of extraction.
A sound finally tore from her throat, a raw growl that raised every hair on Aya's arms. Amina's free hand shot out, fingers digging into her marked arm hard enough to leave crescent-moon indentations, trying to hold something in that was determined to get out.
"And it doesn't—" Her words fractured mid-sentence as her whole body convulsed again. Her shoulders jerked forward, then back, puppet-strings cut and retied in rapid succession. "It doesn't want to go."
The blue streams of energy flowing from her arm intensified. The obsidian was drinking them in, channeling them toward compartments that were beginning to open around the platform's perimeter. Their guesses were right. To access the next piece of Çelebi's power, power had to be paid.
"Keep talking," Aya urged. "Stay with me."
"It's like—" Amina's voice broke as another convulsion shook her. "Like something being extracted through my skin. Layer by layer. The djinn presence, the connection, all of it." Tears streamed down her face, carving tracks through the sweat. "The noise in my head, the constant awareness that something else was there, it's going silent. I can feel it leaving."
Her legs buckled, and she dropped to her knees on the platform. The blue fire intensified, and now Aya could see that the marks themselves were changing. The vivid blue was fading, being replaced by silver-white scars that no longer glowed but still spread from her wrist upward like frost.
"In Morocco," Amina gasped, "when the djinn marked me, it was fire. Burning from the outside in." Her words came between ragged breaths. "This is something being torn out of me. This is—fuck—this is worse."
But even as she said it, something shifted in her expression. Beyond the pain, there was relief. Wonder. The constant buzz she'd lived with for months, the presence that had invaded her mind and body, was finally going silent.
The platform had channeled the extracted energy into the hidden compartments, and at the center, a crystalline element that caught the blue light and fractured it into a thousand colors.
The hidden compartments held their secrets no longer.
Two metal rings lay nested in the first alcove, each roughly the diameter of a dinner plate but impossibly light when Aya lifted them. As if they were hollow, though they rang solid when she tapped them. The metal itself was unlike anything she'd seen: not quite bronze, not quite gold, but something between, with a faint iridescence that shifted in the blue light. One ring bore engravings so fine they were almost invisible until the light caught them. The phases of the moon were traced in microscopic detail around its circumference, with notations in what looked like a cipher beneath each phase. The other ring showed planetary orbits, nested circles with gemstone markers for each wandering star, all emanating from a central sun symbol that had been inlaid with actual gold.
"These are calibration rings," Karim said, kneeling beside Aya to examine them. "Look. They're meant to rotate independently." He demonstrated without touching, tracing the air above where one ring could theoretically nest inside the other. "You'd align specific celestial positions to calibrate the focal point.”
The crystalline element sat in the central compartment, protected by layers of oiled silk that crumbled at Aya's touch. The crystal itself was flawless, roughly the size of her fist, multifaceted with clearly deliberate cuts, each angle calculated to refract light in specific ways. When she lifted it, it caught the dying blue glow from the platform and exploded it into a spectrum that painted the workshop walls in rainbow fire. But more than that, it hummed. A vibration so subtle she felt it in her teeth more than heard it, a frequency that resonated with something deep in her chest.
"The focusing heart," Karim breathed. "This would sit at the lens assembly's center. All the collected energy would pass through here before—" He stopped, realizing the implication. "Before channeling into whatever serves as the conduit. I think that’s the last piece we’re missing."
They uncovered a scroll next, rolled tight and sealed with wax that bore Çelebi's personal mark, the eye within radiating lines. 
Leyla carefully broke the seal, unrolling just enough to glimpse its contents: detailed assembly diagrams showing how each component they'd found nested together, plus notations for pieces they hadn't yet recovered. Material specifications were listed in three languages. And in the margins, warnings. The same phrase repeated throughout: "Only when the stars decree. Never before. Never after."
The marks on Amina's arm completed their transformation, the last traces of blue fading to permanent silver-white. The patterns remained, but they were inert. Dead. Beautiful but powerless.
Amina cried out one final time, not quite a scream but a moan. Her whole body shook with the force. And then, suddenly, it was over.
The blue fire cut off abruptly. The platform's hum dropped to a low vibration, then silence. The blinding light faded to a soft glow.
And then came the pulse.
One moment, the platform was merely glowing; the next, every hair on Aya's body stood straight up, reaching toward something building in the air above them. A pressure formed behind her eyes, in her sinuses, in her teeth—an electromagnetic potential looking for ground.
The taste of copper flooded her mouth, sharp and metallic like the air before a lightning strike. The workshop's ambient light took on a strange quality, shadows bending at inconsistent angles, as if the physical space itself was warping around the gathering energy.
Then the platform's hum shifted. Not louder, but deeper, dropping below the threshold of hearing into something felt in bone and marrow. The obsidian beneath Amina began to glow from within, not surface illumination but internal fire rising from impossible depths as its silver inlays went white-hot, blazing afterimages into Aya's vision.
Another pulse erupted outward in a sphere of blue-white incandescence, a shock wave of pure energy. Aya felt it move through her. Through skin, through muscle, through bone, a current that made every nerve fire at once. Her vision whited out. Her lungs seized. For one impossible heartbeat, she could sense the mountain itself: the weight of stone above, the network of passages threading through rock, the ancient water channels and sealed chambers, all of it illuminated by the pulse as it expanded at the speed of light.
The workshop walls vibrated at a frequency that made her inner ear revolt. Dust jumped from every surface as the shock wave passed, creating a momentary fog of suspended particles that glowed in the aftermath. Glass vessels on the shelves rang out in harmony, a choir hitting a single perfect note before spontaneously shattering.
Another sound hit a second later with a pressure wave that made her ears pop painfully, and her vision swim. She staggered, throwing an arm out to catch herself against a workbench. Beside her, Karim dropped to one knee, hand pressed to his temple. Even Leyla, trained field operative that she was, reeled from the intensity.
Then it was gone, the energy dissipating into the rock, into the valley, into the sky above, radiating outward to any instrument sensitive enough to detect it. The mountain groaned around them, settling after being shaken to its foundations. Small stones pattered down from the ceiling joints. And in the sudden, ringing silence, they could all feel it: the certainty that they'd just announced their exact location to anyone who was listening.
"What have we done?" Hamza said from the doorway, his voice hollow with fear. "They'll have felt that across the entire valley. Maybe the entire region. The ones who are digging can track us here."
As if to confirm his words, a distant thud echoed from somewhere above them. Then another.
"We have minutes," Leyla said, already moving to gather the revealed components. "Maybe less."
But Aya's attention was on Amina.
She had collapsed onto the platform, gasping, her marked arm cradled against her chest. The silver-white patterns caught the remaining light, but they were lifeless now. Decorative. The living fire that had animated them for months was gone.
Aya knelt beside her. "Hey. Look at me."
Amina lifted her head, and Aya saw something she hadn't seen in months. Peace. Exhaustion and grief, yes, but underneath it all, peace.
"It's quiet," Amina whispered. Her voice was full of wonder and loss in equal measure. "God, it's fucking quiet. I forgot what this felt like." She touched her arm with her free hand, tracing the now-inert patterns. "No burning. No pulling. No sense of something else breathing behind me." She laughed, and it caught halfway between relief and sorrow.
"You did it," Karim said gently, standing nearby. "You got us through. Thank you."
Aya pulled the glass fragment from its slot in the platform and held it close to Amina, testing.
Nothing.
No flare of recognition. No response from the marks. No sense of connection. It was completely gone.
"I can't feel it anymore at all," Amina said, staring at the fragment in Aya's hand. "Any of it. The artifacts, the energy, the direction they were pulling. It's all just... silent." She looked up at Aya, and her eyes were wet. "I'm free. But I'm also—"
"You're you," Aya interrupted firmly. She pulled Amina into an embrace, holding her until the shaking stopped. "You're exactly who you've always been. The marks never defined that."
When she let go, Amina was steadier. Still overwhelmed, still processing, but whole.
"Well," Amina said, trying for her usual irreverence and almost succeeding, "that sucked. But at least I'm not glowing like a spotlight anymore."
Another thud from above, closer this time.
"They're coming," Hamza said urgently. "They followed the pulse. We need to move. Now."
Aya pulled the two metal rings from the platform's compartments, testing their weight. Impossibly light, impossibly precious. She turned to Karim, who was still cataloging the workshop's instruments, fighting the need to flee.
"Here," she said, pressing one of the calibration rings into his hands. "Keep this safe. If we get separated, at least the rings will be in different hands."
Karim wrapped it carefully in a cloth from his pack, his fingers lingering on the engravings. "If anything happens—"
"Nothing will happen," Aya interrupted, though they both knew it was a lie they needed to tell. “But just in case.”
She turned to Amina, who was still sitting on the platform's edge, exhausted but alert. Aya held out the crystalline element. "Can you carry this?"
Amina looked at it, at the facets that caught and fractured light, and something flickered across her face. Loss, maybe, or grief for the connection she could no longer feel. But she took it, wrapping it in her own cloth and securing it in the inner pocket of her jacket. "Even distribution," she said. "Harder to lose everything at once."
"Exactly," Aya said, keeping the glass fragment and the second calibration ring for herself, tucking them securely into separate exterior pockets.
Leyla rolled the charts tight, securing them with a leather strap before tucking them inside her jacket. "I’ll guard these with my life. These are critical," she said. "Assembly instructions. Material specifications. We lose these, we may as well lose everything."
Karim wrapped the brass cylinder in cloth, double-checking the seal before placing it into a pocket next to the fragment. His hands moved mechanically, efficiently, while his eyes remained fixed on the workshop around them.
Karim hesitated by a shelf where instruments lay in careful rows. His eyes landed on a small brass-colored wafer tucked beneath a folded note of impossibly intricate geometric assembly. The handwriting was Çelebi's own. He'd studied enough samples to recognize it instantly. Three lines, but he could only make out a reference to “stabilization.”
His hand moved before his mind caught up with what he was doing.
The wafer was roughly the size of a large coin and covered with engravings so fine they could only be read in bright light. Geometric patterns radiated from a central aperture, a hole precisely drilled through the wafer's heart. Around its edge, more of Çelebi's cipher, and beneath it, that note in the astronomer's own hand.
It appeared to be made of brass at first glance, but it was impossibly heavy and malleable in Karim’s hand. Constructed of a metal or material he’d never encountered, that the Earth had perhaps rarely encountered.
Karim tilted it toward his headlamp and translated from Turkish on his phone, squinting to make out the words:
"...primary assembly requires stabilization array. Without complete circuit, lens cannot maintain coherence beyond initial pulse. Catastrophic discharge inevitable if..."
The note cut off there, torn, or deliberately left incomplete.
He froze. This was a critical component, possibly the critical component that made the difference between controlled operation and catastrophic failure. The kind of piece that modern engineers would call a "single point of failure." Remove it, and the entire system could collapse. Or worse, explode.
And he knew he had to steal it.
The rationalization formed even as his fingers closed around the wafer: This is preservation. This is preventing a catastrophe. Their alliance was becoming increasingly unpredictable, and he was the only true historian with nothing to hide, no ulterior motive. He would have the final say on whether they used the lens or destroyed it. And if they had to destroy the lens, at least one piece could survive for study. I'm protecting knowledge, he thought.
But underneath the rationalization, a darker truth whispered: You're taking something they'll need. Something Amina just sacrificed herself to help you access. Are you no better than Malik? Do you truly not trust Aya? After everything?
The wafer was cool against his palm, surprisingly heavy for its size. The weight of guilt, he thought grimly. He glanced over his shoulder—Aya helping Amina, Leyla gathering charts, Hamza watching the entrance. No one looking his way. No one seeing.
The wafer slipped into his pocket. It sat there against his hip like a coal. The note's unfinished warning echoed in his mind: "Catastrophic discharge inevitable if..."
If what? If the stabilization array was incomplete? If pieces were missing?
Another impact from above shook the workshop, sending more stones cascading. No time to reconsider. No time to second-guess. The choice was made. He'd live with the consequences, whatever they were.
But the weight in his pocket felt less like preservation and more like theft.
Another blast shook, this one close enough that small stones rained from the ceiling.
"Now or never," Aya said. "We're out of time."
Down one wall, a panel carved with the Orion constellation hung a fraction looser than the rest. Aya touched the same stars she'd seen activated in the chamber before: Betelgeuse, Rigel, the belt stars in sequence, and the seam opened, obedient. The passage beyond ran narrow and sloped, the sort of artery built to move people quickly when there was no time for ceremony.
As they exited, Aya in the lead, the workshop's defense mechanisms activated behind them. Aya heard the moving parts even through the stone. Grinding wheels beginning to turn, shelves retracting into walls, documents being sealed away in hidden compartments. Without Hamza’s secrets, the room would protect itself, hopefully, and hide its most precious secrets, making things difficult for whoever came after.
But it wouldn't stop Aegis for long.
They ran in gasping silence, conserving breath, pushing through burning legs and aching lungs. The passage dropped twice in sudden slides that they had to control with braced legs and scraped palms, and rose once in a ladder of handholds that left Aya's fingers raw.
Then, ahead, a shift in the air.
"Junction," Hamza gasped. "Should be... just ahead."
The passage opened into a wider chamber where three tunnels branched in different directions. The air here moved with conviction, whipping in, sending Aya’s hair into a tangled swirl against her face.
They stumbled into the intersection, all of them breathing hard.
"Not much time for strategy," Aya said, smoothing her curls away from her face and pulling her hair into a ponytail. "They're going to be on top of us. We should split up and divide their forces for the best chance of success."
“Eastern shepherd passage points to Avanos,” Hamza said. “I’ll take Karim with me. I can navigate on instinct, and he can help me keep up the pace. You’ll each be fine on your own.”
"Western aqueducts push toward Nevşehir," Leyla added, eyes on the dark mouth of that passage. "I can get through a checkpoint on papers, no problem. And if they search me, I have the charts tucked where they won't think to look."
"Then we take south," Aya said. She looked at each of them in turn, knowing this might be the last time they saw each other for days. Weeks, if things went wrong. "Rendezvous in Bodrum. However, you can get there. We'll coordinate through Salah's secure channels."
The faint sound of wind carried down the southern passage, and with it a distant, rhythmic whooshing.
"Balloons," Amina said, catching the same sound. Her eyes brightened with possibility despite her exhaustion. "That's the propane burners. The dawn launches must be starting."
Aya listened more carefully. Amina was right. The distinctive roar of hot air balloon burners echoed faintly from somewhere above and south of their position. Rose Valley. The fairy chimneys, where tourist operators launched dozens of balloons every morning at sunrise.
"You're thinking what I'm thinking, habibi?" Amina asked, a familiar gleam of mischief cutting through her exhaustion.
Despite everything—the pain in her side, the weight of components in her pack, the sound of Aegis closing in—Aya grinned. "They'll be watching roads, airports, and bus stations. But balloon operators? Tourist flights over the valleys? Unlikely."
"It's brilliant," Karim said. "They launch from multiple sites, all within a few kilometers of here. Dozens of balloons in the air at once. Extremely difficult to guess and track which one you're in, even if they guess what you've done."
"And they cover distance fast," Amina added. “Twenty, thirty kilometers in a flight. Land somewhere remote, find ground transport from there."
"Most operators have launch sites in Rose Valley," Leyla confirmed. "If the southern cisterns connect to that area—"
"They do," Hamza interjected. "The water systems all drain toward the valley floor. You'll emerge somewhere near the launch fields."
From the passage they'd left, voices grew distinct. Turkish commands. The scrape of boots on stone. They were out of time.
Aya made the decision. "We take the southern route. Find a balloon crew, pay them for emergency transport. Or improvise if we have to."
"You mean hijack," Amina said with a half-smile.
"I mean improvise," Aya replied through her own rueful smile. "We're still good guys now, but we may have to blur a few lines." She gestured to the pack on her shoulder. "We're carrying the hardware. The lens components. If Aegis catches us here, everything's lost—including what Amina sacrificed to get us. The balloons get us out fast and clean."
Karim checked his phone. "Dawn launches usually start around 5:30. It's 5:17 now. They'll be in the air or about to launch."
"Then we move fast," Aya said.
Aya looked at the others one at a time. They were scattering like seeds on the wind, trusting that they'd all find their way to Bodrum and the coast.
Karim's hand found Aya's shoulder, and she covered it once with her own, interlocking their fingers, before letting go. The gesture said everything words couldn't: be careful, come back, we're not finished yet.
Amina pressed her fist against Leyla's. "Stay sharp."
"Always," Leyla replied. Then, softer: "Thank you. For what you did in there. For all of us."
Amina nodded, unable to speak past the emotion in her throat.
Hamza lifted his chin at Aya. Their eyes met, and in that weathered face she saw trust, belief, and something like pride. "The guardianship is yours now," he said quietly. "All of it. My family's burden, Hassan's legacy, Çelebi's secrets. You carry them forward."
"I won't let you down," Aya promised.
"I know."
Behind them, the sound of pursuit grew unmistakable. Flashlight beams swept the distant passage. Voices called out.
"Go," Aya said. "Now."
They didn't argue, didn't hesitate.
Chapter 26
The separation point felt like a physical severing. Aya watched Karim's flashlight bob away into the eastern passage, Hamza in front of him, their silhouettes shrinking until it was swallowed by the curve of ancient stone. Leyla's form disappeared into darkness within the western tunnel. Then there was only the sound of Amina's breathing beside her, the weight of the components they carried, and the knowledge that any of them could be walking toward capture or death.
"South," Aya said. "Stay close."
“You got it. Lead the way, girl.”
The southern cistern route proved mercifully short compared to the labyrinthine passages they'd navigated earlier. The air felt more connected to the outside world, carrying hints of the surface. Morning dampness, the smell of earth warming in early sunlight, traces of thyme from the scrubland above. They moved quickly through passages that showed signs of regular water flow, limestone worn smooth by draining water, and channels cut deep into the floor by patient erosion.
After a frantic sprint through narrowing passages, they encountered a vertical shaft where handholds had been carved into the rock at regular intervals, ascending toward a circle of pre-dawn gray far above. Morning light, faint but unmistakable, filtered down through what appeared to be one of the unmistakable fairy chimneys themselves.
"Up," Aya said, testing the first handhold. The stone was cold and slightly damp, but the holds themselves were deep and secure, cut by hands that understood what failure meant.
They climbed in silence, muscles burning from the overnight exertion. The circle of light above grew larger, brighter, revealing itself as an opening disguised by scrub brush that had been carefully arranged to hide it from casual observation.
Aya emerged first, pushing through the brush, and stopped with one leg still in the shaft.
They were standing inside one of the fairy chimneys. The morning light filtered through the opening above, illuminating walls that curved around them like the interior of a stone cathedral. The volcanic rock had been hollowed out centuries ago, hand-carved into a shelter or storehouse, and the erosion since had only made it more surreal. The walls bore the scraping marks of ancient tools alongside the smooth organic sculpture of wind and water, human intention, and geological time layered into the same surface.
Aya tilted her head back, following the curve of stone upward to where it opened to the sky. The opening framed a perfect circle of dawn, pale blue shot through with gold, a piece of heaven caught in rock. It felt less like emerging from underground and more like being born from the mountain itself.
"How many people over the centuries have used this exact escape route?" Amina asked as she pulled herself out beside Aya, brushing dirt from her jacket. She turned slowly, taking in the carved walls, the absurd beauty of their new hiding place. 
"And how many of them," Aya said, stepping fully out onto the sandy soil, "came out to this?"
She gestured toward the landscape spread before them, and Amina's words died.
The valley was alive with balloons.
Dozens of them dotted the sky at various stages of flight. Some were still tethered to the ground, being inflated, their envelopes fat and colorful against the pale dawn; others were already airborne, drifting at varying altitudes like massive jellyfish in an atmospheric ocean. The distinctive roar of their burners echoed across the valley, a rhythmic thunder that punctuated the otherwise quiet morning.
Amina emerged beside Aya, brushing dirt from her clothes, then stopped mid-motion as she took in the spectacle. "My God," she breathed. "It's—"
"Perfect cover," Aya finished, already scanning the launch area. The nearest operations were just over two hundred meters away across relatively flat ground dotted with scrub and the occasional fairy chimney. Most were large commercial outfits with ground crews managing balloons with baskets large enough for sixteen passengers and long lines of tourists waiting to take in the majestic view from the skies.
But off to the left, slightly separated from the main cluster, sat an almost isolated balloon. The company logo was emblazoned across the balloon and basket in loud green and orange text: "Kapadokya Dream Flights Premium Tours." Aya recognized the company from a series of billboards leading into Göreme. 
The basket could hold eight passengers easily, but it appeared all their other balloons had launched for the day already, so the remaining ground crew consisted of only one young woman managing the inflation fan and an older man checking the propane connections.
"That one," Aya said, pointing. "Come on."
She and Amina moved quickly across the open ground, staying low and using the scattered rock formations as cover where possible. The sound of balloons launching provided audio camouflage, the burners' roar drowning out the sound of their approach.
The woman looked up first as they approached, her expression shifting from surprise to alarm as she took them in. Disheveled, dirty, exhausted, desperate.
"Merhaba," Aya called. "I need you both to step away from the balloon."
The older man straightened immediately, his weathered face hardening. "No. Absolutely not." His hand moved toward the radio clipped to his belt. "Mehmet! We have a situation at—"
Aya pulled the gun from her waistband, not pointing it at them but letting them see it, letting them understand. The man stopped speaking immediately.
"Please don't make me do this. We're not going to hurt you, but we need your balloon."
"You're insane," the woman called out, her voice rising. She planted herself more firmly beside the basket, as if her body could protect it. "Do you know what this thing costs?"
"I can guess," Aya said. The weight of what they were doing settled heavily with each word. "And I'm sorry. We wouldn't be doing this if there was any other way."
The man's hand was still hovering near his radio. "There are controls everywhere, and tons of police around this morning. You'll never get away with this, even if you do know how to fly."
"That's exactly why we need to leave now," Amina said, moving forward with her own handgun visible. Her voice was harder than Aya's, more willing to play the role of threat. "The keys to the equipment trailer. Now."
"This is my job," the woman said. "Our whole—"
"Look, I understand," Aya interrupted, and she meant it. The gun felt wrong in her hand, heavier than it should be, carrying weight that had nothing to do with its physical mass. This was someone innocent whose life she was disrupting. "But right now, this second, people will die if we don't get out of this valley. Not might die but will die. That's not hyperbole."
The man studied her face, searching for the lie. Whatever he saw there—desperation, sincerity, the exhaustion that came from making impossible choices—made his shoulders sag slightly.
"You'll crash it," he said finally, quieter. "You don't know how to pilot a balloon."
"I've done it before," Aya said. It wasn't entirely a lie. She'd watched, she'd learned, she understood the principles. "I'll bring it down safely. I'll make sure you get it back."
The man fumbled with the keyring on his belt, his hands shaking with anger and fear. Amina took it gently, then gestured toward the equipment trailer. "Inside. Both of you. Sit down, stay quiet for twenty minutes, and you'll be fine. You'll walk away from this without a scratch."
"The company will sue you," the man said even as he complied, moving toward the trailer with his daughter beside him. "Full charges. Theft, assault with a weapon, endangering commercial aviation—"
"We'll take our chances," Amina said.
"Please," Aya added, softer, as the woman paused at the trailer door. "We'll do everything we can to return it safely. And when this is over, when you see the news, you'll understand why we had to do this."
“Oh, come on, this company has insurance,” Amina said. “It’s not like these two own the balloon.”
Aya shrugged. “We’ll do our best to get it back, but we need to take your company’s balloon now, and we need you to cooperate. Lives are on the line.”
"That's not how this works," the woman said, her voice tight with fear and anger.
"I know," Aya said quietly before exiting. "And I am sorry. It’s an emergency."
Amina secured the trailer door from outside with a length of rope, leaving it loose enough to ensure they'd be found easily once the launches were completed, but giving enough time for escape.
"Okay, let’s hope I can pull this off," Aya said to Amina as they moved to the balloon.
The man had nearly completed the inflation process. The envelope lay on its side, about sixty percent full, the fan still running to force cold air into the opening. The burner assembly was mounted above the wicker basket, which lay tipped on its side, attached to the envelope by steel cables.
"You've done this before?" Amina asked, studying the complex array of equipment with wide-eyed skepticism.
"Sort of," Aya said. "Once in Namibia on a heist that required aerial surveillance for three days before I moved on the target. I spent enough time with the pilot to understand the basics, and I’m naturally curious. I rode a balloon with my parents in New Mexico when I was a kid, too. Had a book about balloons from the library." She checked the burner. "The principles are pretty straightforward. Hot air rises, cold air falls. We control the altitude, not the direction. The wind controls the rest."
"That's reassuring. You know how much I love giving up control."
"We still have some, and it's going to have to be enough."
Aya checked the propane tanks—four of them mounted on the basket's frame, gauges showing roughly three-quarters full. Plenty for a flight of ninety minutes, maybe two hours if they were conservative. She examined the burner controls, finding the pilot light already ignited, a small blue flame waiting to be fed.
Around them, other balloons continued their launch sequences, ground crews shouting instructions, passengers climbing into baskets, cameras flashing as tourists captured the moment. No one was paying attention to their small operation on the edge of the launch area.
"Help me with this," Aya said, gripping one side of the basket. Together they heaved it upright, the wicker frame surprisingly heavy. The basket settled onto the ground with a solid thump, the burner assembly now positioned correctly above them.
Aya grabbed the blast valve, a simple lever connected to the propane feed, and pulled it down.
The burner roared to life, twin jets of flame shooting upward into the envelope's throat. The heat was instant and intense, a wall of thermal energy that made Aya's face feel sunburned in seconds. She held the blast for a full ten seconds, watching the balloon begin to stir and lift as hot air flooded into it.
"The ground crew usually holds it," Aya explained, releasing the valve. "But we don't have that luxury. Get in the basket. When it starts to lift, we go immediately."
Amina climbed over the edge, settling into one corner of the wicker basket with her pack. Aya followed, positioning herself near the burner controls. The basket rocked slightly as they moved, the envelope above beginning to rise, its colorful panels, red and gold in a diamond pattern, catching the strengthening dawn light.
Another blast of the burner. The envelope rose further, taking shape, the fabric going taut as pressure built inside. The basket shifted, lifted an inch off the ground, then settled back down.
Aya hit the burner again, holding it longer this time. The roar drowned out the distant shouting of the man and woman they’d locked inside their workroom. The balloon filled, becoming a perfect teardrop of heated air straining against the cables that connected it to the basket.
Chapter 27
The basket left the ground with a gentle, almost imperceptible lifting, like the earth deciding it no longer wanted to hold them. One moment they were on solid ground, the next they were six inches up, then a foot, then three feet, rising with a stately inevitability.
"Well, we're flying," Amina said, her voice caught between fear and wonder. “Good job so far, habibi.”
Aya blasted the burner again, adding heat, increasing their rate of ascent. The ground fell away beneath them with increasing speed. Ten feet, twenty, fifty. 
Members of other grounds crews were running toward them now, shouting and gesticulating, but too late, far too late.
Other balloons hung in the air around them at various altitudes, creating a scattered fleet of colorful orbs. Aya guided them into the middle of the cluster, using the other balloons as visual camouflage. From a distance, from the ground, they'd be just another tourist flight to Aegis operatives, indistinguishable from the dozens of others taking to the air for the dawn show.
Aya settled into the unique sensation of flight with no engine vibration, no wings to bank or tilt, no sense of speed. It was just a profound floating, as if gravity had simply forgotten them. The basket swayed gently, responding to air currents, the wicker creaking softly. Between burner blasts, the silence was absolute and startling.
Beneath them, Rose Valley spread in all its surreal alien beauty, as strange as anything Dali could dream up. Those distinctive cone-shaped rock formations that gave Cappadocia its otherworldly appearance stretched in every direction like a petrified forest. Erosion had carved the soft volcanic tuff into fantastic shapes: spires and pyramids, mushrooms and towers, each one unique, each one impossible.
Then the sun fully broke over the eastern horizon, and the world ignited.
Golden light washed across the valley, painting the pale stone in shades of honey and amber and rose. Shadows stretched long and dramatic, filling the spaces between formations, making the already complex topography into a three-dimensional maze of light and dark. The rock itself seemed to glow, the iron oxides in the tuff catching the dawn and reflecting it in warm tones.
"It's beautiful," Amina said softly, her hand gripping the basket's edge, knuckles white despite the wonder in her voice.
Around them, other balloons drifted at different altitudes. A red one above and to the left, its shadow passing over them. A small blue and white striped balloon was descending below them, beside a massive commercial balloon with sixteen passengers in its basket, all of them pointing cameras outward, capturing the dramatic scenery. The balloons moved independently, each one responding to slightly different wind currents, creating a slowly shifting aerial ballet.
Aya blasted the burner again, adjusting their altitude to match the larger balloons nearby. The heat from the flames was intense against her upturned face, a pocket of summer in the cool morning air. Between blasts, the temperature dropped noticeably, the altitude stealing warmth from the air.
They rose above the immediate valley next, giving them a panoramic view of the region. To the north, the town of Göreme spread across the landscape, its buildings nestled among more fairy chimneys, cave dwellings carved directly into the rock faces visible even from their height. To the south, Love Valley stretched with its distinctly phallic rock formations, morning light making them almost glow. To the east, Pigeon Valley twisted through the terrain, named for the thousands of pigeon houses carved into its cliffs by farmers who'd harvested the bird droppings for fertilizer.
The scale of it all was overwhelming. From ground level, the fairy chimneys were impressive. From above, the full scope of the geological phenomenon revealed itself. Thousands of formations covered hundreds of square kilometers. Centuries of wind and water had sculpted the landscape into something that belonged more to dreams than geology.
Aya checked the altimeter mounted on the basket's frame. They were at eight hundred meters now, higher than most of the other balloons, high enough that individual people on the ground were invisible, reduced to abstract dots moving through the landscape.
The wind at this altitude came from the southeast, gentle but persistent, pushing them toward the northwest. No way to steer, no way to change direction, they could only control whether they went up or down, and hope the wind at different altitudes might take them where they needed to go.
A radio mounted on the basket's frame crackled to life: "Dream Flights balloon, this is flight control. You are operating without clearance. We don’t have you scheduled to go up today. Identify yourself or return to the launch site immediately."
Aya ignored it without comment, reaching over to turn the volume down. They'd be tracked now, their position noted, but in the chaos of dozens of balloons in the air simultaneously, it would take time to coordinate any meaningful response.
Between burner blasts, the silence returned, profound and peaceful, broken only by the occasional creak of wicker, the whisper of wind against the balloon, the distant roar of other pilots firing their burners. 
As the minutes drifted by, on top of the world, it was easy to forget that they were fugitives carrying stolen artifacts, that armed operatives were hunting them, that they'd commandeered this balloon at gunpoint. Easy to just exist in the moment, suspended between earth and sky, drifting through one of the most beautiful landscapes Aya had ever seen.
For the first time in hours, they had a moment to breathe. No tunnels pressing in, no footsteps echoing behind them, no immediate threat demanding action. Just wind and silence and the strange peace of floating. Amina stared at her arm, at the silver-white marks that had replaced the burning blue. She traced one of the constellations with her finger. Her other hand gripped the basket's edge so tightly her knuckles had gone white, and Aya knew it wasn't fear of heights, it was something else.
"In Egypt," Amina said suddenly, her voice quiet enough that Aya almost missed it under the wind, "I left you because I was scared."
Aya's hand tightened on the burner control. She kept her eyes fixed on the horizon, on the distant mountains, anywhere but on Amina's face. "We've been over this for what feels like the hundredth time now."
"Not scared of them." Amina continued undeterred, turning so her back was against the basket's rim, so she was facing Aya fully, even though Aya refused to meet her eyes. "Of needing you. Of being that vulnerable. I realized that I wanted to abandon the job and save you, and that thought terrified me, so I did the opposite, and I’ve never stopped regretting it."
The words hung in the thinning air between them. Aya fired the burner, using the roar as an excuse not to respond immediately. Ten seconds of flame, ten seconds of deafening noise, her face tilted up toward the heat, away from Amina. When she released the valve, the silence felt even heavier than before.
"I ran because it was easier than staying," Amina continued. Her fingers traced the silver-white marks on her arm, a nervous gesture Aya recognized from a hundred previous conversations. "And I told myself it was tactical. That I was making the smart choice, getting clear before things collapsed completely."
Aya finally looked at Amina, and immediately regretted it. Amina's face was open in a way she rarely allowed, stripped of the usual armor of sarcasm and confidence. Vulnerable. Real.
"But the truth is," Amina said, her voice dropping even quieter, "I was terrified of how much I needed you. How completely you'd gotten under my skin." She laughed, but there was no humor in it. "At the idea that you might change me, that you were changing me, when I'd worked so hard my entire life to be exactly who I needed to be to survive."
Aya turned away again, studying the complex array of propane gauges, the altimeter, anything that wasn't Amina's face. Her chest felt tight, her breathing shallow.
"And now," Amina continued, and Aya could hear her shifting closer, could feel the warmth of her presence in the confined space of the basket, "I'm terrified for different reasons."
Aya’s body tensed involuntarily. "Don't."
"Because you're with someone who deserves you," Amina pressed on, ignoring the warning. "Someone who could give you the life you say you wanted. Stable, academic, building something that lasts." A pause, heavy with everything unsaid. "And I can't compete with that. I'm rougher around the edges. I'm the bad choice, the one to walk away from. But I know that I was wrong; I don’t need to be that person anymore. You were changing me, allowing me to grow and evolve beyond that hardened personality I created to survive."
"That's not fair," Aya said, her tone harsher than intended. She whirled to face Amina and found her closer than expected, close enough to see the flecks of gold in her dark eyes. "You can't just—"
"Love isn't fair," Amina interrupted, and her hand found the basket's rim near Aya's, not quite touching but close enough that Aya could feel the heat radiating from her skin. Close enough that moving an inch would bridge the gap. "But I'm still here. Still hoping you'll choose the life you actually live instead of the one you think you should want."
"What does that mean?"
Amina's eyes held hers with uncomfortable intensity, refusing to look away, refusing to let Aya escape. "The adrenaline. The danger. The never knowing what's coming next." She gestured around them. The stolen balloon, the impossible escape, the components they carried that could reshape the world. "That's who you are, Aya. That's what gets you going, what turns you on. You're not some professor's quiet, stable girlfriend cataloging pottery shards in temperature-controlled archives."
"You don't know what I want," Aya said, but the words came out limp.
"Don't I?" Amina's fingers shifted, brushing against Aya's knuckles. The contact sent electricity up her arm. "You come alive when everything's going wrong. When you're three moves ahead of whoever's chasing you. When you're stealing balloons at gunpoint and flying them over Cappadocia at dawn." Her voice dropped, intimate and certain. "It's messier, but fuck if it's not more exciting.”
"What does that mean?"
“It's who you are. It's who we are."
The silence between them stretched, filled only by wind and the distant roar of other balloons' burners. Aya couldn't look away from Amina's face, from the challenge and the plea mixed.
They were suspended in more than just air. Aya knew they’d had many approaches to this conversation, but this was it. This was a moment that would define everything that came after. Every choice, every relationship, every version of herself she'd become. She could feel it crystallizing, time slowing to allow her to choose which direction gravity would pull her.
Karim's face flashed in her mind. Good, steady, deserving Karim, who'd never asked her to be anything other than what she was. Who'd supported her, trusted her, followed her into danger without complaint, fighting to protect his dream of what they could build together.
And here was Amina, who'd left her bleeding in Egypt, who'd returned full of apologies and buried feelings, who represented every unstable, dangerous, exhilarating thing Aya had been trying to convince herself she didn't need.
Amina's hand was still there, close enough to touch, waiting.
Aya contemplated an answer, a refusal, a question, something that would break the moment and return time to its normal flow.
Aya opened her mouth to argue, to defend Karim and the future they'd discussed, but her body betrayed her. Her hand, which had been gripping the basket rim as an anchor, relaxed. Her shoulders, which had been squared defensively, softened. Her breath, which she'd been holding like a shield, released in a long exhale that carried more truth than any words could.
Images flashed unbidden: Karim in his element at a dig site, brush in hand, explaining pottery fragments with infectious enthusiasm. The gentle way he'd held her after they escaped the tunnels. The future he'd sketched for them, full of quiet mornings, academic conferences, a life built on scholarship and safety.
It was a good life, one she’d felt drawn to. A life many people would kill for, one she’d fought for, longed for.
But it made her chest feel tight, her lungs unable to fully expand.
She thought of the past seventy-two hours: the star chamber's discovery, Amina's sacrifice on the platform, the pulse of ancient power, this stolen balloon ride over an alien landscape. Her pulse had been elevated for three days straight. She'd stolen, lied, threatened people with guns, destroyed property, and fled from authorities. And she felt more herself than she had in a long time.
Her hands started shaking from the physical weight of recognition. That moment when your body knows a truth your mind hasn't caught up to yet. The vertigo of standing on an edge and realizing you've already been falling.
"Aya?" Amina's voice was softer now, concerned.
Aya looked down at her trembling hands, then at the artifacts in her pack, then at the impossible landscape spreading below them. She'd spent the last six months telling herself she wanted stability. She had let the idea take place in her mind, fueled by guilt, fueled by guesses about her father’s opinions of her life. But he’d been on this quest, too, in his own way. She convinced herself that she was tired of the chase, ready to settle, prepared to become someone who built rather than took. 
But her body was telling a different story. It always had been.
The adrenaline didn't terrify her. It clarified her. Made her sharper, faster, more alive. The danger wasn't something to escape; it was the element she'd been designed to move through, like a fish returned to water.
And Amina had always known this about her. Had seen it when Aya herself was still pretending otherwise.
Her hands steadied. The shaking stopped. Not because the truth had changed, but because she'd finally stopped fighting against its weight. 
Then, before she could speak, before she could give voice to the recognition burning in her chest, movement in the distance caught her eye. 
A helicopter, black and sleek, was rising from the valley floor to the north. It banked toward the balloon cluster with unmistakable focus. 
"Shit," Aya said. "Did they find us?"
Chapter 28
Aya's hand moved instinctively toward the burner, ready to add altitude, then froze.
"Wait," Amina said, tracking the helicopter's approach. "If they know we're in a balloon, we're already dead. A helicopter can outrun us, outmaneuver us, force us down anywhere they want."
Aya's mind raced through scenarios. "So either they don't know we're here specifically..."
"Or they're searching all the balloons." Amina squinted at the approaching aircraft. "Look at the pattern. It's not coming straight for us. It's weaving between balloons at a fair distance, checking each one."
She was right. The helicopter moved methodically, approaching one balloon, hovering briefly as whoever was inside examined it, then moving to the next. Systematic. Thorough. Not the behavior of a team that had already identified their target.
"They saw the pulse," Aya said, understanding clicking into place. "They know we're somewhere in this valley, probably guess we might try to escape by air, given the balloon launches. But there are dozens of balloons up here."
Amina nodded. "And we all look the same from a distance."
The helicopter was ten balloons away, working its way through the cluster. Close enough that Aya could see it was a civilian model, not military, which likely meant there was limited time before authorities questioned what they were doing, harassing tourist flights. And that Aegis hadn’t diverted many resources to the air. They didn’t actually believe they were in a balloon.
"Options," Aya said, thinking aloud. "We can go up, try to hide in the higher balloons, hope they check those last."
"Or?"
"Or we go down. Fast. Get out of the search pattern entirely, land somewhere they're not expecting."
Amina studied the approaching helicopter, calculating. "If we go up, we're trapped up here with finite fuel. If we go down, we're on the ground where they have vehicles, ground teams."
"But on the ground, we can hide, move, disappear into the terrain. Up here, we're sitting ducks once they narrow the search. And someone could always find the two we locked up."
The helicopter checked another balloon. Nine away now. They were probably photographing each basket, trying to identify passengers, looking for anyone matching their descriptions, or Karim’s or Leyla’s.
"Your call," Amina said. "You're the pilot."
Aya watched the helicopter move to the next balloon in line. She thought about fuel consumption, about how long they could stay up, about the odds of hiding in plain sight versus the odds of disappearing on the ground.
"Down," she decided. "We go down now, fast, before they get close enough to photograph us clearly."
"Then let's move."
"Hold on," Aya said, reaching for the red vent line, the parachute valve at the top of the envelope that would dump hot air and kill their lift.
She pulled it gently at first, just enough to feel the balloon respond. The rate of descent increased, the altimeter needle swinging counterclockwise with increasing speed. 
Eight hundred meters. Seven-fifty. Seven hundred.
They dropped past a large commercial balloon, its massive envelope blocking the sun for a moment, casting them in shadow. Passengers in that basket pointed at them, some concerned, some curious, a few already filming with their phones. 
Aya tried to turn away from the cameras and kept pulling the vent line hard.
Six hundred meters. Five-fifty.
The helicopter's approaching sound changed pitch, rotors echoing off the rock formations differently as they descended into the valley. Aya risked a glance up. The aircraft was checking another balloon two positions away from where they'd been, still working through its methodical search pattern.
But they were falling fast now, dropping below the main cluster of balloons, though surely drawing a lot of attention. The other pilots were maintaining cruise altitude, giving their passengers the extended tour. Aya was doing the opposite, deliberately crash-diving toward the ground.
Five hundred meters. Four-fifty. The ground was no longer an abstract beauty but a rapidly approaching reality. Details emerging from the general landscape. Individual rock formations. Sparse vegetation. The texture of the earth itself.
"Aya, shit," Amina called over the roaring wind. "That's really fast."
"I know, sorry." Aya released the vent line and nudged the burner, trying to slow their descent. The flames roared, blasting heat into the envelope, but thermal inertia meant nothing happened immediately. The balloon needed time to respond, time to heat up, time they didn't have.
“Let’s not end up splattered on a rock. I’d like to hear what you were about to say to me before they crashed our romantic date in the sky.”
Four hundred meters. Three-fifty.
Aya fired the burner again, longer this time, feeling the heat sear her face and arms. The descent rate slowed slightly, but they were still falling faster than any controlled landing should be.
Three hundred meters. The terrain below was rougher than it had looked from altitude. Rocky outcrops, scattered boulders, patches of thorny scrub. Landing sites were sparse and getting sparser as the ground rushed up.
"There," Aya said, spotting a relatively flat area between two fairy chimney formations. The ground looked unforgiving, but it was the best option in their rapidly shrinking window of choice.
She aimed for it by controlling their descent rate, firing the burner in measured blasts, trying to time it so they'd arrive over the flat area with minimal forward velocity and manageable downward speed.
Two hundred meters. One-fifty. The wind shifted, catching them from the side, pushing them toward the larger of the two rock formations. Aya fired the burner desperately, trying to add enough heat to lift them over the obstacle, but they were too low, too heavy, too committed.
One hundred meters.
"We're going to hit those rocks!" Amina shouted.
Aya yanked the vent line fully open, dumping all the hot air she dared, willing the balloon to drop now rather than drift forward into the formation. The envelope collapsed partially, losing shape, and they fell like a stone.
Seventy meters. Fifty.
The flat area was beneath them now, but they were coming in too hot, with too much forward momentum. Aya fired the burner in one final, sustained blast, five seconds of flame, trying to arrest their fall.
Thirty meters. Twenty.
"Brace!" Aya screamed, desperately clutching the basket’s rails and leaning back.
The basket hit the ground and shot pain through Aya's legs and spine, the wicker frame flexing and cracking, but miraculously holding together. Her teeth clacked together hard, and she tasted a hint of blood as she heard the shriek of wicker against stone, Amina's sharp cry of pain, and the whoosh of the balloon beginning to settle.
But then the basket left the ground again, became airborne for one impossible second, rising three meters on residual lift and momentum before gravity reclaimed them.
The second impact was worse.
They came down at an angle and bounced, the basket tilting as it hit. Aya was thrown sideways, unable to hold on against the force, her shoulder slamming into the wicker wall. Amina crashed into her, and both of them tangled as the basket tipped further, threatening to spill them out entirely.
The balloon still had some lift and was dragging them forward across the rough ground. The basket scraped over rocks, tilted further, then caught on a boulder and overcorrected, slamming back down on its base with a jolt before continuing back in the opposite direction.
Scraping. Bouncing. Tilting. The world reduced to violent motion and the desperate need to hold on, to not be thrown out over the ledge, to survive the next second, and the next.
Desperately flailing, Aya's hand found the vent line again and pulled it with her body weight. 
The envelope deflated rapidly, the last hot air rushing out through the parachute valve, the fabric crumpling, losing its remaining structure.
They skidded across the ground, basket dragging through scrub that tore at the wicker, over stones that sent them bouncing and tilting. Aya saw the large rock outcrop ahead, gray stone rising from the earth like a wall, and knew if they hit it at this speed, the basket would shatter and them with it.
But the deflating envelope was killing their momentum, creating drag. They slowed. The outcrop loomed closer. Ten meters. Five.
The basket ground to a stop with one final bone-rattling impact, the wicker frame hiding against the rock with a hollow thump that echoed down into the valley.
Then silence. Complete, ringing silence after the violence of the landing.
Aya lay tangled with Amina in the bottom of the basket, both of them breathing hard, neither moving. Dust settled around them. The envelope draped over the basket like a shroud, still radiating heat, the fabric making small ticking sounds as it cooled.
"Guess we’re not dead?" Amina asked, her voice muffled against Aya's hair and shoulder.
"I don't think so."
Aya pushed herself up on trembling arms, her body cataloging damage: ribs screaming where she'd hit the basket edge, her stitched side throbbing with renewed fury, left shoulder potentially separated from the impact, dozens of smaller pains that would reveal themselves fully once the adrenaline wore off.
But Amina didn’t follow.
"Hey," Aya said, fear cutting through the pain fog. She rolled Amina onto her back, hands running over her quickly, checking for blood, for obvious breaks, for anything that meant immediate danger. "Amina. Look at me."
Amina's eyes opened, unfocused for a moment, then found Aya's face. "Ow."
"Where? Tell me where."
"Everywhere?" Amina tried to laugh, but it came out as a wheeze. "Mostly my head. Hit something. Having a hard time focusing."
Aya's hands found Amina's head, running her fingers gently through her hair for any telltale softness of a fracture or the wet heat of bleeding. Then she found it. A lump was forming on the back left, already the size of a walnut. But no blood, no give in the bone beneath.
"Concussion," Aya said. "Can you focus on my finger?"
She held up her index finger, moved it slowly left and right. Amina's eyes tracked it smoothly, pupils equal and reactive despite the pain evident in her expression.
"Good. That's good." Aya's hands moved lower, slowly checking Amina's body for pain or instability. Amina sucked in a sharp breath when Aya's fingers found the right side of her ribcage.
"Bruised, not broken," Aya assessed, pressing gently. "Does it hurt to breathe deeply?"
Amina took a careful breath, deeper than before. "A little, but it’s not sharp. Not like in Prague."
Aya smiled at the memory, but continued her assessment, hands moving over Amina’s body with an old, practiced familiarity. Hips, legs, ankles. Everything intact. Scratches and forming bruises everywhere, but nothing that wouldn't heal.
"You're going to be okay," Aya said, some of the tension leaving her voice. "Banged up, but okay."
"Your turn." Amina sat up slowly, grimacing. "And don't lie to me about how bad it is."
She rested her hand gently against Aya's side, where the stitches were, where the wound from Istanbul had reopened during their underground escape. Aya couldn't suppress the hiss of pain.
"Still bleeding?" Amina asked.
"Don't think so. Just angry about being thrown around."
Amina lifted Aya's shirt carefully, examining the wound through its bandage. The white gauze showed a small red bloom, fresh but not actively flowing. "Stitches held. That's something."
Her hands moved to Aya's shoulder, the one that had taken the brunt of the first impact. She probed it gently, rotated Aya's arm through its range of motion.
“Fuck,” Aya said through an exhale. "Separated?"
