"Don't think so. But close. You're going to have limited use of that arm for a while." Amina's fingers lingered on Aya's shoulder, her touch shifting from medical assessment to something softer, more concerned. "You're a disaster, habibi."
Aya laughed. "Says the woman who bruises easily and has a concussion. Your whole body will be black and purple by evening."
They looked at each other and started laughing. Everything hurt, but Aya couldn't stop shaking with laughter born from relief, residual adrenaline, and the sheer absurdity of stealing a hot air balloon and crash-landing it in the middle of Cappadocia.
Aya's ribs ached where she'd slammed into the basket edge, and her stitched side throbbed with renewed vigor, but nothing felt broken or torn. "We need to move. They'll spot the balloon from the air."
They climbed out of the basket on unsteady legs. The deflated balloon lay sprawled across the ground like a fallen parachute, dancing slightly in the breeze. Forty meters of red and gold fabric that might as well be a flare announcing their position.
"Behind those rocks," Amina said, pointing to a cluster of large formations thirty meters away. "If we can get it into shadow..."
They each grabbed a section of fabric and pulled. The rig was heavier than Aya expected, the material thick, and the sheer volume of it created drag as they hauled it across the rough ground. Her shoulder screamed with each pull, and she found herself drenched in sweat despite the cool air.
Halfway to the rocks, Aya stopped and glanced skyward. "Shit. We need to go faster," she panted. 
The helicopter was nearly overhead, still distant but growing louder.
They pulled harder, stumbling backward, the fabric catching on every rock and bush. 
They reached the shadow of the rock formation and shoved the envelope into a crevice between two large boulders, stuffing the fabric in desperately, Amina climbing partway up to push it deeper while Aya gathered armfuls and fed it to her. The bright colors disappeared into shadow, red and gold fading to dark shapes barely distinguishable from the stone.
The helicopter’s whir grew distinct enough to identify the direction, northeast, working its way through a search pattern that would bring it overhead within minutes.
The basket was still visible, sitting in full sun like an advertisement. They went back for it and grabbed the wicker frame, lighter than the mass of fabric but more awkward, and dragged it into the shadow, wedging it beside the fabric. Aya grabbed handfuls of scrub brush and scattered it over both, creating a hasty camouflage that wouldn't survive long but might fool a disinterested helicopter pilot scanning from altitude.
What had been a whir grew into a roar. Aya and Amina pressed themselves against the rock face as the aircraft passed directly overhead, low enough that Aya could feel the rotor wash, see the shadow slide across the ground.
It kept moving, the sound fading gradually, the search pattern continuing.
"That was lucky. We can't stay here," Amina said once the helicopter had moved on. "Too exposed. If they do a second pass and actually look at the ground..."
Aya surveyed the landscape, already exhausted, already hurting, but forcing herself to think tactically through the fog. They needed somewhere defensible, concealed, where they could rest without being spotted from the air or ground.
"That way," she said, pointing about half a kilometer away to what looked like a structure partially collapsed into the landscape. Not fairy chimneys, but human construction. An old farmhouse, maybe, or shepherd's shelter. "Stay low, use cover."
They moved in a crouch, using the curving contours of the land and the twisting rock formations to mask their movement. Every twenty meters, they stopped, listened, and scanned the sky. The helicopter made another pass, further south now, its search expanding outward.
“No other birds,” Amina said. “Hopefully, that means those bastards don’t realize that was us.”
Aya nodded. “Hopefully.”
By the time they reached the structure, Aya's legs were shaking from exhaustion, and her shoulder had gone from screaming pain to a deep, nauseating throb.
The farmhouse, if that's what it had once been, was older than she'd thought from a distance. The roof had partially collapsed decades ago, timbers had rotted, and tiles were scattered across the interior. Three walls still stood, the fourth crumbled to knee-high rubble. No doors, no windows intact, but the remaining walls provided concealment from above.
They entered cautiously, checking for snakes or other wildlife, for signs of recent habitation. 
The floor was packed earth, covered in layers of dust and debris: broken dishes, a rusted plow in the far left corner, pieces of rotted furniture that had begun their return to the soil. 
"Home sweet home," Amina said, her voice heavy with exhaustion. 
Aya turned around and joined her. In what had once been a storage area, she’d found a stockpile of hay. Old hay, musty and gray, but still dry, still providing insulation from the cold ground.
They collapsed into it without ceremony, too tired to care about comfort, too battered to worry about anything except not moving for a while. Their clothes were still damp from the cistern crossing earlier, and the landing had added new layers of dirt and sweat. Aya's hands shook with a tremor from the aftereffects of fear and exertion.
"That was insane," Amina said, a laugh escaping that sounded slightly unhinged. "We stole a hot air balloon. Flew it over Cappadocia. And crash-landed in the middle of nowhere."
"Successfully crash-landed," Aya corrected. "We're alive."
"We always make it."
They fell silent, listening to the wind outside, the creak of old wood, their own breathing gradually slowing. Amina shifted closer, seeking warmth, and Aya didn't pull away. They lay together in the hay, exhaustion claiming them piece by piece.
"What you said earlier," Aya began, then stopped, unsure how to finish.
"Forget it," Amina said quickly. "Bad timing. Bad place. I shouldn't have—"
"You weren't wrong," Aya interrupted. "About who I am. What I want." She went quiet, attempting to straighten out confusing thoughts before giving up. "I've been trying to convince myself I could be someone else. Someone who could settle down, teach students, and live a normal life. But every time things go wrong, every time we're running from something or stealing something or one bad decision away from death, I feel—"
"Alive," Amina finished softly.
"Yeah."
"So what does that mean? For us? For him?"
"I don't know," Aya admitted. "I care about Karim. I meant everything I said about wanting a future. But maybe..." She trailed off, the thought incomplete, the answer still forming.
Amina didn't push. She just lay there, close enough that Aya could feel her warmth, could see the new silver tattoos catching the light that filtered through the broken roof. The patterns were beautiful beyond aesthetics, a permanent reminder of forces beyond human understanding.
Aya reached out, tracing one of the patterns with a fingertip, following the eight-pointed star on Amina's forearm. Amina's breath hitched, but she didn't pull away.
"They're quiet now," Amina said. "The markings. No burning, no pulling. Just... there. Like they've become part of me."
"They're beautiful," Aya said. “They suit you.”
Their eyes met, and the world narrowed to the space between them.
Aya momentarily regretted her words, suddenly acutely aware of how close they were lying. Close enough to see the different shades of brown in Amina's eyes, the way gold flecks caught the light.
Amina's gaze dropped to Aya's mouth, just for a second, then back to her eyes. She smiled before lying back further, getting more comfortable by her side.
Every nerve in Aya's body was awake despite the exhaustion, attuned to Amina's proximity, to the heat radiating from her skin, to the infinitesimal movements that suggested intention.
Amina turned closer, barely an inch, but in the confined space of their bodies pressed together in the hay, it felt monumental. Her hand, which had been resting on her own side, moved. Fingers brushed Aya's hip, light as breath, then settled there with more certainty.
Aya's heart hammered. Her own hand, the one that had been tracing the silver marks on Amina's arm, stilled. She could close the gap. Should she? Every cell in her body screamed while her mind threw up a thousand reasons why this was complicated, impossible, a betrayal of Karim, who'd done nothing but support her.
"Aya," Amina whispered as she cozied up in the hay. Her voice was rough with exhaustion. Not quite a question, not quite permission. Just her name, weighted with everything they'd left unsaid for months.
Aya's breath caught. She could feel the moment crystallizing, becoming one of those pivotal choices that would define everything after. Lean forward or pull back. Act on this or let it pass. Choose the chaos of what she felt or the safety of what she'd committed to.
Aya made her decision. Started to close the distance momentarily. Hesitated. Saw Amina's pupils dilate, saw her lips part slightly—
Then Amina's eyes fluttered closed, exhaustion claiming her, pulling her under before Aya could act on the moment hanging between them, on Amina’s earlier words.
Aya froze, suspended in that almost-kiss, then slowly relaxed back, her heart pounding, hands shaking. Relief and disappointment warred in her chest in equal measure.
Maybe it was better this way, Aya thought. Maybe the universe was giving them both an out, a pause button on a decision neither was ready to make with a clear head.
Aya lay awake, watching light move across the broken ceiling, listening to Amina's breathing deepen. She thought about the balloon ride, about the sheer beauty of sunrise over Cappadocia, about the terror of the landing. She thought about Amina's words on every topic, about her own response, about the truth buried in both.
Once Aegis was stopped, what did she actually want? She still didn't have an answer.
Chapter 29
Twenty kilometers east, Karim half-dragged, half-carried Hamza through a passage so narrow their shoulders scraped both walls. The older man's weight pressed against his side, one arm slung over Karim's shoulders, each step punctuated by gasps of pain.
"When stone rings like a bell," Hamza managed between breaths, "you're beneath temple ruins." He lifted his free hand and rapped his knuckles against the wall. The sound returned pure and resonant, like striking crystal. "Byzantine monastery overhead. Collapsed five hundred years ago, but the foundations remain."
They reached a junction where the passage split three ways. Karim stopped, letting Hamza rest against the wall while he examined each opening with his phone's dim light.
"Right," Hamza said, reading his hesitation. "Always take the ascending path when pursued underground."
"Even with your ankle?"
Hamza looked down at his injured foot, the joint swollen to twice its normal size, the improvised splint already working loose. "Especially with my ankle. I slow you down on flat ground. Going up, we can use handholds."
The climb was brutal. The passage was more ladder than corridor, with handholds and footholds carved at regular intervals. Hamza pulled himself up with his arms while Karim climbed below, one hand always braced against the older man's back, ready to catch him if he slipped.
Ten meters up, Hamza's grip faltered. He swung sideways with a choked cry. Karim lunged, wedging his shoulder under Hamza's weight, pinning him against the wall until he could find a new purchase.
They hung there, breathing hard.
"I'm a liability," Hamza said quietly. "You should leave me. I'll find my way out."
"Absolutely not." The words came harder than Karim intended. "We move together or not at all."
"Aya would want you to complete the mission. To protect what you found."
"I don't abandon people." Karim adjusted his position, taking more weight. "Besides, you know these caves. I don't. I need you."
The stabilization wafer in his pocket seemed to burn against his hip with a reminder that he'd already made one selfish choice today. He wouldn't make another.
"If I fall, you let me go," Hamza said. "Promise me."
"I promise to catch you. That's the only promise I'm making. Now move."
They climbed. Behind them, voices echoed from below, growing closer.
"They're faster than us," Hamza whispered.
"Then we need to be smarter."
At the top, the passage widened into a chamber where three routes branched off. They collapsed against the wall, gasping. Below, flashlight beams swept the junction, powerful tactical lights making Karim's phone look pathetic.
"They'll check all three," Karim said. "We can't outrun them."
"No." Hamza's eyes swept the chamber. "But we can outlast them. They're outsiders. They don't know these passages like my blood knows them."
He pointed to a section of the wall. "There. Press the fourth stone from the floor. The one shaped like a tooth."
Karim found it, pressed hard. The stone shifted with a grating sound, and a section of wall swung inward, revealing a narrow crawlspace.
"In. Quickly."
They squeezed through. Karim pulled the door closed, and total darkness engulfed them.
He reached for his phone, but Hamza's hand stopped him. "No light. They might see it through cracks."
They sat in darkness so complete that Karim couldn't see his own hand. Their shoulders touched. Their breathing sounded impossibly loud.
Footsteps entered the chamber outside. Multiple sets. The scrape of equipment.
"Three passages. Alpha team takes the descent. Bravo, check the left branch. We'll take the climbing route."
"They went up. Blood over here. One of them is injured."
Karim's heart hammered.
Two sets of boots headed for the climbing route. Then a pause.
"Hold on. Dust pattern here is wrong."
Karim felt a chill run down his spine. He hadn’t entertained the idea of entering a fight without Aya or Amina.
"What do you mean?"
"Climbing route shows recent disturbance. But there's dust settled around this wall section that shouldn't be there."
Silence.
Then: "I don’t see anything, but there could be a hidden passage. These caves are full of them."
"Mark it for follow-up. Right now, at least one of them is just ahead of us and injured. We can come back after we have them in custody."
"Understood. Moving up."
The boots ascended. The sounds faded. Karim and Hamza sat frozen, neither daring to move.
Minutes passed. Five. Ten. Karim counted his heartbeats, forced his breathing to slow.
Finally, Hamza whispered, "They've moved on. But they'll come back. We need to use the time we have."
Karim pulled out his phone, brightness at minimum. The passage ahead was a crawlspace, barely a meter high. They'd have to move on hands and knees. "Where does this lead?" he asked.
"To the shepherd's network. The old routes my grandfather had me walk when I was a boy." Hamza’s voice carried something between nostalgia and grief. "I hated those games, but he was teaching me to survive."
"Can you do this?" Karim asked.
"I have to. We both do."
They crawled. The passage was claustrophobic, walls and ceiling pressing close. Hamza pulled himself forward with his arms, dragging his injured leg. Every few meters, he'd stop, breathing hard through gritted teeth, then force himself onward.
Karim followed, mind bouncing between fear and guilt. He'd taken and hidden the lens component, thinking he was preserving something, protecting Çelebi's legacy. But watching Hamza drag himself through agony after the man had shared everything, trusted them completely, the brass piece felt less like salvation and more like betrayal.
"Hamza," Karim said, the words escaping before he could stop them. "I need to tell you something."
"Save your breath. Talking wastes energy."
"This isn’t a waste. Look, I took something. From the workshop. A small wafer with engravings, but it’s curious. I may be losing it, but it seems like it’s some kind of alien metal. I can’t imagine what it could be. Every other component has been made of typical elements, but not this one. I can’t wait to study it."
Hamza stopped crawling. The silence stretched.
"I hid it from everyone, and I’m not sure why I did it," Karim continued. "I guess I thought if we had to destroy the lens, at least one piece could survive for study. But really. I think I just... I wanted to keep something. For my research. Because at the end of the day, I’m not cut out for this, I’m meant to be a historian."
More silence. Then: "Show me."
Karim pulled out the thin, intricate component and shone his phone on it. Hamza took it in dirty, shaking hands, examining the engravings, the precise hole through its center.
"Do you know what this is?" Hamza asked finally.
"Part of a stabilization array. Çelebi's notes said that without it, the lens can't maintain coherence."
"So without this piece, the lens cannot work safely."
"Yes."
Hamza was quiet for a long moment, turning the wafer over. "My family protected these secrets for fifteen generations. Not because we understood them, but because we believed they were important. We were part of the legacy. We believed someone would come who could use them wisely." He looked at Karim. "Was I wrong to trust you?"
The question hit like a physical blow. "I don't know," Karim admitted. "I wanted to be worthy of that trust. I thought I was. But then in the workshop, surrounded by all that knowledge, all that history... I couldn't let it all disappear."
"This is not the way."
Karim's throat tightened.
"Let me tell you a story,” Hamza continued. “It’s about Aya's father, Hassan. I didn’t tell Aya this full story yet, but his work saved my grandfather's life in a way." Hamza's eyes grew distant. "Nineteen years ago, Hassan came to Cappadocia. He'd found references to the Astral Lens in Ottoman archives, was tracking its components with another researcher here in Turkey. My grandfather was still alive then, still the keeper for the Silent Meridian. Hassan spent three months here, studying what we had, decoding Çelebi's notes.
"Our family has always kept channels open to others who guard similar secrets. Then, when Hassan died in Colombia, word reached us within days through the old networks."
"What happened?"
"He assembled a partial system in our workshop. He never ventured deep underground, and never got nearly as far as you all have. He had just three components, enough to test the principles at the time." Hamza's voice dropped. "When he activated it during a minor planetary alignment... the energy discharge was catastrophic. Not large-scale, thank God. He'd had the sense to do it in an isolated chamber. But the heat, the light, the wrongness of what came through those dimensional fractures..."
Karim felt cold spread through his chest. "What did he see?"
"He wouldn't speak of it. Not in detail. But afterward, he spent two days dismantling everything, scattering components, destroying or encrypting his research. My grandfather asked why, because if the system worked, even partially, wasn't that proof it should be studied further?" Hamza shook his head. "He said some knowledge was too dangerous. That what he'd glimpsed on the other side of those fractures... it was aware. Intelligent. Waiting."
"And then?"
"He left. Went to Cambodia to investigate similar phenomena, but we did not hear from him for a long time. Then, we got word that he died in Colombia in 2019. Equipment failure during a climb, if I remember correctly." Hamza's expression darkened. "But my grandfather never believed it. Said Hassan was too careful, too experienced. And there were rumors of another researcher with him, a Turkish academic named Malik, who my grandfather distrusted."
The name landed like a stone. "Osman Malik, or Malik Osman?"
"You know him?"
"He's the one hunting us now. Leading Aegis, orchestrating all of this."
Hamza's eyes widened. "I don’t know the whole story, but I believe they’d been partners when Hassan was here working on his Astral Lens research, but they’d disagreed strongly about what to do with their discoveries and parted ways after Hassan destroyed his work. Then, Malik reappears with Hassan in Colombia when he died."
"You think Malik killed him?"
"Or failed to prevent his death when he could have. Either way, Hassan's death served Malik's purposes and removed the one man who understood the system well enough to oppose him, leaving the research scattered and vulnerable." Hamza gripped Karim's shoulder. "But your friend Aya, she's walking in her father's footsteps. Fighting the same battle he fought. And Malik... he will carry a grudge, no matter what he says. He will believe Hassan betrayed him, chose fear over progress. This will be personal for him."
Karim's mind raced, pieces clicking together. The intensity of Malik's pursuit. The resources committed. This wasn't just about the lens—it was about finishing what Hassan had tried to destroy. About proving Hassan wrong.
"Aya doesn't know, but she may suspect something," Karim said quietly. "She knows her father died in Colombia, and that he used to work with Malik, but not about his direct involvement there."
"Then you need to tell her. Before you reach Bodrum, before you face whatever comes next."
Hamza handed the component back. "This is not your choice to make alone. If the lens must be destroyed, then all of it must be destroyed. If it can be used wisely, then all of it must be available. You understand?"
Karim closed his fingers around the wafer, feeling its weight differently."Yes."
"Will you give it back to Aya? Let her decide its fate with the rest?"
Every instinct screamed against it. This piece of Çelebi's genius, potentially the last surviving fragment, was consigned to possible destruction. But Hamza was right. This wasn't his choice to make.
"Yes," Karim said quietly. "You’re right, I'll give it back."
"Good. Then my role is nearly complete." Hamza was quiet for a moment, his breathing labored in the narrow space. "But there is one more thing."
"What?"
"The woman. Leyla." Hamza's voice dropped even lower. "Something about her doesn't settle right with me."
Karim frowned. "She's been invaluable. Her intelligence background, her contacts—"
"Yes. Exactly." Hamza's hand found Karim's arm in the darkness, gripping with surprising strength. "In my experience, when someone appears exactly when you need them and has exactly what you need..." He trailed off. “She is not as I remember her from our previous meetings.”
"You think she's working for someone else? Aegis? That’s impossible, I’ve watched her gun down some of their operatives."
"All I think is, I've watched her these past days. The way she documents everything. The way her eyes track Aya like she's memorizing escape routes." Hamza's grip tightened. "I may be wrong. I hope I am. But if I'm right, she's positioning herself for something, and you won't see it coming until it's too late."
Karim thought about Leyla going west alone, carrying the assembly charts. About how she'd been the one to suggest splitting up.
"What should I do?"
"Watch her, because something’s different, but don't confront her. That would tip your hand. But watch and be ready to move when the moment comes, if it is necessary. I’m an old man, I’m not always right." Hamza released his arm. "Now. We survive."
They crawled for what felt like hours. The passage gradually widened until they could crouch, then finally stand. Eventually, a faint daylight appeared ahead, but after the absolute dark, it looked like salvation to Karim.
They emerged into a shepherd's cave overlooking Avanos. The town spread below, houses catching the rising sun. The air was cold and clean.
Hamza collapsed, his ankle finally refusing all weight. Karim examined the joint. It was grotesquely swollen, skin tight and shiny, darkening with bruises that looked black in the dawn light.
"We need to get you to a hospital," Karim said.
"Later. First, we need to make sure you reach Bodrum safely." He gestured weakly at the components in Karim's pack. "That this reaches its destination."
Karim sat beside Hamza, both watching the sun paint the valley gold and rose.
"Aya's father would be proud of what you’re all doing," Hamza said suddenly.
Karim shook his head. "I don't know about that."
"I do. Not of your deception, that was foolish. But you admit your mistake and are willing to make it right." Hamza's hand found Karim's shoulder, gripped with surprising strength. "Hassan died protecting knowledge from the wrong hands. You're honoring that legacy by choosing to trust the right person with it. You may not be cut out for this life, as you said, but you’re doing what you must, when you must. Your old life is always waiting for you."
Chapter 30
Twenty kilometers west, Leyla emerged from darkness into dawn.
The drainage grate fought her, its rusted hinges screaming as she pushed upward, her muscles burning from hours of navigating the Roman aqueduct system. When her head broke the surface, the smell hit first. Not just sewage, though that was there, but diesel fumes, hot asphalt, the metallic tang of Nevşehir's industrial district waking for another day.
She pulled herself through the opening, knees scraping concrete, hands finding purchase on ground that felt impossibly solid after the slick, ancient stonework below. For a moment, she knelt there on the sidewalk, breathing air that wasn't filtered through two thousand years of mineral deposits.
A truck rumbled past, its driver doing a double-take in his mirror, watching a woman climbing from a drainage system, covered in filth. Leyla watched his brake lights flare. Then, after a three-second calculation, they released, and the truck continued.
Two more workers passed during her walk to the equipment cache, one averting his eyes entirely and the other staring but not stopping. Leyla cataloged their faces anyway, filed them under potential complications. But they kept walking, and she reached the abandoned building without incident.
The cache was where she'd left it six months ago, during the operational prep phase when she'd still believed this assignment had a clean endpoint, when she and Emre had worked side-by-side.
She pulled the weatherproof bag from behind loose bricks and laid out its contents. Clean clothes. Laptop. Fresh identity papers. First aid kit. Cash. A backup phone, the one her team didn't know existed, wrapped in a Faraday bag.
And a small photograph printed on thin printer paper, worn at the edges from too much handling.
Emre laughed, shirtless, his hand reaching toward her as she took the photo. Taken eight months ago in Derinkuyu, the day before they'd separated for what was supposed to be two weeks. The day before he'd vanished into Aegis's machinery.
Leyla pressed her thumb against his face, smearing the photo with underground grime she hadn't noticed on her hands. She tried to clean it, but only made it worse.
It had been three months since she'd heard his voice, held his hand, woken up next to him in their flat overlooking the Bosphorus. And all that time, following orders from people who held his life as collateral for her cooperation.
She set the photo carefully atop the cache contents and began stripping.
The tactical gear came off in layers. Each piece removed felt like peeling away an identity. Leyla the ally, Leyla the field operative, Leyla who'd fought beside Aya and Amina and Karim with genuine camaraderie that made what came next so much worse.
She stood naked in the abandoned building's cool air, feeling suddenly exposed with goosebumps rising, and looked down at herself. Bruises from the underground escape. Scrapes from rough stone. Dirt ground so deeply into her skin that the first wipe with a cloth barely made a difference.
She found a bottle of water in the cache and poured it over her hands first, watching dirt run in dark rivulets down her wrists. The water was cold, shocking, tightening against her skin. She used a clean t-shirt as a cloth, starting at her fingertips and working up each arm, revealing skin beneath the grime.
She poured more water, let it cascade down her torso, and she closed her eyes against the sensation. Cold drops slid down her chest, pooling in her navel, running down the plane of her stomach. Her skin came clean in patches, revealing the contrast between filth and flesh. She wiped the cloth slowly, washing away the evidence of the woman she'd become. The water was nearly gone when she finally felt clean.
This is what betrayal looks like, she thought. This is the cost.
Partially refreshed, Leyla extracted her clean clothes and quickly dressed, transforming. Dark jeans, a silk blouse in cream, and a light jacket cut to conceal the handgun holstered at her hip. She released her hair from its ponytail, shook it out, then ran her fingers through to work out the worst tangles. She found her makeup kit and applied just enough foundation to cover the shadows, along with some subtle eye work to look alert.
When Leyla looked in the compact mirror, her invented persona of Elif Yılmaz looked back. Sales representative, textile company, Ankara. Normal woman on a normal business trip making normal money doing normal work.
She packed the tactical gear into a trash bag, walked three blocks, and dropped it in a dumpster behind a restaurant. Evidence destroyed. Identity erased.
Twenty minutes after emerging from the drainage system, she walked into a roadside café to grab breakfast before hitting the highway.
The interior of the small establishment was trying too hard. Plastic plants, laminated menus, and photographs of Istanbul tourist sites that had nothing to do with Nevşehir. Turkish pop music played too loudly from speakers mounted in corners. The coffee smelled burnt.
Leyla ordered tea at the counter and found a table in the back where she could watch both the entrance and the kitchen exit, then pulled out her phone.
Her hands trembled as she unwrapped it from the Faraday bag.
This phone had no contacts saved, no message history, nothing that could compromise her if discovered. Just a single number she knew by heart, burned into memory the day they'd given it to her along with the photograph and the promise: you will be contacted by your colleague Omar soon, and he will tell you about a plan to collect and safeguard the Astral Lens, along with Aya Amrani and her associates. Cooperate with them fully, support them, and report everything to us. When this is over, your boyfriend lives.
She stood sharply to make the call outside. She couldn't bear to do it while sitting, while comfortable, while pretending this was just another operational check-in. The café's rear courtyard was deserted except for three plastic tables, dying plants in concrete planters, and a morning sun just beginning to paint the high walls with light. Leyla stood with her back against the sun-warmed brick and dialed from memory.
The line clicked to a voice she knew too well.
"Leyla." Malik's tone carried excitement, barely contained, thrumming beneath his usual measured control. "I received your transmission. The photographs are... remarkable."
"The workshop held more than we expected," she said carefully. "The calibration rings alone—"
"I saw." She could hear him moving, pacing, the sound of his footsteps echoing in whatever space he occupied. "Çelebi's genius, preserved for four centuries. And now, finally, within reach." A pause, then quieter: "Hassan would have been devastated. All his work to scatter these pieces in old Silent Meridian sites, and his own daughter brings them together."
Leyla said nothing. She'd learned not to engage when Malik mentioned Hassan.
"The Bodrum site is confirmed, then?" he continued.
"Yes. The tidal calculations from the charts point there. They'll move within twenty-four hours, once everyone regroups."
"Good. Good." More pacing. "Do you understand what waits for them there?"
"The coastal workshop. More components, presumably."
"Not just components." Malik's voice dropped, taking on the reverent tone he used when discussing the lens's potential. "Based on your photos, the crown lens, Leyla. The control mechanism. The piece that allows the system to be safely operated, powered down, or to sustain the connection indefinitely."
"I thought the crown lens was lost."
"No, these diagrams point to a specific chamber that can only be accessed at certain tidal conditions. A chamber that..." He paused, and she could hear the smile in his voice. "A chamber that may require Hassan's bloodline to open, after his failed experiments in Colombia. "
"His bloodline?"
"The Silent Meridian's security measures are elegant. Çelebi designed his most dangerous cache to respond only to those already touched by the lens's energy. Once initiated, you could initiate others, and so on. The way of the secret society. But I believe Hassan's activation attempt nineteen years ago, even though he destroyed the failed assembly afterward, left a mark. A resonance. That resonance may have passed to his daughter. The… situation with Ms. Said’s djinn contact in Morocco confirms my suspicions when dealing with these energy artifacts and their failsafes."
Leyla suppressed her anger and replied evenly. "So Aya is the key."
"She's always been the key. Why do you think I've allowed her to live this long? To gather the components I couldn't reach? To solve puzzles that required her father's research?" Malik laughed, a sound that grew unhinged beneath the surface, in a way it hadn’t months ago. "She's been doing my work for me, Leyla. And she doesn't even know it."
"What happens in Bodrum?"
"My dive team is already in position. They'll tail the boat, wait underwater while Aya retrieves the crown lens, in case she's the only one who can. Once it's extracted, once she surfaces with it in her hands..." Another pause. "She stops being useful."
The words crackled through the line. "You're going to kill her."
"I'm going to complete what Hassan was too afraid to finish. The alignment is in three days, Leyla. Three days until Mars, Jupiter, and Saturn form the configuration Çelebi documented. Three days until the lens can channel energy from sources Hassan only glimpsed before he ran away." Malik's voice hardened. "I won't make his mistake. I won't let fear steal humanity's greatest opportunity for evolution. Anyone in the way stands in opposition to humanity’s progress."
Leyla gripped the phone so tightly her knuckles ached. She'd known this moment would come, known from the beginning that Aegis's endgame wasn't preservation but use, but hearing it stated so plainly, so casually, felt painful. Aya's death was discussed like a line item on a project plan.
"And the others?" she asked. "Amina? Karim?"
"Collateral. We can use them as leverage if needed, dispose of them if not. They're not essential to the mechanism." A pause. "Though Amina might be interesting to study afterward. The djinn energy that was extracted in the workshop, the residual connection... There may be applications."
Leyla closed her eyes. Amina, who'd screamed as ancient power was ripped from her body. Who'd risked her life to help them. Who might now become a lab specimen if Malik had his way.
"What do you need from me?" Leyla asked, forcing her voice steady.
"Stay with them until Bodrum. Guide them to the coastal site, help them locate the crown lens." Malik's voice sharpened with focus. "My dive team will be positioned underwater, waiting. They'll tail you during the descent. When Aya retrieves the crown lens, assuming the chamber requires her bloodline, as I suspect, my team will move as soon as they begin to surface."
"An ambush. At the dive site."
"A retrieval operation," Malik corrected. "Clean, efficient, final. They surface with the crown lens. We deal with them underwater, board, and take all the components. The fragments, rings, cylinder, focusing heart, and the crown lens itself. Everything in one coordinated action."
Leyla's throat tightened. "And Aya's team?"
"Aya dies the moment she hands over the crown lens. It's unfortunate, I genuinely admired her father, but she's served her purpose and proves too dangerous to be left alive." His tone became clinical. "Karim has archaeological knowledge that might prove useful during assembly and calibration. If he fights, he dies with her, but if he can be subdued, I can find a place for him here."
"And Amina?"
A pause. "The residual djinn connection is... Intriguing. After what we observed in the workshop, the way dimensional energy responds to her, even with the marks burned out... Yes. Amina should be captured alive if possible. Tranquilize her during the ambush. We'll need to study how the purification affected her, whether the connection can be reestablished or amplified."
Leyla felt nauseous. "You want to experiment on her."
"I want to understand what makes her function as a conduit. That understanding could be the difference between controlled activation and catastrophic failure." He paused. "But Leyla, if capturing her alive endangers the operation or the components, she dies. The lens is the priority. Everything else is secondary."
"What's my role during the ambush?"
"When my people surface and initiate, you help secure the components. Prevent escape from Aya, Amina, or Karim. Deal with the ship’s captain if necessary. Terminate any resistance aboard the boat." His voice softened slightly. "You've come this far. Three more days. One final action. Then you and Emre disappear together, somewhere warm, somewhere far from all of this."
Leyla closed her eyes. She'd just agreed to participate in an underwater execution. To help murder people who trusted her, fought beside her, and saved her life.
All for Emre. All to get him back. She steeled herself to eliminate one more obstacle.
"Okay, but there's a complication," Leyla said. "The old man. Hamza."
"What about him?"
"He's suspicious. I saw him watching me after we surfaced, when I was sending the photographs. The way he looked at me..." She swallowed. "He knows something's wrong. He may have warned the others."
Silence on the line. When Malik spoke again, his voice had gone flat, businesslike. "Where is he now?"
"With Karim, taking the eastern route. They should reach the rendezvous point by evening."
"He's old. Injured, according to the surveillance reports. Accidents happen in these mountains." A pause. "I'll dispatch a team. Clean, quiet, nothing that raises questions. Karim can survive until the dive if he doesn't interfere. After all, he’s proven himself very useful in solving these archaeological puzzles."
Leyla felt nauseous. She'd just ordered a man's death, a man who'd shared his family's secrets, who'd trusted them with knowledge kept hidden for generations.
"Leyla." Malik's voice softened, and somehow that was worse. "I know this is difficult. But we're so close now. Three days. You've played your role perfectly, better than I could have hoped. And when it's over, when the alignment passes, and the lens fulfills its purpose..."
"Emre," she said. "You promised."
"And I keep my promises. He's safe. Healthy. Waiting for you." A pause. "Do you want proof? I can have someone bring him to a video call within the hour."
Despite what she'd just agreed to, hope flared in Leyla’s chest. "Yes. Please."
"Done. Check your secure channel in sixty minutes." Malik's voice warmed with something that might have been affection, or might have been a very good imitation. "You've done well, Leyla. Hold course for three more days. Then you and Emre disappear together, somewhere warm, somewhere far from all of this. You'll have earned it."
The line went dead.
Leyla stood frozen in the courtyard, phone still pressed to her ear, listening to the silence where Malik's voice had been.
She'd just signed Hamza's death warrant. Agreed to participate in Aya's murder. Set in motion events that would give a madman access to power beyond comprehension.
And she'd done it for Emre. For the chance to hold him again, to see him smile, to build the life they'd planned before Aegis took everything. Could that justify what she was becoming?
She lowered the phone slowly, stared at it like something venomous. Powered it down. Wrapped it carefully.
When she walked back inside, Leyla Yılmaz's pleasant expression was firmly in place. But behind the mask, Leyla was counting. Sixty minutes until she'd see Emre's face. Three days until the alignment. Three days until she found out if anything she'd done was worth it.



Night settled over Cappadocia fully, the temperature dropping in the dramatic desert shift. In the abandoned farmhouse, Amina slept deeply. Aya lay awake beside her, watching the diffused starlight paint gold patterns across Amina's skin.
Her phone vibrated with incoming messages on their secured channel.
Two shepherds found the lost lamb. Coming home slow but whole.
Karim and Hamza were safe, moving carefully, both alive. "Slow" suggested injury, but nothing critical.
One owl watching from the west tower. Dusk brings me home.
Leyla had made it through the western route, was en route, had spotted surveillance, but evaded it.
Aya typed her reply. Two birds singing above the valley. Nest when shadows grow long.
She and Amina were safe. They'd reach the rendezvous before sunrise, Hamza's clifftop refuge, the final fallback point before continuing.
Aya should have been able to sleep; her body demanded it. Every muscle ached, her injured side throbbed with each breath, and her shoulder sent sharp complaints with any movement. Exhaustion had settled so deeply into her bones that even lying still hurt. But sleep wouldn't come.
She lay on her back in the hay, listening to Amina's deep, even breathing beside her. The other woman had fallen asleep almost instantly, her body finally claiming the rest it desperately needed. But Aya's mind wouldn't quiet, circling the same questions on an endless loop.
After ten minutes of further stillness, she gave up.
Moving carefully to avoid waking Amina, she extracted herself from the hay and stood, testing her injuries. The stitches in her side pulled but held. Her shoulder was still usable if she avoided raising her arm above horizontal. Everything else was just pain, familiar and manageable.
Her pack sat where she'd left it, and she found herself kneeling beside it, checking the components by touch. The glass fragment, wrapped in cloth, was still faintly warm. The calibration ring, its metal cool, was impossibly light. She held them both, feeling their weight, physical and otherwise.
Tomorrow they'd have all the pieces. Karim with the cylinder and his ring. Leyla with the charts. Amina with the crystal heart. And her with these, plus whatever they had yet to discover in the sea.
Everything they needed to assemble the Astral Lens. To recreate what Hassan had seen nineteen years ago and been so terrified by that he'd spent the rest of his life trying to keep it scattered. But what would they do when the time came?
She thought about her father, about the man she'd barely known. He'd died when she was nineteen, young enough that she'd still believed the world was simpler than it was, that people were categorically good or bad, that choices had clear answers.
He'd seen something. Experienced something. Probably not dissimilar to what they’d encountered in the depths below Morocco’s Atlas Mountains. He’d been touched by whatever existed on the other side of dimensional fractures, and that touch had changed him. Made him understand that humanity's reach sometimes exceeded its grasp by such a margin that reaching at all was dangerous.
And Malik had been there. Had seen the same thing. Had drawn the opposite conclusion.
Aya wondered if they'd argued about it. If there'd been a moment where their friendship fractured, where one man's caution met another man's ambition and neither could give ground. If Colombia had been an attempt at reconciliation, or if something darker had happened in those mountains.
She suspected she'd never know for certain. But the pattern was clear enough.
With a sigh, Aya turned her attention back to Amina. She'd given up her connection to the djinn energy to open the workshop. Had endured pain that Aya could barely imagine to give them access to components that might doom them all.
And during the balloon ride, before the helicopter interrupted, Amina had called her out to embrace the life she actually lived instead of the one she thought she should want.
Aya had felt the truth of it in her body.
She thought about Karim, and guilt twisted in her stomach. He deserved better than this, better than someone who couldn't give him certainty, who couldn't promise the stable future he wanted. Who almost kissed her ex-lover in a farmhouse while he was underground alone, trusting her, carrying components he'd probably die to protect.
She thought about Amina and felt something entirely different. Recognition. Understanding. The magnetic pull of someone who saw you completely and didn't ask you to be different.
But there wasn't space for that right now. Couldn't be. Not with everything waiting in Bodrum, not with Malik hunting them, not with decisions about the lens looming that would define whether she became her father's daughter or something else entirely.
Chapter 31
The sun hung low over Cappadocia, painting the fairy chimneys in shades of amber and rust as Aya and Amina climbed toward Hamza's refuge. They'd walked most of the day, keeping to backroads and dirt paths, avoiding main routes where Aegis would have checkpoints. Aya's legs burned from the constant elevation changes.
Beside her, Amina moved with visible exhaustion, the concussion from yesterday's crash landing manifesting in occasional moments where she'd pause, press her palm to her forehead, and wait for her vision to clear.
"There," Aya said, pointing up the slope where a figure stood silhouetted against the deepening sky, and extending an arm around Amina’s shoulders. “We made it.”
Mehmet stood outside, waving them forward. Once inside, the temperature dropped ten degrees in three steps. Karim rose from where he'd been sitting, his shoulders dropping when he saw them. He crossed the space and embraced Aya, arms wrapping around her with careful attention to her injuries.
But there was something different. A self-consciousness that hadn't existed before. Both of them were aware of Amina standing in the entrance, watching casually. When they separated, Karim's hand lingered on her arm. "You made it."
"We did." Aya looked past him. "Where's Hamza?"
"Here," a voice called from deeper in the cave. Hamza emerged, leaning heavily on a carved walking stick, his left ankle wrapped in fresh compression bandages. Mehmet's sister followed close behind. "Takes more than a twisted ankle to keep an old storyteller down."
Aya crossed to him, steadying him as he limped to the stone table. "You should be resting."
"I'll rest when I'm dead," Hamza said with a slight smile. "Right now, I want to hear how we're going to stop Malik."
The sound of an engine climbing the access road drew everyone's attention. Mehmet's hand dropped to where he carried a weapon, then relaxed. "Leyla."
The rental car that appeared was a late-model SUV that looked better suited to the city than their mountain path. Leyla emerged looking equally out of place. Her hair was freshly washed, makeup subtle but present, dressed in plainclothes. She moved with energy that spoke of actual rest.
"Looks like you found a hotel?" Aya asked as Leyla entered.
"I don’t deny I had the easiest exit route." Leyla gestured to the canvas bags she'd brought. "Also brought coffee and food. Figured you'd be hungry."
They gathered around the stone table. The coffee was thick, gritty, and sweet, and for several minutes no one spoke; they just ate.
Finally, Leyla pulled out an iPad. "We need to discuss our current situation."
She opened a map showing the region with red markers dotted along major roads. "I spoke with Salah and began making plans. New checkpoints went up this morning across Cappadocia. Government forces, responding to Aegis pressure. They're looking for four individuals traveling together, armed, also now wanted for the balloon incident."
She swiped to an Instagram feed that had footage from inside another balloon. The video showed Aya and Amina’s stolen balloon plummeting rapidly through the cluster of morning flights, clearly out of control. Another clip captured the crash landing from a distance. The violent impact, the basket bouncing, the envelope deflating in dramatic billows.
"Armed and dangerous. The operators filed theft and armed robbery reports with aviation authorities," Leyla continued.
"Which it was," Karim said quietly.
"It got us out," Amina countered. "We were surrounded. It was the right tactical decision."
"Those people were innocent,” Karim said.
"And we're trying to prevent a much larger catastrophe," Aya said, hearing the edge in her voice. "Sometimes that requires difficult choices."
The tension hung in the air like smoke.
Leyla cleared her throat. "Regardless, it complicates movement. Every hotel, restaurant, and tour operator has your descriptions. We have to avoid populated areas where possible."
"What's the timeline for Bodrum?" Aya asked.
"Tight." Leyla pulled up tidal charts. "As we know, the underwater chamber is tide-dependent. Access is only possible during slack tide, that brief window when the current essentially stops. Next optimal window opens in thirty-six hours. Twenty to twenty-five minutes of accessible conditions. After that, currents likely become too dangerous for three more days."
"Thirty-six hours," Aya repeated.
"It gets tighter," Leyla said and shook her head. "Aegis communications. They identified Bodrum as a high-probability location this morning. They're moving assets there now. Ground teams, surveillance, and marine resources. It’s going to be the same shit-show we had here, at the mosque, you name it. We’re still running parallel."
"There's more you need to know," Leyla said quietly. She pulled up surveillance photos of workers moving equipment at Galata Tower in Istanbul. "Aegis has been busy. Based on the pace of their preparations and cross-referencing with Salah's astronomical data, the major planetary alignment happens in approximately two days. That's when Malik will attempt activation."
The number hung in the air like a countdown timer.
She swiped through additional thermal scans and surveillance footage of heavily guarded equipment transfers. "And he's not starting from scratch. These scans suggest he already has at least three major components secured at the tower. They have to be ones we don’t know about, or reproductions. If they’re reproductions, that could suggest he has even more. We can't identify them precisely through the shielding, but the energy signatures match what we've been carrying."
Leyla pulled up her own inventory on the tablet. "Let's take stock of what we have versus what's still missing. We have the glass fragment, the primary lens element. The brass cylinder for energy channeling. Both calibration rings from the Cappadocia platform. The focusing array and crystal heart from the star chamber. We have the assembly charts from the cemetery plans."
She gestured to the map of Bodrum's coastline. "And we're going for the crown lens, the termination mechanism your father emphasized. That's seven of eight components. The only one we haven't located is the stabilization wafer, and honestly, it might not exist outside of Çelebi's theoretical notes."
Karim didn’t react. He let the sentence pass, and the weight in his pocket felt heavier.
"Which makes the crown lens absolutely critical," he said finally. "Without the wafer for fine-tuning, the crown lens becomes the only way to control or terminate an activation sequence."
"Which makes it the key," Aya said.
Karim spoke quickly. "More than that, without the crown lens, any activation attempt becomes inherently unstable. The system might function briefly, but catastrophically. The crown lens is what makes controlled operation possible, allegedly, according to Çelebi’s notes."
"So even if we prevent Malik from getting it," Aya said, "if he attempts activation with his partial system—"
"The results could be worse than if he'd succeeded," Karim finished. "Uncontrolled dimensional fractures, but potentially on a much larger scale."
“And finally,” Leyla continued. “I have support ready in Istanbul. A certain rogue carpet salesman you all know and love is already setting up safe houses and extraction routes. Omar’s on the ground.”
The effect was immediate.
Amina laughed. “Oh, thank God. If anyone can sweet-talk half the city into helping us, it’s him.”
Karim grinned. “We have been missing his special blend of chaos.”
Aya shook her head in disbelief. “So he slipped past Aegis?”
Leyla nodded. “It appears his former associates have too much on their plate now to keep too close of tabs on him. He said, and I quote, ‘Tell my favorite troublemakers to bring some souvenirs back from the beach.’”
That earned small smiles around the table, thin and tired, but real. Just the idea of Omar waiting with his bad jokes and a celebratory bottle of wine gave them something solid again.
Still smiling, Aya pulled out her phone and checked the secure channel. A message from Salah: Leyla will fill you in, but tower confirmed. Three-day window holding. They're moving like men who believe they'll succeed, but you have the upper hand. Be careful, habibti.
She sent a quick message of appreciation and stood. "We need a specific plan," Aya said. "Accounting for the fact that Aegis is already in Bodrum and Istanbul."
An hour of intense coordination followed as Leyla conveyed as much as possible about the dive site. Depth, currents, access points, timing windows.
"Guessing it’s at least twenty meters down," she said, pointing to nautical charts.
"Current?" Karim asked.
"Significant outside the slack tide window. Strong enough to be dangerous in confined spaces."
She outlined equipment needs: high-capacity tanks, backup regulators, reef hooks, and underwater lights.
"Who dives?" Karim asked.
Amina looked at Aya's side, where the bandage showed through her shirt. "That wound is barely sealed. At depth, with pressure changes, physical exertion." She shook her head. "Too risky. Water pressure alone could compromise the sutures."
"I can—," Aya started.
"I'll go," Amina said before Aya could finish. "I've logged more dives than anyone here. Done the Blue Hole in Dahab more times than I can count. I can handle current, low vis, confined spaces—"
"No," Leyla interrupted. "You can't."
Amina's expression shifted to frustration as she followed Leyla's gaze. "The concussion."
"You were unconscious yesterday," Leyla said flatly. "You've been pressing your palm to your forehead every twenty minutes since you got here. Pressure changes at depth with a recent head injury? You'd risk blackout underwater, nitrogen narcosis at shallower depths than normal, and spatial disorientation. Any of those things kills you and whoever's diving with you. It has to be Aya."
"It's minor," Amina protested. "I can—"
"You can stay on the surface and coordinate," Karim said gently. "Where we need you conscious and functional, not passed out at twenty meters."
Amina's eyes narrowed. "Fuck."
"Which means I'm going," Aya said, voice carrying finality. "I know the system best, I've done wreck diving in the Philippines and Vietnam, and I can tell you’ve never dived before or you’d already have volunteered."
"Your wound—" Leyla started.
"Can be managed." Aya turned to her. "You have access to medical supplies in Bodrum, right? Field medic gear?"
"Yes, but—"
"Then get some second skin, a waterproof seal. I’ll apply it over the sutures and create a flexible, waterproof barrier. It won't be comfortable, but it'll hold for a dive."
Leyla studied her for a long moment, then sighed. "You're going to do this regardless of what I say."
"Yes."
"Then yes, I can source that. And antibiotics for after, because that wound is definitely getting contaminated despite our best efforts." She looked at Karim. "You're going with her? You said you're certified?"
"Advanced Open Water," Karim confirmed. "Certified in Dakhla four years ago. Haven't dived since, but I’m sure it's like riding a bicycle. I can refresh the basics, stay close to Aya, follow her lead."
"You'll need to," Leyla said. She pulled up more detailed information on her tablet. "This isn't recreational diving. The chamber is at least twenty meters down, possibly deeper. Strong currents outside the slack tide window. Limited visibility. Confined space entry. This is technical diving without technical training."
"Then we stay conservative," Aya said. "We don't push bottom time, we don't take unnecessary risks, we get in and out during the slack tide window. Twenty minutes maximum."
"And if something goes wrong?" Amina asked, voice tight with the frustration of being sidelined. "If Aya's wound opens, if the current shifts, if you get disoriented in low vis?"
"You’ll be coordinating rescue from the surface," Aya said, meeting her eyes. "We'll have tether lines, underwater lights, and communication signals. You and Leyla will track our position, monitor the current, and watch the clock. That's not a backup role, that's life support."
Amina held her gaze for a moment, then nodded reluctantly. "Fine. But if you die down there because you're too stubborn to acknowledge physical limitations or underestimate me, I'm going to be very angry with your corpse."
"Noted," Aya said. She kept her voice even, but was touched by the concern.
Karim stepped forward, showing the same mix of determination and fear that Aya felt. "So we're decided? Aya and I dive, Amina and Leyla handle surface coordination?"
"We're decided," Leyla confirmed. She made notes on her tablet. "I'll source dive equipment in Bodrum. Two full kits, high-capacity tanks, backup regulators, reef hooks, underwater lights, tether lines, the works. Plus, the materials for Aya's wound and enough antibiotics to fight off whatever infection she's going to come back with."
"Sounds perfect," Aya said, ignoring the way her side throbbed at the thought of pressure changes and physical exertion.
"This is a terrible plan," Amina muttered, but without real protest.
"Most of our plans are terrible," Aya said. "This one's just slightly more dangerous than usual."
"Not reassuring. What if Aegis is down there?" Amina asked.
"Then it gets complicated," Leyla interrupted. "But they'll hopefully still be setting up surveillance, preparing their own team, unsure on the optimal time window and location. Remember, they didn’t recover the detailed charts we found, and they don’t have Hassan’s research. If we hit the window fast and clean, we might be in and out before they realize we've moved."
Might. The word hung in Aya’s mind.
"Before we finalize," Hamza said, his voice cutting through the tactical discussion with unexpected gravity, "you need to understand what you're up against. Not Aegis, that's just resources and firepower. I'm talking about Malik specifically."
The group fell quiet, attention shifting to the old man.
"He learned from your father, Aya. Learned from Hassan's resistance." Hamza's weathered hands gestured slowly, emphasizing his point. "Malik doesn't force anymore; he cultivates. He lets you do the work, then offers partnership when you're exhausted and desperate and facing impossible odds. Every confrontation you've had has been a recruitment pitch disguised as a threat. The cemetery, the safehouse, the mosque. He could have killed you at any of those moments. But he didn't."
"Because he wants the components," Karim said.
"Because he wants you to want to use the components," Hamza corrected. "There's a crucial difference. If he forces you, you're victims. If he convinces you, you're collaborators. Complicit. It changes the narrative from oppressor and oppressed to philosophical disagreement between equals. It makes him right and your father wrong."
Aya grimaced and nodded. "That's why he keeps showing up personally."
"Exactly. He's not there to threaten, he's there to teach. To make his case. To show you that Hassan's fear was misplaced." Hamza looked at each of them in turn. "The Silent Meridian has watched this pattern for centuries. The most dangerous enemies aren't the ones who attack; they're the ones who seduce. Who make oppression look like partnership, who make danger look like opportunity, who make the terrible choice seem reasonable."
"So what do we do?" Amina asked.
"Recognize it for what it is," Hamza said. "He's brilliant. His strategy is sound. If you were ordinary archaeologists, if these were normal components, if the stakes were anything less than catastrophic, he'd probably convince you. Hassan was almost convinced in the early years. That's how good Malik is at making his vision seem inevitable and right."
He placed his hand on the case containing the components. "But you're not ordinary, and the stakes are everything. So you do what Hassan did: you choose principle over persuasion, caution over courage, protection over progress. You choose, knowing that Malik will call it cowardice. And you carry that weight because someone has to."
"As soon as night falls, we roll out," Aya said.
"Yalla," Amina said, banging her hand on the table.
Hamza shifted on the stone bench, wincing as his injured ankle protested. He leaned forward, hands clasped. "Before you all scatter to prepare," he said, looking at each of them in turn, "I want you to understand what you're walking into. What this truly means."
The group fell silent, attention focusing on the old man.
"Hassan died for this," Hamza continued. "My grandfather spent his entire life protecting these secrets. The Silent Meridian has bled for four centuries, keeping this knowledge from the wrong hands. Men and women whose names you'll never know, whose families never understood why they died, who were buried with lies and half-truths because the truth was too dangerous to speak."
His weathered hands gestured to the packs containing the components. "You carry that legacy now. All of you. Every choice you make from this moment forward is measured against centuries of sacrifice. Don't waste it. Don't dishonor it. Don't—"
The sound was almost imperceptible. A distant crack, barely audible over the wind whistling through the cave entrance.
Aya registered it instantly: supersonic round. 
High-powered rifle. Large caliber.
Hamza's head snapped back violently, a fine mist of red spraying across the stone wall behind him. His body remained upright for a single heartbeat, held by momentum and the bench beneath him, then slumped sideways. He collapsed onto the cave floor with an impossibly quiet thump.
For one frozen moment, the universe contracted. Hamza's body sprawled across stone, blood spreading in a dark pool that caught the lamplight and threw it back in crimson reflections.
Then Mehmet screamed.
"DOWN!" Aya roared, already moving, her body reacting before her mind finished processing. She tackled Leyla sideways as a second round cracked through the air. The bullet sparked off the back wall, stone chips exploding outward, the ricochet whining past Amina's head.
Karim grabbed Mehmet, dragging him behind the stone table. The younger man fought against his grip, trying to reach his uncle.
Amina was already in motion, weapon drawn, moving toward the cave entrance in a combat crouch. Another round punched through the opening, forcing her back into cover behind a natural pillar. "Sniper!" she called, pressing herself against the stone. "Long range. Eight hundred meters minimum. Elevated position, southeast ridge."
Aya's hands moved on autopilot, checking Hamza's pulse even though the exit wound at the base of his skull had already told her everything she needed to know. Nothing. No pulse. No breath. No life.
"Mehmet, lights!" Amina shouted. "Kill them all!"
Chapter 32
Mehmet's sister moved with surprising speed for someone in shock. She killed the oil lamps one by one, darkness spreading through the cave like spilled ink. Outside, the last daylight was fading to twilight, shadows deepening across the valley.
"They tracked us," Aya said. "Bastards have been watching. Waiting for everyone to arrive. Waiting for the perfect shot."
"Why just him?" Karim's voice came from the darkness, hollow with horror. "They could have killed all of us. They had time, they had position. Why execute Hamza specifically?"
"Message," Amina said grimly, still scanning the darkening landscape for muzzle flash, for any sign of the shooter. "Showing us they can reach anyone, anywhere. That no one is safe. But still leaving us alive, toying with us, letting us do their work for them."
"Or they needed him dead," Aya said, pieces clicking together with brutal clarity. "He was the keeper. The last direct connection to the Silent Meridian's knowledge of this region. The one person who knew locations, safe houses, and protocols we haven't discovered. They eliminated a strategic asset. Removed him from the board."
Mehmet was making sounds that made Aya's chest ache, the grief and rage and helplessness compressed into wordless heaves. His sister had her arms around him, holding him back from Hamza's body, holding him together, barely holding herself together.
"We have to move," Leyla said, her voice tight. "Sniper means ground team. They'll be closing on this position to confirm the kill, to trap us while we're pinned down."
As if in response, vehicle engines growled in the distance. Multiple engines. Growing louder. Approaching from the access road.
"Mehmet," Aya said, gripping his shoulder hard through the fog of shock. "Look at me. Mehmet, look at me."
His eyes found hers, red and wild and broken.
"I'm sorry," she said. "I am so, so sorry. But we need to get you and your sister away from here. Right now. Is there another exit?"
He stared at her like she was speaking a language he didn't understand. For a moment, she thought he'd simply shut down, would sit with his uncle's corpse and wait for Aegis to arrive. Then something shifted behind his eyes, the same stubborn determination she'd seen in Hamza.
"Northwest exit," he managed, voice raw. “Drops down to the valley. Three kilometers to the village."
"Take it," Aya ordered. "Both of you. Right now. Don't stop, don't look back. When you reach the village, call the authorities. Report a shooting."
"What do I tell them?" Mehmet's voice cracked. "What do I say happened?"
"You were showing your uncle the caves. Someone opened fire without warning. Random violence. You don't know why. You fled. His body is still here. That's the story. That's all you know." Aya hated every word. 
"I can't leave him," Mehmet said, looking at Hamza's body.
"You have to," Aya said firmly. "Your uncle died protecting knowledge from these people. Died protecting us so we could finish the mission. If you stay, if they capture you, they'll torture you for information, things you may not even realize you know. Hamza learned so much from his father and grandfather without even realizing why it was important. Every safe house, every cache, every contact the Silent Meridian has left. You think Hamza would want that?"
Mehmet nodded once, sharp and painful. He pulled away from his sister, moved to Hamza's body, and knelt beside it. He pressed his forehead to his uncle's, whispered something that Aya couldn't hear.
His sister did the same, her own tears falling on Hamza's weathered face.
Then they were moving.
"Components," Aya snapped, already at the table. "Everything we inventoried. Grab it all. We leave together in Leyla's vehicle."
Glass fragment and cylinder into Aya's pack. Calibration rings split between Karim and Leyla. Crystal heart with Amina. Assembly charts divided and distributed.
Through the cave entrance, Aya could see headlights cutting through the dusk, multiple vehicles climbing the distant access road.
"They'll have thermal imaging," Leyla said, checking her pistol, ensuring a round was chambered. "Possibly drones. We can't outrun that in one vehicle on mountain roads."
"So we don't run," Aya said. "We drive like civilians. Lights on, normal speed, looking like we belong here. Two cars. Mehmet's truck going one direction, your rental going another. They don't know which one we're in."
"They'll stop both," Karim said.
"Maybe," Aya agreed. "But Mehmet's story gives us cover. Witnesses heard shots, reported violence, and fled in panic. That's reasonable behavior. We're just terrified tourists who got caught up in something horrible."
"I'll lead, you follow,” Leyla said.
They divided quickly. Leyla took Amina in the SUV. Aya climbed into Mehmet's truck with Karim; the interior smelled of cigarettes and old leather. The engine coughed twice before catching, rougher than the rental but functional.
"Normal speed," Aya reminded through the window.
Both vehicles pulled onto the access road, heading down the mountain, headlights cutting through the growing darkness.
The SUV's headlights illuminated the winding road ahead. Behind them, Cappadocia's fairy chimneys stood indifferent to human grief, standing as they'd stood for millennia while countless lives played out and ended in their shadows.
For the first hour, Karim said nothing. He sat in the passenger seat, hands clenched in his lap, staring at the road ahead as fairy chimneys gave way to rolling hills, then pine forest. Ahead of them, Leyla's SUV maintained a steady distance, taillights marking their path.
Finally, as they descended into a thicker forest and the adrenaline began to wear off, Karim spoke.
"I need to tell you something," he said quietly. "Hamza told me, before—" His voice caught. "Before."
Aya glanced at him, then back to the road. "About my father."
"Yeah." Karim swallowed. "He said Hassan and Malik knew each other, and he’d interacted with them. That they were colleagues before everything went wrong."
The truck's headlights illuminated the winding mountain road, pine trunks flashing past on either side. Aya's hands tightened on the steering wheel.
"You already know they worked together," Karim began, his voice careful. "Those papers you decoded in Istanbul proved the academic collaboration, the years of joint research. But Hamza knew the rest of it. The personal side. Hassan confided in him during those visits to Cappadocia."
Aya's hands tightened on the steering wheel. "What personal side?"
"They met at an archaeology conference in the mid-nineties," Karim continued. "Hassan was presenting research on Ottoman astronomical instruments. Malik was there representing Aegis, a young upstart agent already interested in historical technology with potential applications. They connected over the precision of medieval engineering. Started corresponding."
The highway curved through switchbacks, descending toward sea level. Aya navigated automatically, her mind elsewhere.
"For nearly a decade, they collaborated," Karim said. "Co-authored those papers you found, shared research, built what Hassan thought was a genuine friendship. Until the Cappadocia visit nineteen years ago, when Hassan tried activating a partial system. That was the catastrophe Hamza described. Partial reproduced components, enough to test the principles."
"And he saw something," Aya said quietly. "Through the dimensional fractures."
"Something aware. Intelligent. Waiting." Karim's voice was steady but strained. "After that, Hassan tried to convince Malik to abandon it. Spent months arguing that this wasn't progress, it was opening doors that should stay closed. Malik refused. Saw it as humanity's next great leap forward, proof of technology that could transcend modern limitations and obliterate understandings of science."
"They had a falling out."
"Complete break. Stopped speaking. Hassan went underground and joined the Silent Meridian, or maybe he'd always been part of it. Started hiding components, scattering them across continents, making it impossible for any one person or organization to assemble the complete system. He knew Malik would keep searching indefinitely with Aegis funding. So he dedicated his professional life to keeping the lens fractured, to preventing this type of research."
Karim glanced at Aya. "Hamza said your father visited Cappadocia at least four more times after that initial experiment he worked on. Sometimes staying for weeks, working with Hamza's family on Silent Meridian protocols, encoding hiding places, and establishing fail-safes. They became close. Hassan needed someone who understood what he'd given up by choosing caution. Hamza was that person."
The highway straightened, cutting through a valley where morning mist clung to hollows.
"Hassan talked about Malik constantly those last years," Karim continued. "Not with anger, but with grief. Like he'd lost a brother. That's why Hamza knew so much about Malik's methods, his psychology. Hassan had spent years trying to understand how his closest colleague had become so willing to risk everything."
"What actually ended it?" Aya asked.
Karim nodded slowly. "Apparently, Hassan called Malik from Colombia, not to reconcile, but to warn him one final time. He emailed Hamza about it to warn him that Malik might come to speak to him." He pulled out his phone, found the note he'd written of Hamza’s story. "Hassan had found evidence of a failed activation that left permanent damage. A thin place in reality that still existed eight hundred years later, still leaking energy, still affecting the area. Mutations in local wildlife. Unexplained electromagnetic anomalies. People reporting visions, dissociative episodes."
The car hummed along the empty highway.
"Hassan realized that activation didn't just succeed or fail; it left scars on our world that never healed," Karim said. "He told Malik that the lens isn't a tool for transcendence. It's a weapon that wounds reality itself, and those wounds are permanent." Karim's voice hardened. "Malik said they’d account for those mistakes, control the variables, and achieve clean activation. He said Hassan’s fear was understandable, but not wise. Cowardice."
"He called my father a coward."
Karim shrugged. "Anyway, the conversation ended with Hassan saying: 'If you do this, Malik, you do it without me. And I'll make sure you never get the chance.' Malik's response was silence. Hassan died in a tunnel collapse sixteen hours later."
The implications hung heavy.
"Hamza didn't think it was an accident," Karim said quietly. "But he also said Hassan was working in unstable conditions, taking risks he normally wouldn't because he was desperate to find evidence that would convince Malik to stop. So maybe it was an accident, maybe it was murder, maybe it was Hassan's desperation catching up to him. We'll never know for certain. What we do know is that Malik has spent seven years trying to achieve the clean activation Hassan said was impossible. Trying to show that the fear that ended their friendship, that likely got your father killed, was misplaced."
"This…" Aya took a long moment to process Karim's words. "So my father and Malik weren't just colleagues who disagreed. They were close. Brothers, basically. And Malik did murder him. I could feel it, I knew it."
She gripped the steering wheel tightly in the dashboard light.
"Hamza told me right before we split up in Cappadocia," Karim said quietly.
“Fuck, I wish I could ask him questions. Why wouldn’t he have told me?”
 "He made me promise to wait until after Bodrum to tell you. Didn't want you distracted during the dive, didn't want your anger at Malik affecting your judgment underwater."
Aya nodded slowly. "He was trying to protect the mission. Being a guardian. He was right.”
"Sorry if I should have honored his request. But sitting here, keeping it from you, felt wrong. You deserve to know what Hassan fought for. What Hamza died protecting. What we're still fighting against."
Ahead, Leyla's taillights flashed twice. They were approaching a town, lights visible through the trees. Time to look normal, innocent, push everything else away.
"When we face Malik," Aya said, voice steady, "I want him to know Hassan won. That all his hunting and killing and corporate resources couldn't break what my father built. That Çelebi and Hamza and the guardians held the line."
"They did," Karim said quietly. "Right up until a bullet found Hamza. But now we carry the torch, you carry the torch. Hassan would be proud of what you've done, what you're still doing."
Aya nodded, not trusting herself to speak.
Karim fell silent, staring at the road ahead. His hand moved unconsciously to his pocket, to the small weight he'd been carrying since the workshop.
‘Tell her,’ Hamza had said. Let her decide its fate with the rest. You’re a team.
But now Hamza was dead, killed by the very people they were racing to stop. And Karim found himself wondering: if the wafer was the difference between controlled operation and catastrophic failure, wasn't it safer in his hands? Wasn't it insurance against Malik succeeding even if everything else went wrong?
He opened his mouth to tell Aya. Closed it. Opened it again.
"What?" Aya asked, catching his hesitation.
"Nothing," he said. "Just... thinking about Hamza."
He told himself it was temporary. He'd tell her after Bodrum. After the dive. When they had time to discuss it properly.
Hamza's voice echoed in his memory: This is not your choice to make alone. Karim pushed it down and watched the road.
Chapter 33
They reached Bodrum after dawn had fully broken over the harbor, the city cascading down hillsides toward the harbor in tiers of whitewashed buildings. The Castle of St. Peter dominated the waterfront, a massive Crusader fortress of honey-colored stone, imposing even after six centuries. The marina spread before it, a forest of masts and rigging, luxury yachts bobbing gently in the swell.
Aya slowed as they entered the city proper, navigating through traffic that had thickened considerably. Tourists walked in every direction: couples strolling hand-in-hand, families with children, groups of young travelers with backpacks. The season was ending, but Bodrum still held onto the last clutches of summer crowds, the restaurants and shops doing brisk business despite the calendar inching toward autumn.
"There," Leyla said quietly, nodding toward a black SUV parked near the marina entrance. "Government plates. Two men inside, watching the docks."
Aya spotted them through the flow of pedestrians. Too alert, too focused. They weren't enjoying the view; they were working.
"Hello, Aegis," she confirmed. "Nice official cover."
"Question is whether they’ve figured out specifically what we're after or just covering likely entry points."
Aya pulled Mehmet’s truck into a public parking lot three blocks from the marina, tucking it between a tour bus and a panel van. The Renault SUV followed moments later, Karim finding a spot two rows over. They gathered between the vehicles, speaking quietly.
"They're positioned to watch anyone approaching the marina by vehicle," Amina said. "But they can't watch everyone on foot. There’s way too much tourist traffic."
"We split up," Aya decided. "Pairs, approach from different directions, lose ourselves in the crowds. Meet at the boat. Leyla, which berth?"
