"C-17, west side of the marina. Blue and white gulet, twenty-five meters."
They separated into pairs, with Aya and Amina taking the northern approach through the commercial district and Karim and Leyla heading south along the waterfront promenade. Aya watched Karim walk away, his hand finding the strap of his pack, carrying components that could level cities, unsure what he would do with them if given the choice.
She and Amina moved through narrow streets that climbed briefly before descending toward the harbor. The district was a maze of boutiques selling jewelry, beach toys, and clothes, cafés with tables spilling onto cobblestones. Tourist traffic provided perfect cover, and they were just two more women navigating the midday crowds.
But Aya felt exposed in a way she hadn't since Cairo. Her face had been broadcast across social media. The balloon theft had made news, and someone might recognize her, might make the connection, might place a call to authorities who would be very interested in her location.
They reached the marina without incident, entering through a pedestrian gate that led past a harbourmaster's office. Rows of floating docks extended into the harbor, each lined with vessels ranging from small sailing dinghies to massive motor yachts worth millions of euros.
"C-17 is this way," Amina said, reading the berth numbers.
They found Captain Deniz's gulet near the end of the western dock. The vessel was beautifully adorned with teak decking, brass fittings that caught the sun, and a hull painted in classic blue and white stripes. It looked like it had been built decades ago and maintained with obsessive care ever since.
A man stood on the aft deck, coiling rope. Sixties, sun-darkened skin weathered by decades at sea, relaxed eyes that Aya doubted would be surprised by much.
"Captain Deniz?" Aya called from the dock.
He looked up, smiled slightly. "Merhaba, come aboard. Omar's friends are my friends. Though he warned me you bring interesting troubles."
"Yeah, we need discreet transport to a specific offshore site," Aya said. "Diving equipment. And a captain who won't ask questions or talk about us to anyone else."
Deniz's smile didn't change. "Questions I don't ask, then I don't have to lie about the answers later." He gestured toward the dock where the black SUV was visible in the distance. "Those government people showed up yesterday. Asking about divers, about underwater ruins. Making the harbor nervous. But do not worry, I am no fan of the government."
"Do they know specifically where we're going?"
"The ruins they mentioned offshore, many locals know of them. It’s a popular diving spot for adventurous tourists." Deniz shrugged. "But Omar mentioned an underwater tidal chamber? That’s a different story altogether. Few remember those old stories. If they have researchers, they might piece it together. If not, they're just watching and waiting. They didn’t ask enough specifics."
Karim and Leyla arrived minutes later, approaching from the opposite direction. They boarded quickly, Deniz casting off mooring lines immediately, firing up the engine with a short rumble that sent gulls scattering from nearby pilings.
They motored out of the marina at idle speed, Deniz navigating through the dense traffic of ferries heading to Greek islands, tour vessels taking sightseers around the peninsula, and private yachts coming or going. No one paid them particular attention, just another traditional gulet heading out for a day sail.
As they cleared the harbor breakwater and the open water spread before them, Aya felt her tension ease. On the water, they were harder to track, harder to trap, and there were fewer people to spot her face. 
The gulet rose and fell with the gentle Mediterranean swell, wind filling the canvas awning that provided shade over the aft deck.
"Do we go to these ruins, yes? They are three kilometers offshore," Deniz said once they'd cleared the coastal traffic. He gestured toward the horizon, where nothing but blue water was visible. "Twenty-something meters down. The bottom is mixed with rock, sand, and some coral. The current can be tricky."
Aya nodded. “I guess so.”
Deniz opened a hatch, revealing the equipment storage below. Wetsuits in various sizes hung from hooks. BCDs, tanks, and regulators, all neatly organized and clearly well-maintained. Reef hooks, backup regulators, and underwater lights in waterproof cases.
"Standard recreational setup," Deniz explained, "but with modifications for the conditions. Higher-capacity tanks so you'll have extended bottom time if you need it. Reef hooks to anchor against any unexpected current. Two backup regulators per diver because the chamber entrance can be tight. It is better to have redundancy."
Karim examined the equipment, checking pressure gauges, testing the regulator function. "What about visibility?"
"During slack tide, good. Maybe fifteen feet inside the chamber, twenty or more outside in the main ruins area. The water's calm, sediment settles. But if the current picks up, which it will, visibility drops to nothing. You'll be in a washing machine."
Deniz spread a laminated chart on the deck, showing the dive site in detail. Depth contours, compass bearings, and notable features marked in waterproof ink.
"We’ll approach from the east," he said, tracing the route. "Follow this reef line down. It drops in stages. You'll hopefully find any tidal chamber somewhere around there." He tapped a specific point. "Look for a natural cave mouth, maybe two meters wide. That'll be your entry."
"Navigation underwater?" Amina asked, studying the chart with professional focus.
"They’ll have compasses. Not complicated if conditions are good. But you need to time it perfectly. Slack tide is at 4 AM tomorrow. Twenty minutes, maybe twenty-five if we're lucky. That's your window."
"What happens if we're inside when the current picks up?" Karim asked.
Deniz met his eyes. "Don't be. Tidal chambers fill and empty with the tide cycle. When the current flows in, it pushes water through that narrow entrance with incredible force. You'd be pinned against the back wall, unable to exit, waiting for the cycle to reverse."
"How long is the full cycle?" Leyla asked.
"Twelve hours from slack to slack. If you miss the window tomorrow morning, the next safe entry is tomorrow evening. I’m chilling, but I think you don't have that kind of time."
“So that’s it,” Aya said. “One chance. Twenty minutes. Everything is riding on precision timing. The crown lens will be there, protected by time and tide, waiting for someone who understands the rhythms well enough to retrieve it.” 
Amina punched Aya and Karim both on the shoulder. “The two of you have this, no problem at all. But I’d kill to be down there and have your backs.”
Deniz killed the engine, and they drifted on the open water, rising and falling in the rolling waves. The coast of Bodrum was visible in a line of white buildings against distant green hills.
“Alright,” Karim said. “Who wants to go over diving again with me? It’s been a while.”
“I did not hear that,” Deniz said as he retreated to the steering wheel. “I’m finding somewhere to anchor nearby that isn’t too conspicuous. After you do what you have to do, keep up appearances just in case, and act like tourists. Swim, snorkel, sunbathe. I’ll prepare dinner.”
Chapter 34
The gulet rocked gently in the afternoon swells as Aya and Amina worked to give Karim a diving refresher. The sun beat down from a cloudless sky, heat radiating off the teak deck, the Mediterranean stretching in all directions, blindingly blue.
"Okay," Amina said, checking Karim's BCD for the third time. "Show me how you clear your regulator if it floods."
Karim sat at the edge of the dive platform, legs dangling in water. He demonstrated the technique with a sharp exhale through the regulator, clearing water from the mouthpiece.
"Good. Now, buddy breathing. Your air runs out, I'm your backup. What do you do?"
"Signal you," Karim said, making the throat-cutting gesture that meant out of air. "Take your backup regulator, purge it, breathe."
"Show me."
They worked through the drills. Emergency ascents. Buoyancy control. Mask clearing. Compass navigation. Karim’s muscle memory slowly returned under Amina's coaching.
"You're doing fine," Amina said after an hour. "Rusty, but competent. You'll remember the rest once you're down there."
"What if I don't?" Karim asked quietly.
"Then I'll handle it," Aya said with a soft smile. "That's why we dive in teams. Nobody's alone down there."
Aya sat in the shade of the awning, watching Karim regain confidence was reassuring. Watching him depend on Amina created a knot in her chest she didn't want to examine.
Around them, the Mediterranean stretched impossibly blue, a shade between turquoise and sapphire that didn't exist anywhere but the Aegean. The water was so clear that Aya could see the sandy bottom fifteen meters down, every ripple distinct, every piece of broken shell. Other boats dotted the horizon with white hulls and tall masts, some under sail, others anchored like their gulet. The hills behind Bodrum rose golden and brown, terraced with olive trees and dotted with white villas that caught the afternoon light.
Deniz appeared from below with cold beers and a suggestion. "You want to look like tourists? Actually be tourists for a few hours. Swim. Snorkel. Enjoy the water. Those government people are watching the harbor through binoculars. Give them something boring to see."
"Right on," Aya said. She looked at Amina. "Feel like a swim?"
"Always."
Aya dove from the platform, the brief moment of freefall followed by the plunge into blue, bubbles roaring past as she descended three meters before kicking back toward light. The water was perfect. Cool enough to be refreshing against sun-heated skin, warm enough that entering it felt like slipping into silk.
She surfaced, shaking water from her hair, and found Amina already in, floating on her back. The afternoon sun painted her olive skin in gold, caught the silver tattoos that traced from shoulder to fingertips.
Karim entered more cautiously, climbing down the ladder rather than diving. Aya could tell he was still preoccupied by the impending dive.
"Relax," Amina called to him. "This is the calm before the storm."
They swam without purpose, lazing about in the dense saltwater, diving to touch the sandy bottom, chasing fish that scattered at their approach, and floating in the gentle current.
Aya found herself drawn to watching Amina move through the water. Her strong legs kicking, body undulating with each stroke, the way she'd surface with water streaming from her hair. The way her one-piece swimsuit was cut high on her hips, water turning it darker, making it cling even tighter.
"Race you to the anchor line," Amina said, already kicking away before Aya could respond.
Aya followed, diving deep and kicking hard, lungs starting to burn by the time she touched the chain. Amina was already there, grinning in victory, bubbles streaming from her mouth as she headed back toward the light.
From below, Aya watched Amina's body move in slow motion against the water, back arching with each kick, swimsuit riding up even higher on her hips. Fifteen meters of crystal water turned her into something ethereal, Amina’s body backlit by afternoon sun, every detail sharp in impossible clarity.
They surfaced together, breathing hard, Amina's shoulder brushing Aya's as they treaded water.
"Still got it," Amina said.
"You cheated. Started before me."
"That's a tactical advantage." Amina's grin was unrepentant. "If you know you can’t win, find a way to change the rules. You taught me that."
They floated there, faces inches apart, water lapping between them.
"We should head back," Aya blurted out suddenly, voice rougher than intended. "Sun's getting lower."
Amina held her gaze for a moment longer than necessary. "Yeah. We should."
Neither of them moved immediately.
Then Karim called from the boat, suggesting rest and asking about dinner plans. The moment broke.
They swam back separately. Amina reached the ladder first, gripped the rails, and pulled herself up. While she climbed, Aya treaded water directly behind her, checking the horizon, adjusting her swimsuit straps, and studying the boat's hull with sudden fascination. Anything to avoid watching the stretch of fabric and expanses of exposed skin still dripping saltwater.
By the time Aya finally hauled herself up the ladder, Amina had already moved to the towels Deniz had laid out, wringing water from her hair, and moving to lie out in the sun.


By the time the sun descended toward the western horizon, the smell of grilled fish and charcoal permeated the air. Aya and Amina retreated to deck chairs, letting the last warmth soak into skin that smelled of salt and sunscreen. Karim had gone below to study his dive charts again, seeking comfort in preparation.
Leyla emerged from the cabin where she'd spent most of the afternoon, face carefully neutral. She caught Aya's eye briefly, unspoken communication passing between them, then retreated below.
"She's become distant," Amina observed quietly.
"She's intelligence trained," Aya said. "Probably compartmentalizing."
The sun touched the horizon, seemed to pause there, then slipped below in the sudden way of Mediterranean sunsets, light to dusk in minutes with no prolonged twilight.
Deniz appeared on deck, wiping his hands on a towel. "Food's ready. Come eat before it gets cold."
They ate on the aft deck under strings of lights Deniz had hung from the rigging. The night air was cool against skin still warm from the afternoon sun.
The meal was simple: fresh fish that flaked perfectly under fork pressure, bread for soaking up olive oil and lemon, olives, cheese, and tomatoes sliced thick. After days of running, of eating standing up or in vehicles or not at all, the ritual of sitting around a table felt almost ceremonial.
No one spoke much. The exhaustion of the past week was catching up, making conversation feel like too much effort. They ate with the single-minded focus of people who'd pushed their bodies past reasonable limits, who knew another test was coming in hours, who wanted to feel normal for just a few more minutes.
Karim finished first, excused himself quietly, and disappeared into the forward cabin with his notebook. Aya watched him go, saw the tension in his shoulders.
Leyla silently followed suit, heading into her cabin with a solemn look affixed to her face.
That left Aya and Amina alone with Deniz, who was already standing and clearing plates.
"Big operation later," he said. "You guys should rest. I’ll take care of these."
"Thanks. Yeah," Aya agreed, though rest felt impossible. "We should."
But neither of them moved immediately. They sat in the gentle rocking of the boat, watching stars emerge in overwhelming profusion, the Milky Way a river of light overhead. To the north, the lights of Bodrum's harbor glowed softly against the hills. Behind them, the darker silhouettes of other anchored boats rocked in perfect synchronization with their own, halyards clinking against masts in a rhythm like distant bells.
"Tomorrow's going to be interesting," Amina said finally. "Underwater, limited visibility, narrow spaces. Perfect conditions for everything to go wrong."
"We've both done harder dives," Aya said.
"True. But never with stakes this high." Amina's hand found her water glass, traced condensation on the outside. "Never with the knowledge that if we fail, Malik wins. That he activates something that could give the most evil people we know a stranglehold on global energy, undoing everything we sacrificed in Morocco. Or tear holes in reality. Or both."
“We won't fail," Aya said with more confidence than she felt.
"But you're worried about Karim."
It wasn't a question. Aya didn't pretend otherwise.
"He's got so little diving experience," she admitted. "And it's more than that. He's getting too invested in the preservation question. Starting to sound like he's rationalizing reasons to study the lens rather than destroy it."
"Academics fall in love with their subjects," Amina said. "It's what makes them good at what they do. Also, what makes them dangerous when the subject is dangerous." She looked at Aya directly. "But you can't pull him back from that edge on your own. He has to choose for himself."
They sat in silence as the last light faded completely, leaving only afterglow and stars and the gentle phosphorescence in the water where fish disturbed the surface. The Mediterranean was so calm it reflected the stars perfectly, above and below, light doubling, making it feel like floating in space rather than anchored on water. Small waves lapped in irregular rhythm.
"Hamza would have loved this," Amina said quietly, breaking the silence. "Mehmet told me he used to take his grandchildren fishing off Antalya every summer."
Aya's chest tightened. They hadn't spoken about it since the escape, all of them carrying the weight privately. "He deserved better than dying in a cave, talking about legacy."
"He got exactly what he wanted," Amina replied. "Died protecting what mattered, surrounded by people continuing his work. Most people don't get that." She paused. "My grandfather died in a hospital, machines beeping, fluorescent lights. He would have preferred Hamza's way."
"Doesn't make it fair."
"No," Amina agreed. "But fair stopped being relevant a long time ago. We're just trying to make his death mean something."
They fell silent again, the words settling over them like the darkness.
"Tomorrow when you're underwater," Amina said finally, "trust me to hold things down topside. I know I'm not the mystical compass anymore. But I'm still good at this. I'll keep everyone safe."
"I know," Aya said. And she did.
Aya and Amina sat in comfortable silence, both avoiding the moment when they'd have to retreat to separate spaces, when the illusion of normalcy would end, and reality would reassert itself.
Deniz finished cleaning, then disappeared below with a muttered goodnight. As he disappeared into his own cabin, Aya retreated to the forward cabin, hoping Karim might be asleep. 
He wasn't.
Karim was lying on his back in the narrow berth, staring at the cabin ceiling, notebook and pen resting on his chest. The single overhead light cast harsh shadows that made his face look older, more worn than his thirty-two years.
"Hey," Aya said quietly, closing the door behind her.
"Hey." He didn't look up.
The silence stretched. Aya stood awkwardly in the confined space, uncertain whether to stay or leave. The cabin felt smaller than it had that morning, the air thicker.
"You've been distant," Karim said finally.
"I'm focused," Aya replied, moving to sit on the edge of the berth. "Big dive in hours."
"It's more than that." He turned onto his side, looking at her with eyes that had always been too perceptive for comfort. "We used to talk. Really talk. Now it's just logistics and planning and avoiding."
Aya wanted to deny it, but couldn't. "Things are complicated, but I don’t feel like you’ve been very open with me either. I’m not sure what happened."
"Are we okay?" The question came out smaller than he'd intended.
"Are we?" Aya turned it back.
"You tell me."
"I don't know how to answer that."
"That's the problem," Karim said, frustration breaking through. "I used to be certain about us. Now I’m not, and you’re not either. I don’t know what happens after all this ends. It feels like it’ll never end. We’ve been fighting and surviving for months. I don’t know how long I can do this."
He sat up suddenly, closing the distance between them. "Maybe we just need to—" He kissed her before finishing the thought, choosing physical connection over words.
Aya kissed him back, aware of how mechanical it felt even as she did it, fueled by little desire.
He pulled her closer with something that felt like desperation, hands pulling off her shirt, trying to prove something to both of them through touch when words had failed.
She responded the way she thought she should, hands on his shoulders, but her mind stayed detached. Going through the motions, performing rather than feeling. And she could tell he knew it, could feel it in the way his movements became more urgent, more insistent, like he was trying to force a connection that wasn't there. And she knew he felt the same; it was in his eyes.
"Karim—" she started to pull back.
"Please." His voice was rough. "Just... let's just try."
But trying was the problem. Trying meant they were both pretending.
His hands moved across her skin with increasing urgency, mouth on her neck, breath coming faster. But there was no heat in it. Just two people desperately attempting to resurrect something that had died without either of them noticing when.
She pulled back more firmly. "This isn't working."
He froze, then sat back hard, running both hands through his hair. "No. It's not."
The admission hung between them.
"When did we—" Aya started.
"I don't know." Karim's voice carried exhaustion and something like relief. "Somewhere between Morocco and here. Maybe before. Maybe it never really worked, and we were just too busy surviving to notice."
"That's not true. It was good once."
"It was. Better than good." He looked at her. "But were we just convenient for each other? Two people caught in the same chaos, looking for something stable to hold onto?"
Aya opened her mouth to argue, but couldn't find words to counter his, because deep down she feared he might be right.
"I think—" Karim stopped, then started again. "I think we've been holding onto what we thought we should be. What looked right on paper. The timid scholar and the recovering thief turned into action heroes. Makes a good story."
"Karim—"
"But you're not here anymore," he continued, voice steady despite everything. "You're somewhere else. Someone else. And I've been pretending not to notice because admitting it meant admitting I picked wrong. That I followed you into all this danger for something that was already ending."
"I never meant—"
"I know." He turned away, facing the cabin wall. "Neither of us did. But that doesn't change what is. We’re both feeling the same thing."
They lay in the narrow berth, not touching despite the confined space. Both awake. Both aware that words, once spoken, couldn’t be taken back.
"I'm sorry," Aya said quietly.
"So am I." Karim's voice was barely audible.
After an hour of pretending to sleep, Aya gave up. She slipped from the cabin carefully, climbed the ladder to the deck, where the air felt less suffocating, and the stars seemed less oppressive than the silence below.
She found Amina already there, knees bent, leaning against the mast, looking at stars reflected on dark water.
"Couldn't sleep either?" Aya asked.
"Body clock remembers dive schedules," Amina replied without turning. "My adrenaline's already starting to spike for you guys."
Aya sat beside her, leaving careful space and settling into silence. The same careful space they'd been maintaining for weeks, the same pretense that proximity didn't matter.
Above them, stars wheeled in their ancient patterns. Around them, water lapped gently against the hull, the sound hypnotic and eternal.
"What do you want, Aya?" Amina asked finally, voice quiet but direct. “You never answered me.”
"I don't know anymore."
"Yes, you do." Amina turned to look at her.
They sat in silence, Aya growing hyperaware of Amina beside her, the way she smelled of salt and sunscreen. She hadn’t come looking for her, but here they found each other. This was the moment to leave, to maintain the boundary. To think about Karim sleeping twenty feet away, about the comfortable relationship that made sense.
But Aya had spent months doing what she should do, being who she should be, choosing the life that looked right on paper, looked like salvation after years of questionable morals. And she was exhausted from it.
"I'm not asking you to choose or make any declarations," Amina said quietly. "Not tonight. I just—"
She stopped, whatever she was going to say dying as they looked at each other.
The moment extended, tension building until it was unbearable, until maintaining the careful distance felt more dishonest than closing it.
Aya knew Karim deserved better. Knew she'd regret this. But she leaned in anyway.
The kiss was gentle, questioning, giving Amina the chance to pull back. Then it exploded. Weeks of denial, tension. Years of unresolved history combusting. Amina's hand in Aya's hair, pulling her closer. Aya's hands gripping Amina's waist, then sliding under her shirt, finding warm skin that felt like coming home.
It was a betrayal, but it also felt real in a way nothing had felt real in as long as Aya could remember, like being herself without apology or performance.
Then they were kissing deeper, guilt and desire warring until desire won. Amina's back was against the mast, Aya pressed against her, hands relearning exactly how they fit together. How Amina tasted. How she made sounds low in her throat at Aya’s touch.
They broke apart suddenly, both breathing hard.
Aya pulled back further, pressing her spine against the opposite side of the mast. "We can't. Karim is right there."
"I know." Amina's voice was rough, but she didn't move closer. "I'm sorry. I shouldn't have—"
"It wasn't you." Aya's hands were shaking. "It's me. I wanted—" She stopped, couldn't finish.
They sat in charged silence. The guilt was crushing. Just the deck and a ladder and a thin cabin wall between this moment and Karim sleeping, unaware. The man who'd followed her into danger. Who deserved honesty at minimum, decency at least. She needed to talk to him first.
But the longing was crushing too. Years of it, suppressed and rationalized and carefully ignored. And now here it was, unavoidable.
"We should stop," Aya said, not moving.
"We should," Amina agreed, not moving either.
The moment stretched, and Aya could feel the weight of the choice pressing down.
Then footsteps sounded on the ladder below.
Deniz's head appeared briefly, checking on the anchor watch. “All good up here?” he called.
“Just can’t sleep,” Aya said.
He nodded. “I get it. Well, take care.”
There was a long silence after he disappeared below. Aya could still feel everywhere they'd been pressed together.
"That can't happen again," Aya said, voice unsteady.
"Okay," Amina replied.
Neither of them believed it.
Aya stood frozen another moment, torn between going back to Amina and going below. Every instinct screamed to finish what they'd started, to stop pretending she wanted anything else.
Aya went below and took the settee berth in the salon, lying awake. She couldn't face Karim right now. Not because the relationship was dead, that was true, but it didn't excuse it. Because she'd been a coward and should have been honest. 
She couldn't lie beside him with Amina's taste still in her mouth, with the phantom of her touch still lingering on her skin, with the certainty of what Aya wanted finally crystal clear, no more pretending.
At 3:30 AM, an alarm would sound. They'd suit up. She and Karim would descend into darkness to retrieve something that could save or doom them all. Amina would stay topside, watching them disappear, all carrying the certainty that everything was about to change. If the dive didn't kill them first.















3
Chapter 35
The alarm shattered what little peace the pre-dawn hours had offered. 3:30 AM came too soon and not soon enough. Aya woke on the salon settee, body stiff, mouth dry, conscience screaming. She lay still for a moment, remembering. She could still feel the way her body had responded with an honesty that made everything else feel like lying. And that was the worst part. Not that she'd done it, but that it had felt right.
On deck, Deniz was already checking equipment with methodical efficiency, his weathered hands moving over regulators and gauges.
The pre-dawn air carried the salt-mineral smell of the Mediterranean.
Karim emerged from the forward cabin, not looking at Aya, moving carefully, slowly. He had his wetsuit pulled up to his waist already, the neoprene creased from where he'd stored it. His hair stuck up at odd angles from restless sleep.
"Morning," Aya said, voice rough from too little sleep. She'd left last night, and she hadn't gone back.
"Morning," Karim replied, still not looking, busying himself with his dive computer, checking settings he'd already verified twice the night before.
Amina emerged from the aft berth, silent. She moved to her own gear and began her pre-dive checks, not acknowledging the tension crackling through the small space.
Deniz ran through emergency procedures again, current patterns, what to do if separated, and how to abort if conditions became unsafe. His dark eyes moved between Aya and Karim, noting the careful distance they maintained, saying nothing.
"Slack tide starts 4:05 AM," he said, checking his watch against the tide tables he'd printed and laminated, covered in pencil notations. "Twenty minutes, maybe twenty-two if the wind stays light. After that, the current picks up fast. You need to be topside before 4:30, understand?"
They both nodded.
Suiting up required a certain intimacy, as they checked each other’s gear. Karim moved behind Aya to test her tank connections, his fingers testing the O-ring seal, ensuring the first stage regulator was properly secured, avoiding eye contact even though his face was inches from hers.
Four years of rust showed in the way he had to consciously think through each step. He rotated the valve, testing air flow, then did it again as if unsure he'd done it correctly the first time. Finally satisfied, he gave her shoulder strap a tug that was perhaps too hard, overcompensating.
"Hamza was right," Karim said quietly as he checked her shoulder straps. "About the crown lens being critical. About Hassan understanding what he was protecting." He paused, hands still on her gear. "I should have told you earlier. About Hassan and Malik. I'm sorry."
"I know why you didn't," Aya said, and meant it. "But we're even now. No more secrets."
"No more secrets," he agreed.
Something passed between them, not quite romance but understanding. Respect. The acknowledgment that they'd both been trying to protect each other in their own flawed ways, that they'd both made mistakes, that moving forward meant accepting those failures and choosing better.
"Are we good?" Karim asked quietly. 
"We're good," Aya said, the words feeling like glass in her throat.
"Breathe," he instructed.
Aya took a breath from her regulator. Air flowed smoothly, tasting of the rubber mouthpiece and the faintly metallic tang of compressed gas. She gave him the okay sign.
She turned to check his setup, adjusted the shoulder straps of his BCD, and tested his air pressure gauge. The needle sat at 3000 PSI. A full tank, good for forty minutes at depth if he controlled his breathing. Their hands didn't touch as she worked. Both were being careful about that, maintaining the millimeters of professional contact.
"Are we good?" Karim asked quietly.
"We're good," Aya said, unsure if he meant anything beyond their gear.
At 4:03 AM, as the sky began shifting from black to deep purple in the east, they entered the water.
The cold was shocking despite their wetsuits. Dark water closed over Aya’s head, only their headlamp beams cutting through the blue-black, creating narrow tunnels of visibility that ended in absolute darkness.
They descended the anchor line together, Aya leading, Karim following three meters behind. Hand over hand down the rope, the nylon felt rough even through her neoprene gloves. 
At ten meters, the residual surface light faded completely. At twenty meters, they existed in a small bubble of lamplight entombed by infinite darkness, the beams catching suspended particles that drifted past like snow.
Fish scattered from their lights, silver flashes that were there and gone, bream or sea bass, impossible to identify in the brief glimpse.
Aya forced herself to focus, tuning out all personal distractions. The dive required full attention, with no room for guilt or second-guessing or replaying the kiss with Amina over and over. Just the immediate reality of depth and pressure and nitrogen loading slowly in her blood, creating the underwater narcosis that could kill you if you weren't careful, that could make you think you were fine as you made decisions that would drown you.
After another few meters down, the structure emerged from the darkness like something from a dream.
Marble, Greco-Roman columns lay broken but precisely fitted, the stone still showing chisel marks after two thousand years. Each column was fluted, the classical style, but now covered in a thin layer of marine growth that gave them a fuzzy, softened appearance. 
Modifications became visible as they descended. Brick additions arched between the ancient pillars, creating a hybrid structure that spoke of different empires, different purposes, layers of civilization stacked like geological time.
The beauty struck Aya even through her professional focus. She knew this was what Karim loved, the layering of history, the way each culture built on what came before rather than destroying it. She glanced back at him, saw his eyes wide behind his mask, his head turning slowly to take it all in. Even in the middle of a mission, even with everything broken between them, he couldn't help but marvel at the architecture, couldn't help but see the human story written in stone. And part of her loved it.
The structure was far larger than expected, more imposing. An octagonal seal was set into a stone doorframe, each panel carved with star patterns that matched workshop diagrams they’d been seeing as they uncovered pieces of Çelebi’s network across Turkey. 
The central star was eight-pointed, radiating lines creating the sunburst effect. Around it, smaller stars formed constellation patterns. Pleiades, Orion's belt, and the Southern Cross. Three mounting points for calibration rings stood out from the surface, brass fittings that gleamed even after centuries underwater, the metal somehow immune to corrosion.
Aya removed the two brass rings from her waterproof pouch, handling them carefully despite thick neoprene gloves that made her fingers clumsy. The first ring, the one marked with lunar phases around its circumference, fit into the top mounting point. She had to rotate it slightly to find the alignment, the mechanism resisting before clicking into place with a vibration she felt through her gloves. The sound carried through the water, a pure tone like striking crystal.
The second ring, planetary orbits etched into its surface, went into the right-hand mount. This one seated smoothly, and as it locked, Aya felt something shift in the water around them. Not movement but pressure, as if the ocean itself was holding its breath.
Suddenly, the weight of their depth felt heavier, growing.
The third mounting point remained empty, save for deeply grooved markings. Aya moved closer, her lamp illuminating symbols carved around the mount's perimeter. Not decorative, but instructional. Numbers, and a phrase in Turkish that she couldn't read but photographed.
She signaled to Karim, pointing at the inscription. He swam closer, his lamp joining hers, studying the text. Behind his mask, she could make out his eyes narrowing in the faint glow.
After a few moments of study, he pulled out his dive slate and wrote: "Turn rings to align stars with cardinal directions, I think.”
He quickly erased, then wrote again: “Third mount opens when pattern matches this exact moment. Barrier of knowledge."
Aya looked at the two rings they'd already installed. She'd thought they just locked in place, but now she saw they could rotate within their mounts. Each ring had tiny hash marks along its edge, degree markings. She grabbed the first ring, tried to turn it. 
Resistance, then slow movement. The ring rotated smoothly, bronze on bronze, mechanisms that had waited four centuries beneath the water, but still functioned.
But turn it to what position? Aya thought. Shit. She looked to Karim and shook her head. The narcosis was making her head feel like it was full of honey.
He wrote again: "Eight-pointed star = eight directions? Star tip must point to true north."
Aya oriented herself using her dive compass, found magnetic north. She rotated the first ring, watching as the eight-pointed star etched on its face turned. When the primary tip aligned with true north, she felt something shift. A vibration through her gloves, subtle but present.
The second ring required yet another different alignment. Karim studied the orbits etched into its surface, made calculations on his slate, numbers and angles. Finally, he wrote: "Venus’ position at the winter solstice. 73 degrees from north."
Aya rotated the second ring, counting hash marks, stopping at 73 degrees. Another vibration, stronger this time. 
The water pressure around them shifted with a pulse of whatever was within, becoming heavier.
The third mounting point began to glow, a faint phosphorescence coming from within the stone itself. The empty mount was now showing them what it needed: a shadow, a void, a specific absence.
Karim studied it. He wrote: "Not weight. Shadow. Block light at exact angle."
His hand shook slightly from the effort of maintaining neutral buoyancy. His fins kicked unconsciously, small adjustments that showed he was fighting the current more than he should be. Aya saw him glance at his depth gauge twice, then his air gauge, checking and rechecking.
He’ll be alright. He’s made it this far, Aya told herself. She held up her hands. “What angle?”
Karim shook his head, and they were both left to stare at the mount. Finally, he removed his dive knife and positioned it above the glowing mount, casting a shadow. 
Too far left. Nothing. 
Too far right. Nothing. 
He adjusted carefully, rotating the knife's angle, moving it millimeter by millimeter until the shadow fell across the mount in different patterns. The glow intensified where shadow met light, creating a key of darkness against illumination.
Click.
The sound was definitive, mechanical, utterly certain. Hidden bronze hinges activated somewhere deep in the stone, mechanisms that had waited for this specific combination: aligned rings, correct angles, shadow cast at precise position during slack tide when water pressure equalized.
The door swung inward slowly, fighting water pressure and centuries of disuse, but still functional, still perfect in its engineering.
Beyond the seal was a small, octagonal reliquary room.
Aya's lamp swept across the space, revealing details that made her breath catch. The mosaic floor was intact, depicting constellation patterns rendered in colored stone. Blue for the night sky, gold for the stars, each tiny tile no larger than her thumbnail. The precision was staggering. Someone had spent months, maybe years, creating this floor that would be seen by perhaps a handful of people before being sealed away and sunk into the ocean.
And at the center, on a golden shelf that rose from the mosaic floor, Aya saw what they were looking for. It had to be. The crown lens.
It was the size of a large fist, crystal worked in impossible internal complexity, facets within facets, like looking into a kaleidoscope. Abstract patterns that seemed to shift as light passed through, creating three-dimensional structures that couldn't exist in normal glass. Every surface was etched with microscopic calculations, formulas too small to make out without magnification.
Aya swam closer, her lamp beam hitting the lens and—
The light fractured.
Not randomly, but into structured rainbow patterns that splashed across the chamber walls. Colors too pure, too saturated to be natural refraction. The blue wasn't just blue; it was every shade of blue simultaneously, from pale sky to deep indigo, all existing in the same space. And the light didn't just bend, it seemed to organize itself, finding patterns that shouldn't exist, creating geometries that made her eyes hurt when she tried to follow them.
Aya's vision began to swim. She'd experienced nitrogen narcosis before, knew the drunk feeling, the slowed thoughts. This was different. The patterns on the walls wouldn't hold still. They shifted when she wasn't looking directly at them, reorganized themselves in her peripheral vision, creating movement that made her inner ear scream, insisting she was tilting when she knew she was level. 
Her eyes couldn’t focus.
She reached for the wall to steady herself but missed on the first try, her depth perception suddenly unreliable. Her hand passed through space where she'd been certain stone existed. She tried again, connected, felt solid rock beneath her glove.
Her regulator was still feeding good air. Her dive computer was showing safe depth, safe nitrogen levels, everything normal. But her body was responding to something the instruments couldn't measure.
She forced herself to look away from the lens, focused on Karim instead. Solid. Real. Not shifting. Her equilibrium slowly returned, the vertigo fading but not disappearing completely, leaving a residue of wrongness that made her want to surface, to escape whatever the lens was doing to her perception.
She signaled with a thumbs-up and a head jerk to Karim. Photograph quickly, then cover it. 
He understood immediately, seeing her unsteadiness, moving to secure the lens in its padded bag while she forced herself to breathe slowly, to ride out whatever effect the refracted light was having on her nervous system.
An object of sublimely terrifying beauty and power.
Looking at it, Aya understood why Çelebi had hidden it. Why the Silent Meridian kept it secret. This wasn't just a lens. It was a key to something humans weren't ready to unlock, maybe would never be ready to unlock. Perhaps more based in scientific method than the djinn they’d awoken in Morocco, but no less powerful. No less potential for misuse and destruction.
Aya photographed the chamber with the underwater camera. The walls, the floor, the pedestal, the crown lens from every angle. Flash bursts illuminated the space in brief, brilliant intervals, each one revealing details invisible in their lamp beams: subtle variations in the mosaic patterns, wear marks on the pedestal, tool marks in the stone.
Karim swam slowly around the perimeter and paused at one section to Aya’s left, his hand hovering over a series of calculations, reading, his lips moving slightly behind his regulator.
He pulled out his dive slate. "Try not to look into the lens directly."
Aya read his translation, understanding why her equilibrium had failed when she'd stared at the fractured light too long. She'd looked. Something had looked back.
Karim continued around the chamber, photographing inscriptions with his own camera, the flashes creating strobing illumination. He was documenting every inch, unable to leave without preserving the knowledge.
Aya understood the impulse even as it made her anxious. Someone did need to understand what Çelebi had built. But whether that someone should be them, whether this knowledge should be preserved or destroyed, she still didn't know the answer.
The stabilization component from Cappadocia flashed across his mind. He should have told Aya about it already. Should have confessed what he'd taken from the workshop while they fled from Aegis. But watching her now, seeing how she documented everything with clinical precision and prepared to hide it all away, preparing to let this incredible knowledge disappear again, he couldn't bring himself to admit he'd saved a piece, perhaps the most interesting of them all.
Later, he told himself. After we stop Malik. Then I'll decide what to do with it. Then I'll tell her.
While Karim secured the crown lens in a padded bag clipped to his BCD, Aya swam back to the doorway where the calibration rings remained mounted.
She gripped the first ring, the one marked with lunar phases, and tried to rotate it counterclockwise. Resistance. The bronze had seated itself in the mechanism, creating friction. She pulled harder, her thick neoprene gloves making it difficult to maintain grip on the smooth metal. Finally, it gave, unscrewing with a grinding sensation she felt through her hands. She secured it in her waterproof pouch.
The second ring fought her even more. The planetary orbits etched into its surface gave her nothing to grip. She had to work her gloved fingers underneath its edge, prying and rotating simultaneously. Thirty seconds of wrestling before it released. Into the pouch with the first.
As she sealed the pouch, the water movement changed around them.
It was subtle at first, just a shift in how the current pushed against Aya's body, the way suspended particles began drifting with purpose instead of floating. 
Then it became more pronounced, the pull becoming insistent, the Mediterranean beginning its twice-daily rush as the tide turned.
The chamber began swirling in a slow current, as water pressure poured through ventilation channels cut high in the walls. Water swirling with more force now, creating eddies and counter-currents that pulled at their fins.
Deniz's voice crackled over their underwater comm system, slightly distorted by water and distance: "Current picking up topside. Need to surface soon. You've got maybe five minutes before it becomes dangerous."
Aya signaled ascent, thumb pointed up. Karim nodded, following as they swam toward the chamber entrance, fighting the current now, having to kick hard to make progress. His lamp flickered, and he fumbled with it, trying to tap it back to life, before giving up and relying on Aya's light.
They reached the doorway and pulled themselves through, grabbing the stone frame for leverage as the current tried to sweep them past. Outside the chamber, the full force of the tide hit them. It was no longer subtle, no longer a gentle pull, but something insistent that required constant swimming just to maintain position.
They had to fight toward the anchor line, swimming at an angle to make progress, the rope visible as a pale vertical line in Aya's lamp beam. Her legs burned with the effort, her breathing rate climbing, burning through air far faster than she wanted. Behind her, Karim's exhaust bubbles came faster too, irregular.
She signaled to him when they reached the line: ascend slowly. He nodded, breathing hard. They'd burned through air fighting the current, and they still had a safety stop ahead.
They began pulling themselves up hand over hand, the current still strong but lessening as they rose. 
The old knife wound in her side flared with each movement, the scar tissue protesting the exertion, the BCD's waist strap digging into exactly the wrong spot. She'd been favoring it unconsciously, compensating with her right side, and now both her obliques screamed in protest, and for a moment, fear cut through the exhaustion. What if the stitches hadn't held? She pushed the thought away. No choice but to keep moving. 
At twelve meters, Aya's dive computer began beeping. Ascent rate warning. She was climbing too fast, pushed by adrenaline and fear. She forced herself to slow, count to three between pulls, regulate her breathing despite every instinct screaming to reach the surface, to reach air and safety.
At eight meters, the current eased further. At six meters, it became merely annoying rather than dangerous. Aya's computer showed their target: five meters depth, three-minute safety stop mandatory. Skip it and risk decompression sickness. Follow protocol and risk running out of air or being swept away if the current picked up again.
She checked her pressure gauge: 700 PSI. Maybe ten minutes of air at this depth if she stayed calm. Karim's gauge showed worse: 500 PSI. They'd pushed their bottom time too long, and fighting the current had burned through their reserves.
At five meters, they stopped, wrapping their arms around the anchor line, locking their bodies in place. At least there’s still no sign of Malik, she thought.
Chapter 36
The ascent couldn't be direct. Nitrogen buildup at their depth required staged decompression or they'd get the bends, nitrogen bubbles forming in their blood and joints, causing pain, paralysis, and possibly death. A safety stop at five meters would be mandatory, three minutes watching their dive computers count down while the current tried to rip them away from the anchor line, their oxygen supplies diminished, and Aegis invariably gained ground.
They gripped the rope with both hands, bodies streaming horizontally in the current like flags in high wind. Aya's shoulders burned with the effort of holding on. She could feel the pull trying to separate her fingers from the rope, trying to sweep her away into the open Mediterranean where the current ran even faster.
The three minutes crawled past with agonizing slowness. Aya counted her breaths, forcing herself to breathe slowly and deeply. In. Hold. Out. Hold. She watched the computer display tick down: 2:47, 2:46, 2:45.
Karim's eyes behind his mask were wide, showing white around the edges. His air gauge had dropped into the red zone, signaling he had five minutes left, less if he kept breathing this hard. Aya's own gauge showed 600 PSI. Tight, but manageable if nothing else went wrong.
Something felt wrong. 
Aya couldn't articulate what, just a prickling awareness. 
She looked down into the darkness below them, into the empty blue where visibility ended. Empty. Too empty. 
No curious fish who should be investigating their bubbles? 
Then she understood. Something had scared them away. 
The lights appeared from below.
Three beams, cutting through the darkness from the direction of the ruins. Moving fast. Moving with purpose.
Aegis divers. They'd been waiting, watching from a distance, letting Aya and Karim do the work of retrieving the crown lens, then moving in for the attack.
The first diver reached them in seconds, rising from below like a shark. Black wetsuit, full face mask, a pistol leveled at Aya's chest. The second and third flanked them, boxing them against the anchor line.
No warning. No negotiation. The lead diver fired.
The shot went wide by inches, the projectile leaving a trail of bubbles as it passed, the physics all wrong, the trajectory curved by water resistance. But at this range, wrong could still be fatal. 
Aya twisted, kicked hard, releasing the anchor line, putting distance between herself and the shooter as he adjusted aim for a second shot. Her hand went to the knife at her thigh.
Everything became slow and fast simultaneously. The strange time-dilation of underwater combat, where sound traveled wrong, and movement felt like swimming through honey, but death came just as quickly as it did on land.
The second diver went for Karim.
Karim wasn't a fighter. Aya knew it, the diver knew it. But Karim had one advantage. He was holding the anchor line with both hands, and when the diver grabbed for the bag clipped to his BCD, Karim used the extra leverage and swung his legs up and wrapped them around the man's neck.
Not technique. Desperation. But it worked.
The diver's hands went to his throat, trying to break the leg-lock, his mask fogging with exertion. Karim squeezed harder, face contorted behind his own mask, holding on with everything he had while his air gauge dropped toward empty.
Aya didn't have time to help him.
The lead diver was on her, knife out, the blade catching her lamp beam as it slashed toward her throat. Aya blocked with her forearm, and the edge scraped across her dive computer, cracking the display. She countered with an elbow to his mask. The polycarbonate held, but the impact snapped his head back and bought her a second.
She drew her own blade, a ceramic knife weighted for underwater use, the grip textured for wet hands.
They circled each other in the darkness, five meters down and increasing, current trying to sweep them both away. Aya's lungs burned. Her air gauge was dropping faster now, adrenaline making her breathe too hard, burning through reserves she couldn't afford to lose.
The diver feinted left, came right. Aya read it, slipped the thrust, and drove her blade into the gap between his tank and BCD. Not deep, the angle was wrong, but enough to make him flinch, enough to create an opening.
She kicked him in the chest, both feet, the force sending him tumbling backward through the water. His regulator came loose. He grabbed for it, found it, but the distraction cost him.
Aya was already moving, swimming toward the third diver who had Karim pinned against the anchor line, one hand on Karim's regulator, trying to rip it from his mouth while Karim still grappled with the second diver.
She hit the third diver from behind, driving her blade into the gap between his neck and shoulder where the wetsuit ended and the hood began. The ceramic edge found the subclavian artery. She knew it immediately from how the blood came, not seeping but pulsing, hot even through the water, blooming dark in her headlamp beam.
The diver's hands flew to his neck, regulator falling from his mouth as he tried to stem bleeding he couldn't stop. His eyes behind the mask were wide, understanding, terrified.
Aya twisted the blade before pulling it free, making sure. His body went slack, drifting, trailing black clouds that dispersed in the current.
Karim kicked free, but his movements were sluggish. His eyes behind the mask were unfocused. Nitrogen narcosis, low air, panic, all of it combining into the slow drowning that killed experienced divers every year.
Stay with me, Aya thought. Don't you dare die down here.
The lead diver had recovered and was coming at her again. Meanwhile, the second diver was shaking off Karim’s leg-lock, reaching for his own weapon. 
They were running out of time, out of air, out of options.
Then a shape descended from above.
No tank. No BCD. No fins.
Just Amina, free-diving from the surface, her body cutting through the water like a missile plummeting toward them.
The concussion, the depth, the pressure—everything they'd warned her about, all risk factors playing through Aya’s mind.
But Amina wasn't thinking about risk.
She hit the lead diver with a fishing spear she'd grabbed from the boat's dive locker. No time to load it properly in the speargun, no time to aim. Just a three-foot shaft of steel with a barbed tip, and Amina drove it like a javelin into the diver's back.
The point punched through his wetsuit, through the gap between his tank and BCD, finding the space between ribs. The diver jerked, bubbles erupting from his regulator in a scream, body convulsing.
Amina pulled the shaft free, barbs catching along the way, and drove it in again. And again. Brutal, desperate, her lungs already burning from depth and no air, but she couldn't stop because if he recovered, he'd kill them all.
Ten seconds. Fifteen. The diver's movements slowed, then stopped.
Amina released him, pushed off his sinking body, and grabbed the anchor line. Her chest was heaving, cheeks puffed with held breath, eyes wide with the strain of depth and exertion.
Underwater, without air, the fight was a race against Amina’s own lungs.
Go up, Aya signaled desperately. GO UP.
But the second diver was still active, swimming for them.
Aya met him halfway. They collided in a tangle of limbs and bubbles, knife work too close for clean strikes, reduced to elbows and knees and the desperate intimacy of trying to kill someone who was trying to kill you.
Her blade found his shoulder, his found her thigh. A hot line of pain that she couldn't afford tore through Aya’s consciousness. As he struggled to remove the knife she'd planted in his shoulder, she clawed for his mask, ripping it off, and gouged at his eyes while he kicked at her body frantically.
He broke free, started swimming for the surface despite being blinded, bloodied, and drowning on seawater that flooded his sinuses.
But Amina was there, catching him from below. She had the speargun shaft again, wrestled from the lead diver's corpse, and she drove it upward into his abdomen with both hands, planting her legs firmly against his chest for leverage.
The diver folded around the impact, his ascending momentum halted. Amina held him there for three seconds, making sure, then released him and let him sink, trailing blood and bubbles, joining his companions in the dark.
Amina’s face was turning purple, lips pressed tight, eyes bulging with oxygen starvation, consciousness seconds from failing.
Aya grabbed her, pulled her close, and pressed her regulator to Amina's lips, forcing it into her mouth.
One breath. Two. Aya felt the vacuum in her own lungs, felt her vision starting to tunnel, but Amina's eyes were already rolling back. Another three more seconds without air, and she'd pass out and drown.
Three breaths. Four. Amina's eyes focused slightly, her hands coming up to grip Aya's shoulders.
Aya took the regulator back, sucked on nothing. Her tank was empty, reading zero.
She grabbed Amina, shoved her upward, and kicked with everything she had left.
They rose together, too fast, the dive computer screaming warnings that neither of them could hear over the roaring in their ears. Karim was above them, ascending on his own, his movements weak, barely conscious.
Any further safety stop was impossible now. They'd skip it and deal with the consequences, or they'd die down here. Those were the only options.
Aya's gauge hit zero as they passed through eight meters. She took one last breath of nothing, felt her throat close on a vacuum, and kicked harder.
Five meters. Three. One.
They broke through into dawn light, all three of them, gasping and choking and alive. Aya's joints already ached with the warning signs of nitrogen still dissolving in her blood. They'd skipped the stop, and they'd pay for it, but at least they'd pay while breathing. Amina coughed up seawater, her whole body shaking. Karim's face was gray, his breathing ragged, showing the early signs of decompression stress.
Aya spit out her empty regulator, dragging air into lungs that burned like she'd swallowed fire. Her thigh was bleeding freely now, the saltwater making the wound scream, but she could move it, could kick, which meant nothing critical had been severed.
"Go, go!" Deniz was shouting from the boat, his voice cutting through any exhaustion. "Boats inbound! Aegis!"
Aya spun in the water, following his pointing arm. An RIB was approaching fast from the south, maybe five hundred meters away and closing. Twin outboards screamed, throwing up a tail of spray, the bow rising as it planed across the surface at speed.
They'd been waiting. Watching the dive site. Probably had drones up, had been tracking every boat on the water’s position since they arrived.
Deniz already had the engines started, diesel rumbling, exhaust smoking. Amina was at the dive platform, leaning over to grab Aya's hand, hauling her up. Aya collapsed on deck, still in full gear, no time to remove the heavy tank that made every movement awkward. 
Karim came up next, Amina and Leyla working together to haul his weight. He sprawled on deck beside Aya, breathing hard, fins scraping wood as the boat surged forward. 
Deniz didn't wait for him to clear the platform before gunning the throttle, the sudden acceleration almost throwing her back into the water. Aya grabbed a cleat and held on.
"They appeared fifteen minutes ago on radar," Leyla called from the cabin doorway, one hand gripping the frame for balance as the boat accelerated, the other holding binoculars. "Came fast from the north. They were definitely waiting for us."
The gulet wasn't built for speed. It’s twenty-five meters of wood and tradition designed for leisurely cruising, not pursuit. But Deniz's hand was steady on the wheel, his eyes scanning the water with purpose. He pointed left, shouted something in Turkish, and spun the wheel. 
The gulet heeled hard, nearly throwing Aya against the rail.
A reef marker passed fifty meters to their right, warning boats away from submerged rocks. Deniz had steered toward it, not away. 
Karim started to shout a warning, but Leyla grabbed his arm. 
"He knows," she called.
Aya watched the water change colors ahead, dark blue to turquoise to pale green. Deniz was reading depth the way she'd learned to on reef dives, finding safe channels through coral that would tear their hull open.  Dark blue was safe water, ten meters or more. Light blue was getting shallow, five meters. Turquoise, and it was about three meters, risky for most boats. Green meant less than one meter, death for anything with a keel.
Deniz threaded through channels Aya couldn't see, following darkness that indicated safe passage between coral formations, adjusting course based on water color alone. No GPS, no charts, just eyes and memory and decades of fishing these waters before tourists arrived.
He pushed the engines past their normal operating range, the diesel clattering with protest, black smoke pouring from the exhaust. 
The gulet's bow rose as they built speed, the hull beginning to pound against the waves instead of cutting through them smoothly. Every impact jarred through the wooden deck.
Aya watched behind them as the Aegis RIB was gaining. Modern engines, lightweight construction, designed for exactly this kind of pursuit. Twenty knots became twenty-five, the gap closing despite Deniz's best efforts. Aya could see figures in the RIB now. At least three, maybe four, all wearing tactical gear, one pointing ahead at them while speaking into a radio. Maybe other boats.
Deniz spun the wheel hard right, the gulet heeling over with the sudden turn, water washing across the deck. 
They were heading toward a section of coastline where the water showed dark blue, becoming light blue, becoming green, the color changes indicating depth, indicating a reef.
"He's insane," Karim muttered, watching the water ahead. "That's too shallow. We'll ground."
"He knows what he's doing," Leyla said, but her knuckles were white where she gripped the railing.
The RIB was gaining, its modern engines and lightweight construction designed for exactly this, following the gulet's wake with confidence.
Then, Deniz did something unexpected. Instead of running parallel to the reef line, maintaining safe water, he turned directly toward it. The gulet's bow pointed at shallow water, at foam marking where waves broke over submerged coral.
Aya and Amina both braced for impact, Karim following suit.
"Trust me," Deniz called back, seeing the fear on their faces. "RIB draws less water than us. Should be advantage for them. But RIB has propellers, twin outboards mounted below the hull. We have a shaft drive, propeller deep in the keel's protection. Makes difference in rocks."
He'd been watching the tide, Aya realized. Watching how the waves broke, how the foam patterns changed. Reading the reef structure from surface indicators, seeing what lay beneath.
Deniz aimed for a section where the foam broke irregularly, not in consistent lines. Irregular meant a gap in the reef, Aya realized.
The gulet's keel kissed sand, a scraping sound that made Aya's teeth ache. Deniz adjusted the course three degrees starboard. The scraping stopped. They were through, in the channel, threading between coral heads that rose to within a meter of the surface on both sides.
The RIB followed without hesitation.
Chapter 37
“Shit,” Amina yelled.
The boat had followed through the gap and continued gaining on them. Then the RIB hit something submerged at twenty-five knots.
The impact was catastrophic. The rigid hull bucked upward, the bow lifting out of the water. Aya watched as one of the contractors flew forward, hit the windscreen, and collapsed. 
The twin outboards screamed as the props came out of water, cavitating, the engines over-revving. The captain killed them immediately, but momentum carried the RIB forward, broadside to the current now, sliding sideways toward more reef.
The left pontoon scraped over coral. The reinforced material was designed to withstand impacts, but not sustained grinding against razor-sharp coral at speed. 
The pontoon deflated with a hiss that carried across the water, the RIB immediately listing, taking on water through the hull breach, dead in the water.
Deniz threaded through one more narrow section, this one marked only by the way the morning sun glinted off ripples, showing him where the water flowed faster and deeper. 
Finally, they emerged into open water. Behind them, the Aegis RIB was sinking. Its crew was deploying a life raft, one man holding tight onto a radio.
Deniz throttled back finally, the engines returning to normal rumble. He wiped sweat from his face, hands steady despite what he'd just done, despite how close they'd come to grounding, to being disabled just like their pursuers.
"Reef diving since I was ten," he said, as if that explained everything. Perhaps it did.
Aya's heart was still racing, adrenaline making her hands shake. She looked around the deck, taking stock.
Amina had collapsed against the cabin wall, hands pressed to her temples, face contorted. "Stupid," she gasped. "Stupid, stupid—"
"You saved our lives," Aya said, crawling toward her despite her own bleeding leg, the knife wound from the Aegis diver leaving a dark trail across the teak decking.
"I free-dove to fifteen meters with a fresh concussion." Amina's laugh was half-sob. "Everything's spinning. I can't—I can't see straight."
"Just breathe. Slow breaths." Aya gripped her shoulder, feeling Amina trembling beneath her hand.
Karim was worse. He sat against the rail, shivering despite the Mediterranean warmth, his skin taking on a mottled quality that Aya recognized. Decompression symptoms.
"We might need to get him to a hyperbaric chamber," Leyla said, kneeling beside him, checking his pupils.
"We can't," Aya replied. "The moment we show up at any medical facility, Aegis will have us."
"Then we manage it." Leyla's voice was flat, professional. "Keep him hydrated, watch for neuro symptoms, hope the exposure was short enough that his body can handle it."
Aya looked down at her own leg, at the gash that ran from mid-thigh to just above her knee. The Aegis diver's blade had caught her during the underwater melee. Blood welled steadily, mixing with seawater, dripping onto the deck.
"That needs stitches," Leyla said, following her gaze.
"Later. After we're clear."
Leyla disappeared below and returned with the first aid kit, pressing gauze against the wound hard enough to make Aya hiss. "Hold this. Pressure until we can close it properly."
As Leyla worked to stabilize the others, Aya watched Karim's hand move unconsciously to his pocket. Checking. The same gesture she'd seen three times now. The same pocket, the same momentary relief when his fingers found whatever he was carrying.
Another question for later, when they weren't bleeding and shaking and running for their lives.
Deniz navigated into Bodrum harbor at a leisurely pace, just another gulet returning from a morning dive, blending into the traffic of fishing boats, ferries, and tourist vessels just beginning their day. 
They ghosted past a police boat. The officers glanced at them, radio crackling on their belts, but made no move to intercept. Just another day on the water.
By the time they secured the boat back at the marina, the early morning sun was warming the deck. Aya was exhausted, hands shaking slightly from adrenaline crash, the post-crisis weakness that hit when your body realized it didn't need to be in fight-or-flight mode anymore.
But they had done it.
Aya went below to her bag, pulled out the padded case, and opened it carefully. The crown lens sat inside, catching the cabin light and fracturing it into impossible patterns. The key to everything.
"We need to move," Leyla called from the companionway, her phone showing news alerts. "Aegis will call that in. We need to be gone before they arrive."
"Rental villa," Deniz said, already checking coordinates on his phone. "I know a place. Quiet, private, the owner's in Istanbul for the season. You go there, wait for the evening, then leave for Istanbul. I'll handle the authorities; it was my boat. I’ll tell them I saw nothing, rented to tourists who wanted diving, and they left after their dive. Simple."
Twenty minutes later, they split into two taxis, gear and components distributed between them. The drive from Bodrum harbor took them along the coast road, past tourist hotels and beach clubs, climbing into hills where development thinned, where villas spread apart behind walls and gates, where privacy became the luxury people paid for.
The taxi stopped at a wrought iron gate, painted white, half-hidden behind bougainvillea that cascaded in violent magenta blooms. Leyla punched in the access code Deniz had sent. 
The gate swung open on well-oiled hinges, and they followed a paved drive up through terraced landscaping. Olive trees, palms, and a pomegranate tree heavy with fruit.
The villa revealed itself in stages as they climbed: first the upper story with its modern lines and traditional Turkish stone, then the covered terrace with its pergola wrapped in vines, finally the pool and lawn stretching toward the view, with the Mediterranean spreading blue and infinite below, Greek islands visible as dark shapes on the horizon.
Amina paid both drivers, waited until they'd turned around and driven back through the gate before she spoke. "This feels too nice for what we're doing."
"Everything feels too nice for what we're doing," Karim said, looking around the infinity-edged pool that reflected the fresh sky, dropping off to reveal the coast.
They carried their bags inside through French doors that opened onto a living area. Terra cotta floors, cool and smooth.
Leyla checked quickly and found three rooms and two baths, everything clean and ready. Sliding doors opened onto a balcony with the same commanding view, the same sense of being suspended between land and sea. “Looks clear.”
"Sweep?" Amina asked.
"Sweep," Aya confirmed.
Nothing. The villa was clean. Just someone's beautiful home between seasons, waiting for the next guests, indifferent to who those guests were or why they needed privacy and isolation.
Aya stood on the terrace, coming down from the rush of the dive, the escape, being deep underwater with Karim. She looked out at the pool's still water, the lawn's green carpet, the Mediterranean spreading toward horizons she couldn't see. Beautiful. Peaceful. The kind of place where normal people came to relax, to celebrate, to build memories with people they loved.
They were here to prepare for what might be the end of everything, carrying components that could fracture reality, running from people who would kill them to get those components back.
The contrast was almost obscene.
"Clear," Leyla called from inside. "We're alone."
For a long moment, nobody moved. They stood in the beautiful villa, sun-drenched, peaceful, designed for joy, and tried to reconcile it with what they'd just done.
Aya had killed someone underwater, watched the light leave his eyes through the mask. Felt his body go slack under her knife. The Aegis divers had tried to kill them first, but that didn't change the weight settling in her chest. It wasn’t new, but it always felt that way.
Karim stared at his hands. He'd choked a man and held on while the diver fought for his life. That wasn't archaeology. That wasn't research.
Amina pressed her forehead against the cool glass of the window. "We keep getting better at this," she said. "Killing. Fighting. Surviving. Morocco was supposed to be the end, but here we are, drowning people to protect artifacts."
"I need a minute," Karim said quietly, and disappeared without waiting for a response.
"They came for us," Amina called. “We had no choice.”
"They always come for us. And we always have a reason." Aya's reflection in the glass looked haunted. "How many people have died because of these components? How many more before this ends?"
“As many as it takes,” Amina said. “That’s what we committed to.”
Aya turned from the view and walked back inside, limping on her freshly stitched leg. Amina had closed the wound during the drive without an anesthetic, just gritted teeth and Amina's hand to squeeze, but every step still sent fire up her thigh.
Karim was still shivering, his joints aching with what might be the bends or might just be exhaustion. Only time would tell. They'd know within twelve hours if he needed emergency treatment, if the nitrogen bubbles in his blood would dissolve on their own, or start causing permanent damage.
"Sleep," Aya told him. "We'll wake you if anything changes."
He nodded, too exhausted to argue, and disappeared into the nearest bedroom.
Amina took the couch, ice pack pressed to her head, eyes closed against the light that had become unbearably bright. "Wake me if we're dying," she mumbled. "Otherwise, I need to not exist for a while."
"You shouldn't have come down there," Aya said quietly.
"You'd be dead if I hadn't." Amina's eyes stayed closed. "Worth it. Now let me suffer in peace."
That left Aya and Leyla to take stock.
They sat at the villa's dining table, the afternoon sun streaming through floor-to-ceiling windows, the Mediterranean glittering impossibly blue beyond the terrace. It looked like a postcard. It felt like a fever dream.
Aya set the crown lens case at the table's center.
For a long moment, nobody spoke. Exhaustedly, they stared at a padded case that contained something that shouldn't exist, something that had waited four hundred years to be found, something that could change or destroy everything depending on whose hands held it.
Seven pieces. The complete lens system, minus the missing stabilization wafer. Everything Çelebi had scattered, everything Hassan had spent his life protecting, everything Malik needed to tear reality open. All of it sitting on a villa dining table in afternoon sunlight.
Finally, Karim reached out, opened the case.
The crown lens sat in its foam cradle, catching the afternoon sunlight that streamed through the villa's windows. The light fractured as before, bending, shifting, dancing.
Karim picked it up carefully, holding it in both hands, examining every facet, every inscription, every detail of its construction, his face hungry to understand.
"We have everything. It’s finished," Leyla said quietly. "Every component your father scattered across three continents. Every piece Malik's been hunting for years."
"We have it all," Aya agreed. The words felt strange in her mouth. Unreal. After months of running, fighting, losing people. After Hamza's blood sprayed across the cave stone, after the underwater ambush that should have killed them, they'd actually done it.
She looked at the components spread across the table. The glass fragment, still faintly warm. The brass cylinder with its impossible precision. Both calibration rings, one marked with lunar phases, one with planetary orbits. The focusing array they'd pulled from the Cappadocia platform. The crystal heart that hummed at frequencies she felt in her teeth. The assembly charts, four centuries old and still legible. And now the crown lens. The control mechanism, the termination switch, the piece that made everything else dangerous or safe, depending on whose hands held it.
"Without this," Aya said, touching the lens case, "Malik can't achieve controlled activation. Even if he tries with what he has, it'll cascade. Self-defeating. He'd tear himself apart before he accomplished anything."
"So what now?" Leyla asked.
"We leave Turkey. Tonight, if we can manage it. Cross into Bulgaria. Amina and I have contacts there that can help us, and these components disappear. Scatter them again for safety. Different countries, different continents. Hide them in places Aegis will never find, and usher in a new era of guardians. The new Silent Meridian."
"And Malik?"
"Without these, he's finished." Aya leaned back in her chair, feeling the exhaustion settle into her bones. "His alignment window closes in less than two days. After that, he'd have to wait possibly years for another opportunity. By then, we'll have made sure these pieces are unfindable. Buried them so deep that nobody—not Aegis, not any government, not any researcher—will ever assemble them again."
Leyla was quiet for a moment, studying Aya's face. "So we won."
"We won."
The words hung in the air between them. Victory. It should have felt triumphant. Should have felt like vindication for Hamza, for the years of Hassan's life spent protecting these secrets. For Aya’s father.
Instead, it felt hollow. Exhausted. Like reaching the summit of a mountain and discovering the view was just more mountains, stretching endlessly toward horizons she'd never reach.
"You should rest," Leyla said, standing. "You've been running on adrenaline for days. Your body needs to recover, especially with that leg wound."
"What about you?"
"I'll keep watch. Make sure no one followed us, monitor the secure channels for any Aegis chatter." Leyla's smile was tired but genuine. Or practiced enough to look genuine. "Someone has to stay alert. Might as well be the one who didn't almost die underwater today."
Aya studied her face. There was something slightly off in Leyla's expression. Too controlled. Too careful.
"Everything okay?" Aya asked.
"Of course." Leyla's eyes didn't waver. "Why?"
"Just checking." Aya stood, testing her wounded leg. "Wake me in four hours. We'll switch."
"Of course." 
But Leyla's gaze had already shifted to the components on the table. To the crown lens catching the afternoon light. And for just a moment, so brief Aya almost missed it, her face changed. Not relief. Not exhaustion. Hunger.
Then it was gone, replaced by that same tired smile, and Aya told herself she'd imagined it.
Chapter 38
Aya woke to a sense of wrongness.
Her thoughts were thick, sluggish, like pushing through wet concrete. The bedroom was bright. How long had she been asleep? It felt like days, like drowning in black water, consciousness fighting to surface through pharmaceutical fog.
Four hours. Leyla was supposed to wake her after four hours.
But it was already morning, a full glow entering her bedroom. She'd slept far longer than four hours. Much longer.
Aya tried to sit up, but her body didn't respond properly. Her limbs felt disconnected, heavy, like they belonged to someone else. When she finally managed to push herself upright, the room spun violently, and nausea surged up her throat. She gripped the edge of the mattress, breathing hard through her nose, waiting for the vertigo to pass.
She reached for the lamp, her hand moving too slowly, her coordination off. The switch clicked uselessly. Nothing. She tried it twice more before her brain registered. The power was out.
She reached for her phone on the nightstand and pressed the side button. Dead. She'd plugged it in. It should have had hours of battery left.
The wrongness crystallized into fear.
She moved through the eerily still villa on instinct, favoring her good leg, one hand trailing along the wall. The main room was visible in the haze of light streaming in through the windows.
The dining table was bare.
Every case. Every pouch. Every carefully wrapped piece they'd carried across Turkey, fought for, bled for, nearly died for.
Gone.
