"No," Aya heard herself gasp, the word escaping before thought could catch it. "No, no, no—"
She lunged for the table, hands sweeping across the surface as if the components might be there and she just couldn't see them, as if this was a trick of the light, a nightmare she'd wake from. Her palms scraped across empty wood. Not even dust remained where the cases had sat hours ago.
"AMINA!" The scream tore from her throat, raw and desperate. "KARIM!"
She ran for the couch where Amina had been sleeping and found her there, slumped sideways, face slack, breathing shallow. Aya grabbed her shoulders and shook hard.
"Amina, wake up. Wake UP!"
Nothing. Amina's head lolled, her eyes stayed closed, her body limp. Aya pressed fingers to her neck and found a pulse, steady but too slow. And there, on the side of her neck, barely visible: a small red mark. 
Needle puncture. Drugged.
"No." Aya's voice cracked, the word coming out strangled. "This isn't… She wouldn't—"
She ran for Karim's room, slammed the door open hard enough to crack the frame. He was in bed where she'd left him, but his breathing was wrong. That same shallow rhythm, that same unnatural stillness. She found the same needle mark on his neck, the same absence behind his closed eyes.
Aya stood in the doorway, chest heaving, her mind racing through possibilities and finding only one that fit. One that she'd been too exhausted, too trusting, too stupid to see coming.
Leyla volunteered to keep watch alone.
Leyla always had the right contacts, the right information, the right resources.
Leyla was the one who pushed for Aya to dive. Who insisted the bloodline authentication wasn't negotiable.
The realization doubled her over, forcing air from her lungs in a sound that was half-gasp, half-sob.
She staggered back into the main room, looking for something, anything—proof that she was wrong, that there was another explanation, that the woman who'd fought beside them, who'd helped Amina through her sacrifice, who'd stitched Aya's leg while she gritted her teeth against the pain.
A phone sat on the kitchen counter. Not Aya's. Not Amina's or Karim's.
Leyla's. Left deliberately. A message, or a confession, or both.
Aya's hands shook so badly that she nearly dropped it twice. The phone was unlocked, showing a single sent message:
“Components secured. En route to Istanbul. Team neutralized but alive. I couldn't do it. Tell Malik I completed the mission. Release Emre.”
She read it once. Twice. A third time, each word burned itself into her brain like a brand.
“Team neutralized but alive. I couldn't do it.”
Couldn't do what? Kill them? Had that been the plan? Had Leyla been supposed to murder them in their sleep, put bullets in their heads while they trusted her to keep watch, and this, leaving them drugged and helpless and hours behind, was her version of mercy?
“Tell Malik I completed the mission.”
Malik. Not MİT. Not Turkish intelligence protecting national interests. Malik. Aegis. The enemy they'd been running from since Istanbul.
Leyla had been theirs from the beginning.
The phone slipped from Aya's fingers, clattering against the tile floor. She barely heard it. Blood roared in her ears, her vision tunneling, rage and grief and humiliation crashing together into something that felt like drowning.
Every moment replayed in brutal clarity.
The underwater ambush. Aegis divers waiting at exactly the right depth, at exactly the right time. Because Leyla had told them. Had probably sent their coordinates while pretending to monitor for threats.
Hamza.
The name hit her like a fist to the chest.
Hamza, taking a sniper's bullet to the skull mid-sentence. Hamza, who'd warned Karim about something being wrong with Leyla, Hamza's last gift of wisdom that they'd received too late. Hamza, who'd seen through her cover when the rest of them were too desperate for help to question good fortune.
And Leyla had ordered his death.
Had called it in, just another loose end to tie up before the endgame.
"Fuck!"
Aya grabbed the nearest object, a ceramic vase filled with decorative branches, and hurled it against the wall. It exploded into fragments, dried flowers scattering like bones, but the destruction wasn't enough, didn't touch the fury building in her chest. She swept her arm across the counter, sending dishes crashing to the floor, crystal shattering, ceramic breaking.
"God damn it!"
She sank to her knees in the debris, hot tears rolling down her face. Her wounded leg strained, but the pain was nothing compared to the hollow devastation.
Everything they'd sacrificed. Everything they'd survived. The balloon escape that should have killed them. The subterranean crawl in Cappadocia. The desperate dive into hostile waters while her wounded side screamed with every kick. Amina risking sacrificing herself, screaming on that obsidian platform while ancient power was ripped from her body. Karim nearly dying from decompression, his skin going gray as nitrogen bubbled in his blood.
All of it, every wound, every loss, every impossible victory, had just been delivered to Malik on a silver platter. And by someone they'd trusted.
Aya didn't know how long she knelt there in the broken glass and ceramic.
Finally, movement from the couch. A groan. The sound of someone fighting through pharmaceutical fog.
"Aya?" Amina's voice was slurred. "What… What time is it? Why does my head feel like—"
She stopped, processing the destruction in the room. The broken glass. The shattered door. Aya on her knees, bleeding, crying.
"What happened?" Amina pushed herself upright, swayed, and grabbed the couch arm. Her voice sharpened with each word as adrenaline burned through the sedative. "Where's Leyla? Where are the components?"
"Gone." The word came out flat. Dead. Like something inside Aya had been cauterized. "She took everything. She was Aegis from the beginning. She drugged us and took everything."
"That's—" Amina stopped. Her face cycled through the same stages Aya had experienced: confusion, denial, the desperate search for an alternative explanation. And then, as the truth landed, rage. Pure, incandescent rage that Aya recognized because she felt it too.
"I'm going to fucking hunt her down and kill her," Amina said quietly. The words weren't hyperbole. They were a promise.
"Get in line."
From the bedroom, a thud of moving furniture. Karim was awake.
Aya forced herself to stand, her wounded leg buckling, catching herself on the counter. Glass bit into her palm. She didn't care.
"Help me with him," she said. "We need to move if we can. She's hours ahead of us."
They found Karim struggling to sit up, his face gray and confused, still fighting through the drug fog. Amina helped him swing his legs over the side of the bed while Aya stood in the doorway, something nagging at the back of her mind.
His hand went to his pocket instinctively. Checking. Always checking.
"Karim." Aya’s voice came out sharper than intended. "What's in your pocket?"
"What?" He blinked at her, uncomprehending.
"Your pocket. You've been checking it for days. Touching it like it's something precious. What are you carrying?"
He stared at her, the fog slowly clearing, understanding dawning.
"She took everything by the way," Aya pressed, the words coming faster now. "Every component. The crown lens, the fragments, the rings, the charts, all of it. So whatever you've been hiding, whatever you took from Cappadocia that you never told anyone about, I need to know right now if we still have a chance."
The color drained from Karim's face. "I knew you’d noticed, but I didn’t know how to tell you. I would have though, eventually. But Leyla never noticed. She didn't search me," he said slowly, the words thick with disbelief. "She must have thought she'd gotten everything from the table. She didn't—"
"What is it, Karim?"
He pulled out the small wafer, intricate and ancient, covered in engravings too fine to make out, with a precisely drilled hole through its center. It was flexible, but weighed at least two kilograms.
Aya stared at it.
"The stabilization array," Karim said. "I took it from the workshop in Cappadocia. During the escape, when everything was in chaos. I wanted to hold it just in case there was another way. To study it and see how this impossible metal was constructed, to see if it came from deep space. I couldn't—I couldn't let it all be lost. I was going to tell you, I swore to Hamza I would, but then things kept happening, and I kept finding reasons to wait and—"
"What does it do?" Aya interrupted, her voice flat with exhaustion.
"Without it, I don’t think the lens can maintain coherence." Karim met her eyes, his expression shifting from guilt to desperate certainty. "Malik can assemble everything else. Every piece Leyla gave him. But if he tries to activate without this component, the system should cascade into catastrophic failure. Uncontrolled dimensional fractures. It'll tear itself apart, and probably him with it."
The words hung in the air.
"So even with everything she took—" Amina started.
"He can't achieve a completely stable activation. Not without this." Karim held up the wafer, brass catching a sunbeam. "This is the one piece that makes the difference between controlled operation and catastrophe."
Aya took the wafer from his hand. It was heavier than it looked, the brass dense and warm. This tiny piece of metal, stolen in a moment of academic selfishness, hidden out of guilt and indecision, and the lure of the historical find.
It was the only thing standing between Malik and success.
"You should have told me," Aya said quietly. “So you and Leyla were both lying to us.”
"I know, yeah, but it’s different."
"Hamza told you to tell the rest of us about it. And you didn't."
"I know." Karim's voice cracked. "I was wrong. I kept rationalizing—thinking I'd give it to you after Bodrum, after we were safe, after I'd decided what to do. But I was lying to myself. I wanted to keep it because I wanted the option to study it. To understand." He laughed, the sound hollow and bitter. "I told myself I was different from Malik. That my motives were pure. But I was doing the same thing he does—deciding that I knew better than everyone else, that my judgment should override centuries of wisdom."
Aya worked to suppress her anger at the secrecy, the broken trust, the months of careful distance between her and Karim that had apparently included this fundamental deception. But she was also holding their only hope in her hand. Because Karim might have accidentally saved them, in his academic selfishness, his scholarly compulsion to preserve.
"We'll talk about this later," she said finally. "Right now, we need to get to Istanbul and find Malik.”
"How?" Amina had made her way to the doorway, leaning heavily against the frame. "We have no car, no components, no plan. Leyla has hours on us."
Aya looked at Leyla's abandoned phone on the floor and dialed the one number that might still answer.
"Omar," she said when the line connected. "We need extraction from Bodrum. ASAP. Leyla was working for Aegis. She took everything, and she's heading to hand it all over to Malik."
A long pause. When Omar spoke, his voice was stripped of its usual humor, hard and focused in a way she'd rarely heard. "How fast can you move?"
"As fast as we have to."
"Give me five minutes. I need to make calls. I'll get back to you."
The line went dead.
Aya stood in the wreckage of the villa, Leyla's phone in her hand, fighting the urge to hurl it against the wall. Five minutes. They had maybe hours before Leyla delivered everything to Malik.
The phone rang.
"Two rental cars are being delivered to your location in forty minutes," Omar said without preamble. "SUVs, clean plates, full tanks. The rental company thinks they're doing a favor for a corporate client. Keys will be left in the ignition."
"Two cars?"
"One more chance, and a little diversion. Seven-hour drive to Istanbul if you push it. You can be there by early evening." His voice shifted, became more personal. "And I'm coming personally."
Aya's chest tightened. "Omar, you don't have to—"
"Yes, I do." His voice was firm, allowing no argument. "I've been running logistics from the background this entire time. Sending you into danger while I stay safe in my networks. Not this time. You need support on the ground in Istanbul. Someone who has resources, who can coordinate in real-time. That's me. I'm already packing."
"Omar—"
"I fucked us, Aya. I should have seen this coming. I've worked on and off with Leyla for years. Trusted her. Vouched for her to you. She played all of us, but I'm the one who brought her into the circle. This is on me." He paused. "I can't undo that. But I can be there when you face Malik. I can provide what you need on the ground. Let me do this. Plus, I’ll beat you there by plane; you’re the one with a long drive ahead of you."
"What about Malik? What do you know?" Aya asked. "Where's he setting up?"
"Galata Tower, just as we suspected. He's been moving equipment there for days, using the renovation as cover. The alignment is tonight at 11:30 PM. Whatever he's planning, that's when it happens. It’s almost 9:30. You have maybe fourteen hours."
Fourteen hours. They had to cross Turkey, infiltrate one of Istanbul's most iconic landmarks, and stop a madman with the complete Astral Lens from tearing holes in reality.
With nothing but a single brass wafer and the rage of three ragged people who'd been betrayed.
"I'll arrange everything I can from the road," Omar continued. "Weapons. Intel on Galata's current security. Building schematics. Aegis movement patterns. By the time you reach Istanbul, I'll have a safe house ready—one Leyla doesn't know about—and everything else you need to hit that tower."
"Thank you," Aya said, and meant it.
"Don't thank me yet. Thank me when we've stopped this, and you're all still breathing." A pause. "Drive safe. See you in Istanbul."
Aya stood in the ruins of the villa's main room, the brass wafer heavy in her palm, Leyla's phone heavy in her pocket.
“Team neutralized but alive. I couldn't do it.”
Leyla had shown them mercy. Had chosen not to kill the people she'd fought beside, even when the mission demanded it.
Aya hoped she'd have the same luxury when they finally caught up with her, but she doubted it.
Chapter 39
The highway north from Bodrum was empty at 4 AM, taillights sparse, darkness absolute beyond the rental car's headlights. 
Leyla drove with both hands locked on the wheel, the bags of components secured in the trunk, wrapped and padded like they contained something more fragile than the future.
Three syringes lay in the passenger footwell, spent. One for each of them. She'd moved through the villa like a ghost, checking pulses after each injection, making sure the doses were right. Too much and they'd never wake up. Too little and they'd fight.
The memory of Aya's face, peaceful in drugged sleep, made her stomach turn.
I couldn't do it.
That's what she'd written. Truth and confession. Malik had been explicit three days ago. Retrieve all components. Eliminate team. No witnesses.
But when she'd stood over Aya with the gun in her hand, safety off, watching Aya's chest rise and fall with drugged breaths—
She couldn't.
Amina on the couch, silver tattoos catching moonlight.
Karim in the bedroom, face gray from decompression, looking young and vulnerable without his glasses.
She'd aimed. Counted down from three. Twice.
Then put the gun away, grabbed the components, and ran.
Emre's face filled her mind. Not the recent video Malik would use as bait, but the first one. Two months ago. His nose was broken, blood crusting his lip, voice cracking. "Please, Leyla. I can't, I can't take much more of this. Just give them what they want. Please, my work doesn’t matter to me anymore."
That had been the moment. When she'd heard him break, heard the man she loved, the smartest person she’d ever met, reduce himself to begging. That's when she'd known there was no choice.
She would burn the world to save him.
If that made her a monster, so be it.
The coast road climbed into the hills, leaving the Mediterranean behind. Dawn was coming, the eastern sky shifting from black to deep purple. By the time the sun rose, she'd be in Istanbul. By tonight, Malik would have everything he needed.
And Emre would be free.
Or you've doomed him along with everyone else. Malik doesn't release hostages. He eliminates loose ends.
She pushed the thought away. Had to believe. Had to hope. The alternative was admitting she'd betrayed people who'd fought beside her for nothing. Betrayed the world for nothing.
Her hands tightened on the wheel until her knuckles went white.
Behind her, somewhere in Bodrum, she knew they were waking up. Finding the empty table. Reading her message on the backup burner phone she’d left behind.
Aya would come for her, no matter what she said. Leyla knew it with absolute certainty. Aya didn't forgive betrayal. She would hunt her, and when they met again—
Leyla’s phone lit up. Unknown number.
She answered on speaker, both hands staying on the wheel.
"You're making good time." Malik's voice, smooth and pleasant, like he was discussing dinner plans.
"I have everything," Leyla said, voice flat. "Crown lens, calibration rings, focusing array, crystal heart. All of it."
"And the team?"
"Alive. Drugged. They'll be hours behind me."
Silence on the line. Long enough that Leyla's pulse started racing, wondering if mercy had just signed Emre's death warrant.
Then: "Interesting choice."
"I completed the mission. You have what you need."
"True." A pause. "Tell me, Leyla, did you leave them alive out of compassion or calculation? Hoping they'll stop me before I can proceed? That Aya Amrani will be your redemption?"
"I left them alive because killing them wasn't originally part of our deal," Leyla said. The lie came smoothly. "You wanted components. I delivered. Plus, they’re so drugged they have no chance of recovering and stopping you."
"Hmm." The sound carried weight. "Well. Either way, it doesn't matter. By the time they reach Istanbul, you’re right, it will be far too late. The alignment window is closing. Even if they come—and they will—they'll arrive to find it finished."
"And Emre?"
"Will be waiting. Galata Tower. You'll deliver the components to the eighth-floor assembly chamber, then collect your fiancé. A clean exchange."
"I want to see him first. Proof of life."
"You'll see him when I say you see him." Malik's voice hardened. "You're not negotiating from strength, Dr. Yilmaz. You have what I want, but I also have what you want. The difference is, once our exchange is made, I will still have the resources to finish you or Emre. Can you say the same?"
Leyla's jaw clenched. "No."
"Then drive safely. We'll see you in a few hours."
The line went dead.
Leyla threw the phone into the passenger seat, her hands shaking.
She'd done it. Crossed every line, burned every bridge, betrayed people who'd treated her like family. And for what? The promise that a man who dealt in murder and manipulation would keep his word?
But what else could she have done?
You could have trusted them, she told herself. Told Aya everything. Worked together to save him.
Too late for different choices.
The highway stretched ahead, carrying her toward Istanbul, toward Malik, toward whatever ending waited in a medieval tower where ancient power and modern ambitions would collide.
Behind her, the sun finally broke the horizon.
Chapter 40
For a moment, they stood there, surrounded by the debris of betrayal and the kindness of strangers. The contrast felt obscene.
"Quick coffee first," Aya said, grabbing a cup. "I need to shake this grogginess from whatever she shot us up with. Then we drive."
She tried to drink but her hands shook, trembling from exhaustion and rage and too many emotions compressed into too small a space. Coffee sloshed over the rim, burning her fingers. She gripped the counter, forced her hands steady, tried again.
Beside her, Karim filled a water bottle and drank without pause, draining it, filling it again. Water ran down his chin. He didn't seem to notice, just kept drinking like his body couldn't get enough.
Amina stood at the window, eating a sandwich mechanically, chewing and swallowing without tasting, staring at nothing. When Aya touched her shoulder, she startled like she'd been hit.
"We need to move," Aya said gently.
"I know." Amina's voice was rough. "Just give me a minute to stop wanting to murder her."
"Take your time. We've got thirteen and a half hours until the alignment."
Amina checked her phone. "It's 10 AM. If we leave in thirty minutes, seven-hour drive... we arrive Istanbul around 5:30. That gives us maybe six hours to prep and infiltrate."
"Not enough time," Aya said.
They loaded two rental cars in the villa's driveway. White Toyota. Silver Nissan.
"I'll take the Toyota with Amina," Aya said. "You take the Nissan. Stay close, don't lose us in traffic."
Karim nodded, keys in hand. His face was drawn, exhausted. "Straight through? No stops?"
"Not unless we have to. We're already behind."
They left Bodrum at 10:27 AM.
Aya started the engine and pushed the Toyota hard, weaving through morning traffic, the speedometer climbing past 140 kph. Behind them, Karim's Nissan struggled to keep pace.
For the first hour, Aya and Amina drove in silence. The highway stretched endlessly ahead, gray asphalt under gray sky.
Behind them, Karim's silver Nissan maintained steady distance. Not too close, not too far.
"Say it," Aya finally said, eyes on the road.
"Say what?"
"Whatever you're thinking. Get it out."
Amina was quiet for a long moment, staring out the passenger window at landscape blurring past. Then:
"We trusted her."
"Yes."
"We fought beside her. Bled with her. Amina's hand moved unconsciously to where the djinn marks used to burn. "I told her things. About my past. About what happened in Morocco. About us. Things I don’t tell anyone."
"I know."
"And she drugged us and left us helpless while she delivered every weapon we've been trying to keep away from Malik straight into his hands."
"Yes."
"And now we're driving north with nothing but rage and a stolen component to stop a man who has everything."
Aya's hands tightened on the wheel. "Yes."
"Good." Amina leaned back, closing her eyes. "Just wanted to make sure we were on the same page."
They drove in silence again after that. Kilometers disappearing, white lines pulsing under their wheels, the sun climbing higher.
The coast gave way to hills, then mountains. Towns blurred past. Traffic thickened as they approached the Marmara region.
Then, north of Denizli, everything stopped.
Brake lights stretched ahead for kilometers. A semi had jackknifed across two lanes. Police lights strobed. Ambulances. The highway had become a parking lot.
"Shit," Aya breathed, checking the time. 3:47 PM. "Shit, shit, shit."
They sat in gridlock for forty-three minutes while the sun crawled toward the horizon and their window narrowed with every passing second.
"What are you going to do when you see her again?" Amina asked finally, eyes still closed.
"I don't know."
"Bullshit. You're Aya Amrani. You always know."
"Then I'll do what needs doing." The words came out harder than intended. "We stop Malik. Everything else is secondary."
"Even her?"
"Even her."
Amina opened her eyes, looked at her. "I don't believe you."
"Neither do I," Aya admitted. "But I'm going to try."
More silence. The kind that wasn't comfortable but wasn't hostile either.
"You know what's funny?" Amina said after another moment passed. "In a really fucked up way?"
"What?"
"I get it. In a really fucked up way." Amina's laugh was bitter. "Not that I'd do what she did. But I understand the impulse. When someone has leverage on the person you love—when it's their life or the mission—your brain just... breaks."
Aya glanced at her. "You walked away from me in Egypt."
"I know." Amina's voice went quiet. "I chose the tactical play over you. And you know I've hated myself for it every day since." She paused. "But that's the thing, I made the 'right' choice. Mission over person. And it destroyed me anyway. Leyla made the opposite choice. Person over mission. And it's destroying everything." Another pause. "There's no good answer when someone you love is the leverage."
"She still chose wrong."
"Yeah. She did." Amina turned to look at her directly. "But I chose wrong too. Just in a different direction. And now I'm here kissing you on a boat deck, knowing it would hurt Karim, knowing it introduces instability in the middle of the most important mission we'll ever have. Knowing it's selfish and messy and destructive." She looked away. "Maybe I haven't learned as much as I thought."
"That's not the same as what Leyla did."
"No. The scale is different. But we're both putting what we want ahead of what's right. She chose Emre over the world. I choose you over... whatever the hell honor or professionalism or basic decency would've been." She laughed again, darker. "We're all just choosing our own flavor of selfish and calling it something else."
Aya didn't have an answer to that. Aya laughed. It came out more bitter than amused, edged with hysteria, but it was still a laugh.
They drove through a small town, its streets quiet in late morning, old women in doorways watching them pass, children kicking a ball in a dusty square.
"I'm sorry," Aya said after the laughter faded. "For putting you in that position. For making this mess."
"You didn't make me do anything. I knew what I was offering. You knew what you were choosing, too. We're adults. It's messy, but it's honest."
"That doesn't make it right."
"No," Amina agreed. "But it makes it real. Which is more than you can say about a lot of things that are supposedly right."
When traffic finally moved again, they made it another hour before Karim's headlights flashed twice. Their signal.
At the next rest area, Karim pulled alongside them. "We need to consolidate," he called through the window. "Two rental cars from Bodrum arriving at the same time? Aegis is watching the highways. We're painting a target."
Aya nodded immediately. "Leave the Nissan. We finish this together."
Karim grabbed his pack from the abandoned rental and climbed into the Toyota's back seat.
"What's wrong?" Aya asked, merging back onto the highway. “I can tell something’s up.”
"I've been thinking about what Hamza said." Karim's voice came from the darkness of the back seat. "About me being like Malik."
"You're not—"
"Let me finish." He leaned forward between the seats. "He was right. Not completely, but enough. I stole that wafer because I couldn't stand the idea of knowledge being destroyed. I lied to you for days because I thought I knew better than centuries of guardians." His voice cracked. "That's exactly Malik's logic."
Aya kept her eyes on the road, weaving through traffic. "The difference between you and Malik isn't the impulse. It's what you do with it. You handed me the wafer. No conditions. You surrendered it when it mattered."
"What happens to it after?" Karim asked quietly. "The wafer. The other components. Do we destroy them? Scatter them? Lock them away?"
"Ask me after we stop Malik."
"And if we don't stop him?"
"Then it won't matter."
Silence filled the car. Aya checked the mirror. Karim's face was barely visible in the dimness, but she could see the exhaustion, the shame.
Beside her, Amina reached back and squeezed his shoulder once. "You're still one of the good ones. Remember that."
After a moment, Karim settled back. "Thanks."
They drove on. Kilometers disappeared, hours ticked down.
Traffic thickened to near-gridlock. Horns. The call to prayer. The eternal chaos.
The sun was setting when they finally hit Istanbul's outer sprawl. The city announced itself in layers. Concrete apartment blocks, industrial zones, then the smell through the vents: exhaust, grilled meat, salt water, millions of people.
Aya checked the time: 5:53 PM.
"We made it," Amina said.
"Barely." Aya's hands ached from gripping the wheel. "We have five and a half hours to save Istanbul."
“I’ll take our odds,” Amina said, before rolling the window down and lighting a cigarette.
Chapter 41
As Amina continued to blow smoke out the window, they navigated through Karaköy's narrow streets, following the GPS coordinates Omar had sent. Stone buildings pressed close, laundry hung from balconies, and cats watched from windowsills.
"There," Amina said, pointing.
A textile warehouse stood at the corner, paint peeling, a faded sign advertising import-export services. And in front, backlit by a streetlamp, a figure waited.
For a moment, Aya thought exhaustion was making her hallucinate.
Then he moved with his distinctive rolling walk, arms spreading wide, and she knew it was real.
"SURPRISE!" Omar's voice boomed across the street, cutting through evening sounds, making heads turn. "In the flesh, okti! In the actual flesh!"
Aya pulled to the curb, killed the engine, and was out of the car before she'd consciously decided to move. She crossed the distance in three strides and crashed into him, arms wrapping tight.
He smelled like cologne and tobacco and safety. Like the carpet shop where this had all started. His arms came around her, solid and real, and she felt release.
"Easy, easy," he murmured, holding her. "I'm here. You're not alone." He pulled back, hands on her shoulders, examining her face. "But, habibti, you look terrible. Absolutely terrible. Mashallah, good thing I'm here to fix you up."
Aya wanted to laugh or cry or both. Settled for something between. "When did you even leave Morocco?"
"As soon as you called, I headed for the airport, and bless Turkish Airlines for having on-board wifi." 
He released her and turned to embrace Amina as she approached. "Amina! Still beautiful, still approaching murder. I can see it in your eyes. The silver suits you, by the way. Much better than what my country did to you."
He held her at arm's length, examining the marks on her arms. "Salah told me what happened in Cappadocia. About the sacrifice. Are you okay?"
"Define okay," Amina said, but she was smiling despite herself. "I'm functional. That counts for something."
"It counts for everything." Omar turned to Karim, who'd hung back slightly, and before Karim could protest, grabbed his face in both hands. "Doctor Archaeologist! Still following Aya into disasters, I see. Someone has to document them for history, yes?"
"Good to see you, Omar," Karim managed, awkward but clearly relieved to see a friendly face.
Omar released him, stepped back, and studied all three of them with eyes that missed nothing. The exhaustion, the barely-contained fury, the way they held themselves.
"Come," he said, his voice gentling. "We have work to do, yes. But first—" He gestured toward the warehouse. "First, you eat. You shower. You breathe. Then we plan how to stop this madness."
He led them through the warehouse's main floor. Real business, Omar explained—bolts of fabric on industrial shelving, a few employees finishing their evening shift, completely legitimate. At the back, an unmarked door led to stairs.
"Check it out, check it out!" Omar called as they climbed. "My impromptu war room. Better than the carpet shop in Marrakech, no?"
The second-floor loft opened before them.
Aya stopped in the doorway, taking it in. Omar had three computer monitors arranged around a table, with sleeping mats arranged around the perimeter. A kitchenette had two bags of groceries on the island, while two duffel bags were stacked in the corner, and a ladder led to a rooftop.
"Omar," Aya breathed. "This is—"
"Magnificent? I know." He moved through the space like a proud homeowner, pointing out features. "Legitimate business cover downstairs. War room up here. Roof access for surveillance. Everything you need to stop an apocalypse in style."
He'd laid out food on the central table. Bread, cheese, olives, tomatoes, cucumbers, cold chicken from a rotisserie, and bottles of ayran. The sight of it made Aya realize she was starving.
"Eat first," Omar said. "Then I'll show you the good stuff."
Aya ate standing up because sitting felt like surrender. Her leg throbbed in time with her pulse, and the stitched skin pulled every time she shifted weight. Karim’s hands had stopped shaking, but his jaw was tight, his gaze fixed on nothing as he chewed like it was a task. Amina didn’t startle this time; she just looked empty, cigarette pinned between her fingers like an anchor.
"Come," Omar said. "Before you rest, you need to see this."
He led them up the ladder to the roof.
Istanbul sprawled in every direction. The Golden Horn below, catching city lights. The old city across the water, with its mosques and minarets, silhouetted against the darkening sky. The Bosphorus beyond, carrying ships between continents.
And dominating the skyline, Galata Tower.
That distinctive medieval stone cylinder, topped with its conical roof, rising above the surrounding buildings like a warning. Like a promise. Even at this hour, tiny figures moved on the observation deck—tourists taking photographs, couples watching the city lights come on.
"Three blocks," Omar said, handing Aya binoculars. "Close enough for rapid response and coordination. Far enough for safety, hopefully, if things explode spectacularly."
Aya trained the binoculars on the tower. It looked peaceful. Innocent.
"Just under five hours until alignment," Omar said, checking his watch. "11:30 PM. That's our window."
Aya's stomach dropped. "Five hours to infiltrate, position, and stop him?"
"To infiltrate, yes. To stop him?" Omar's expression hardened. "We make that happen however we can. But first, Salah needs to brief you. Now. While you eat."
"Simple," Amina said dryly.
"Everything's doable with a little planning and magnificent improvisation." Omar grinned. "As you know, I specialize in both."
They descended back into the loft.
"Showers," Omar ordered, pointing. "Five minutes each. You look like hell and smell worse. I'll get Salah on the line."
The showers were brutally fast. Aya let hot water pound her shoulders for three minutes, scrubbing away Bodrum and the road, then forced herself out. No time for absolution. No time for anything but moving forward.
When she emerged in clean clothes Omar had laid out, Salah's face filled the center monitor.
"There she is," Salah said from Marrakech. "Looking significantly less drugged, that’s a bonus for these trying times. How're you feeling?"
"Like I want to kill someone slowly." Aya moved to the table, grabbed bread and cheese, and ate standing up. "What do we know?"
Beside her, Karim emerged from the bathroom, hair still wet, and immediately reached for the food Omar had laid out. Amina was last, coming out in only a towel and mechanically tearing chicken while Salah began his briefing.
"My fine folks, I have more than yesterday, but less than I'd like," Salah said, sharing his screen. Satellite imagery appeared, showing Galata Tower and the surrounding neighborhood. "Malik hasn't left the tower since yesterday afternoon. Floor eight is lit up constantly."
"Components?" Karim asked around a mouthful of bread.
"Can't confirm specifics, but based on intercepted communications, Malik is confident. His Turkish government contact received an encrypted message that reads: 'Alignment activation confirmed for 23:30.'" Salah pulled up more data. "They have personnel in place, but they're keeping a low profile. No tactical gear visible yet."
"And Emre?" Aya asked.
"Exact location unknown. But he's in the tower."
Aya studied the tower schematics Salah displayed. "These plans—"
"Are approximations," Salah finished. "The tower's been modified constantly since 1348. Genoese construction, Ottoman renovations, modern changes. There are definitely spaces not documented." He highlighted sections. "Floor eight is the observation deck. But above that, there's a sealed chamber that appears in Ottoman documents, but nothing modern. That's likely where Malik will attempt activation."
"Entry points?" Aya asked.
"Main entrance is heavily secured. Cameras, guards, metal detectors. You'd be made in thirty seconds." Salah highlighted another area. "Maintenance entrance at rear: fewer cameras, still monitored. They'll expect you there too."
He paused, pulled up older hand-drawn plans. "Then we have option three: the Genoese tunnel system. Connects from Bankalar Caddesi area through Byzantine cisterns to the tower foundation. Original construction dates to the 1300s. Whether it's still navigable, whether it actually reaches the tower, whether it's been sealed—I can't verify any of that from here."
"Theoretical," Amina said. "Our favorite word."
"Better than impossible," Salah countered. "But I won't sugarcoat it. Once you're underground, you're on your own. My maps end at the cistern level. After that, you're navigating passages that I don’t believe have been mapped in centuries."
“And if not, the backup plan is to go in guns blazing at the rear entrance?” Amina asked.
Salah grimaced. “Just remember, my tech’s good, and we’ve gotten the drop on Aegis before. But theirs is better, and they’re always one step ahead of us. We have to hope they’re getting arrogant or missed something, like in the Sahara.”
Aya nodded. “Malik’s more calculating, but he has a bigger ego than Léglise. That’s a strong advantage for us.”
"Okay, so the tower was originally a military installation," Karim said, studying the plans. "Defensive position controlling the Golden Horn. Hidden access for siege scenarios would make tactical sense. But modern renovations almost certainly sealed those passages."
“We have to hope they didn’t,” Aya said. “Or that we can break through.”
Omar's grin widened. "Funny you should mention Bankalar Caddesi. I happen to have a very legitimate import-export business contact with a very convenient basement. The previous owner didn't know it was connected to abandoned tunnels. A few strategic walls removed, and voilà. Private access to Istanbul's underground. If we’re lucky, it should link up."
"How did you—" Aya started.
"Trade secrets, okti." Omar's grin was pure mischief. "Let's just say in my past, I was exceptionally good at finding places to hide merchandise. And at negotiating leases with landlords who don't ask questions."
"That’s wicked, Omar," Salah said, shaking his head with admiration. "You beautiful bastard. All right, I'll be monitoring all channels once you're inside. Any changes—reinforcements, timeline shifts, Malik moving equipment that I can see—you'll know immediately."
"Thanks, Salah," she said. "We'll check in once we're inside."
"Inshallah, you all come back up with titanic struggle long behind us," Salah said. "And when you do, the first round of drinks is on me. Marrakech or Istanbul, your choice. Just survive, yeah?"
The screen went dark.
"Time until we need to move?" Aya asked Omar.
Omar checked his watch. "It's 6:47 PM. I'd say you enter those tunnels by 7:00. That gives you four and a half hours to navigate, breach, and position before alignment."
"Thirteen minutes," Karim said quietly. "We have thirteen minutes to gear up and go."
The room fell silent with the weight of it settling over them.
"Then let's move," Aya said.
Omar moved to his tactical cache. "Gear time, ladies and gentleman. We outfit you properly, then you go save Istanbul."
Aya stood, her wounded leg protesting. The knife wound from the underwater fight pulled with every movement, reminding her of Bodrum, of Leyla's betrayal, of everything that had led them here.
He distributed equipment with practiced efficiency. Encrypted earpieces. Glock 19s with suppressors. Magazines. Smoke grenades. Compact breaching charges. Climbing gear. Water. Energy bars. Tools.
"No body armor," Omar said. "You need to move fast, move quiet."
Aya loaded her magazines, checking each round by feel, muscle memory taking over. Beside her, Karim handled his weapon carefully, like he was afraid it might bite. Amina field-stripped hers, checking everything, reassembling in seconds.
"The wafer," Aya said, handing it to Karim. Inside, the brass stabilization array sat cushioned in foam.
He lifted it carefully, examined it in the light. "This is our only advantage. Malik thinks he has everything. But without this—"
"The system cascades," Aya finished. "And we make sure we're positioned to survive when it does."
"Positioned where, exactly?" Amina asked. "If the discharge happens, if dimensional fractures start opening, where's safe?"
Karim studied the schematics on Omar's screen. "Nowhere in the tower's completely safe. But Hassan's notes mentioned that the lens system requires line-of-sight to the alignment. The assembly chamber will be positioned with a clear view of the sky. That's ground zero." He traced a path with his finger. "We need to be at least two floors below, ideally with stone walls between us and the chamber. Let the medieval architecture absorb the worst of it."
"So we infiltrate," Aya said, thinking it through. "Look for a chance to stop them by force—kill Malik, seize the components. If we can't, we observe from a distance. Let Malik think he's winning. And when he attempts activation—"
"We watch him fail," Amina interrupted. "Watch it start to cascade. Then we step in with the shut-off valve before it destroys Istanbul."
"Exactly."
"And Leyla?" Omar asked carefully.
The question hung in the air. The one everyone kept dancing around.
"She made her choice," Aya said finally. "She's standing with him now. If she's in that chamber when it happens—"
"She knew the risks," Amina said, her voice hard. "Whatever happens to her is her own doing. We shouldn't think twice about ending her if we have to."
"She also couldn't kill us," Karim said quietly. "She showed mercy when she didn't have to."
"Mercy doesn't absolve betrayal," Aya said. "It just makes it more complicated."
They sat with that truth for a moment. Then Omar cleared his throat.
"Time check. 6:58 PM. You need to move. Now."
Aya secured the brass component in its waterproof pouch, tucked it inside her tactical vest, where it pressed against her ribs.
"If something happens to me," she said, looking at Amina and Karim, "one of you gets this. Make sure it stays away from Malik."
"Nothing's going to happen to you," Amina said.
"But if it does—"
"If it does," Amina promised, "I'll make sure Malik fails and dies the most miserable, pitiful death we can inflict."
Omar checked his watch. "6:58 PM. Time to move."
He led them down the stairs, through the warehouse, out into Istanbul's night. The contrast was jarring, from tactical silence to the chaos of the city.
They moved through Karaköy like ghosts, Omar's distinctive walk purposeful, none of his usual theatrical energy. This was Omar the smuggler, the man who'd navigated unseen for decades.
They emerged onto Bankalar Caddesi. A warehouse district, quieter than the tourist areas. Omar stopped at a nondescript building, fumbled with keys.
"This is it," he said. "The tunnels start in the basement. From there, you're on your own."
The door opened. Darkness beyond. The smell of dust and damp and centuries.
Omar turned to face them. For a moment, he didn't speak, just looked at each of them in turn.
"I'll monitor from the loft," he finally said. "Salah will feed me updates. If anything changes, you'll know immediately. And when you come back up—" His voice caught slightly. "—when, not if—I'll have hot tea waiting."
Aya embraced him first. He held her tight.
"You come back," he said into her ear. "You hear me?"
"I will."
He released her, turned to Amina. "Keep her alive. Keep each other alive.  I've grown surprisingly fond of you all, and I'd hate to have to find new friends."
"We're not dying today," Amina said.
Finally, Karim. Omar grabbed his face in both hands.
"You're a good man," Omar said. "Trust yourself. Trust your team. Come back so I can truly corrupt you."
Karim managed a small laugh. "I'll do my best."
Omar gestured toward the darkness. "Go forth. Stop this madness."
Aya entered and activated her headlamp. The beam revealed concrete stairs descending into the earth.
"Comms check," she said into her mic.
"Reading you clear," Omar's voice crackled in her earpiece. "Salah confirms. You're live."
"Copy. We're moving."
They descended. Aya leading, Amina in the middle, Karim bringing up the rear.
The temperature dropped with each step. The air grew damp, cool, carrying the mineral smell of deep earth. The outside world faded until it was just their breathing and their footsteps and the steady drip of water somewhere ahead.
At the bottom, the Byzantine cistern stretched before them, vast enough that her narrow beam couldn't reach the far wall. The brick ceiling held up by hundreds of columns, each one different, like they were scavenged from Roman temples and Greek buildings, and structures Aya couldn’t identify. Corinthian capitals with acanthus leaves. Ionic scrollwork. Plain Doric simplicity.
Shallow water covered the floor, reflecting their headlamp beams like a dark mirror.
"Beautiful," Karim breathed.
"It's also massive," Amina said. "How do we find the right path?"
"Follow the lights, smugglers have been using these tunnels," Aya said, spotting faint reflective strips along what looked like a maintained route.
"This is it," Aya said. "Omar, we might be going dark for a bit. The passages might block the signal."
"Understood. Call when you can. And Aya?" A pause. "You’ve got this."
They moved forward into the water, into the darkness, into the ancient passages that would, if they were lucky, lead them to Galata Tower's foundation.
Time collapsed toward 11:30 PM, toward whatever ending had been written in the stars four centuries ago when Çelebi first understood what he'd created and chose to create a secret society to hide it from the world.
Chapter 42
Leyla's hands shook as she positioned the calibration ring, aligning the lunar-phase markings with the indicators Malik had etched into the mounting bracket. The brass was cool under her fingers.
"Steady," Malik said from across the chamber. He was working on the focusing array, his movements precise and assured. “If the alignment is off by even a degree, the entire flow cascades.” He didn’t look up as he said it, just thumbed a knurled micro-adjuster on the focusing array. A barely audible tick. Another. The brass ring shifted by a fraction.
"I know." Her voice came out sharp. The ring clicked into place with a sound that felt too final.
The chamber Malik had chosen was perfect. The sealed space was between the observation deck and the conical roof, eight-sided, reflecting Çelebi's obsession with astronomical symbolry. Windows on each wall were well-positioned to track planetary movements, and in the center, the assembly platform where components now gathered like pieces of a puzzle.
Crown lens. Crystal heart. Brass cylinder. Both calibration rings. The focusing array. Everything Aya's team had fought for, bled for, nearly died for.
Everything Leyla had stolen while they slept, drugged and helpless.
"Second ring," Malik said, not looking up. "Planetary orbit. Three o'clock position."
Leyla retrieved it from the case. She hadn't eaten since the previous day. Hadn't slept except in brief, nightmare-plagued intervals where she saw Aya's face peaceful in drugged sleep and her own hand holding the gun she couldn't force herself to use.
I couldn't do it.
The words from her message haunted her. Truth and confession and weakness all compressed into four words.
She positioned the second ring, aligned the planetary markings. Click. Another piece of the machine was assembled. Another step toward whatever Malik believed would happen at 11:30 PM when the planets aligned and he attempted to open doors Hassan had died trying to keep closed.
"Where is he?" Leyla asked without planning to. "Emre. You said he'd be here. That I could see him once I delivered everything."
Malik finally looked up. His face was calm, the expression of a teacher explaining something to a slow student. "And you will. After activation. After we've confirmed success. Then you collect your fiancé and go wherever you want, free. Stay in Turkey, travel Europe, relax on a beach in the Maldives. I'll provide papers, money, and a fresh start. You have my word."
"Your word." The laugh escaped before she could stop it. "Yeah, the word of a man who kidnaps and tortures to get what he wants."
"I merely apply appropriate leverage." Malik returned to his work. "And I haven't tortured Emre. He's been quite cooperative since you started delivering results."
The casual way he said it made her want to scream. Or cry. Or grab one of the brass components and beat him with it until he stopped moving.
But she couldn't. Because he had Emre. Because she'd come too far.
"They're coming," she said quietly. "You know that. Aya won't let this go. She'll find a way inside."
"Let her come." Malik positioned the crystal heart in its cradle, checked alignment. “By the time she arrives, it will be too late.” He seated the crystal heart, then leaned in and adjusted its cradle with two fingers, nudging it and easing it back until the heart stopped trembling and the faint blue thread in the channel ran straight instead of wavering. 
“The activation window is only a few hours away,” he continued. “Even if she finds a way in, she'll arrive to find it finished. And then she'll have a choice: accept what I've accomplished or die like her father." He smiled. "I'm hoping she chooses acceptance. She has potential in our organization and a place at the table when we accomplish great things for humanity. Her father certainly did, before fear consumed him."
"Hassan was right to be afraid," Leyla said. "This power, it's not meant to be used."
"Says who? Çelebi, who lived in an age of superstition? The Silent Meridian, who hoarded knowledge rather than advancing human understanding?" Malik's voice rose with passion. "We stand at a threshold, Leyla.” 
He slid a thin gauge between two brass teeth, felt the drag, and tightened one set screw by a quarter turn. The gap vanished. The machine obeyed. Malik’s mouth curved as if the world should be as compliant as metal. 
“Climate collapse, resource depletion, geopolitical chaos. Humanity needs an evolutionary leap, not incremental progress. This lens doesn't destroy. It opens. It allows us to access energy states beyond our current understanding. To see things as they truly are rather than through the limited aperture of conventional perception."
"And if you're wrong?" Leyla asked. "If Hassan was right, that partial assembly creates uncontrolled fractures? If activation tears holes in reality that can't be closed?"
"Then we'll have learned something, and Aegis will continue its march toward progress." Malik turned to face her fully. "But I'm not wrong. I have Hassan's research, Çelebi's calculations, and components assembled according to precise specifications. The system will work because I've ensured every variable is controlled."
"Except the one variable you can't control," Leyla said quietly. "The one where this all goes catastrophically wrong."
"Faith, Leyla. Sometimes we must have faith in human ingenuity and a belief in powers we can’t comprehend in this world." He checked his watch. "Go. Rest. You've been working for hours. I need you sharp for tonight, not dead on your feet. There's a break room on floor six with a cot, basic facilities. Sleep for an hour. I'll call you when I need you."
Leyla wanted to argue, but exhaustion was winning. Her eyes burned. Her hands ached. Her mind felt like fog.
"And Emre?"
"Is fine. Sleeping, probably. Like you should be." Malik's expression softened slightly. "I keep my promises, Leyla. You delivered everything I needed. When this is over, when I've proven what's possible, you'll have him back. Both of you free and clear, far away from here. That's what you want, isn't it? To disappear together? To pretend none of this happened?"
"Yes," she whispered.
"Then trust me. A little more time. Then it's over, and you can begin again."
Leyla left the chamber, descended the spiral stairs to floor six, and found the break room Malik had mentioned. Cot with a thin mattress. Sink with rust stains. A window where the sun climbed higher over a city that had no idea what was coming.
She lay down without removing her shoes. Closed her eyes. And tried not to see Aya's face, tried not to hear Amina's laugh, tried not to remember Karim explaining Byzantine architecture like they had all the time in the world.
Tried not to calculate how many people might die if Malik was wrong.
Tried to believe that saving one person she loved was worth the risk of destroying everything.
But exhaustion was merciful. Within minutes, she was asleep, dreamless and deep, while above her, Malik continued assembly, and below her, Istanbul lived its oblivious afternoon, and three people descended into tunnels carrying the piece that could make his grand vision collapse into catastrophe.
Chapter 43
The passage beyond the cistern closed around them like a fist tightening. Their headlamps didn’t push the dark back so much as carve narrow cones into it, the edges trembling with dust. Each breath came damp and metallic, as if the stone was stealing oxygen a sip at a time. 
Behind Aya, Amina's breathing echoed in the confined space, steady but close. The passages had been built six centuries prior for moving soldiers and supplies unseen. The walls pinched her shoulders when she inhaled, then let go when she exhaled, as if the tunnel was timing itself to her lungs.
"Omar, comms check?"
Static. Then his voice crackled through, distorted but present. "Still here, habibti. Signal's weak but holding."
"Copy. How long until we lose you completely?"
"Hard to say. Medieval stone plays hell with radio waves. Could be another hundred meters, could be ten. If you lose me, don't panic. I should be able to pick you up again when you're closer to the tower foundation."
"Understood."
Aya counted steps. Forty-seven. Sixty-two. Ninety-one.
At one hundred and fifteen, the passage leveled out and widened slightly. Relief flooded through her. She could breathe without feeling the walls press against her ribs.
Then her headlamp caught something carved into the wall.
She stopped, raised her hand. Behind her, Amina and Karim halted immediately.
The symbol was small. An eight-pointed star within a circle, the rays extending just beyond the border. Exactly matching the designs from Cappadocia, from the cemetery, from every Silent Meridian site they'd uncovered.
"Çelebi's people," Karim whispered, moving beside her. "This is the right path."
"Or it's a very old dead end," Amina said from behind them.
"Only one way to find out." Aya touched the carving, her gloved fingers tracing the channels worn deep. "Keep moving."
Aya checked her watch. 7:32 PM. Four hours until alignment, to breach a fortified tower, and stop Malik.
They were already behind schedule.
The tunnel branched twice over the next twenty minutes. Each time, they found the eight-pointed star marking the correct path. Left at the first junction. Right at the second. The Silent Meridian's breadcrumb trail, four hundred years old and still legible.
At the third branch, Aya drew up quick, and Amina slammed into her from behind.
“Shit.”
The passage ahead was sealed, deliberately bricked up. Someone had wanted to make sure this route stayed closed.
“Of course,” Amina said with a sigh.
"Omar?" Aya tried her mic. Static greeted her. "Omar, if you can hear me, we've got a sealed section. We'll have to breach."
Nothing but white noise in response.
Karim moved forward, examining the brickwork with his headlamp. His hands traced the mortar lines, testing for weakness. "This is probably sixteenth century, roughly the same era as Çelebi. They sealed it after hiding the lens components."
"Can we get through?" Aya asked.
"We brought explosives for this." Karim pulled a compact breaching charge from his pack. "But in a confined space like this, we need to be careful. The blast wave has nowhere to go. And there’s no telling who will hear it."
“Or if the whole damned thing will cave in,” Amina added.
"Okay, we’ll back up fifty meters and pray the ceiling doesn't collapse on us,” Aya said. “We have no choice now.”
Karim worked quickly, placing the small explosive device against the mortar at strategic weak points. But his hands shook as he ran the detonation wire back to their position.
"First time placing charges in a confined medieval tunnel," he muttered. "Let me just... there."
"Ready?" Aya asked.
"No," Amina said. "Do it anyway."
The detonation didn't work.
Karim stared at the trigger in his hand. "That should have—"
"Bad connection," Aya said, moving forward. "We need to re-place the charge."
They worked in silence for another fifteen minutes, sweat dripping despite the cool air, every second ticking away. Finally, Karim had the charge properly seated.
"Now?" he asked.
"Now."
This time, the explosion was catastrophic in the confined space. The pressure wave slammed into Aya's chest like a hammer, pushing her back hard against the tunnel wall. Her ears rang with sharp, painful clarity.
The dust rolled toward them in a slow wave, crawling into mouths and noses and the seams of their gloves. Aya blinked, and the beam of her headlamp turned into a milky column with no depth, like the tunnel had erased distance. 
When the air cleared enough to see, the brick wall had a hole punched through it. Jagged-edged, barely wide enough for a person, but open.
Aya checked her watch through the dust. 8:16 PM. Three hours and fourteen minutes.
"That'll do," Amina said, coughing. "Let's move."
They squeezed through one at a time, and beyond the breach, the tunnel changed.
The walls were older here. Not cut stone but a natural cave, smoothed by water. The floor sloped downward, and every surface was damp.
And on the wall were more markers. Not just the eight-pointed star but other writing.
Karim pulled out his phone. "'Beyond lies the threshold,'" he translated, reading by headlamp. "'Where earth meets artifice, where knowledge sleeps.'"
"Poetic," Amina said. "Does it mention how far to the tower?"
"No. Just that we're close."
They pushed deeper. Stalactites hung from the ceiling like stone teeth. Water dripped constantly, the sound echoing, making it impossible to hear anything else. Every shadow could hide a threat or nothing at all.
Aya's headlamp caught movement ahead.
She raised her fist. Behind her, Amina and Karim froze, weapons appearing in their hands.
The movement came again.
A scatter of bodies broke the dark. Slick backs, claws on stone, eyes catching light and vanishing. The squeals ricocheted, multiplying in the chamber until it sounded like footsteps.
Aya exhaled through her teeth. “Rats.”
"I hate rats," Karim muttered.
"I much prefer them to Aegis," Amina said.
They continued forward, nerves stretched tight, every sound amplified by paranoia and darkness. Then the passage opened abruptly into a chamber.
Aya swept her headlamp across the space and stopped breathing.
They'd found it. The Silent Meridian's final waypoint.
In the center stood a carved stone table, waist-high, its top surface marked with channels that formed the now-familiar eight-pointed star. And on that pedestal sat something small and wrapped in deteriorated cloth.
Karim moved toward it.
"Wait," Aya said, grabbing his arm. "Could be trapped."
"It's been here for centuries. Surely any trap would have degraded by now."
"Or it's exactly as deadly as it was four hundred years ago."
But Karim was already reaching for the object. He unwrapped the cloth carefully, revealing what lay beneath.
A small brass piece covered in familiar etchings.
"Is this another component?" Karim asked.
Aya shrugged. "We’re seriously flying blind. Take it. We need to keep moving."
Karim pulled out his phone, raising it to photograph the inscriptions.
“No,” Aya said, grabbing his wrist in disbelief. “We don’t have time.”
“But these markings—”
“Will still be here later. If we survive.”
She snapped a quick photo herself, one flashless frame, then wrapped the disc back in the cloth and pocketed it. “Move. Now.”
Karim hesitated a fraction, then nodded. “Done. Let’s go.”
Karim looked at the chamber one last time. Then he turned and followed.
Aya checked her watch. 8:48 PM. Two hours and forty-two minutes.
The passage beyond the chamber climbed steeply, carved stairs ascending through bedrock. Aya's legs burned with each step, her wounded side sending sharp complaints that she ignored.
They climbed for twenty minutes before Amina stopped.
"Wait. This doesn't match the schematics." She pointed at a junction ahead. "We should've hit a straight climb. This branches."
Aya swept her headlamp over both passages. Neither showed the eight-pointed star. "Shit. We took a wrong turn back at the chamber."
They backtracked, boots splashing through water that had grown deeper, retraced their steps to the waypoint chamber. Found the passage they'd missed—narrower, easier to overlook in their rush.
"Here," Karim said, spotting a faint star carved near the floor.
Fifteen minutes wasted. Fifteen minutes they didn't have.
9:11 PM. Two hours and nineteen minutes.
The stairs wound upward in a tight spiral, making Aya dizzy, making the walls feel like they were closing in.
Then her headlamp again caught something ahead that made her stop short.
Brick. Modern brick. Blocking the passage completely.
"Another seal," Karim said, moving forward to examine it. His hands traced the work, feeling for weak points. "They sealed the passage from above when they renovated the tower."
"Can you breach it?"
"Yes. But we're directly under the tower foundation now. The explosion will be heard topside."
"How long until Aegis responds?"
"Minutes. Maybe less if they're already on alert for these tunnels."
Aya tried her comm unit. "Omar? Omar, if you're receiving, we're breaching the tower foundation in ninety seconds. Confirm if you copy."
Static. Then, faint beneath the interference: "...copy...breach...monitoring...sixty seconds..."
His voice cut out completely.
"Do it," Aya said to Karim. "Fast and loud. We're out of time for subtle."
Karim placed the breaching charge against the brick, using twice the explosive they'd used earlier. They retreated down the stairs, took cover, and he triggered the detonation.
The explosion shook the passage. Dust filled the air. When it cleared, the wall was barely cracked.
"Fuck," Amina breathed.
"The foundation's reinforced," Karim said, examining the damage. "Modern concrete behind the old brick. We need more charges, but we’re getting close."
They placed two more devices, using most of their remaining explosives. Retreated again. Waited.
This time, the blast was massive. The shockwave punched the air out of Aya’s lungs. The ceiling answered with a deep, settling groan, and dust sifted down in a fine, deliberate rain, as if the tower was deciding whether to keep them. 
Then the groaning stopped. The dust began settling.
The wall had a ragged hole punched through it. Large enough to climb through, but only just.
Aya checked her watch. 9:43 PM. One hour and forty-seven minutes.
"Move," she ordered.
She moved forward through the haze, weapon raised, ready for Aegis to be waiting on the other side.
The breach opened into darkness.
Aya climbed through, boots finding purchase on rubble, emerging into a space that felt different. The air moved here. Temperature shifted. 
She was inside the tower.
The foundation breach had eaten their breaching charges, but they still had shaped charges left: directional, precise, meant to punch through stone, not bring a structure down.
Her headlamp revealed a maintenance corridor, narrow and utilitarian. Electrical conduits ran along the ceiling. Water pipes. Service access.
Behind her, Amina and Karim climbed through the breach.
"We're in," Aya whispered into her mic. "Omar, do you copy?"
His voice crackled back immediately, stronger now. "Copy! You're inside, probably the ground floor maintenance level, northwest corner. I have building schematics displayed. You should be able to reach the service stairs, about twenty meters to your right, through the door at the corridor's end."
"Aegis positions?"
"Copy. Moving to stairs."
“Time check. 9:47 PM,” Amina called out. “One hour and forty-three minutes until 11:30.”
At the corridor's end, a service door stood slightly ajar. Beyond it, Aya could hear voices.
She pressed against the wall beside the door, signaling Amina and Karim to hold position. Carefully, she risked a glance through the gap.
The service stairwell beyond was empty. But voices echoed from above, at least two, maybe more.
"Clear for now," she whispered into her mic. "But they're close. Very close."
Aya eased the door open and slipped through.
They climbed lightly, ensuring their wet boots made almost no sound on concrete stairs. Each landing brought them higher into the tower's interior, closer to whatever Malik was building on floor eight.
At the second-floor landing, the voices stopped them, descending. 
Their conversation sounded casual, Aya guessing a shift change or equipment check, impossible to be sure. But they were coming down, and there was nowhere to hide.
Aya raised her weapon. Beside her, Amina did the same. 
Three meters away. Two meters. Close enough to hear their breathing now.
The first operative rounded the landing corner.
Amina fired twice. Suppressed shots that sounded like coughs in the enclosed stairwell. The first man's chest bloomed red. He collapsed backward, taking his partner down with him.
The second operative shouted, reaching for his weapon.
Amina's shot caught him in the throat. He gurgled, trying to scream, hands going to his neck. She fired again, and the gurgling stopped.
Silence fell, broken only by blood dripping onto concrete.
"Drag them," Aya ordered. "Side corridor. We need to hide the bodies."
They worked fast, pulling both corpses into a maintenance closet, closing the door on dead eyes and spreading blood. It wouldn't fool anyone who actually looked, but it might buy them minutes.
"Omar?" Aya whispered into her mic as they continued climbing.
"Here. What happened?"
"Two down. We're compromised if anyone finds them."
"Then don't let anyone find them. You're four floors below target."
They ascended faster, caution balanced against time. Each landing brought them higher, brought the thrumming in the stone stronger. The tower itself seemed to be holding its breath, waiting for whatever was unleashed at 11:30.
At the sixth-floor landing, Aya caught something through a small window in the stairwell door. She pressed her face to the glass, looking into the floor beyond, and saw him.
Emre.
He sat in what looked like a break room, shoulders slumped, hands wrapped around a cup of tea he wasn't drinking. He looked smaller than in Leyla's photographs. Thinner. Broken in ways that had nothing to do with visible injuries.
But alive.
"Omar," Aya whispered. "We have visual on Emre. Floor six, break room. He's alive."
"Copy. Continue to floor eight. Emre is leverage but not the mission."
"Understood."
Aya pulled away from the window and forced herself to keep climbing. Behind her, she felt Amina's hand briefly touch her shoulder.
They reached the seventh-floor landing at 10:44 PM.
Aya checked her watch. Forty-six minutes until alignment.
Through the stairwell walls, she could hear machinery humming, along with a deep vibration that made her teeth ache. A sound that didn't belong in the physical world.
"He's already starting," Karim whispered, pressing his hand against the wall. "Preliminary activation. Aligning the components."
"Then we're out of time for careful planning," Amina said.
"Omar," Aya whispered. "Floor seven. We’re in position here, ready to plant charges in the ceiling and wait for the action to begin."
"Copy," Omar said in her ear.
Aya eased the stairwell door open, checking the corridor beyond. Empty. Storage rooms and maintenance access, exactly as the schematics had shown. They slipped through, moving directly below where Malik worked.
Karim began unpacking demolition equipment, laying out shaped charges designed to breach upward through medieval stone. Amina positioned herself at the corridor entrance, weapon raised, watching for patrols.
"We have forty-five minutes before full activation," Aya said. "Maybe less if he accelerates the sequence. Karim, how fast can you place those charges?"
"Ten minutes if we're lucky. Fifteen if the ceiling's reinforced."
"Do it. As fast as you can. Amina, watch the corridor. Omar—"
"Still here," his voice crackled. "I'm monitoring everything. You're cutting this close, habibti."
"Story of my life," Aya said.
She secured the brass wafer in its pouch one more time, felt its weight against her ribs. Their one advantage. Their only chance if the breach failed.
Above them, the tower thrummed with building energy, stone resonating with something ancient and terrible and alien.
"I'll check the breach point," Karim said, moving down the corridor with his equipment. "Map the ceiling structure, find the weakest points. Give me five minutes."
His footsteps faded, leaving Aya and Amina alone in the storage room.
Aya slid down the wall, sitting on the cold concrete floor, her wounded leg throbbing after hours of climbing. Through the walls, the terrible thrumming continued.
Amina sat beside her, close enough that their shoulders touched.
"You ready?" Amina asked quietly.
"No," Aya admitted. "But I don't think ready exists for this."
"Probably not." Amina's hand found hers in the darkness, squeezed once. "For what it's worth—I'm glad I'm here. Glad I'm doing this with you. Even if it ends badly."
"It won't end badly."
"But if it does—" Amina turned to look at her directly, their faces barely visible in the dim emergency lighting. "—I love you."
Aya squeezed her hand back, but couldn't find words that felt adequate. Her throat closed. "Amina, I—"
"I know. First, we need to stop the apocalypse," Amina replied in monotone. "Then we worry about our messy lives. Priorities."
Aya smiled. "Priorities."
They sat in silence for another moment, shoulders pressed together, hands clasped.
Then Karim's footsteps echoed back down the corridor.
They stood, professional distance restored, weapons checked, ready.
But Aya's hand still felt warm where Amina had held it.
They had forty-three minutes to stop Malik, or forty-three minutes to have a front-row seat to a global catastrophe.
Chapter 44
Karim returned from his reconnaissance, tools in hand.
"Found the optimal breach points," he said, kneeling beside their gear. "Ceiling looks reinforced in places, but there are structural weaknesses we can exploit."
“Okay, let’s go.”
Aya carried the explosives while Amina held their perimeter, dual-wielded handguns locked on both sources of entry.
Back in position, Karim began placing charges, positioning each at the weak points he'd identified. Aya watched him connect detonation wire between charges, creating a circuit that would blow simultaneously.
"Almost done," Karim muttered. "When you trigger this, we'll have maybe three seconds before the ceiling comes down. We go through fast, or we don't go through at all."
"Fast is the only speed we have left."
Amina moved deeper into the room, checking the walls with her headlamp. "Look, there's an access panel here. Probably ventilation or utilities."
Aya joined her. The panel was small, roughly fifty centimeters square, secured with screws.
"Can you open it?" Aya asked.
"Give me a minute." Amina pulled a multi-tool from her vest, began working on the screws. The first three came out smoothly, corroded but functional. The fourth fought her, stripped and fused. She applied pressure, twisted, and the screw head snapped off completely.
"Fuck." Amina stared at the broken screw, then shrugged. "Three out of four."
She pulled out a small pry bar and snapped the panel open, sending a loud clang through the stairwell. Behind the panel, darkness and the smell of old stone greeted them. 
A utility shaft, cut narrow and vertical, that climbed toward the floor above.
Aya stuck her head inside, looked up. Twelve meters of shaft, maybe fifteen. And at the top, dim light bleeding through another grate.
Floor eight, Aya thought. Malik's chamber.
"I'm going up," Aya said.
"The shaft's too narrow," Amina protested. "You'll get stuck."
"I'll fit. I need to see what we're up against."
Aya unhooked her pack and let it drop to Amina, then clipped her pistol tight against her chest to keep it from catching. 
“If I get stuck, pull,” she said, and didn’t wait for agreement.
Before Amina could argue further, Aya hoisted herself into the shaft. The space was tighter than she'd expected, her shoulders scraping both sides simultaneously. She braced her boots against one wall, her back against the other, and began climbing. Friction and pressure tore at her clothes as she used her legs to push herself upward, centimeter by centimeter of rough stone.
The climb was brutal. Every movement sent pain through her wounded side, where stitches pulled, and her leg, where the fresher stitches barely held. Her shoulders screamed from the awkward angle. Sweat ran down her face despite the cool air.
But she climbed.
Her watch read 10:54 PM. Thirty-six minutes until activation. She needed intel, and she needed it fast.
Three meters. Five. Eight.
The light above grew brighter. Voices filtered down, distorted by echo and distance but recognizable. Malik's voice, calm and measured, gave instructions. Another voice responding. Female, familiar. Leyla.
Ten meters. Twelve.
She reached the upper grate, pressed her face against the metal slats, and looked into the chamber beyond, before she nearly gasped.
The array was magnificent.
The entire eighth-floor chamber had been converted into something between laboratory and cathedral, centuries of research compressed into a single space. Computer monitors displayed real-time data feeds, energy measurements, and astronomical alignments, counting down by the second.
But the center was where Malik's obsession had taken physical form.
The array rose from floor to ceiling, a column of brass and crystal and impossible engineering. It looked like something from a steampunk fever dream, all exposed gears and precision-machined components and energy channels that glowed faintly blue. The crown lens sat at its heart, mounted in a cradle of brass rings that rotated on multiple axes, tracking the sky outside through windows positioned for perfect astronomical observation.
Around the lens, their other components had been integrated into the larger system amidst the pipes and gears. The focusing array branched out like metallic branches, each tip holding a crystal that caught light and held it. The calibration rings formed concentric circles, their surfaces etched with calculations so fine they were barely visible. Energy channels ran through everything, connecting components in patterns that hurt to look at directly.
It was beautiful. Terrible.
And it was working.
Even without activation, the system hummed with latent power. The air around it shimmered like heat haze. Small objects near the array floated slightly, gravity already beginning to warp. Reality was holding its breath.
Malik stood at a control panel, his back to Aya's position, hands moving across touchscreens and mechanical switches.
And beside him, helping position a component Aya couldn't identify—
Leyla.
She looked smaller than Aya remembered. Thinner. Dark circles under her eyes spoke of sleepless nights and suppressed guilt. Her hands moved carefully, but there was a tremor Aya could see even from this distance.
And across the chamber, helping lift equipment—
Emre.
He looked broken. Not physically, but the way he moved spoke of damage that went deeper than wounds. His shoulders hunched forward, and his eyes tracked the Aegis operatives stationed around the room's perimeter with the wariness of someone who'd learned not to trust.
But he was alive. And he and Leyla traded frequent glances, small looks of reassurance, of shared determination to survive this. They believed they would leave together when it was over.
Aya's hands clung to the grate.
She wanted to scream at them through the metal slats. Wanted to warn them that Malik would never let unnecessary witnesses live. That they were both dead the moment the activation finished. That every moment they helped him was another step toward their own executions.
But she couldn't. Could only watch as Leyla positioned components, following Emre’s calculated instructions with the compliance of someone who'd been taught what disobedience cost.
"Good work, Emre. The vision you’ve so kindly helped me construct is so close. But the secondary channels need calibration," Malik was saying, his voice carrying clearly through the shaft. "The energy flow must be precisely balanced across all nodes or the system will cascade unevenly."
"I'm seeing fluctuations in the third ring," Leyla responded, her technical knowledge evident. "Watch this. Twelve percent variance from baseline."
"Compensate with the focusing crystal. Angle it three degrees toward Regulus."
Emre shook his head. "That's not enough compensation for—"
"Three degrees. Trust the calculations." Malik's voice carried absolute certainty. "Hassan's research accounted for variance, and that’s what we’re going off of. The system self-corrects once activation begins."
At the mention of her father’s name, Aya’s jaw clenched so hard her teeth ached.
Malik continued working. "Emre, the primary lens needs cleaning. Dust accumulated during transport. Use the microfiber cloth, no pressure, circular motion."
Aya watched as Malik stepped back from the array, examining it with something approaching love. His hand reached out, almost but not quite touching the crown lens, and his expression shifted. Pride. Wonder. The face of someone who believed he was about to change human history.
"Thirty-three minutes," he said quietly. "Thirty-three minutes until alignment. Until we prove Hassan was wrong to hide this, that Çelebi was wrong, that all of history has been wrong to choose fear over progress."
He turned slightly, and for one breathless moment, Aya thought he could see her through the grate. But his gaze passed over her hiding spot, focused on the array, on his life's work finally realized.
"Begin final checks," he ordered. "Verify every connection, every channel, every component. I want zero variables when we activate."
"The stabilization array," Leyla said carefully. "You're certain we don't need—"
"It can’t be reconstructed, but we don't need it. Stability is not part of the bigger picture." Malik's interruption was sharp. "Plus, the system is designed with redundancy. Multiple pathways, multiple failsafes. If one component fails, others compensate. That's the genius of Çelebi's design, it's self-correcting, as organic as the stars it draws energy from once it gets going."
