Except it wasn't, Aya thought, hopefully not. Not without the heavy wafer currently pressed against her ribs. Without it, the self-correction should fail. The system should cascade into instability. And Malik, for all his confidence, had no idea.
Aya committed everything to memory. The array's configuration. The positions of the Aegis operatives, six visible, certainly more out of sight. The locations of Leyla and Emre. The control panel where Malik worked. The energy channels that seemed most critical.
Then she began descending.
The climb down was faster, gravity assisting, her boots and back scraping against stone as she controlled her fall. Finally, she pulled herself through the opening, landing hard in the storage room where Amina and Karim waited.
"Well?" Amina asked immediately.
Aya checked her watch. 11:02 PM. Twenty-eight minutes left.
Aya pulled herself fully out of the shaft and took a moment to steady her breathing. "It's worse than we thought."
She described what she'd seen. The array's complexity. The redundant systems. The energy already building despite the system being inactive. Malik's confidence. The operatives positioned for security.
And Leyla and Emre, working together, believing they would survive.
"They don't know," Karim said quietly.
"No. They don't."
"Should we warn them?"
Aya wanted to. Despite everything, despite the betrayal, despite the rage that still burned in her chest, she wanted to warn them. Give them a chance to escape before the shooting started.
But reality was crueler than sentiment.
"We can't," she said. "Any attempt at communication tips our hand. Malik realizes we're here, he accelerates or aborts. We lose our only advantage."
"So we let them walk into an execution," Amina said flatly.
"We let them make their own choices. If they fight, if they run, if they finally see what Malik really is, that's on them. But we can't save them and stop him. We have to choose."
The silence that followed was heavy with implications.
Finally, Karim spoke. "What about the array itself? From what you described, destroying it won't be simple."
"No." Aya pulled out her phone and sketched what she'd seen.
Karim studied her sketch. "I recognize some of this. Hassan's work, but modified. Malik expanded the focusing array and added redundancy. See these secondary channels? If we only destroy the primary connections, the system routes energy through backup pathways."
"So we destroy everything simultaneously."
"In the middle of an energy discharge, while reality is potentially fracturing around us, while armed operatives are trying to kill us." Karim looked up, met her eyes. "This is suicide. We need to breach now and try to stop them."
"And if we destroy it during cascade, we contain the damage."
"We contain it to us."
The arithmetic was simple and brutal.
Aya pushed the thought away. Couldn't afford it. Not now.
"Charges are set," Karim said, moving back to examine his work. The shaped charges were positioned at structural weak points in the medieval ceiling, wired in series, ready to punch through the limestone in a single coordinated blast. "Detonator is here—" He placed the compact device on a metal shelf, its single red button gleaming dully in their headlamp beams. "Press it, and we have three seconds before the ceiling comes down. Maybe less."
Aya checked her weapon. Fourteen rounds in the magazine, one in the chamber. Forty-five more rounds in her vest pouches. Not enough if things went wrong. Never enough.
"We go now," she said, her decision crystallizing. "Before he activates. We breach, destroy the array, stop this before it starts."
"Agreed." Amina moved to position beside the breach point, her suppressed Glock held in a two-handed grip despite the pain Aya could see in how she favored her wounded side. "Waiting just gives them time to—"
Then she heard footsteps outside.
All three of them froze, weapons rising to low-ready positions. The footsteps echoed in the corridor outside. Multiple people.
Aya pressed her back against the wall beside the door, signaling Amina and Karim to positions with quick hand gestures. Through the narrow gap beneath the door, she could see shadows passing. Four sets of boots. Maybe five. Moving equipment past their position, coordinating on radios, voices too low to make out words.
The shadows stopped. Not now, Aya thought, not when we’re so close.
Aya's pulse hammered in her ears. She forced herself to breathe slowly, quietly, her finger resting alongside her weapon's trigger guard.
Three seconds of silence. Five. Seven.
Then a voice outside, sharp and clear through the door: "Thermal signature. Northwest storage room. Multiple contacts."
Shit.
"Karim—" Aya started, already knowing what had to happen.
The door handle turned.
"GET DOWN!" Amina shouted.
The door slammed open, and something small and cylindrical bounced across the floor, metallic clink loud in the confined space.
Aya's mind processed the shape—M84 stun grenade, pin pulled, spoon released, two-second fuse—and her body reacted before conscious thought could complete the calculation. She was already turning away, mouth open to equalize pressure, eyes squeezed shut, when the flashbang detonated.
Nine million candela.
One hundred and eighty decibels.
Chapter 45
The light hit first, visible even through closed eyelids, in a white-hot supernova that burned itself into her retinas. Then the pressure wave struck like a physical blow, overpressure slamming into her eardrums, her inner ear's balance system scrambled. Up became down. Left became right. The world tilted violently on an axis that didn't exist.
She fell without knowing she was falling.
The concrete floor hit her shoulder. The impact jarred through her chest, driving air from her lungs, her wounded leg screaming as stitches pulled, but the pain felt distant, processed through a nervous system that couldn't properly interpret signals anymore.
The ringing in her ears was so loud it became its own kind of silence. A high-pitched shriek that drowned out everything else. She couldn't hear the gunfire she knew had started, couldn't hear the screaming that might have been hers or someone else's.
But she could see muzzle flashes strobing through the white haze clouding her vision. Five of them. No, six, and more in the hall. Submachine guns firing in controlled bursts, professional spacing even in the confined room. Bullets sparked off metal shelving. Stone chips exploded from the walls. A round passed so close to her head that she felt the air wave part her hair.
Move. Move Aya. Remember your training.
The word surfaced through the chaos of her mind like debris through murky water. Her hand found her sidearm. She couldn't feel her fingers but watched them close around the grip. Muscle memory raised the Glock, combat stance coming automatically despite the vertigo trying to pull her sideways. Her finger found the trigger.
She fired blindly toward the doorway.
Three quick shots. The weapon kicked in her hands, recoil transmitted through bones and sinew. She couldn't hear the shots but felt them. Reset. Another three rounds. Another.
Her vision was clearing in spots, white-out fading to reveal shapes and shadows. An Aegis operative five meters away, MP5 tracking toward her position. Body armor visible. Ceramic plates over vital organs. Center mass shots would be wasted.
She adjusted her aim upward as he fired. His shot went wide, slightly disoriented by the same flashbang that had scrambled her equilibrium. Aya's next three rounds caught him in the throat above his armor's collar line. Blood sprayed in a mist backlit by muzzle flashes. He went down choking, hands clutching at the ruin of his neck.
The ringing didn’t stop, but it thinned, like someone slowly turning down a screaming radio. Sound leaked back in: the cough of suppressed shots, the slap of boots on concrete, someone shouting in the corridor.

She hit the floor hard, rolled, and came up with her weapon tracking. Rounds tore through the metal shelves above her, sparking, ricocheting. The shelves rang like bells under the impacts, a discordant percussion that cut through even the ringing in her ears.
The operative moved to flank her. Professional. Patient.
Aya came up firing and caught him in the face. Two rounds. His head snapped back, body following, falling backward into the doorway.
But more were coming through. She could see them now, pouring through the door, spreading to cover the room's angles. Close quarters battle, the kind of fighting where speed and aggression trumped precision. Where hesitation meant death.
Across the room, Amina moved.
She didn't run. Didn't dive. Just moved with that economy of motion that Aya had seen countless times, a predatory efficiency where every step served a purpose. One moment, she was behind cover, the next, she was closing distance with the nearest operative, inside his range before he could track her.
Her handgun fired twice, point-blank against the man’s face shield. Subsonic rounds punched through at an angle that sent him spinning sideways. He was still falling when Amina took his MP5 in one fluid motion, slung it, kept her pistol up, already tracking the next target.
But Aya could see the cost in how Amina moved, the slight hitch when she turned, the way her left hand pressed briefly against her wounded side before forcing itself back to the weapon. She was fighting through pain that should have dropped her, holding herself together through will and adrenaline and the knowledge that stopping meant dying.
Automatic fire ripped from Amina's captured rifle. Three-rounds. Shift. Three more. Another operative went down, then another, Amina's tactical sense reading the room's geometry, finding angles, exploiting cover.
As Aya tracked her, she caught a glimpse of Karim, pinned behind the shelf where the detonator sat, trying to return fire. His shots went wide, missing badly. An operative saw the weakness, the vulnerability, and advanced on him.
Aya moved. Pure reaction. 
Her Glock fell to the floor as she closed the distance in three strides, eating the space between them before the operative could register the threat shift. He was focused on Karim and realized too late that she was on him.
Her boot caught him behind the knee, a sharp Muay Thai kick that buckled his leg. He started to turn, MP5 swinging toward her, but she was already inside his guard. Her left hand trapped his weapon arm, driving it down and away. Her right hand found the knife strapped to her thigh.
The blade punched up under his chin where the helmet didn't reach, through soft tissue. A mercy kill. Quick. Efficient. He was dead before he understood he was dying.
She stepped back as his body dropped, knife coming free with a wet sound. Blood sprayed across her hands, warm and slick. The MP5 clattered to the floor beside him.
She snatched her Glock back up off the floor without looking, grip finding grip, and slid behind cover before the room could punish her for standing.

Her magazine was empty.
Aya dropped behind cover, ejected the spent magazine, and reached for a fresh one. Her hands were shaking, the aftereffects of the flashbang and her countless injuries still scrambling her fine motor control. The magazine slipped from her fingers, clattered across the floor. She grabbed for it, missed, and grabbed again.
Radio chatter crackled from the corridor. Backup called. More operatives were coming. They had seconds, maybe less, before this went from desperate to impossible.
"Karim!" Aya shouted over the gunfire, her voice sounding distant and wrong through her damaged hearing. "BLOW IT! NOW!"

Nothing.

Aya slapped her fresh magazine home, chambered a round, and sprinted across the room. Bullets tracked her movement, sparking off stone, tearing through the air centimeters from her body. Blocks of stone that had held for six centuries fractured along fault lines, coming down in chunks ranging from fist-sized to the size of a motorcycle.
